Till then I had\r\nonly known as much as the rest of his friends, namely, that he had\r\nrecently undergone experiences during a yachting cruise with a certain\r\nMr \xe2\x80\x9cDavies\xe2\x80\x9d which had left a deep mark on his character and habits.\r\n\r\nAt the end of his narrative\xe2\x80\x94which, from its bearing on studies and\r\nspeculations of my own, as well as from its intrinsic interest and racy\r\ndelivery, made a very deep impression on me\xe2\x80\x94he added that the important\r\nfacts discovered in the course of the cruise had, without a moment\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ndelay, been communicated to the proper authorities, who, after some\r\ndignified incredulity, due in part, perhaps, to the pitiful inadequacy\r\nof their own secret service, had, he believed, made use of them, to\r\navert a great national danger. I say \xe2\x80\x9che believed\xe2\x80\x9d, for though it was\r\nbeyond question that the danger was averted for the time, it was\r\ndoubtful whether they had stirred a foot to combat it, the secret\r\ndiscovered being of such a nature that mere suspicion of it on this\r\nside was likely to destroy its efficacy.\r\n\r\nThere, however that may be, the matter rested for a while, as, for\r\npersonal reasons which will be manifest to the reader, he and Mr\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDavies\xe2\x80\x9d expressly wished it to rest.\r\n\r\nBut events were driving them to reconsider their decision. These seemed\r\nto show that the information wrung with such peril and labour from the\r\nGerman Government, and transmitted so promptly to our own, had had none\r\nbut the most transitory influence on our policy. Forced to the\r\nconclusion that the national security was really being neglected, the\r\ntwo friends now had a mind to make their story public; and it was about\r\nthis that \xe2\x80\x9cCarruthers\xe2\x80\x9d wished for my advice. The great drawback was\r\nthat an Englishman, bearing an honoured name, was disgracefully\r\nimplicated, and that unless infinite delicacy were used, innocent\r\npersons, and, especially, a young lady, would suffer pain and\r\nindignity, if his identity were known. Indeed, troublesome rumours,\r\ncontaining a grain of truth and a mass of falsehood, were already\r\nafloat.\r\n\r\nAfter weighing both sides of the question, I gave my vote emphatically\r\nfor publication. The personal drawbacks could, I thought, with tact be\r\nneutralised; while, from the public point of view, nothing but good\r\ncould come from submitting the case to the common sense of the country\r\nat large. Publication, therefore, was agreed upon, and the next point\r\nwas the form it should take. \xe2\x80\x9cCarruthers\xe2\x80\x9d, with the concurrence of Mr\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDavies\xe2\x80\x9d, was for a bald exposition of the essential facts, stripped of\r\ntheir warm human envelope. I was strongly against this course, first,\r\nbecause it would aggravate instead of allaying the rumours that were\r\ncurrent; secondly, because in such a form the narrative would not carry\r\nconviction, and would thus defeat its own end. The persons and the\r\nevents were indissolubly connected; to evade, abridge, suppress, would\r\nbe to convey to the reader the idea of a concocted hoax. Indeed, I took\r\nbolder ground still, urging that the story should be made as explicit\r\nand circumstantial as possible, frankly and honestly for the purpose of\r\nentertaining and so of attracting a wide circle of readers. Even\r\nanonymity was undesirable. Nevertheless, certain precautions were\r\nimperatively needed.\r\n\r\nTo cut the matter short, they asked for my assistance and received it\r\nat once. It was arranged that I should edit the book; that \xe2\x80\x9cCarruthers\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nshould give me his diary and recount to me in fuller detail and from\r\nhis own point of view all the phases of the \xe2\x80\x9cquest\xe2\x80\x9d, as they used to\r\ncall it; that Mr \xe2\x80\x9cDavies\xe2\x80\x9d should meet me with his charts and maps and\r\ndo the same; and that the whole story should be written, as from the\r\nmouth of the former, with its humours and errors, its light and its\r\ndark side, just as it happened; with the following few limitations. The\r\nyear it belongs to is disguised; the names of persons are throughout\r\nfictitious; and, at my instance certain slight liberties have been\r\ntaken to conceal the identity of the English characters.\r\n\r\nRemember, also that these persons are living now in the midst of us,\r\nand if you find one topic touched on with a light and hesitating pen,\r\ndo not blame the Editor, who, whether they are known or not, would\r\nrather say too little than say a word that might savour of\r\nimpertinence.\r\n\r\nE. C.\r\n\r\n\r\n_March_, 1903\r\n\r\nNOTE\r\n\r\n\r\nThe maps and charts are based on British and German Admiralty charts,\r\nwith irrelevant details omitted.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER I.\r\nThe Letter\r\n\r\n\r\nI have read of men who, when forced by their calling to live for long\r\nperiods in utter solitude\xe2\x80\x94save for a few black faces\xe2\x80\x94have made it a\r\nrule to dress regularly for dinner in order to maintain their\r\nself-respect and prevent a relapse into barbarism. It was in some such\r\nspirit, with an added touch of self-consciousness, that, at seven\r\no\xe2\x80\x99clock in the evening of September 23 in a recent year, I was making\r\nmy evening toilet in my chambers in Pall Mall. I thought the date and\r\nthe place justified the parallel; to my advantage even; for the obscure\r\nBurmese administrator might well be a man of blunted sensibilities and\r\ncoarse fibre, and at least he is alone with nature, while I\xe2\x80\x94well, a\r\nyoung man of condition and fashion, who knows the right people, belongs\r\nto the right clubs, has a safe, possibly a brilliant, future in the\r\nForeign Office\xe2\x80\x94may be excused for a sense of complacent martyrdom,\r\nwhen, with his keen appreciation of the social calendar, he is doomed\r\nto the outer solitude of London in September. I say \xe2\x80\x9cmartyrdom\xe2\x80\x9d, but in\r\nfact the case was infinitely worse. For to feel oneself a martyr, as\r\neverybody knows, is a pleasurable thing, and the true tragedy of my\r\nposition was that I had passed that stage. I had enjoyed what sweets it\r\nhad to offer in ever dwindling degree since the middle of August, when\r\nties were still fresh and sympathy abundant. I had been conscious that\r\nI was missed at Morven Lodge party. Lady Ashleigh herself had said so\r\nin the kindest possible manner, when she wrote to acknowledge the\r\nletter in which I explained, with an effectively austere reserve of\r\nlanguage, that circumstances compelled me to remain at my office. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\r\nknow how busy you must be just now\xe2\x80\x9d, she wrote, \xe2\x80\x9cand I do hope you\r\nwon\xe2\x80\x99t overwork; we shall _all_ miss you very much.\xe2\x80\x9d Friend after friend\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cgot away\xe2\x80\x9d to sport and fresh air, with promises to write and chaffing\r\ncondolences, and as each deserted the sinking ship, I took a grim\r\ndelight in my misery, positively almost enjoying the first week or two\r\nafter my world had been finally dissipated to the four bracing winds of\r\nheaven. I began to take a spurious interest in the remaining five\r\nmillions, and wrote several clever letters in a vein of cheap satire,\r\nindirectly suggesting the pathos of my position, but indicating that I\r\nwas broad-minded enough to find intellectual entertainment in the\r\nscenes, persons, and habits of London in the dead season. I even did\r\nrational things at the instigation of others. For, though I should have\r\nliked total isolation best, I, of course, found that there was a\r\nsediment of unfortunates like myself, who, unlike me, viewed the\r\nsituation in a most prosaic light. There were river excursions, and so\r\non, after office-hours; but I dislike the river at any time for its\r\nnoisy vulgarity, and most of all at this season. So I dropped out of\r\nthe fresh air brigade and declined H\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99s offer to share a riverside\r\ncottage and run up to town in the mornings. I did spend one or two\r\nweek-ends with the Catesbys in Kent; but I was not inconsolable when\r\nthey let their house and went abroad, for I found that such partial\r\ncompensations did not suit me. Neither did the taste for satirical\r\nobservation last. A passing thirst, which I dare say many have shared,\r\nfor adventures of the fascinating kind described in the _New Arabian\r\nNights_ led me on a few evenings into some shady haunts in Soho and\r\nfarther eastward; but was finally quenched one sultry Saturday night\r\nafter an hour\xe2\x80\x99s immersion in the reeking atmosphere of a low music-hall\r\nin Ratcliffe Highway, where I sat next a portly female who suffered\r\nfrom the heat, and at frequent intervals refreshed herself and an\r\ninfant from a bottle of tepid stout.\r\n\r\nBy the first week in September I had abandoned all palliatives, and had\r\nsettled into the dismal but dignified routine of office, club, and\r\nchambers. And now came the most cruel trial, for the hideous truth\r\ndawned on me that the world I found so indispensable could after all\r\ndispense with me. It was all very well for Lady Ashleigh to assure me\r\nthat I was deeply missed; but a letter from F\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94, who was one of the\r\nparty, written \xe2\x80\x9cin haste, just starting to shoot\xe2\x80\x9d, and coming as a\r\ntardy reply to one of my cleverest, made me aware that the house party\r\nhad suffered little from my absence, and that few sighs were wasted on\r\nme, even in the quarter which I had assumed to have been discreetly\r\nalluded to by the underlined _all_ in Lady Ashleigh\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9cwe shall _all_\r\nmiss you\xe2\x80\x9d. A thrust which smarted more, if it bit less deeply, came\r\nfrom my cousin Nesta, who wrote: \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s horrid for you to have to be\r\nbaking in London now; but, after all, it must be a great pleasure to\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x9d (malicious little wretch!) \xe2\x80\x9cto have such interesting and important\r\nwork to do.\xe2\x80\x9d Here was a nemesis for an innocent illusion I had been\r\naccustomed to foster in the minds of my relations and acquaintances,\r\nespecially in the breasts of the trustful and admiring maidens whom I\r\nhad taken down to dinner in the last two seasons; a fiction which I had\r\nalmost reached the point of believing in myself. For the plain truth\r\nwas that my work was neither interesting nor important, and consisted\r\nchiefly at present in smoking cigarettes, in saying that Mr So-and-So\r\nwas away and would be back about October 1, in being absent for lunch\r\nfrom twelve till two, and in my spare moments making _pr\xc3\xa9cis_ of\xe2\x80\x94let us\r\nsay\xe2\x80\x94the less confidential consular reports, and squeezing the results\r\ninto cast-iron schedules. The reason of my detention was not a cloud on\r\nthe international horizon\xe2\x80\x94though I may say in passing that there was\r\nsuch a cloud\xe2\x80\x94but a caprice on the part of a remote and mighty\r\npersonage, the effect of which, ramifying downwards, had dislocated the\r\ncarefully-laid holiday plans of the humble juniors, and in my own small\r\ncase had upset the arrangement between myself and K\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94, who positively\r\nliked the dog-days in Whitehall.\r\n\r\nOnly one thing was needed to fill my cup of bitterness, and this it was\r\nthat specially occupied me as I dressed for dinner this evening. Two\r\ndays more in this dead and fermenting city and my slavery would be at\r\nan end. Yes, but\xe2\x80\x94irony of ironies!\xe2\x80\x94I had nowhere to go to! The Morven\r\nLodge party was breaking up. A dreadful rumour as to an engagement\r\nwhich had been one of its accursed fruits tormented me with the fresh\r\ncertainty that I had not been missed, and bred in me that most\r\ndesolating brand of cynicism which is produced by defeat through\r\ninsignificance. Invitations for a later date, which I had declined in\r\nJuly with a gratifying sense of being much in request, now rose up\r\nspectrally to taunt me. There was at least one which I could easily\r\nhave revived, but neither in this case nor in any other had there been\r\nany renewal of pressure, and there are moments when the difference\r\nbetween proposing oneself and surrendering as a prize to one of several\r\neagerly competing hostesses seems too crushing to be contemplated. My\r\nown people were at Aix for my father\xe2\x80\x99s gout; to join them was a\r\n_pis-aller_ whose banality was repellent. Besides, they would be\r\nleaving soon for our home in Yorkshire, and I was not a prophet in my\r\nown country. In short, I was at the extremity of depression.\r\n\r\nThe usual preliminary scuffle on the staircase prepared me for the\r\nknock and entry of Withers. (One of the things which had for some time\r\nceased to amuse me was the laxity of manners, proper to the season,\r\namong the servants of the big block of chambers where I lived.) Withers\r\ndemurely handed me a letter bearing a German postmark and marked\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUrgent\xe2\x80\x9d. I had just finished dressing, and was collecting my money and\r\ngloves. A momentary thrill of curiosity broke in upon my depression as\r\nI sat down to open it. A corner on the reverse of the envelope bore the\r\nblotted legend: \xe2\x80\x9cVery sorry, but there\xe2\x80\x99s one other thing\xe2\x80\x94a pair of\r\nrigging screws from Carey and Neilson\xe2\x80\x99s, size 1\xe2\x85\x9c, _galvanised_.\xe2\x80\x9d Here\r\nit is:\r\n\r\nYacht _Dulcibella_,\r\nFlensburg, Schleswig-Holstein, _Sept_. 21.\r\n\r\n_Dear Carruthers_,\xe2\x80\x94I daresay you\xe2\x80\x99ll be surprised at hearing from me, as\r\nit\xe2\x80\x99s ages since we met. It is more than likely, too, that what I\xe2\x80\x99m\r\ngoing to suggest won\xe2\x80\x99t suit you, for I know nothing of your plans, and\r\nif you\xe2\x80\x99re in town at all you\xe2\x80\x99re probably just getting into harness\r\nagain and can\xe2\x80\x99t get away. So I merely write on the offchance to ask if\r\nyou would care to come out here and join me in a little yachting, and,\r\nI hope, duck-shooting. I know you\xe2\x80\x99re keen on shooting, and I sort of\r\nremember that you have done some yachting too, though I rather forget\r\nabout that. This part of the Baltic\xe2\x80\x94the Schleswig fiords\xe2\x80\x94is a splendid\r\ncruising-ground\xe2\x80\x94A1 scenery\xe2\x80\x94and there ought to be plenty of duck about\r\nsoon, if it gets cold enough. I came out here _via_ Holland and the\r\nFrisian Islands, starting early in August. My pals have had to leave\r\nme, and I\xe2\x80\x99m badly in want of another, as I don\xe2\x80\x99t want to lay up yet for\r\na bit. I needn\xe2\x80\x99t say how glad I should be if you could come. If you\r\ncan, send me a wire to the P.O. here. Flushing and on by Hamburg will\r\nbe your best route, I think. I\xe2\x80\x99m having a few repairs done here, and\r\nwill have them ready sharp by the time your train arrives. Bring your\r\ngun and a good lot of No. 4\xe2\x80\x99s; and would you mind calling at\r\nLancaster\xe2\x80\x99s and asking for mine, and bringing it too? Bring some\r\noilskins. Better get the eleven-shilling sort, jacket and trousers\xe2\x80\x94not\r\nthe \xe2\x80\x9cyachting\xe2\x80\x9d brand; and if you paint bring your gear. I know you\r\nspeak German like a native, and that will be a great help. Forgive this\r\nhail of directions, but I\xe2\x80\x99ve a sort of feeling that I\xe2\x80\x99m in luck and\r\nthat you\xe2\x80\x99ll come. Anyway, I hope you and the F.O. both flourish.\r\nGood-bye.\r\n\r\nYours ever,\r\n_Arthur H. Davies_.\r\n\r\nWould you mind bringing me out a _prismatic compass_, and a pound of\r\nRaven mixture?\r\n\r\nThis letter marked an epoch for me; but I little suspected the fact as\r\nI crumpled it into my pocket and started languidly on the _voie\r\ndouloureuse_ which I nightly followed to the club. In Pall Mall there\r\nwere no dignified greetings to be exchanged now with well-groomed\r\nacquaintances. The only people to be seen were some late stragglers\r\nfrom the park, with a perambulator and some hot and dusty children\r\nlagging fretfully behind; some rustic sightseers draining the last\r\ndregs of the daylight in an effort to make out from their guide-books\r\nwhich of these reverend piles was which; a policeman and a builder\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ncart. Of course the club was a strange one, both of my own being closed\r\nfor cleaning, a coincidence expressly planned by Providence for my\r\ninconvenience. The club which you are \xe2\x80\x9cpermitted to make use of\xe2\x80\x9d on\r\nthese occasions always irritates with its strangeness and discomfort.\r\nThe few occupants seem odd and oddly dressed, and you wonder how they\r\ngot there. The particular weekly that you want is not taken in; the\r\ndinner is execrable, and the ventilation a farce. All these evils\r\noppressed me to-night. And yet I was puzzled to find that somewhere\r\nwithin me there was a faint lightening of the spirits; causeless, as\r\nfar as I could discover. It could not be Davies\xe2\x80\x99s letter. Yachting in\r\nthe Baltic at the end of September! The very idea made one shudder.\r\nCowes, with a pleasant party and hotels handy, was all very well. An\r\nAugust cruise on a steam yacht in French waters or the Highlands was\r\nall very well; but what kind of a yacht was this? It must be of a\r\ncertain size to have got so far, but I thought I remembered enough of\r\nDavies\xe2\x80\x99s means to know that he had no money to waste on luxuries. That\r\nbrought me to the man himself. I had known him at Oxford\xe2\x80\x94not as one of\r\nmy immediate set; but we were a sociable college, and I had seen a good\r\ndeal of him, liking him for his physical energy combined with a certain\r\nsimplicity and modesty, though, indeed, he had nothing to be conceited\r\nabout; liked him, in fact, in the way that at that receptive period one\r\nlikes many men whom one never keeps up with later. We had both gone\r\ndown in the same year\xe2\x80\x94three years ago now. I had gone to France and\r\nGermany for two years to learn the languages; he had failed for the\r\nIndian Civil, and then had gone into a solicitor\xe2\x80\x99s office. I had only\r\nseen him since at rare intervals, though I admitted to myself that for\r\nhis part he had clung loyally to what ties of friendship there were\r\nbetween us. But the truth was that we had drifted apart from the nature\r\nof things. I had passed brilliantly into my profession, and on the few\r\noccasions I had met him since I made my triumphant _d\xc3\xa9but_ in society I\r\nhad found nothing left in common between us. He seemed to know none of\r\nmy friends, he dressed indifferently, and I thought him dull. I had\r\nalways connected him with boats and the sea, but never with yachting,\r\nin the sense that I understood it. In college days he had nearly\r\npersuaded me into sharing a squalid week in some open boat he had\r\npicked up, and was going to sail among some dreary mudflats somewhere\r\non the east coast. There was nothing else, and the funereal function of\r\ndinner drifted on. But I found myself remembering at the _entr\xc3\xa9e_ that\r\nI had recently heard, at second or third hand, of something else about\r\nhim\xe2\x80\x94exactly what I could not recall. When I reached the savoury, I had\r\nconcluded, so far as I had centred my mind on it at all, that the whole\r\nthing was a culminating irony, as, indeed, was the savoury in its way.\r\nAfter the wreck of my pleasant plans and the fiasco of my martyrdom, to\r\nbe asked as consolation to spend October freezing in the Baltic with an\r\neccentric nonentity who bored me! Yet, as I smoked my cigar in the\r\nghastly splendour of the empty smoking-room, the subject came up again.\r\nWas there anything in it? There were certainly no alternatives at hand.\r\nAnd to bury myself in the Baltic at this unearthly time of year had at\r\nleast a smack of tragic thoroughness about it.\r\n\r\nI pulled out the letter again, and ran down its impulsive staccato\r\nsentences, affecting to ignore what a gust of fresh air, high spirits,\r\nand good fellowship this flimsy bit of paper wafted into the jaded\r\nclub-room. On reperusal, it was full of evil presage\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cA1 scenery\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94but\r\nwhat of equinoctial storms and October fogs? Every sane yachtsman was\r\npaying off his crew now. \xe2\x80\x9cThere ought to be duck\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94vague, very vague.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf it gets cold enough\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94cold and yachting seemed to be a gratuitously\r\nmonstrous union. His pals had left him; why? \xe2\x80\x9cNot the \xe2\x80\x98yachting\xe2\x80\x99\r\nbrand\xe2\x80\x9d; and why not? As to the size, comfort, and crew of the yacht\xe2\x80\x94all\r\ncheerfully ignored; so many maddening blanks. And, by the way, why in\r\nHeaven\xe2\x80\x99s name \xe2\x80\x9ca prismatic compass\xe2\x80\x9d? I fingered a few magazines, played\r\na game of fifty with a friendly old fogey, too importunate to be worth\r\nthe labour of resisting, and went back to my chambers to bed, ignorant\r\nthat a friendly Providence had come to my rescue; and, indeed, rather\r\nresenting any clumsy attempt at such friendliness.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER II.\r\nThe _Dulcibella_\r\n\r\n\r\nThat two days later I should be found pacing the deck of the Flushing\r\nsteamer with a ticket for Hamburg in my pocket may seem a strange\r\nresult, yet not so strange if you have divined my state of mind. You\r\nwill guess, at any rate, that I was armed with the conviction that I\r\nwas doing an act of obscure penance, rumours of which might call\r\nattention to my lot and perhaps awaken remorse in the right quarter,\r\nwhile it left me free to enjoy myself unobtrusively in the remote event\r\nof enjoyment being possible.\r\n\r\nThe fact was that, at breakfast on the morning after the arrival of the\r\nletter, I had still found that inexplicable lightening which I\r\nmentioned before, and strong enough to warrant a revival of the pros\r\nand cons. An important pro which I had not thought of before was that\r\nafter all it was a good-natured piece of unselfishness to join Davies;\r\nfor he had spoken of the want of a pal, and seemed honestly to be in\r\nneed of me. I almost clutched at this consideration. It was an\r\nadmirable excuse, when I reached my office that day, for a resigned\r\nstudy of the Continental Bradshaw, and an order to Carter to unroll a\r\ngreat creaking wall-map of Germany and find me Flensburg. The latter\r\nlabour I might have saved him, but it was good for Carter to have\r\nsomething to do; and his patient ignorance was amusing. With most of\r\nthe map and what it suggested I was tolerably familiar, for I had not\r\nwasted my year in Germany, whatever I had done or not done since. Its\r\npeople, history, progress, and future had interested me intensely, and\r\nI had still friends in Dresden and Berlin. Flensburg recalled the\r\nDanish war of \xe2\x80\x9964, and by the time Carter\xe2\x80\x99s researches had ended in\r\nsuccess I had forgotten the task set him, and was wondering whether the\r\nprospect of seeing something of that lovely region of\r\nSchleswig-Holstein, _[See Map A]_ as I knew from hearsay that it was,\r\nwas at all to be set against such an uncomfortable way of seeing it,\r\nwith the season so late, the company so unattractive, and all the other\r\ndrawbacks which I counted and treasured as proofs of my desperate\r\ncondition, if I _were_ to go. It needed little to decide me, and I\r\nthink K\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99s arrival from Switzerland, offensively sunburnt, was the\r\nfinishing touch. His greeting was \xe2\x80\x9cHullo, Carruthers, you here? Thought\r\nyou had got away long ago. Lucky devil, though, to be going now, just\r\nin time for the best driving and the early pheasants. The heat\xe2\x80\x99s been\r\nshocking out there. Carter, bring me a Bradshaw\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94(an extraordinary\r\nbook, Bradshaw, turned to from habit, even when least wanted, as men\r\nfondle guns and rods in the close season).\r\n\r\nBy lunch-time the weight of indecision had been removed, and I found\r\nmyself entrusting Carter with a telegram to Davies, P.O., Flensburg.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThanks; expect me 9.34 p.m. 26th\xe2\x80\x9d; which produced, three hours later,\r\na reply: \xe2\x80\x9cDelighted; please bring a No. 3 Rippingille stove\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94a\r\nperplexing and ominous direction, which somehow chilled me in spite of\r\nits subject matter.\r\n\r\nIndeed, my resolution was continually faltering. It faltered when I\r\nturned out my gun in the evening and thought of the grouse it ought to\r\nhave accounted for. It faltered again when I contemplated the\r\nmiscellaneous list of commissions, sown broadcast through Davies\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nletter, to fulfil which seemed to make me a willing tool where my\r\nchosen _r\xc3\xb4le_ was that of an embittered exile, or at least a\r\ncondescending ally. However, I faced the commissions manfully, after\r\nleaving the office.\r\n\r\nAt Lancaster\xe2\x80\x99s I inquired for his gun, was received coolly, and had to\r\npay a heavy bill, which it seemed to have incurred, before it was\r\nhanded over. Having ordered the gun and No. 4\xe2\x80\x99s to be sent to my\r\nchambers, I bought the Raven mixture with that peculiar sense of injury\r\nwhich the prospect of smuggling in another\xe2\x80\x99s behalf always entails; and\r\nwondered where in the world Carey and Neilson\xe2\x80\x99s was, a firm which\r\nDavies spoke of as though it were as well known as the Bank of England\r\nor the Stores, instead of specialising in \xe2\x80\x9crigging-screws\xe2\x80\x9d, whatever\r\nthey might be. They sounded important, though, and it would be only\r\npolite to unearth them. I connected them with the \xe2\x80\x9cfew repairs,\xe2\x80\x9d and\r\nawoke new misgivings. At the Stores I asked for a No. 3 Rippingille\r\nstove, and was confronted with a formidable and hideous piece of\r\nironmongery, which burned petroleum in two capacious tanks, horribly\r\nprophetic of a smell of warm oil. I paid for this miserably, convinced\r\nof its grim efficiency, but speculating as to the domestic conditions\r\nwhich caused it to be sent for as an afterthought by telegram. I also\r\nasked about rigging-screws in the yachting department, but learnt that\r\nthey were not kept in stock; that Carey and Neilson\xe2\x80\x99s would certainly\r\nhave them, and that their shop was in the Minories, in the far east,\r\nmeaning a journey nearly as long as to Flensburg, and twice as\r\ntiresome. They would be shut by the time I got there, so after this\r\nexhausting round of duty I went home in a cab, omitted dressing for\r\ndinner (an epoch in itself), ordered a chop up from the basement\r\nkitchen, and spent the rest of the evening packing and writing, with\r\nthe methodical gloom of a man setting his affairs in order for the last\r\ntime.\r\n\r\nThe last of those airless nights passed. The astonished Withers saw me\r\nbreakfasting at eight, and at 9.30 I was vacantly examining\r\nrigging-screws with what wits were left me after a sulphurous ride in\r\nthe underground to Aldgate. I laid great stress on the 3/8\xe2\x80\x99s, and the\r\ngalvanism, and took them on trust, ignorant as to their functions. For\r\nthe eleven-shilling oilskins I was referred to a villainous den in a\r\nback street, which the shopman said they always recommended, and where\r\na dirty and bejewelled Hebrew chaffered with me (beginning at 18_s_.)\r\nover two reeking orange slabs distantly resembling moieties of the\r\nhuman figure. Their odour made me close prematurely for 14_s_., and I\r\nhurried back (for I was due there at 11) to my office with my two\r\ndisreputable brown-paper parcels, one of which made itself so\r\nnoticeable in the close official air that Carter attentively asked if I\r\nwould like to have it sent to my chambers, and K\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94 was inquisitive to\r\nbluntness about it and my movements. But I did not care to enlighten\r\nK\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94, whose comments I knew would be provokingly envious or wounding to\r\nmy pride in some way.\r\n\r\nI remembered, later on, the prismatic compass, and wired to the\r\nMinories to have one sent at once, feeling rather relieved that I was\r\nnot present there to be cross-examined as to size and make. The reply\r\nwas, \xe2\x80\x9cNot stocked; try surveying-instrument maker\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94a reply both\r\npuzzling and reassuring, for Davies\xe2\x80\x99s request for a compass had given\r\nme more uneasiness than anything, while, to find that what he wanted\r\nturned out to be a surveying-instrument, was a no less perplexing\r\ndiscovery. That day I made my last _pr\xc3\xa9cis_ and handed over my\r\nschedules\xe2\x80\x94Procrustean beds, where unwilling facts were stretched and\r\ntortured\xe2\x80\x94and said good-bye to my temporary chief, genial and lenient\r\nM\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94, who wished me a jolly holiday with all sincerity.\r\n\r\nAt seven I was watching a cab packed with my personal luggage and the\r\ncollection of unwieldy and incongruous packages that my shopping had\r\ndrawn down on me. Two deviations after that wretched prismatic\r\ncompass\xe2\x80\x94which I obtained in the end secondhand, _faute de mieux_, near\r\nVictoria, at one of those showy shops which look like jewellers\xe2\x80\x99 and\r\nare really pawnbrokers\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x94nearly caused me to miss my train. But at 8.30\r\nI had shaken off the dust of London from my feet, and at 10.30 I was,\r\nas I have announced, pacing the deck of a Flushing steamer, adrift on\r\nthis fatuous holiday in the far Baltic.\r\n\r\nAn air from the west, cooled by a midday thunderstorm, followed the\r\nsteamer as she slid through the calm channels of the Thames estuary,\r\npassed the cordon of scintillating lightships that watch over the\r\nsea-roads to the imperial city like pickets round a sleeping army, and\r\nslipped out into the dark spaces of the North Sea. Stars were bright,\r\nsummer scents from the Kent cliffs mingled coyly with vulgar\r\nsteamer-smells; the summer weather held immutably. Nature, for her\r\npart, seemed resolved to be no party to my penance, but to be\r\nimperturbably bent on shedding mild ridicule over my wrongs. An\r\nirresistible sense of peace and detachment, combined with that\r\ndelicious physical awakening that pulses through the nerve-sick\r\ntownsman when city airs and bald routine are left behind him, combined\r\nto provide me, however thankless a subject, with a solid background of\r\nresignation. Stowing this safely away, I could calculate my intentions\r\nwith cold egotism. If the weather held I might pass a not intolerable\r\nfortnight with Davies. When it broke up, as it was sure to, I could\r\neasily excuse myself from the pursuit of the problematical ducks; the\r\nwintry logic of facts would, in any case, decide him to lay up his\r\nyacht, for he could scarcely think of sailing home at such a season. I\r\ncould then take a chance lying ready of spending a few weeks in Dresden\r\nor elsewhere. I settled this programme comfortably and then turned in.\r\n\r\nFrom Flushing eastward to Hamburg, then northward to Flensburg, I cut\r\nshort the next day\xe2\x80\x99s sultry story. Past dyke and windmill and still\r\ncanals, on to blazing stubbles and roaring towns; at the last, after\r\ndusk, through a quiet level region where the train pottered from one\r\nlazy little station to another, and at ten o\xe2\x80\x99clock I found myself,\r\nstiff and stuffy, on the platform at Flensburg, exchanging greetings\r\nwith Davies.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s awfully good of you to come.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot at all; it\xe2\x80\x99s very good of you to ask me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe were both of us ill at ease. Even in the dim gaslight he clashed on\r\nmy notions of a yachtsman\xe2\x80\x94no cool white ducks or neat blue serge; and\r\nwhere was the snowy crowned yachting cap, that precious charm that so\r\neasily converts a landsman into a dashing mariner? Conscious that this\r\nimpressive uniform, in high perfection, was lying ready in my\r\nportmanteau, I felt oddly guilty. He wore an old Norfolk jacket, muddy\r\nbrown shoes, grey flannel trousers (or had they been white?), and an\r\nordinary tweed cap. The hand he gave me was horny, and appeared to be\r\nstained with paint; the other one, which carried a parcel, had a\r\nbandage on it which would have borne renewal. There was an instant of\r\nmutual inspection. I thought he gave me a shy, hurried scrutiny as\r\nthough to test past conjectures, with something of anxiety in it, and\r\nperhaps (save the mark!) a tinge of admiration. The face was familiar,\r\nand yet not familiar; the pleasant blue eyes, open, clean-cut features,\r\nunintellectual forehead were the same; so were the brisk and impulsive\r\nmovements; there was some change; but the moment of awkward hesitation\r\nwas over and the light was bad; and, while strolling down the platform\r\nfor my luggage, we chatted with constraint about trivial things.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy the way,\xe2\x80\x9d he suddenly said, laughing, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m afraid I\xe2\x80\x99m not fit to be\r\nseen; but it\xe2\x80\x99s so late it doesn\xe2\x80\x99t matter. I\xe2\x80\x99ve been painting hard all\r\nday, and just got it finished. I only hope we shall have some wind\r\nto-morrow\xe2\x80\x94it\xe2\x80\x99s been hopelessly calm lately. I say, you\xe2\x80\x99ve brought a\r\ngood deal of stuff,\xe2\x80\x9d he concluded, as my belongings began to collect.\r\n\r\nHere was a reward for my submissive exertions in the far east!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou gave me a good many commissions!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I didn\xe2\x80\x99t mean those things,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, absently. \xe2\x80\x9cThanks for\r\nbringing them, by the way. That\xe2\x80\x99s the stove, I suppose; cartridges,\r\nthis one, by the weight. You got the rigging-screws all right, I hope?\r\nThey\xe2\x80\x99re not really necessary, of course\xe2\x80\x9d (I nodded vacantly, and felt a\r\nlittle hurt); \xe2\x80\x9cbut they\xe2\x80\x99re simpler than lanyards, and you can\xe2\x80\x99t get\r\nthem here. It\xe2\x80\x99s that portmanteau,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, slowly, measuring it with a\r\ndoubtful eye. \xe2\x80\x9cNever mind! we\xe2\x80\x99ll try. You couldn\xe2\x80\x99t do with the\r\nGladstone only, I suppose? You see, the dinghy\xe2\x80\x94h\xe2\x80\x99m, and there\xe2\x80\x99s the\r\nhatchway, too\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94he was lost in thought. \xe2\x80\x9cAnyhow, we\xe2\x80\x99ll try. I\xe2\x80\x99m afraid\r\nthere are no cabs; but it\xe2\x80\x99s quite near, and the porter\xe2\x80\x99ll help.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSickening forebodings crept over me, while Davies shouldered my\r\nGladstone and clutched at the parcels.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAren\xe2\x80\x99t your men here?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked, faintly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMen?\xe2\x80\x9d He looked confused. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, perhaps I ought to have told you, I\r\nnever have any paid hands; it\xe2\x80\x99s quite a small boat, you know\xe2\x80\x94I hope you\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t expect luxury. I\xe2\x80\x99ve managed her single-handed for some time. A\r\nman would be no use, and a horrible nuisance.\xe2\x80\x9d He revealed these\r\nappalling truths with a cheerful assurance, which did nothing to hide a\r\nna\xc3\xafve apprehension of their effect on me. There was a check in our\r\nmobilisation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s rather late to go on board, isn\xe2\x80\x99t it?\xe2\x80\x9d I said, in a wooden voice.\r\nSomeone was turning out the gaslights, and the porter yawned\r\nostentatiously. \xe2\x80\x9cI think I\xe2\x80\x99d rather sleep at an hotel to-night.\xe2\x80\x9d A\r\nstrained pause.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, of course you can do that, if you like,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, in\r\ntransparent distress of mind. \xe2\x80\x9cBut it seems hardly worth while to cart\r\nthis stuff all the way to an hotel (I believe they\xe2\x80\x99re all on the other\r\nside of the harbour), and back again to the boat to-morrow. She\xe2\x80\x99s quite\r\ncomfortable, and you\xe2\x80\x99re sure to sleep well, as you\xe2\x80\x99re tired.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe can leave the things here,\xe2\x80\x9d I argued feebly, \xe2\x80\x9cand walk over with my\r\nbag.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I shall have to go aboard anyhow,\xe2\x80\x9d he rejoined; \xe2\x80\x9cI _never_ sleep\r\non shore.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe seemed to be clinging timidly, but desperately, to some diplomatic\r\nend. A stony despair was invading me and paralysing resistance. Better\r\nface the worst and be done with it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome on,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, grimly.\r\n\r\nHeavily loaded, we stumbled over railway lines and rubble heaps, and\r\ncame on the harbour. Davies led the way to a stairway, whose weedy\r\nsteps disappeared below in gloom.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you\xe2\x80\x99ll get into the dinghy,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, all briskness now, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll pass\r\nthe things down.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI descended gingerly, holding as a guide a sodden painter which ended\r\nin a small boat, and conscious that I was collecting slime on cuffs and\r\ntrousers.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHold up!\xe2\x80\x9d shouted Davies, cheerfully, as I sat down suddenly near the\r\nbottom, with one foot in the water.\r\n\r\nI climbed wretchedly into the dinghy and awaited events.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow float her up close under the quay wall, and make fast to the ring\r\ndown there,\xe2\x80\x9d came down from above, followed by the slack of the sodden\r\npainter, which knocked my cap off as it fell. \xe2\x80\x9cAll fast? Any knot\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\ndo,\xe2\x80\x9d I heard, as I grappled with this loathsome task, and then a big,\r\ndark object loomed overhead and was lowered into the dinghy. It was my\r\nportmanteau, and, placed athwart, exactly filled all the space\r\namidships. \xe2\x80\x9cDoes it fit?\xe2\x80\x9d was the anxious inquiry from aloft.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBeautifully.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCapital!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nScratching at the greasy wall to keep the dinghy close to it, I\r\nreceived in succession our stores, and stowed the cargo as best I\r\ncould, while the dinghy sank lower and lower in the water, and its\r\nprecarious superstructure grew higher.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCatch!\xe2\x80\x9d was the final direction from above, and a damp soft parcel hit\r\nme in the chest. \xe2\x80\x9cBe careful of that, it\xe2\x80\x99s meat. Now back to the\r\nstairs!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI painfully acquiesced, and Davies appeared.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a bit of a load, and she\xe2\x80\x99s rather deep; but I _think_ we shall\r\nmanage,\xe2\x80\x9d he reflected. \xe2\x80\x9cYou sit right aft, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll row.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI was too far gone for curiosity as to how this monstrous pyramid was\r\nto be rowed, or even for surmises as to its foundering by the way. I\r\ncrawled to my appointed seat, and Davies extricated the buried sculls\r\nby a series of tugs, which shook the whole structure, and made us roll\r\nalarmingly. How he stowed himself into rowing posture I have not the\r\nleast idea, but eventually we were moving sluggishly out into the open\r\nwater, his head just visible in the bows. We had started from what\r\nappeared to be the head of a narrow loch, and were leaving behind us\r\nthe lights of a big town. A long frontage of lamp-lit quays was on our\r\nleft, with here and there the vague hull of a steamer alongside. We\r\npassed the last of the lights and came out into a broader stretch of\r\nwater, when a light breeze was blowing and dark hills could be seen on\r\neither shore.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m lying a little way down the fiord, you see,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies. \xe2\x80\x9cI hate\r\nto be too near a town, and I found a carpenter handy here\xe2\x80\x94 There she\r\nis! I wonder how you\xe2\x80\x99ll like her!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI roused myself. We were entering a little cove encircled by trees, and\r\napproaching a light which flickered in the rigging of a small vessel,\r\nwhose outline gradually defined itself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cKeep her off,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, as we drew alongside.\r\n\r\nIn a moment he had jumped on deck, tied the painter, and was round at\r\nmy end.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou hand them up,\xe2\x80\x9d he ordered, \xe2\x80\x9cand I\xe2\x80\x99ll take them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was a laborious task, with the one relief that it was not far to\r\nhand them\xe2\x80\x94a doubtful compensation, for other reasons distantly shaping\r\nthemselves. When the stack was transferred to the deck I followed it,\r\ntripping over the flabby meat parcel, which was already showing ghastly\r\nsigns of disintegration under the dew. Hazily there floated through my\r\nmind my last embarkation on a yacht; my faultless attire, the trim gig\r\nand obsequious sailors, the accommodation ladder flashing with varnish\r\nand brass in the August sun; the orderly, snowy decks and basket chairs\r\nunder the awning aft. What a contrast with this sordid midnight\r\nscramble, over damp meat and littered packing-cases! The bitterest\r\ntouch of all was a growing sense of inferiority and ignorance which I\r\nhad never before been allowed to feel in my experience of yachts.\r\n\r\nDavies awoke from another reverie over my portmanteau to say, cheerily:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll just show you round down below first, and then we\xe2\x80\x99ll stow things\r\naway and get to bed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe dived down a companion ladder, and I followed cautiously. A complex\r\nodour of paraffin, past cookery, tobacco, and tar saluted my nostrils.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMind your head,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, striking a match and lighting a candle,\r\nwhile I groped into the cabin. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99d better sit down; it\xe2\x80\x99s easier to\r\nlook round.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere might well have been sarcasm in this piece of advice, for I must\r\nhave cut a ridiculous figure, peering awkwardly and suspiciously round,\r\nwith shoulders and head bent to avoid the ceiling, which seemed in the\r\nhalf-light to be even nearer the floor than it was.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou see,\xe2\x80\x9d were Davies\xe2\x80\x99s reassuring words, \xe2\x80\x9cthere\xe2\x80\x99s plenty of room to\r\n_sit_ upright\xe2\x80\x9d (which was strictly true; but I am not very tall, and he\r\nis short). \xe2\x80\x9cSome people make a point of head-room, but I never mind\r\nmuch about it. That\xe2\x80\x99s the centreboard case,\xe2\x80\x9d he explained, as, in\r\nstretching my legs out, my knee came into contact with a sharp edge.\r\n\r\nI had not seen this devilish obstruction, as it was hidden beneath the\r\ntable, which indeed rested on it at one end. It appeared to be a long,\r\nlow triangle, running lengthways with the boat and dividing the\r\nnaturally limited space into two.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou see, she\xe2\x80\x99s a flat-bottomed boat, drawing very little water without\r\nthe plate; that\xe2\x80\x99s why there\xe2\x80\x99s so little headroom. For deep water you\r\nlower the plate; so, in one way or another, you can go practically\r\nanywhere.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI was not nautical enough to draw any very definite conclusions from\r\nthis, but what I did draw were not promising. The latter sentences were\r\nspoken from the forecastle, whither Davies had crept through a low\r\nsliding door, like that of a rabbit-hutch, and was already busy with a\r\nkettle over a stove which I made out to be a battered and disreputable\r\ntwin brother of the No. 3 Rippingille.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99ll be boiling soon,\xe2\x80\x9d he remarked, \xe2\x80\x9cand we\xe2\x80\x99ll have some grog.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMy eyes were used to the light now, and I took in the rest of my\r\nsurroundings, which may be very simply described. Two long\r\ncushion-covered seats flanked the cabin, bounded at the after end by\r\ncupboards, one of which was cut low to form a sort of miniature\r\nsideboard, with glasses hung in a rack above it. The deck overhead was\r\nvery low at each side but rose shoulder high for a space in the middle,\r\nwhere a \xe2\x80\x9ccoach-house roof\xe2\x80\x9d with a skylight gave additional cabin space.\r\nJust outside the door was a fold-up washing-stand. On either wall were\r\nlong net-racks holding a medley of flags, charts, caps, cigar-boxes,\r\nhanks of yarn, and such like. Across the forward bulkhead was a\r\nbookshelf crammed to overflowing with volumes of all sizes, many upside\r\ndown and some coverless. Below this were a pipe-rack, an aneroid, and a\r\nclock with a hearty tick. All the woodwork was painted white, and to a\r\nless jaundiced eye than mine the interior might have had an enticing\r\nlook of snugness. Some Kodak prints were nailed roughly on the after\r\nbulkhead, and just over the doorway was the photograph of a young girl.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s my sister,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, who had emerged and saw me looking at\r\nit. \xe2\x80\x9cNow, let\xe2\x80\x99s get the stuff down.\xe2\x80\x9d He ran up the ladder, and soon my\r\nportmanteau blackened the hatchway, and a great straining and squeezing\r\nbegan. \xe2\x80\x9cI was afraid it was too big,\xe2\x80\x9d came down; \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m sorry, but you\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nhave to unpack on deck\xe2\x80\x94we may be able to squash it down when it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nempty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen the wearisome tail of packages began to form a fresh stack in the\r\ncramped space at my feet, and my back ached with stooping and moiling\r\nin unfamiliar places. Davies came down, and with unconcealed pride\r\nintroduced me to the sleeping cabin (he called the other one \xe2\x80\x9cthe\r\nsaloon\xe2\x80\x9d). Another candle was lit and showed two short and narrow berths\r\nwith blankets, but no sign of sheets; beneath these were drawers, one\r\nset of which Davies made me master of, evidently thinking them a\r\nprincely allowance of space for my wardrobe.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou can chuck your things down the skylight on to your berth as you\r\nunpack them,\xe2\x80\x9d he remarked. \xe2\x80\x9cBy the way, I doubt if there\xe2\x80\x99s room for all\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99ve got. I suppose you couldn\xe2\x80\x99t manage\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d I said shortly.\r\n\r\nThe absurdity of argument struck me; two men, doubled up like monkeys,\r\ncannot argue.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you\xe2\x80\x99ll go out I shall be able to get out too,\xe2\x80\x9d I added. He seemed\r\nmiserable at this ghost of an altercation, but I pushed past, mounted\r\nthe ladder, and in the expiring moonlight unstrapped that accursed\r\nportmanteau and, brimming over with irritation, groped among its\r\ncontents, sorting some into the skylight with the same feeling that\r\nnothing mattered much now, and it was best to be done with it;\r\nrepacking the rest with guilty stealth ere Davies should discover their\r\ncharacter, and strapping up the whole again. Then I sat down upon my\r\nwhite elephant and shivered, for the chill of autumn was in the air. It\r\nsuddenly struck me that if it had been raining things might have been\r\nworse still. The notion made me look round. The little cove was still\r\nas glass; stars above and stars below; a few white cottages glimmering\r\nat one point on the shore; in the west the lights of Flensburg; to the\r\neast the fiord broadening into unknown gloom. From Davies toiling below\r\nthere were muffled sounds of wrenching, pushing, and hammering,\r\npunctuated occasionally by a heavy splash as something shot up from the\r\nhatchway and fell into the water.\r\n\r\nHow it came about I do not know. Whether it was something pathetic in\r\nthe look I had last seen on his face\xe2\x80\x94a look which I associated for no\r\nreason whatever with his bandaged hand; whether it was one of those\r\ninstants of clear vision in which our separate selves are seen divided,\r\nthe baser from the better, and I saw my silly egotism in contrast with\r\na simple generous nature; whether it was an impalpable air of mystery\r\nwhich pervaded the whole enterprise and refused to be dissipated by its\r\nmost mortifying and vulgarising incidents\xe2\x80\x94a mystery dimly connected\r\nwith my companion\xe2\x80\x99s obvious consciousness of having misled me into\r\njoining him; whether it was only the stars and the cool air rousing\r\natrophied instincts of youth and spirits; probably, indeed, it was all\r\nthese influences, cemented into strength by a ruthless sense of humour\r\nwhich whispered that I was in danger of making a mere commonplace fool\r\nof myself in spite of all my laboured calculations; but whatever it\r\nwas, in a flash my mood changed. The crown of martyrdom disappeared,\r\nthe wounded vanity healed; that precious fund of fictitious resignation\r\ndrained away, but left no void. There was left a fashionable and\r\ndishevelled young man sitting in the dew and in the dark on a\r\nridiculous portmanteau which dwarfed the yacht that was to carry it; a\r\nyouth acutely sensible of ignorance in a strange and strenuous\r\natmosphere; still feeling sore and victimised; but withal sanely\r\nashamed and sanely resolved to enjoy himself. I anticipate; for though\r\nthe change was radical its full growth was slow. But in any case it was\r\nhere and now that it took its birth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGrog\xe2\x80\x99s ready!\xe2\x80\x9d came from below. Bunching myself for the descent I\r\nfound to my astonishment that all trace of litter had miraculously\r\nvanished, and a cosy neatness reigned. Glasses and lemons were on the\r\ntable, and a fragrant smell of punch had deadened previous odours. I\r\nshowed little emotion at these amenities, but enough to give intense\r\nrelief to Davies, who delightedly showed me his devices for storage,\r\npraising the \xe2\x80\x9croominess\xe2\x80\x9d of his floating den. \xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s your stove, you\r\nsee,\xe2\x80\x9d he ended; \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve chucked the old one overboard.\xe2\x80\x9d It was a weakness\r\nof his, I should say here, to rejoice in throwing things overboard on\r\nthe flimsiest pretexts. I afterwards suspected that the new stove had\r\nnot been \xe2\x80\x9creally necessary\xe2\x80\x9d any more than the rigging-screws, but was\r\nan excuse for gratifying this curious taste.\r\n\r\nWe smoked and chatted for a little, and then came the problem of going\r\nto bed. After much bumping of knuckles and head, and many giddy\r\nwrithings, I mastered it, and lay between the rough blankets. Davies,\r\nmoving swiftly and deftly, was soon in his.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s quite comfortable, isn\xe2\x80\x99t it?\xe2\x80\x9d he said, as he blew out the light\r\nfrom where he lay, with an accuracy which must have been the fruit of\r\nlong practice.\r\n\r\nI felt prickly all over, and there was a damp patch on the pillow,\r\nwhich was soon explained by a heavy drop of moisture falling on my\r\nforehead.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose the deck\xe2\x80\x99s not leaking?\xe2\x80\x9d I said, as mildly as I could.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m awfully sorry,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, earnestly, tumbling out of his bunk.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt must be the heavy dew. I did a lot of caulking yesterday, but I\r\nsuppose I missed that place. I\xe2\x80\x99ll run up and square it with an\r\noilskin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s wrong with your hand?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked, sleepily, on his return, for\r\ngratitude reminded me of that bandage.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing much; I strained it the other day,\xe2\x80\x9d was the reply; and then\r\nthe seemingly inconsequent remark: \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m glad you brought that prismatic\r\ncompass. It\xe2\x80\x99s not really necessary, of course; but\xe2\x80\x9d (muffled by\r\nblankets) \xe2\x80\x9cit may come in useful.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER III.\r\nDavies\r\n\r\n\r\nI dozed but fitfully, with a fretful sense of sore elbows and neck and\r\nmany a draughty hiatus among the blankets. It was broad daylight before\r\nI had reached the stage of torpor in which such slumber merges. That\r\nwas finally broken by the descent through the skylight of a torrent of\r\nwater. I started up, bumped my head hard against the decks, and blinked\r\nleaden-eyed upwards.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSorry! I\xe2\x80\x99m scrubbing decks. Come up and bathe. Slept well?\xe2\x80\x9d I heard a\r\nvoice saying from aloft.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFairly well,\xe2\x80\x9d I growled, stepping out into a pool of water on the\r\noilcloth. Thence I stumbled up the ladder, dived overboard, and buried\r\nbad dreams, stiffness, frowsiness, and tormented nerves in the\r\nloveliest fiord of the lovely Baltic. A short and furious swim and I\r\nwas back again, searching for a means of ascent up the smooth black\r\nside, which, low as it was, was slippery and unsympathetic. Davies, in\r\na loose canvas shirt, with the sleeves tucked up, and flannels rolled\r\nup to the knee, hung over me with a rope\xe2\x80\x99s end, and chatted\r\nunconcernedly about the easiness of the job when you know how, adjuring\r\nme to mind the paint, and talking about an accommodation ladder he had\r\nonce had, but had thrown overboard because it was so horribly in the\r\nway. When I arrived, my knees and elbows were picked out in black\r\npaint, to his consternation. Nevertheless, as I plied the towel, I knew\r\nthat I had left in those limpid depths yet another crust of discontent\r\nand self-conceit.\r\n\r\nAs I dressed into flannels and blazer, I looked round the deck, and\r\nwith an unskilled and doubtful eye took in all that the darkness had\r\nhitherto hidden. She seemed very small (in point of fact she was seven\r\ntons), something over thirty feet in length and nine in beam, a size\r\nvery suitable to week-ends in the Solent, for such as liked that sort\r\nof thing; but that she should have come from Dover to the Baltic\r\nsuggested a world of physical endeavour of which I had never dreamed. I\r\npassed to the \xc3\xa6sthetic side. Smartness and beauty were essential to\r\nyachts, in my mind, but with the best resolves to be pleased I found\r\nlittle encouragement here. The hull seemed too low, and the mainmast\r\ntoo high; the cabin roof looked clumsy, and the skylights saddened the\r\neye with dull iron and plebeian graining. What brass there was, on the\r\ntiller-head and elsewhere, was tarnished with sickly green. The decks\r\nhad none of that creamy purity which Cowes expects, but were rough and\r\ngrey, and showed tarry exhalations round the seams and rusty stains\r\nnear the bows. The ropes and rigging were in mourning when contrasted\r\nwith the delicate buff manilla so satisfying to the artistic eye as\r\nseen against the blue of a June sky at Southsea. Nor was the whole\r\neffect bettered by many signs of recent refitting. An impression of\r\npaint, varnish, and carpentry was in the air; a gaudy new burgee\r\nfluttered aloft; there seemed to be a new rope or two, especially round\r\nthe diminutive mizzen-mast, which itself looked altogether new. But all\r\nthis only emphasised the general plainness, reminding one of a\r\nrespectable woman of the working-classes trying to dress above her\r\nstation, and soon likely to give it up.\r\n\r\nThat the _ensemble_ was businesslike and solid even my untrained eye\r\ncould see. Many of the deck fittings seemed disproportionately\r\nsubstantial. The anchor-chain looked contemptuous of its charge; the\r\nbinnacle with its compass was of a size and prominence almost comically\r\nimpressive, and was, moreover the only piece of brass which was\r\nburnished and showed traces of reverent care. Two huge coils of stout\r\nand dingy warp lay just abaft the mainmast, and summed up the\r\nweather-beaten aspect of the little ship. I should add here that in the\r\ndistant past she had been a lifeboat, and had been clumsily converted\r\ninto a yacht by the addition of a counter, deck, and the necessary\r\nspars. She was built, as all lifeboats are, diagonally, of two skins of\r\nteak, and thus had immense strength, though, in the matter of looks,\r\nall a hybrid\xe2\x80\x99s failings.\r\n\r\nHunger and \xe2\x80\x9cTea\xe2\x80\x99s made!\xe2\x80\x9d from below brought me down to the cabin, where\r\nI found breakfast laid out on the table over the centreboard case, with\r\nDavies earnestly presiding, rather flushed as to the face, and sooty as\r\nto the fingers. There was a slight shortage of plate and crockery, but\r\nI praised the bacon and could do so truthfully, for its crisp and\r\nsteaming shavings would have put to shame the efforts of my London\r\ncook. Indeed, I should have enjoyed the meal heartily were it not for\r\nthe lowness of the sofa and table, causing a curvature of the body\r\nwhich made swallowing a more lengthy process than usual, and induced a\r\nperiodical yearning to get up and stretch\xe2\x80\x94a relief which spelt disaster\r\nto the skull. I noticed, too, that Davies spoke with a zest, sinister\r\nto me, of the delights of white bread and fresh milk, which he seemed\r\nto consider unusual luxuries, though suitable to an inaugural banquet\r\nin honour of a fastidious stranger. \xe2\x80\x9cOne can\xe2\x80\x99t be always going on\r\nshore,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, when I showed a discreet interest in these things. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nlived for ten days on a big rye loaf over in the Frisian Islands.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd it died hard, I suppose?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery hard, but\xe2\x80\x9d (gravely) \xe2\x80\x9cquite good. After that I taught myself to\r\nmake rolls; had no baking powder at first, so used Eno\xe2\x80\x99s fruit salt,\r\nbut they wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t rise much with that. As for milk, condensed is\xe2\x80\x94I hope\r\nyou don\xe2\x80\x99t mind it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI changed the subject, and asked about his plans.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet\xe2\x80\x99s get under way at once,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cand sail down the fiord.\xe2\x80\x9d I\r\ntried for something more specific, but he was gone, and his voice\r\ndrowned in the fo\xe2\x80\x99c\xe2\x80\x99sle by the clatter and swish of washing up.\r\nThenceforward events moved with bewildering rapidity. Humbly desirous\r\nof being useful I joined him on deck, only to find that he scarcely\r\nnoticed me, save as a new and unexpected obstacle in his round of\r\nactivity. He was everywhere at once\xe2\x80\x94heaving in chain, hooking on\r\nhalyards, hauling ropes; while my part became that of the clown who\r\ndoes things after they are already done, for my knowledge of a yacht\r\nwas of that floating and inaccurate kind which is useless in practice.\r\nSoon the anchor was up (a great rusty monster it was!), the sails set,\r\nand Davies was darting swiftly to and fro between the tiller and\r\njib-sheets, while the _Dulcibella_ bowed a lingering farewell to the\r\nshore and headed for the open fiord. Erratic puffs from the high land\r\nbehind made her progress timorous at first, but soon the fairway was\r\nreached and a true breeze from Flensburg and the west took her in its\r\nfriendly grip. Steadily she rustled down the calm blue highway whose\r\nsoft beauty was the introduction to a passage in my life, short, but\r\npregnant with moulding force, through stress and strain, for me and\r\nothers.\r\n\r\nDavies was gradually resuming his natural self, with abstracted\r\nintervals, in which he lashed the helm to finger a distant rope, with\r\nsuch speed that the movements seemed simultaneous. Once he vanished,\r\nonly to reappear in an instant with a chart, which he studied, while\r\nsteering, with a success that its reluctant folds seemed to render\r\nimpossible. Waiting respectfully for his revival I had full time to\r\nlook about. The fiord here was about a mile broad. From the shore we\r\nhad left the hills rose steeply, but with no rugged grandeur; the\r\noutlines were soft; there were green spaces and rich woods on the lower\r\nslopes; a little white town was opening up in one place, and scattered\r\nfarms dotted the prospect. The other shore, which I could just see,\r\nframed between the gunwale and the mainsail, as I sat leaning against\r\nthe hatchway, and sadly missing a deck-chair, was lower and lonelier,\r\nthough prosperous and pleasing to the eye. Spacious pastures led up by\r\nslow degrees to ordered clusters of wood, which hinted at the presence\r\nof some great manor house. Behind us, Flensburg was settling into haze.\r\nAhead, the scene was shut in by the contours of hills, some clear, some\r\ndreamy and distant. Lastly, a single glimpse of water shining between\r\nthe folds of hill far away hinted at spaces of distant sea of which\r\nthis was but a secluded inlet. Everywhere was that peculiar charm\r\nengendered by the association of quiet pastoral country and a homely\r\nhuman atmosphere with a branch of the great ocean that bathes all the\r\nshores of our globe.\r\n\r\nThere was another charm in the scene, due to the way in which I was\r\nviewing it\xe2\x80\x94not as a pampered passenger on a \xe2\x80\x9cfine steam yacht\xe2\x80\x9d, or even\r\non \xe2\x80\x9ca powerful modern schooner\xe2\x80\x9d, as the yacht agents advertise, but\r\nfrom the deck of a scrubby little craft of doubtful build and\r\ndistressing plainness, which yet had smelt her persistent way to this\r\ndistant fiord through I knew not what of difficulty and danger, with no\r\napparent motive in her single occupant, who talked as vaguely and\r\nunconcernedly about his adventurous cruise as though it were all a\r\nprotracted afternoon on Southampton Water.\r\n\r\nI glanced round at Davies. He had dropped the chart and was sitting, or\r\nrather half lying, on the deck with one bronzed arm over the tiller,\r\ngazing fixedly ahead, with just an occasional glance around and aloft.\r\nHe still seemed absorbed in himself, and for a moment or two I studied\r\nhis face with an attention I had never, since I had known him, given\r\nit. I had always thought it commonplace, as I had thought him\r\ncommonplace, so far as I had thought at all about either. It had always\r\nrather irritated me by an excess of candour and boyishness. These\r\nqualities it had kept, but the scales were falling from my eyes, and I\r\nsaw others. I saw strength to obstinacy and courage to recklessness, in\r\nthe firm lines of the chin; an older and deeper look in the eyes. Those\r\nodd transitions from bright mobility to detached earnestness, which had\r\npartly amused and chiefly annoyed me hitherto, seemed now to be lost in\r\na sensitive reserve, not cold or egotistic, but strangely winning from\r\nits paradoxical frankness. Sincerity was stamped on every lineament. A\r\ndeep misgiving stirred me that, clever as I thought myself, nicely\r\nperceptive of the right and congenial men to know, I had made some big\r\nmistakes\xe2\x80\x94how many, I wondered? A relief, scarcely less deep because it\r\nwas unconfessed, stole in on me with the suspicion that, little as I\r\ndeserved it, the patient fates were offering me a golden chance of\r\nrepairing at least one. And yet, I mused, the patient fates have\r\ncrooked methods, besides a certain mischievous humour, for it was\r\nDavies who had asked me out\xe2\x80\x94though now he scarcely seemed to need\r\nme\xe2\x80\x94almost tricked me into coming out, for he might have known I was not\r\nsuited to such a life; yet trickery and Davies sounded an odd\r\nconjuncture.\r\n\r\nProbably it was the growing discomfort of my attitude which produced\r\nthis backsliding. My night\xe2\x80\x99s rest and the \xe2\x80\x9cascent from the bath\xe2\x80\x9d had,\r\nin fact, done little to prepare me for contact with sharp edges and\r\nhard surfaces. But Davies had suddenly come to himself, and with an \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nsay, are you comfortable? Have something to sit on?\xe2\x80\x9d jerked the helm a\r\nlittle to windward, felt it like a pulse for a moment, with a rapid\r\nlook to windward, and dived below, whence he returned with a couple of\r\ncushions, which he threw to me. I felt perversely resentful of these\r\nluxuries, and asked:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan\xe2\x80\x99t I be of any use?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, don\xe2\x80\x99t you bother,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered. \xe2\x80\x9cI expect you\xe2\x80\x99re tired. Aren\xe2\x80\x99t we\r\nhaving a splendid sail? That must be Ekken on the port bow,\xe2\x80\x9d peering\r\nunder the sail, \xe2\x80\x9cwhere the trees run in. I say, do you mind looking at\r\nthe chart?\xe2\x80\x9d He tossed it over to me. I spread it out painfully, for it\r\ncurled up like a watch-spring at the least slackening of pressure. I\r\nwas not familiar with charts, and this sudden trust reposed in me,\r\nafter a good deal of neglect, made me nervous.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou see Flensburg, don\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s where we are,\xe2\x80\x9d dabbing\r\nwith a long reach at an indefinite space on the crowded sheet. \xe2\x80\x9cNow\r\nwhich side of that buoy off the point do we pass?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI had scarcely taken in which was land and which was water, much less\r\nthe significance of the buoy, when he resumed:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever mind; I\xe2\x80\x99m pretty sure it\xe2\x80\x99s all deep water about here. I expect\r\nthat marks the fairway for steamers.\r\n\r\nIn a minute or two we were passing the buoy in question, on the wrong\r\nside I am pretty certain, for weeds and sand came suddenly into view\r\nbelow us with uncomfortable distinctness. But all Davies said was:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s never any sea here, and the plate\xe2\x80\x99s not down,\xe2\x80\x9d a dark\r\nutterance which I pondered doubtfully. \xe2\x80\x9cThe best of these Schleswig\r\nwaters,\xe2\x80\x9d he went on, \xe2\x80\x9cis that a boat of this size can go almost\r\nanywhere. There\xe2\x80\x99s no navigation required. Why\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d At this moment a faint\r\nscraping was felt, rather than heard, beneath us.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAren\xe2\x80\x99t we aground?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked with great calmness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, she\xe2\x80\x99ll blow over,\xe2\x80\x9d he replied, wincing a little.\r\n\r\nShe \xe2\x80\x9cblew over\xe2\x80\x9d, but the episode caused a little na\xc3\xafve vexation in\r\nDavies. I relate it as a good instance of one of his minor\r\npeculiarities. He was utterly without that didactic pedantry which\r\nyachting has a fatal tendency to engender in men who profess it. He had\r\ntossed me the chart without a thought that I was an ignoramus, to whom\r\nit would be Greek, and who would provide him with an admirable subject\r\nto drill and lecture, just as his neglect of me throughout the morning\r\nhad been merely habitual and unconscious independence. In the second\r\nplace, master of his _m\xc3\xa9tier_, as I knew him afterwards to be,\r\nresourceful, skilful, and alert, he was liable to lapse into a certain\r\namateurish vagueness, half irritating and half amusing. I think truly\r\nthat both these peculiarities came from the same source, a hatred of\r\nany sort of affectation. To the same source I traced the fact that he\r\nand his yacht observed none of the superficial etiquette of yachts and\r\nyachtsmen, that she never, for instance, flew a national ensign, and he\r\nnever wore a \xe2\x80\x9cyachting suit\xe2\x80\x9d.\r\n\r\nWe rounded a low green point which I had scarcely noticed before.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe must jibe,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies: \xe2\x80\x9cjust take the helm, will you?\xe2\x80\x9d and,\r\nwithout waiting for my co-operation, he began hauling in the mainsheet\r\nwith great vigour. I had rude notions of steering, but jibing is a\r\ndelicate operation. No yachtsman will be surprised to hear that the\r\nboom saw its opportunity and swung over with a mighty crash, with the\r\nmainsheet entangled round me and the tiller.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJibed all standing,\xe2\x80\x9d was his sorrowful comment. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re not used to\r\nher yet. She\xe2\x80\x99s very quick on the helm.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere am I to steer for?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked, wildly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, don\xe2\x80\x99t trouble, I\xe2\x80\x99ll take her now,\xe2\x80\x9d he replied.\r\n\r\nI felt it was time to make my position clear. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m an utter duffer at\r\nsailing,\xe2\x80\x9d I began. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll have a lot to teach me, or one of these days\r\nI shall be wrecking you. You see, there\xe2\x80\x99s always been a crew\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCrew!\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94with sovereign contempt\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cwhy, the whole fun of the thing is to\r\ndo everything oneself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I\xe2\x80\x99ve felt in the way the whole morning.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m awfully sorry!\xe2\x80\x9d His dismay and repentance were comical. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\njust the other way; you may be all the use in the world.\xe2\x80\x9d He became\r\nabsent.\r\n\r\nWe were following the inward trend of a small bay towards a cleft in\r\nthe low shore.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s Ekken Sound,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies; \xe2\x80\x9clet\xe2\x80\x99s look into it,\xe2\x80\x9d and a minute\r\nor two later we were drifting through a dainty little strait, with a\r\npeep of open water at the end of it. Cottages bordered either side,\r\nsome overhanging the very water, some connecting with it by a rickety\r\nwooden staircase or a miniature landing-stage. Creepers and roses\r\nrioted over the walls and tiny porches. For a space on one side, a rude\r\nquay, with small smacks floating off it, spoke of some minute\r\ncommercial interests; a very small tea-garden, with neglected-looking\r\nbowers and leaf-strewn tables, hinted at some equally minute tripping\r\ninterest. A pervading hue of mingled bronze and rose came partly from\r\nthe weather-mellowed woodwork of the cottages and stages, and partly\r\nfrom the creepers and the trees behind, where autumn\xe2\x80\x99s subtle fingers\r\nwere already at work. Down this exquisite sea-lane we glided till it\r\nended in a broad mere, where our sails, which had been shivering and\r\ncomplaining, filled into contented silence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cReady about!\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, callously. \xe2\x80\x9cWe must get out of this again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nAnd round we swung.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy not anchor and stop here?\xe2\x80\x9d I protested; for a view of tantalising\r\nloveliness was unfolding itself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, we\xe2\x80\x99ve seen all there is to be seen, and we must take this breeze\r\nwhile we\xe2\x80\x99ve got it.\xe2\x80\x9d It was always torture to Davies to feel a good\r\nbreeze running to waste while he was inactive at anchor or on shore.\r\nThe \xe2\x80\x9cshore\xe2\x80\x9d to him was an inferior element, merely serving as a useful\r\nannexe to the water\xe2\x80\x94a source of necessary supplies.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet\xe2\x80\x99s have lunch,\xe2\x80\x9d he pursued, as we resumed our way down the fiord. A\r\nvision of iced drinks, tempting salads, white napery, and an attentive\r\nsteward mocked me with past recollections.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll find a tongue,\xe2\x80\x9d said the voice of doom, \xe2\x80\x9cin the starboard\r\nsofa-locker; beer under the floor in the bilge. I\xe2\x80\x99ll see her round that\r\nbuoy, if you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t mind beginning.\xe2\x80\x9d I obeyed with a bad grace, but\r\nthe close air and cramped posture must have benumbed my faculties, for\r\nI opened the port-side locker, reached down, and grasped a sticky body,\r\nwhich turned out to be a pot of varnish. Recoiling wretchedly, I tried\r\nthe opposite one, combating the embarrassing heel of the boat and the\r\nobstructive edges of the centreboard case. A medley of damp tins of\r\nvaried sizes showed in the gloom, exuding a mouldy odour. Faded legends\r\non dissolving paper, like the remnants of old posters on a disused\r\nhoarding, spoke of soups, curries, beefs, potted meats, and other\r\nhidden delicacies. I picked out a tongue, re-imprisoned the odour, and\r\nexplored for beer. It was true, I supposed, that bilge didn\xe2\x80\x99t hurt it,\r\nas I tugged at the plank on my hands and knees, but I should have\r\nmyself preferred a more accessible and less humid wine-cellar than the\r\ncavities among slimy ballast from which I dug the bottles. I regarded\r\nmy hard-won and ill-favoured pledges of a meal with giddiness and\r\ndiscouragement.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow are you getting on?\xe2\x80\x9d shouted Davies; \xe2\x80\x9cthe tin-opener\xe2\x80\x99s hanging up\r\non the bulkhead; the plates and knives are in the cupboard.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI doggedly pursued my functions. The plates and knives met me half-way,\r\nfor, being on the weather side, and thus having a downward slant, its\r\ncontents, when I slipped the latch, slid affectionately into my bosom,\r\nand overflowed with a clatter and jingle on to the floor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat often happens,\xe2\x80\x9d I heard from above. \xe2\x80\x9cNever mind! There are no\r\nbreakables. I\xe2\x80\x99m coming down to help.\xe2\x80\x9d And down he came, leaving the\r\n_Dulcibella_ to her own devices.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think I\xe2\x80\x99ll go on deck,\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy in the world couldn\xe2\x80\x99t you lunch\r\ncomfortably at Ekken and save this infernal pandemonium of a picnic?\r\nWhere\xe2\x80\x99s the yacht going to meanwhile? And how are we to lunch on that\r\nslanting table? I\xe2\x80\x99m covered with varnish and mud, and ankle-deep in\r\ncrockery. There goes the beer!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have stood it on the table with this list on,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nDavies, with intense composure, \xe2\x80\x9cbut it won\xe2\x80\x99t do any harm; it\xe2\x80\x99ll drain\r\ninto the bilge\xe2\x80\x9d (ashes to ashes, dust to dust, I thought). \xe2\x80\x9cYou go on\r\ndeck now, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll finish getting ready.\xe2\x80\x9d I regretted my explosion,\r\nthough wrung from me under great provocation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cKeep her straight on as she\xe2\x80\x99s going,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, as I clambered up\r\nout of the chaos, brushing the dust off my trousers and varnishing the\r\nladder with my hands. I unlashed the helm and kept her as she was\r\ngoing.\r\n\r\nWe had rounded a sharp bend in the fiord, and were sailing up a broad\r\nand straight reach which every moment disclosed new beauties, sights\r\nfair enough to be balm to the angriest spirit. A red-roofed hamlet was\r\non our left, on the right an ivied ruin, close to the water, where some\r\ncontemplative cattle stood knee-deep. The view ahead was a white strand\r\nwhich fringed both shores, and to it fell wooded slopes, interrupted\r\nhere and there by low sandstone cliffs of warm red colouring, and now\r\nand again by a dingle with cracks of greensward.\r\n\r\nI forgot petty squalors and enjoyed things\xe2\x80\x94the coy tremble of the\r\ntiller and the backwash of air from the dingy mainsail, and, with a\r\nsomewhat chastened rapture, the lunch which Davies brought up to me and\r\nsolicitously watched me eat.\r\n\r\nLater, as the wind sank to lazy airs, he became busy with a larger\r\ntopsail and jib; but I was content to doze away the afternoon,\r\ndrenching brain and body in the sweet and novel foreign atmosphere, and\r\ndreamily watching the fringe of glen cliff and cool white sand as they\r\npassed ever more slowly by.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER IV\r\nRetrospect\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWake up!\xe2\x80\x9d I rubbed my eyes and wondered where I was; stretched myself\r\npainfully, too, for even the cushions had not given me a true bed of\r\nroses. It was dusk, and the yacht was stationary in glassy water,\r\ncoloured by the last after-glow. A roofing of thin upper-cloud had\r\nspread over most of the sky, and a subtle smell of rain was in the air.\r\nWe seemed to be in the middle of the fiord, whose shores looked distant\r\nand steep in the gathering darkness. Close ahead they faded away\r\nsuddenly, and the sight lost itself in a grey void. The stillness was\r\nabsolute.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe can\xe2\x80\x99t get to Sonderburg to-night,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s to be done then?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked, collecting my senses.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! we\xe2\x80\x99ll anchor anywhere here, we\xe2\x80\x99re just at the mouth of the fiord;\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ll tow her inshore if you\xe2\x80\x99ll steer in that direction.\xe2\x80\x9d He pointed\r\nvaguely at a blur of trees and cliff. Then he jumped into the dinghy,\r\ncast off the painter, and, after snatching at the slack of a rope,\r\nbegan towing the reluctant yacht by short jerks of the sculls. The\r\nmenacing aspect of that grey void, combined with a natural preference\r\nfor getting to some definite place at night, combined to depress my\r\nspirits afresh. In my sleep I had dreamt of Morven Lodge, of heather\r\ntea-parties after glorious slaughters of grouse, of salmon leaping in\r\namber pools\xe2\x80\x94and now\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust take a cast of the lead, will you?\xe2\x80\x9d came Davies\xe2\x80\x99s voice above the\r\nsplash of the sculls.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere is it?\xe2\x80\x9d I shouted back.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever mind\xe2\x80\x94we\xe2\x80\x99re close enough now; let\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94 Can you manage to let go the\r\nanchor?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI hurried forward and picked impotently at the bonds of the sleeping\r\nmonster. But Davies was aboard again, and stirred him with a deft touch\r\nor two, till he crashed into the water with a grinding of chain.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe shall do well here,\xe2\x80\x9d said he.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIsn\xe2\x80\x99t this rather an open anchorage?\xe2\x80\x9d I suggested.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s only open from that quarter,\xe2\x80\x9d he replied. \xe2\x80\x9cIf it comes on to blow\r\nfrom there we shall have to clear out; but I think it\xe2\x80\x99s only rain.\r\nLet\xe2\x80\x99s stow the sails.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnother whirlwind of activity, in which I joined as effectively as I\r\ncould, oppressed by the prospect of having to \xe2\x80\x9cclear out\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94who knows\r\nwhither?\xe2\x80\x94at midnight. But Davies\xe2\x80\x99s _sang froid_ was infectious, I\r\nsuppose, and the little den below, bright-lit and soon fragrant with\r\ncookery, pleaded insistently for affection. Yachting in this singular\r\nstyle was hungry work, I found. Steak tastes none the worse for having\r\nbeen wrapped in newspaper, and the slight traces of the day\xe2\x80\x99s news\r\ndisappear with frying in onions and potato-chips. Davies was indeed on\r\nhis mettle for this, his first dinner to his guest; for he produced\r\nwith stealthy pride, not from the dishonoured grave of the beer, but\r\nfrom some more hallowed recess, a bottle of German champagne, from\r\nwhich we drank success to the _Dulcibella_.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish you would tell me all about your cruise from England,\xe2\x80\x9d I asked.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must have had some exciting adventures.
