Here are the charts; let\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ngo over them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe must wash up first,\xe2\x80\x9d he replied, and I was tactfully introduced to\r\none of his very few \xe2\x80\x9cstanding orders\xe2\x80\x9d, that tobacco should not burn,\r\nnor post-prandial chat begin, until that distasteful process had ended.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt would never get done otherwise,\xe2\x80\x9d he sagely opined. But when we were\r\nfinally settled with cigars, a variety of which, culled from many\r\nports\xe2\x80\x94German, Dutch, and Belgian\xe2\x80\x94Davies kept in a battered old box in\r\nthe net-rack, the promised talk hung fire.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m no good at description,\xe2\x80\x9d he complained; \xe2\x80\x9cand there\xe2\x80\x99s really very\r\nlittle to tell. We left Dover\xe2\x80\x94Morrison and I\xe2\x80\x94on the 6th of August; made\r\na good passage to Ostend.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou had some fun there, I suppose?\xe2\x80\x9d I put in, thinking of\xe2\x80\x94well, of\r\nOstend in August.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFun! A filthy hole I call it; we had to stop a couple of days, as we\r\nfouled a buoy coming in and carried away the bobstay; we lay in a dirty\r\nlittle tidal dock, and there was nothing to do on shore.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, what next?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe had a splendid sail to the East Scheldt, but then, like fools,\r\ndecided to go through Holland by canal and river. It was good fun\r\nenough navigating the estuary\xe2\x80\x94the tides and banks there are\r\nappalling\xe2\x80\x94but farther inland it was a wretched business, nothing but\r\npaying lock-dues, bumping against schuyts, and towing down stinking\r\ncanals. Never a peaceful night like this\xe2\x80\x94always moored by some quay or\r\ntow-path, with people passing and boys. Heavens! shall I ever forget\r\nthose boys! A perfect murrain of them infests Holland; they seem to\r\nhave nothing in the world to do but throw stones and mud at foreign\r\nyachts.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey want a Herod, with some statesmanlike views on infanticide.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy Jove! yes; but the fact is that you want a crew for that pottering\r\ninland work; they can smack the boys and keep an eye on the sculls. A\r\nboat like this should stick to the sea, or out-of-the-way places on the\r\ncoast. Well, after Amsterdam.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve skipped a good deal, haven\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x9d I interrupted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! have I? Well, let me see, we went by Dordrecht to Rotterdam;\r\nnothing to see there, and swarms of tugs buzzing about and shaving\r\none\xe2\x80\x99s bows every second. On by the Vecht river to Amsterdam, and\r\nthence\xe2\x80\x94Lord, what a relief it was!\xe2\x80\x94out into the North Sea again. The\r\nweather had been still and steamy; but it broke up finely now, and we\r\nhad a rattling three-reef sail to the Zuyder Zee.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe reached up to the bookshelf for what looked like an ancient ledger,\r\nand turned over the leaves.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs that your log?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked. \xe2\x80\x9cI should like to have a look at it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! you\xe2\x80\x99d find it dull reading\xe2\x80\x94if you could read it at all; it\xe2\x80\x99s just\r\nshort notes about winds and bearings, and so on.\xe2\x80\x9d He was turning some\r\nleaves over rapidly. \xe2\x80\x9cNow, why don\xe2\x80\x99t you keep a log of what we do? I\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t describe things, and you can.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve half a mind to try,\xe2\x80\x9d I said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe want another chart now,\xe2\x80\x9d and he pulled down a second yet more\r\nstained and frayed than the first. \xe2\x80\x9cWe had a splendid time then\r\nexploring the Zuyder Zee, its northern part at least, and round those\r\nislands which bound it on the north. Those are the Frisian Islands, and\r\nthey stretch for 120 miles or so eastward. You see, the first two of\r\nthem, Texel and Vlieland, shut in the Zuyder Zee, and the rest border\r\nthe Dutch and German coasts.\xe2\x80\x9d _[See Map A]_\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s all this?\xe2\x80\x9d I said, running my finger over some dotted patches\r\nwhich covered much of the chart. The latter was becoming\r\nunintelligible; clean-cut coasts and neat regiments of little figures\r\nhad given place to a confusion of winding and intersecting lines and\r\nbald spaces.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll _sand_,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, enthusiastically. \xe2\x80\x9cYou can\xe2\x80\x99t think what a\r\nsplendid sailing-ground it is. You can explore for days without seeing\r\na soul. These are the channels, you see; they\xe2\x80\x99re very badly charted.\r\nThis chart was almost useless, but it made it all the more fun. No\r\ntowns or harbours, just a village or two on the islands, if you wanted\r\nstores.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey look rather desolate,\xe2\x80\x9d I said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDesolate\xe2\x80\x99s no word for it; they\xe2\x80\x99re really only gigantic sandbanks\r\nthemselves.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWasn\xe2\x80\x99t all this rather dangerous?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot a bit; you see, that\xe2\x80\x99s where our shallow draught and flat bottom\r\ncame in\xe2\x80\x94we could go anywhere, and it didn\xe2\x80\x99t matter running\r\naground\xe2\x80\x94she\xe2\x80\x99s perfect for that sort of work; and she doesn\xe2\x80\x99t really\r\n_look_ bad either, does she?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked, rather wistfully. I suppose I\r\nhesitated, for he said, abruptly:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnyway, I don\xe2\x80\x99t go in for looks.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe had leaned back, and I detected traces of incipient\r\nabsentmindedness. His cigar, which he had lately been lighting and\r\nrelighting feverishly\xe2\x80\x94a habit of his when excited\xe2\x80\x94seemed now to have\r\nexpired for good.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAbout running aground,\xe2\x80\x9d I persisted; \xe2\x80\x9csurely that\xe2\x80\x99s apt to be\r\ndangerous?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe sat up and felt round for a match.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot the least, if you know where you can run risks and where you\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t; anyway, you can\xe2\x80\x99t possibly help it. That chart may look simple\r\nto you\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94(\xe2\x80\x9csimple!\xe2\x80\x9d I thought)\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cbut at half flood all those banks are\r\ncovered; the islands and coasts are scarcely visible, they are so low,\r\nand everything looks the same.\xe2\x80\x9d This graphic description of a \xe2\x80\x9csplendid\r\ncruising-ground\xe2\x80\x9d took away my breath. \xe2\x80\x9cOf course there _is_ risk\r\nsometimes\xe2\x80\x94choosing an anchorage requires care. You can generally get a\r\nnice berth under the lee of a bank, but the tides run strong in the\r\nchannels, and if there\xe2\x80\x99s a gale blowing\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDidn\xe2\x80\x99t you ever take a pilot?\xe2\x80\x9d I interrupted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPilot? Why, the whole point of the thing\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94he stopped short\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cI did take\r\none once, later on,\xe2\x80\x9d he resumed, with an odd smile, which faded at\r\nonce.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell?\xe2\x80\x9d I urged, for I saw a reverie was coming.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! he ran me ashore, of course. Served me right. I wonder what the\r\nweather\xe2\x80\x99s doing\xe2\x80\x9d; he rose, glanced at the aneroid, the clock, and the\r\nhalf-closed skylight with a curious circular movement, and went a step\r\nor two up the companion-ladder, where he remained for several minutes\r\nwith head and shoulders in the open air.\r\n\r\nThere was no sound of wind outside, but the _Dulcibella_ had begun to\r\nmove in her sleep, as it were, rolling drowsily to some faint send of\r\nthe sea, with an occasional short jump, like the start of an uneasy\r\ndreamer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat does it look like?\xe2\x80\x9d I called from my sofa. I had to repeat the\r\nquestion.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRain coming,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, returning, \xe2\x80\x9cand possibly wind; but we\xe2\x80\x99re\r\nsafe enough here. It\xe2\x80\x99s coming from the sou\xe2\x80\x99-west; shall we turn in?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe haven\xe2\x80\x99t finished your cruise yet,\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9cLight a pipe and tell\r\nme the rest.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right,\xe2\x80\x9d he agreed, with more readiness than I expected.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAfter Terschelling\xe2\x80\x94here it is, the third island from the west\xe2\x80\x94I\r\npottered along eastward.\xe2\x80\x9d _[See Map A]_\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! I forgot. Morrison had to leave me there. I missed him badly, but\r\nI hoped at that time to get \xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94 to join me. I could manage all right\r\nsingle-handed, but for that sort of work two are much better than one.\r\nThe plate\xe2\x80\x99s beastly heavy; in fact, I had to give up using it for fear\r\nof a smash.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAfter Terschelling?\xe2\x80\x9d I jogged his memory.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I followed the Dutch islands, Ameland, Schiermonnikoog, Rottum\r\n(outlandish names, aren\xe2\x80\x99t they?), sometimes outside them, sometimes\r\ninside. It was a bit lonely, but grand sport and very interesting. The\r\ncharts were shocking, but I worried out most of the channels.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose those waters are only used by small local craft?\xe2\x80\x9d I put in;\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cthat would account for inaccuracies.\xe2\x80\x9d Did Davies think that\r\nAdmiralties had time to waste on smoothing the road for such quixotic\r\nlittle craft as his, in all its inquisitive ramblings? But he fired up.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s all very well,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cbut think what folly it is. However,\r\nthat\xe2\x80\x99s a long story, and will bore you. To cut matters short, for we\r\nought to be turning in, I got to Borkum\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s the first of the\r\n_German_ islands.\xe2\x80\x9d He pointed at a round bare lozenge lying in the\r\nmidst of a welter of sandbanks. \xe2\x80\x9cRottum\xe2\x80\x94this queer little one\xe2\x80\x94it has\r\nonly one house on it\xe2\x80\x94is the most easterly Dutch island, and the\r\nmainland of Holland ends _here_, opposite it, at the Ems\r\nRiver\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94indicating a dismal cavity in the coast, sown with names\r\nsuggestive of mud, and wrecks, and dreariness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat date was this?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAbout the ninth of this month.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, that\xe2\x80\x99s only a fortnight before you wired to me! You were pretty\r\nquick getting to Flensburg. Wait a bit, we want another chart. Is this\r\nthe next?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; but we scarcely need it. I only went a little way farther on\xe2\x80\x94to\r\nNorderney, in fact, the third German island\xe2\x80\x94then I decided to go\r\nstraight for the Baltic. I had always had an idea of getting there, as\r\nKnight did in the _Falcon_. So I made a passage of it to the Eider\r\nRiver, _there_ on the West Schleswig coast, took the river and canal\r\nthrough to Kiel on the Baltic, and from there made another passage up\r\nnorth to Flensburg. I was a week there, and then you came, and here we\r\nare. And now let\xe2\x80\x99s turn in. We\xe2\x80\x99ll have a fine sail to-morrow!\xe2\x80\x9d He ended\r\nwith rather forced vivacity, and briskly rolled up the chart. The\r\nreluctance he had shown from the first to talk about his cruise had\r\nbeen for a brief space forgotten in his enthusiasm about a portion of\r\nit, but had returned markedly in this bald conclusion. I felt sure that\r\nthere was more in it than mere disinclination to spin nautical yarns in\r\nthe \xe2\x80\x9chardy Corinthian\xe2\x80\x9d style, which can be so offensive in amateur\r\nyachtsmen; and I thought I guessed the explanation. His voyage\r\nsingle-handed to the Baltic from the Frisian Islands had been a\r\nfoolhardy enterprise, with perilous incidents, which, rather than make\r\nlight of, he would not refer to at all. Probably he was ashamed of his\r\nrecklessness and wished to ignore it with me, an inexperienced\r\nacquaintance not yet enamoured of the _Dulcibella_\xe2\x80\x99s way of life, whom\r\nboth courtesy and interest demanded that he should inspire with\r\nconfidence. I liked him all the better as I came to this conclusion,\r\nbut I was tempted to persist a little.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI slept the whole afternoon,\xe2\x80\x9d I said; \xe2\x80\x9cand, to tell the truth, I\r\nrather dread the idea of going to bed, it\xe2\x80\x99s so tiring. Look here,\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99ve rushed over that last part like an express train. That passage\r\nto the Schleswig coast\xe2\x80\x94the Eider River, did you say?\xe2\x80\x94was a longish one,\r\nwasn\xe2\x80\x99t it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, you see what it was; about seventy miles, I suppose, direct.\xe2\x80\x9d He\r\nspoke low, bending down to sweep up some cigar ashes on the floor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDirect?\xe2\x80\x9d I insinuated. \xe2\x80\x9cThen you put in somewhere?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI stopped once, anchored for the night; oh, that\xe2\x80\x99s nothing of a sail\r\nwith a fair wind. By Jove! I\xe2\x80\x99ve forgotten to caulk that seam over your\r\nbunk, and it\xe2\x80\x99s going to rain. I must do it now. You turn in.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe disappeared. My curiosity, never very consuming, was banished by\r\nconcern as to the open seam; for the prospect of a big drop,\r\nremorseless and regular as Fate, falling on my forehead throughout the\r\nnight, as in the torture-chamber of the Inquisition, was alarming\r\nenough to recall me wholly to the immediate future. So I went to bed,\r\nfinding on the whole that I had made progress in the exercise, though\r\nstill far from being the trained contortionist that the occasion called\r\nfor. Hammering ceased, and Davies reappeared just as I was stretched on\r\nthe rack\xe2\x80\x94tucked up in my bunk, I mean.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI say,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, when he was settled in his, and darkness reigned, \xe2\x80\x9cdo\r\nyou think you\xe2\x80\x99ll like this sort of thing?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf there are many places about here as beautiful as this,\xe2\x80\x9d I replied,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think I shall. But _I_ should like to land now and then and have a\r\nwalk. Of course, a great deal depends on the weather, doesn\xe2\x80\x99t it? I\r\nhope this rain\xe2\x80\x9d (drops had begun to patter overhead) \xe2\x80\x9cdoesn\xe2\x80\x99t mean that\r\nthe summer\xe2\x80\x99s over for good.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, you can sail just the same,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, \xe2\x80\x9cunless it\xe2\x80\x99s very bad.\r\nThere\xe2\x80\x99s plenty of sheltered water. There\xe2\x80\x99s bound to be a change soon.\r\nBut then there are the ducks. The colder and stormier it is, the better\r\nfor them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI had forgotten the ducks and the cold, and, suddenly presented as a\r\nshooting-box in inclement weather, the _Dulcibella_ lost ground in my\r\nestimation, which she had latterly gained.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m fond of shooting,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, \xe2\x80\x9cbut I\xe2\x80\x99m afraid I\xe2\x80\x99m only a fair-weather\r\nyachtsman, and I should much prefer sun and scenery.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cScenery,\xe2\x80\x9d he repeated, reflectively. \xe2\x80\x9cI say, you must have thought it\r\na queer taste of mine to cruise about on that outlandish Frisian coast.\r\nHow would you like that sort of thing?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should loathe it,\xe2\x80\x9d I answered, promptly, with a clear conscience.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWeren\xe2\x80\x99t you delighted yourself to get to the Baltic? It must be a\r\nwonderful contrast to what you described. Did you ever see another\r\nyacht there?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly one,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered. \xe2\x80\x9cGood night!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood night!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER V.\r\nWanted, a North Wind\r\n\r\n\r\nNothing disturbed my rest that night, so adaptable is youth and so\r\nmasterful is nature. At times I was remotely aware of a threshing of\r\nrain and a humming of wind, with a nervous kicking of the little hull,\r\nand at one moment I dreamt I saw an apparition by candle-light of\r\nDavies, clad in pyjamas and huge top-boots, grasping a misty lantern of\r\ngigantic proportions. But the apparition mounted the ladder and\r\ndisappeared, and I passed to other dreams.\r\n\r\nA blast in my ear, like the voice of fifty trombones, galvanised me\r\ninto full consciousness. The musician, smiling and tousled, was at my\r\nbedside, raising a foghorn to his lips with deadly intention. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a\r\nway we have in the _Dulcibella_,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, as I started up on one\r\nelbow. \xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t startle you much, did I?\xe2\x80\x9d he added.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I like the _mattinata_ better than the cold douche,\xe2\x80\x9d I answered,\r\nthinking of yesterday.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFine day and magnificent breeze!\xe2\x80\x9d he answered. My sensations this\r\nmorning were vastly livelier than those of yesterday at the same hour.\r\nMy limbs were supple again and my head clear. Not even the searching\r\nwind could mar the ecstasy of that plunge down to smooth, seductive\r\nsand, where I buried greedy fingers and looked through a medium blue,\r\nwith that translucent blue, fairy-faint and angel-pure, that you see in\r\nperfection only in the heart of ice. Up again to sun, wind, and the\r\nforest whispers from the shore; down just once more to see the uncouth\r\nanchor stabbing the sand\xe2\x80\x99s soft bosom with one rusty fang, deaf and\r\ninert to the _Dulcibella_\xe2\x80\x99s puny efforts to drag him from his prey.\r\nBack, holding by the cable as a rusty clue from heaven to earth, up to\r\nthat _bourgeoise_ little maiden\xe2\x80\x99s bows; back to breakfast, with an\r\nappetite not to be blunted by condensed milk and somewhat _pass\xc3\xa9_\r\nbread. An hour later we had dressed the _Dulcibella_ for the road, and\r\nwere foaming into the grey void of yesterday, now a noble expanse of\r\nwind-whipped blue, half surrounded by distant hills, their every\r\noutline vivid in the rain-washed air.\r\n\r\nI cannot pretend that I really enjoyed this first sail into the open,\r\nthough I was keenly anxious to do so. I felt the thrill of those\r\nforward leaps, heard that persuasive song the foam sings under the\r\nlee-bow, saw the flashing harmonies of sea and sky; but sensuous\r\nperception was deadened by nervousness. The yacht looked smaller than\r\never outside the quiet fiord. The song of the foam seemed very near,\r\nthe wave crests aft very high. The novice in sailing clings desperately\r\nto the thoughts of sailors\xe2\x80\x94effective, prudent persons, with a typical\r\njargon and a typical dress, versed in local currents and winds. I could\r\nnot help missing this professional element. Davies, as he sat grasping\r\nhis beloved tiller, looked strikingly efficient in his way, and\r\nsupremely at home in his surroundings; but he looked the amateur\r\nthrough and through, as with one hand, and (it seemed) one eye, he\r\nwrestled with a spray-splashed chart half unrolled on the deck beside\r\nhim. All his casual ways returned to me\xe2\x80\x94his casual talk and that last\r\nadventurous voyage to the Baltic, and the suspicions his reticence had\r\naroused.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you see a monument anywhere?\xe2\x80\x9d he said, all at once; and, before I\r\ncould answer; \xe2\x80\x9cWe must take another reef.\xe2\x80\x9d He let go of the tiller and\r\nrelit his pipe, while the yacht rounded sharply to, and in a twinkling\r\nwas tossing head to sea with loud claps of her canvas and passionate\r\njerks of her boom, as the wind leapt on its quarry, now turning to bay,\r\nwith redoubled force. The sting of spray in my eyes and the Babel of\r\nnoise dazed me; but Davies, with a pull on the fore-sheet, soothed the\r\ntormented little ship, and left her coolly sparring with the waves\r\nwhile he shortened sail and puffed his pipe. An hour later the narrow\r\nvista of Als Sound was visible, with quiet old Sonderburg sunning\r\nitself on the island shore, and the Dybbol heights towering above\xe2\x80\x94the\r\nDybbol of bloody memory; scene of the last desperate stand of the Danes\r\nin \xe2\x80\x9964, ere the Prussians wrested the two fair provinces from them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s early to anchor, and I hate towns,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, as one section\r\nof a lumbering pontoon bridge opened to give us passage. But I was firm\r\non the need for a walk, and got my way on condition that I bought\r\nstores as well, and returned in time to admit of further advance to a\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cquiet anchorage\xe2\x80\x9d. Never did I step on the solid earth with stranger\r\nfeelings, partly due to relief from confinement, partly to that sense\r\nof independence in travelling, which, for those who go down to the sea\r\nin small ships, can make the foulest coal-port in Northumbria seem\r\nattractive. And here I had fascinating Sonderburg, with its broad-eaved\r\nhouses of carved woodwork, each fresh with cleansing, yet reverend with\r\nage; its fair-haired Viking-like men, and rosy, plain-faced women, with\r\ntheir bullet foreheads and large mouths; Sonderburg still Danish to the\r\ncore under its Teuton veneer. Crossing the bridge I climbed the\r\nDybbol\xe2\x80\x94dotted with memorials of that heroic defence\xe2\x80\x94and thence could\r\nsee the wee form and gossamer rigging of the _Dulcibella_ on the silver\r\nribbon of the Sound, and was reminded by the sight that there were\r\nstores to be bought. So I hurried down again to the old quarter and\r\nbargained over eggs and bread with a dear old lady, pink as a\r\n_d\xc3\xa9butante,_ made a patriotic pretence of not understanding German, and\r\ncalled in her strapping son, whose few words of English, being chiefly\r\nnautical slang picked up on a British trawler, were peculiarly useless\r\nfor the purpose. Davies had tea ready when I came aboard again, and,\r\ndrinking it on deck, we proceeded up the sheltered Sound, which, in\r\nspite of its imposing name, was no bigger than an inland river, only\r\nthe hosts of rainbow jelly-fish reminding us that we were threading a\r\nhighway of ocean. There is no rise and fall of tide in these regions to\r\ndisfigure the shore with mud. Here was a shelving gravel bank; there a\r\nbed of whispering rushes; there again young birch trees growing to the\r\nvery brink, each wearing a stocking of bright moss and setting its foot\r\nfirmly in among golden leaves and scarlet fungus.\r\n\r\nDavies was preoccupied, but he lighted up when I talked of the Danish\r\nwar. \xe2\x80\x9cGermany\xe2\x80\x99s a thundering great nation,\xe2\x80\x9d he said; \xe2\x80\x9cI wonder if we\r\nshall ever fight her.\xe2\x80\x9d A little incident that happened after we\r\nanchored deepened the impression left by this conversation. We crept at\r\ndusk into a shaded back-water, where our keel almost touched the gravel\r\nbed. Opposite us on the Alsen shore there showed, clean-cut against the\r\nsky, the spire of a little monument rising from a leafy hollow.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wonder what that is,\xe2\x80\x9d I said. It was scarcely a minute\xe2\x80\x99s row in the\r\ndinghy, and when the anchor was down we sculled over to it. A bank of\r\nloam led to gorse and bramble. Pushing aside some branches we came to a\r\nslender Gothic memorial in grey stone, inscribed with bas-reliefs of\r\nbattle scenes, showing Prussians forcing a landing in boats and Danes\r\nresisting with savage tenacity. In the failing light we spelt out an\r\ninscription: \xe2\x80\x9cDen bei dem Meeres Uebergange und der Eroberung von Alsen\r\nam 29. Juni 1864 heldenm\xc3\xbcthig gefallenen zum ehrenden Ged\xc3\xa4chtniss.\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cTo\r\nthe honoured memory of those who died heroically at the invasion and\r\nstorming of Alsen.\xe2\x80\x9d I knew the German passion for commemoration; I had\r\nseen similar memorials on Alsatian battlefields, and several on the\r\nDybbol only that afternoon; but there was something in the scene, the\r\nhour, and the circumstances, which made this one seem singularly\r\ntouching. As for Davies, I scarcely recognised him; his eyes flashed\r\nand filled with tears as he glanced from the inscription to the path we\r\nhad followed and the water beyond. \xe2\x80\x9cIt was a landing in boats, I\r\nsuppose,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, half to himself. \xe2\x80\x9cI wonder they managed it. What\r\ndoes _heldenm\xc3\xbcthig_ mean?\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cHeroically.\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cHeldenm\xc3\xbcthig gefallenen,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nrepeated, under his breath, lingering on each syllable. He was like a\r\nschoolboy reading of Waterloo.\r\n\r\nOur conversation at dinner turned naturally on war, and in naval\r\nwarfare I found I had come upon Davies\xe2\x80\x99s literary hobby. I had not\r\nhitherto paid attention to the medley on our bookshelf, but I now saw\r\nthat, besides a Nautical Almanack and some dilapidated _Sailing\r\nDirections_, there were several books on the cruises of small yachts,\r\nand also some big volumes crushed in anyhow or lying on the top.\r\nSquinting painfully at them I saw Mahan\xe2\x80\x99s _Life of Nelson_, Brassey\xe2\x80\x99s\r\n_Naval Annual_, and others.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a tremendously interesting subject,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, pulling down\r\n(in two pieces) a volume of Mahan\xe2\x80\x99s _Influence of Sea Power_.\r\n\r\nDinner flagged (and froze) while he illustrated a point by reference to\r\nthe much-thumbed pages. He was very keen, and not very articulate. I\r\nknew just enough to be an intelligent listener, and, though hungry, was\r\ndelighted to hear him talk.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m not boring you, am I?\xe2\x80\x9d he said, suddenly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should think not,\xe2\x80\x9d I protested. \xe2\x80\x9cBut you might just have a look at\r\nthe chops.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey had indeed been crying aloud for notice for some minutes, and drew\r\ncandid attention to their neglect when they appeared. The diversion\r\nthey caused put Davies out of vein. I tried to revive the subject, but\r\nhe was reserved and diffident.\r\n\r\nThe untidy bookshelf reminded me of the logbook, and when Davies had\r\nretired with the crockery to the forecastle, I pulled the ledger down\r\nand turned over the leaves. It was a mass of short entries, with\r\ncryptic abbreviations, winds, tides, weather, and courses appearing to\r\npredominate. The voyage from Dover to Ostend was dismissed in two\r\nlines: \xe2\x80\x9cUnder way 7 p.m., wind W.S.W. moderate; West Hinder 5 a.m.,\r\noutside all banks; Ostend 11 a.m.\xe2\x80\x9d The Scheldt had a couple of pages\r\nvery technical and _staccato_ in style. Inland Holland was given a\r\ncontemptuous summary, with some half-hearted allusions to windmills,\r\nand so on, and a caustic word or two about boys, paint, and canal\r\nsmells.\r\n\r\nAt Amsterdam technicalities began again, and a brisker tone pervaded\r\nthe entries, which became progressively fuller as the writer cruised on\r\nthe Frisian coast. He was clearly in better spirits, for here and there\r\nwere quaint and laboured efforts to describe nature out of material\r\nwhich, as far as I could judge, was repellent enough to discourage the\r\nmost brilliant and observant of writers; with an occasional note of a\r\nvisit on shore, generally reached by a walk of half a mile over sand,\r\nand of talks with shop people and fishermen. But such lighter relief\r\nwas rare. The bulk dealt with channels and shoals with weird and\r\ndepressing names, with the centre-plate, the sails, and the wind, buoys\r\nand \xe2\x80\x9cbooms\xe2\x80\x9d, tides and \xe2\x80\x9cberths\xe2\x80\x9d for the night. \xe2\x80\x9cKedging off\xe2\x80\x9d appeared\r\nto be a frequent diversion; \xe2\x80\x9crunning aground\xe2\x80\x9d was of almost daily\r\noccurrence.\r\n\r\nIt was not easy reading, and I turned the leaves rapidly. I was\r\ncurious, too, to see the latter part. I came to a point where the rain\r\nof little sentences, pattering out like small shot, ceased abruptly. It\r\nwas at the end of September 9. That day, with its \xe2\x80\x9ckedging\xe2\x80\x9d and\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cboom-dodging\xe2\x80\x9d, was filled in with the usual detail. The log then leapt\r\nover three days, and went on: \xe2\x80\x9c_Sept._ 13. Wind W.N.W. fresh. Decided\r\nto go to Baltic. Sailed 4 a.m. Quick passage E. 1/2 S. to mouth of\r\nWeser. Anchored for night under Hohenh\xc3\xb6rn Sand. _ Sept._ 14, _nil_.\r\n_Sept._ 15, under way at 4 a.m. Wind East moderate. Course W. by S.;\r\nfour miles; N.E. by N. fifteen miles. Norderpiep 9.30. Eider River\r\n11.30.\xe2\x80\x9d This recital of naked facts was quite characteristic when\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cpassages\xe2\x80\x9d were concerned, and any curiosity I had felt about his\r\nreticence on the previous night would have been rather allayed than\r\nstimulated had I not noticed that a page had been torn out of the book\r\njust at this point. The frayed edge left had been pruned and picked\r\ninto very small limits; but dissimulation was not Davies\xe2\x80\x99s strong\r\npoint, and a child could have seen that a leaf was missing, and that\r\nthe entries, starting from the evening of September 9 (where a page\r\nended), had been written together at one sitting. I was on the point of\r\ncalling to Davies, and chaffing him with having committed a grave\r\noffence against maritime law in having \xe2\x80\x9ccooked\xe2\x80\x9d his log; but I checked\r\nmyself, I scarcely know why, probably because I guessed the joke would\r\ntouch a sensitive place and fail. Delicacy shrank from seeing him\r\ncompelled either to amplify a deception or blunder out a confession\xe2\x80\x94he\r\nwas too easy a prey; and, after all, the matter was of small moment. I\r\nreturned the book to the shelf, the only definite result of its perusal\r\nbeing to recall my promise to keep a diary myself, and I then and there\r\ndedicated a notebook to the purpose.\r\n\r\nWe were just lighting our cigars when we heard voices and the splash of\r\noars, followed by a bump against the hull which made Davies wince, as\r\nviolations of his paint always did. \xe2\x80\x9cGuten Abend; wo fahren Sie hin?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\ngreeted us as we climbed on deck. It turned out to be some jovial\r\nfishermen returning to their smack from a visit to Sonderburg. A short\r\ndialogue proved to them that we were mad Englishmen in bitter need of\r\ncharity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome to Satrup,\xe2\x80\x9d they said; \xe2\x80\x9call the smacks are there, round the\r\npoint. There is good punch in the inn.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNothing loth, we followed in the dinghy, skirted a bend of the Sound,\r\nand opened up the lights of a village, with some smacks at anchor in\r\nfront of it. We were escorted to the inn, and introduced to a\r\nformidable beverage, called coffee-punch, and a smoke-wreathed circle\r\nof smacksmen, who talked German out of courtesy, but were Danish in all\r\nelse. Davies was at once at home with them, to a degree, indeed, that I\r\nenvied. His German was of the crudest kind, _bizarre_ in vocabulary and\r\ncomical in accent; but the freemasonry of the sea, or some charm of his\r\nown, gave intuition to both him and his hearers. I cut a poor figure in\r\nthis nautical gathering, though Davies, who persistently referred to me\r\nas \xe2\x80\x9cmeiner Freund\xe2\x80\x9d, tried hard to represent me as a kindred spirit and\r\nto include me in the general talk. I was detected at once as an\r\nuninteresting hybrid. Davies, who sometimes appealed to me for a word,\r\nwas deep in talk over anchorages and ducks, especially, as I well\r\nremember now, about the chance of sport in a certain _Schlei Fiord_. I\r\nfell into utter neglect, till rescued by a taciturn person in\r\nspectacles and a very high cap, who appeared to be the only landsman\r\npresent. After silently puffing smoke in my direction for some time, he\r\nasked me if I was married, and if not, when I proposed to be. After\r\nthis inquisition he abandoned me.\r\n\r\nIt was eleven before we left this hospitable inn, escorted by the whole\r\nparty to the dinghy. Our friends of the smack insisted on our sharing\r\ntheir boat out of pure good-fellowship\xe2\x80\x94for there was not nearly room\r\nfor us\xe2\x80\x94and would not let us go till a bucket of fresh-caught fish had\r\nbeen emptied into her bottom. After much shaking of scaly hands, we\r\nsculled back to the _Dulcibella_, where she slept in a bed of tremulous\r\nstars.\r\n\r\nDavies sniffed the wind and scanned the tree-tops, where light gusts\r\nwere toying with the leaves.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSou\xe2\x80\x99-west still,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cand more rain coming. But it\xe2\x80\x99s bound to\r\nshift into the north.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill that be a good wind for us?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt depends where we go,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, slowly. \xe2\x80\x9cI was asking those fellows\r\nabout duck-shooting. They seemed to think the best place would be\r\nSchlei Fiord. That\xe2\x80\x99s about fifteen miles south of Sonderburg, on the\r\nway to Kiel. They said there was a pilot chap living at the mouth who\r\nwould tell us all about it. They weren\xe2\x80\x99t very encouraging though. We\r\nshould want a north wind for that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t care where we go,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, to my own surprise.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you really?\xe2\x80\x9d he rejoined, with sudden warmth. Then, with a\r\nslight change of voice. \xe2\x80\x9cYou mean it\xe2\x80\x99s all very jolly about here?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOf course I meant that. Before we went below we both looked for a\r\nmoment at the little grey memorial; its slender fretted arch outlined\r\nin tender lights and darks above the hollow on the Alsen shore. The\r\nnight was that of September 27, the third I had spent on the\r\n_Dulcibella_.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VI.\r\nSchlei Fiord\r\n\r\n\r\nI make no apology for having described these early days in some detail.\r\nIt is no wonder that their trivialities are as vividly before me as the\r\ncolours of earth and sea in this enchanting corner of the world. For\r\nevery trifle, sordid or picturesque, was relevant; every scrap of talk\r\na link; every passing mood critical for good or ill. So slight indeed\r\nwere the determining causes that changed my autumn holiday into an\r\nundertaking the most momentous I have ever approached.\r\n\r\nTwo days more preceded the change. On the first, the southwesterly wind\r\nstill holding, we sallied forth into Augustenburg Fiord, \xe2\x80\x9cto practise\r\nsmartness in a heavy thresh,\xe2\x80\x9d as Davies put it. It was the day of\r\ndedication for those disgusting oilskins, immured in whose stiff and\r\nodorous angles, I felt distressfully cumbersome; a day of proof indeed\r\nfor me, for heavy squalls swept incessantly over the loch, and Davies,\r\nat my own request, gave me no rest. Backwards and forwards we tacked,\r\nblustering into coves and out again, reefing and unreefing, now stung\r\nwith rain, now warmed with sun, but never with time to breathe or\r\nthink.\r\n\r\nI wrestled with intractable ropes, slaves if they could be subdued,\r\ntyrants if they got the upper hand; creeping, craning, straining, I\r\nmade the painful round of the deck, while Davies, hatless and tranquil,\r\ndirected my blundering movements.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow take the helm and try steering in a hard breeze to windward. It\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nthe finest sport on earth.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo I grappled with the niceties of that delicate craft; smarting eyes,\r\nchafed hands, and dazed brain all pressed into the service, whilst\r\nDavies, taming the ropes the while, shouted into my ear the subtle\r\nmysteries of the art; that fidgeting ripple in the luff of the\r\nmainsail, and the distant rattle from the hungry jib\xe2\x80\x94signs that they\r\nare starved of wind and must be given more; the heavy list and wallow\r\nof the hull, the feel of the wind on your cheek instead of your nose,\r\nthe broader angle of the burgee at the masthead\xe2\x80\x94signs that they have\r\ntoo much, and that she is sagging recreantly to leeward instead of\r\nfighting to windward. He taught me the tactics for meeting squalls, and\r\nthe way to press your advantage when they are defeated\xe2\x80\x94the iron hand in\r\nthe velvet glove that the wilful tiller needs if you are to gain your\r\nends with it; the exact set of the sheets necessary to get the easiest\r\nand swiftest play of the hull\xe2\x80\x94all these things and many more I\r\nstruggled to apprehend, careless for the moment as to whether they were\r\nworth knowing, but doggedly set on knowing them. Needless to say, I had\r\nno eyes for beauty. The wooded inlets we dived into gave a brief\r\nrespite from wind and spindrift, but called into use the lead and the\r\ncentreboard tackle\xe2\x80\x94two new and cumbrous complexities. Davies\xe2\x80\x99s passion\r\nfor intricate navigation had to be sated even in these secure and\r\ntideless waters.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet\xe2\x80\x99s get in as near as we can\xe2\x80\x94you stand by the lead,\xe2\x80\x9d was his\r\nformula; so I made false casts, tripped up in the slack, sent rivers of\r\nwater up my sleeves, and committed all the other _gaucheries_ that\r\nbeginners in the art commit, while the sand showed whiter beneath the\r\nkeel, till Davies regretfully drew off and shouted: \xe2\x80\x9cReady about,\r\ncentre-plate down,\xe2\x80\x9d and I dashed down to the trappings of that\r\ndiabolical contrivance, the only part of the _Dulcibella_\xe2\x80\x99s equipment\r\nthat I hated fiercely to the last. It had an odious habit when lowered\r\nof spouting jets of water through its chain-lead on to the cabin floor.\r\nOne of my duties was to gag it with cotton-waste, but even then its\r\nchoking gurgle was a most uncomfortable sound in your dining-room. In a\r\nminute the creek would be behind us and we would be thumping our stem\r\ninto the short hollow waves of the fiord, and lurching through spray\r\nand rain for some point on the opposite shore. Of our destination and\r\nobjects, if we had any, I knew nothing. At the northern end of the\r\nfiord, just before we turned, Davies had turned dreamy in the most\r\nexasperating way, for I was steering at the time and in mortal need of\r\nsympathetic guidance, if I was to avoid a sudden jibe. As though\r\ncontinuing aloud some internal debate, he held a onesided argument to\r\nthe effect that it was no use going farther north. Ducks, weather, and\r\ncharts figured in it, but I did not follow the pros and cons. I only\r\nknow that we suddenly turned and began to \xe2\x80\x9cbattle\xe2\x80\x9d south again. At\r\nsunset we were back once more in the same quiet pool among the trees\r\nand fields of Als Sound, a wondrous peace succeeding the turmoil.\r\nBruised and sodden, I was extricating myself from my oily prison, and\r\nlater was tasting (though not nearly yet in its perfection) the unique\r\nexultation that follows such a day, when, glowing all over, deliciously\r\ntired and pleasantly sore, you eat what seems ambrosia, be it only\r\ntinned beef; and drink nectar, be it only distilled from terrestrial\r\nhops or coffee berries, and inhale as culminating luxury balmy fumes\r\nwhich even the happy Homeric gods knew naught of.\r\n\r\nOn the following morning, the 30th, a joyous shout of \xe2\x80\x9cNor\xe2\x80\x99-west wind\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsent me shivering on deck, in the small hours, to handle rain-stiff\r\ncanvas and cutting chain. It was a cloudy, unsettled day, but still\r\nenough after yesterday\xe2\x80\x99s boisterous ordeal. We retraced our way past\r\nSonderburg, and thence sailed for a faint line of pale green on the far\r\nsouth-western horizon. It was during this passage that an incident\r\noccurred, which, slight as it was, opened my eyes to much.\r\n\r\nA flight of wild duck crossed our bows at some little distance, a\r\nwedge-shaped phalanx of craning necks and flapping wings. I happened to\r\nbe steering while Davies verified our course below; but I called him up\r\nat once, and a discussion began about our chances of sport. Davies was\r\ngloomy over them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThose fellows at Satrup were rather doubtful,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cThere are\r\nplenty of ducks, but I made out that it\xe2\x80\x99s not easy for strangers to get\r\nshooting. The whole country\xe2\x80\x99s so very civilised; it\xe2\x80\x99s not _wild_\r\nenough, is it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe looked at me. I had no very clear opinion. It was anything but wild\r\nin one sense, but there seemed to be wild enough spots for ducks. The\r\nshore we were passing appeared to be bordered by lonely marshes, though\r\na spacious champaign showed behind. If it were not for the beautiful\r\nplaces we had seen, and my growing taste for our way of seeing them,\r\nhis disappointing vagueness would have nettled me more than it did.\r\nFor, after all, he had brought me out loaded with sporting equipment\r\nunder a promise of shooting.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBad weather is what we want for ducks,\xe2\x80\x9d he said; \xe2\x80\x9cbut I\xe2\x80\x99m afraid we\xe2\x80\x99re\r\nin the wrong place for them. Now, if it was the North Sea, among those\r\nFrisian Islands\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d His tone was timid and interrogative, and I felt at\r\nonce that he was sounding me as to some unpalatable plan whose nature\r\nbegan to dawn on me.\r\n\r\nHe stammered on through a sentence or two about \xe2\x80\x9cwildness\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cnobody\r\nto interfere with you,\xe2\x80\x9d and then I broke in: \xe2\x80\x9cYou surely don\xe2\x80\x99t want to\r\nleave the Baltic?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy not?\xe2\x80\x9d said he, staring into the compass.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHang it, man!\xe2\x80\x9d I returned, tartly, \xe2\x80\x9chere we are in October, the summer\r\nover, and the weather gone to pieces. We\xe2\x80\x99re alone in a cockle-shell\r\nboat, at a time when every other yacht of our size is laying up for the\r\nwinter. Luckily, we seem to have struck an ideal cruising-ground, with\r\na wide choice of safe fiords and a good prospect of ducks, if we choose\r\nto take a little trouble about them. You can\xe2\x80\x99t mean to waste time and\r\nrun risks\xe2\x80\x9d (I thought of the torn leaf in the log-book) \xe2\x80\x9cin a long\r\nvoyage to those forbidding haunts of yours in the North Sea.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s not very long,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, doggedly. \xe2\x80\x9cPart of it\xe2\x80\x99s canal, and\r\nthe rest is quite safe if you\xe2\x80\x99re careful. There\xe2\x80\x99s plenty of sheltered\r\nwater, and it\xe2\x80\x99s not really necessary\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s it all _for?_\xe2\x80\x9d I interrupted, impatiently. \xe2\x80\x9cWe haven\xe2\x80\x99t _tried_\r\nfor shooting here yet. You\xe2\x80\x99ve no notion, have you, of getting the boat\r\nback to England this autumn?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEngland?\xe2\x80\x9d he muttered. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, I don\xe2\x80\x99t much care.\xe2\x80\x9d Again his vagueness\r\njarred on me; there seemed to be some bar between us, invisible and\r\ninsurmountable. And, after all, what was I doing here? Roughing it in a\r\nshabby little yacht, utterly out of my element, with a man who, a week\r\nago, was nothing to me, and who now was a tiresome enigma. Like swift\r\npoison the old morbid mood in which I left London spread through me.\r\nAll I had learnt and seen slipped away; what I had suffered remained. I\r\nwas on the point of saying something which might have put a precipitate\r\nend to our cruise, but he anticipated me.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m awfully sorry,\xe2\x80\x9d he broke out, \xe2\x80\x9cfor being such a selfish brute. I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t know what I was thinking about. You\xe2\x80\x99re a brick to join me in this\r\nsort of life, and I\xe2\x80\x99m afraid I\xe2\x80\x99m an infernally bad host. Of course this\r\nis just the place to cruise. I forgot about the scenery, and all that.\r\nLet\xe2\x80\x99s ask about the ducks here. As you say, we\xe2\x80\x99re sure to get sport if\r\nwe worry and push a bit. We must be nearly there now\xe2\x80\x94yes, there\xe2\x80\x99s the\r\nentrance. Take the helm, will you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe sprang up the mast like a monkey, and gazed over the land from the\r\ncross-trees. I looked up at my enigma and thanked Providence I had not\r\nspoken; for no one could have resisted his frank outburst of good\r\nnature. Yet it occurred to me that, considering the conditions of our\r\nlife, our intimacy was strangely slow in growth. I had no clue yet as\r\nto where his idiosyncrasies began and his self ended, and he, I\r\nsurmised, was in the same stage towards me. Otherwise I should have\r\npressed him further now, for I felt convinced that there was some\r\nmystery in his behaviour which I had not yet accounted for. However,\r\nlight was soon to break.\r\n\r\nI could see no sign of the entrance he had spoken of, and no wonder,\r\nfor it is only eighty yards wide, though it leads to a fiord thirty\r\nmiles long. All at once we were jolting in a tumble of sea, and the\r\nchannel grudgingly disclosed itself, stealing between marshes and\r\nmeadows and then broadening to a mere, as at Ekken. We anchored close\r\nto the mouth, and not far from a group of vessels of a type that\r\nafterwards grew very familiar to me. They were sailing-barges,\r\nsomething like those that ply in the Thames, bluff-bowed, high-sterned\r\ncraft of about fifty tons, ketch-rigged, and fitted with lee-boards,\r\nvery light spars, and a long tip-tilted bowsprit. (For the future I\r\nshall call them \xe2\x80\x9cgalliots\xe2\x80\x9d.) Otherwise the only sign of life was a\r\nsolitary white house\xe2\x80\x94the pilot\xe2\x80\x99s house, the chart told us\xe2\x80\x94close to the\r\nnorthern point of entrance. After tea we called on the pilot.\r\nPatriarchally installed before a roaring stove, in the company of a\r\nbuxom bustling daughter-in-law and some rosy grandchildren, we found a\r\nrotund and rubicund person, who greeted us with a hoarse roar of\r\nwelcome in German, which instantly changed, when he saw us, to the\r\nfunniest broken English, spoken with intense relish and pride. We\r\nexplained ourselves and our mission as well as we could through the\r\nhospitable interruptions caused by beer and the strains of a huge\r\nmusical box, which had been set going in honour of our arrival.\r\nNeedless to say, I was read like a book at once, and fell into the part\r\nof listener.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, yes,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9call right. There is plenty ducks, but first we\r\nwill drink a glass beer; then we will shift your ship, captain\xe2\x80\x94she lies\r\nnot good there.\xe2\x80\x9d (Davies started up in a panic, but was waved back to\r\nhis beer.) \xe2\x80\x9cThen we will drink together another glass beer; then we\r\nwill talk of ducks\xe2\x80\x94no, then we will kill ducks\xe2\x80\x94that is better. Then we\r\nwill have plenty glasses beer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis was an unexpected climax, and promised well for our prospects. And\r\nthe programme was fully carried out. After the beer our host was packed\r\nbriskly by his daughter into an armour of woollen gaiters, coats, and\r\nmufflers, topped with a worsted helmet, which left nothing of his face\r\nvisible but a pair of twinkling eyes. Thus equipped, he led the way out\r\nof doors, and roared for Hans and his gun, till a great gawky youth,\r\nwith high cheek-bones and a downy beard, came out from the yard and\r\nsheepishly shook our hands.\r\n\r\nTogether we repaired to the quay, where the pilot stood, looking like a\r\ngenial ball of worsted, and bawled hoarse directions while we shifted\r\nthe _Dulcibella_ to a berth on the farther shore close to the other\r\nvessels. We returned with our guns, and the interval for refreshments\r\nfollowed. It was just dusk when we sallied out again, crossed a stretch\r\nof bog-land, and took up strategic posts round a stagnant pond. Hans\r\nhad been sent to drive, and the result was a fine mallard and three\r\nducks. It was true that all fell to the pilot\xe2\x80\x99s gun, perhaps owing to\r\nHans\xe2\x80\x99s filial instinct and his parent\xe2\x80\x99s canny egotism in choosing his\r\nown lair, or perhaps it was chance; but the shooting-party was none the\r\nless a triumphal success. It was celebrated with beer and music as\r\nbefore, while the pilot, an infant on each podgy knee, discoursed\r\nexuberantly on the glories of his country and the Elysian content of\r\nhis life. \xe2\x80\x9cThere is plenty beer, plenty meat, plenty money, plenty\r\nducks,\xe2\x80\x9d summed up his survey.\r\n\r\nIt may have been fancy, but Davies, though he had fits and starts of\r\nvivacity, seemed very inattentive, considering that we were sitting at\r\nthe feet of so expansive an oracle. It was I who elicited most of the\r\npractical information\xe2\x80\x94details of time, weather, and likely places for\r\nshooting, with some shrewd hints as to the kind of people to\r\nconciliate. Whatever he thought of me, I warmed with sympathy towards\r\nthe pilot, for he assumed that we had done with cruising for the year,\r\nand thought us mad enough as it was to have been afloat so long, and\r\nmadder still to intend living on \xe2\x80\x9cso little a ship\xe2\x80\x9d when we could live\r\non land with beer and music handy. I was tempted to raise the North Sea\r\nquestion, just to watch Davies under the thunder of rebukes which would\r\nfollow. But I refrained from a wish to be tender with him, now that all\r\nwas going so well. The Frisian Islands were an extravagant absurdity\r\nnow. I did not even refer to them as we pulled back to the\r\n_Dulcibella_, after swearing eternal friendship with the good pilot and\r\nhis family.\r\n\r\nDavies and I turned in good friends that night\xe2\x80\x94or rather I should say\r\nthat I turned in, for I left him sucking an empty pipe and aimlessly\r\nfingering a volume of Mahan; and once when I woke in the night I felt\r\nsomehow that his bunk was empty and that he was there in the dark\r\ncabin, dreaming.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VII.\r\nThe Missing Page\r\n\r\n\r\nI woke (on the 1st of October) with that dispiriting sensation that a\r\nhitch has occurred in a settled plan. It was explained when I went on\r\ndeck, and I found the _Dulcibella_ wrapped in a fog, silent, clammy,\r\nnothing visible from her decks but the ghostly hull of a galliot at\r\nanchor near us. She must have brought up there in the night, for there\r\nhad been nothing so close the evening before; and I remembered that my\r\nsleep had been broken once by sounds of rumbling chain and gruff\r\nvoices.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis looks pretty hopeless for to-day,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, with a shiver, to\r\nDavies, who was laying the breakfast.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, we can\xe2\x80\x99t do anything till this fog lifts,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered, with a\r\ngood deal of resignation. Breakfast was a cheerless meal. The damp\r\npenetrated to the very cabin, whose roof and walls wept a fine dew. I\r\nhad dreaded a bathe, and yet missed it, and the ghastly light made the\r\ntablecloth look dirtier than it naturally was, and all the accessories\r\nmore sordid. Something had gone wrong with the bacon, and the lack of\r\negg-cups was not in the least humorous.\r\n\r\nDavies was just beginning, in his summary way, to tumble the things\r\ntogether for washing up, when there was a sound of a step on deck, two\r\nsea-boots appeared on the ladder, and, before we could wonder who the\r\nvisitor was, a little man in oilskins and a sou\xe2\x80\x99-wester was stooping\r\ntowards us in the cabin door, smiling affectionately at Davies out of a\r\nround grizzled beard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell met, captain,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, quietly, in German. \xe2\x80\x9cWhere are you bound\r\nto this time?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBartels!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Davies, jumping up. The two stooping figures,\r\nyoung and old, beamed at one another like father and son.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere have you come from? Have some coffee. How\xe2\x80\x99s the _Johannes_? Was\r\nthat you that came in last night? I\xe2\x80\x99m delighted to see you!\xe2\x80\x9d (I spare\r\nthe reader his uncouth lingo.) The little man was dragged in and seated\r\non the opposite sofa to me.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI took my apples to Kappeln,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, sedately, \xe2\x80\x9cand now I sail to\r\nKiel, and so to Hamburg, where my wife and children are. It is my last\r\nvoyage of the year. You are no longer alone, captain, I see.\xe2\x80\x9d He had\r\ntaken off his dripping sou\xe2\x80\x99-wester and was bowing ceremoniously towards\r\nme.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I quite forgot!\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, who had been kneeling on one knee in\r\nthe low doorway, absorbed in his visitor. \xe2\x80\x9cThis is \xe2\x80\x98_meiner Freund_,\xe2\x80\x99\r\nHerr Carruthers. Carruthers, this is my friend, Schiffer Bartels, of\r\nthe galliot _Johannes_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWas I never to be at an end of the puzzles which Davies presented to\r\nme? All the impulsive heartiness died out of his voice and manner as he\r\nuttered the last few words, and there he was, nervously glancing from\r\nthe visitor to me, like one who, against his will or from tactlessness,\r\nhas introduced two persons who he knows will disagree.\r\n\r\nThere was a pause while he fumbled with the cups, poured some cold\r\ncoffee out and pondered over it as though it were a chemical\r\nexperiment. Then he muttered something about boiling some more water,\r\nand took refuge in the forecastle. I was ill at ease at this period\r\nwith seafaring men, but this mild little person was easy ground for a\r\nbeginner. Besides, when he took off his oilskin coat he reminded me\r\nless of a sailor than of a homely draper of some country town, with his\r\nclean turned-down collar and neatly fitting frieze jacket. We exchanged\r\nsome polite platitudes about the fog and his voyage last night from\r\nKappeln, which appeared to be a town some fifteen miles up the fiord.\r\n\r\nDavies joined in from the forecastle with an excess of warmth which\r\nalmost took the words out of my mouth. We exhausted the subject very\r\nsoon, and then my _vis-\xc3\xa0-vis_ smiled paternally at me, as he had done\r\nat Davies, and said, confidentially:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is good that the captain is no more alone. He is a fine young\r\nman\xe2\x80\x94Heaven, what a fine young man! I love him as my son\xe2\x80\x94but he is too\r\nbrave, too reckless. It is good for him to have a friend.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI nodded and laughed, though in reality I was very far from being\r\namused.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere was it you met?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn an ugly place, and in ugly weather,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered, gravely, but with\r\na twinkle of fun in his eye. \xe2\x80\x9cBut has he not told you?\xe2\x80\x9d he added, with\r\nponderous slyness. \xe2\x80\x9cI came just in time. No! what am I saying? He is\r\nbrave as a lion and quick as a cat. I think he cannot drown; but still\r\nit was an ugly place and ugly\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you talking about, Bartels?\xe2\x80\x9d interrupted Davies, emerging\r\nnoisily with a boiling kettle.\r\n\r\nI answered the question. \xe2\x80\x9cI was just asking your friend how it was you\r\nmade his acquaintance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, he helped me out of a bit of a mess in the North Sea, didn\xe2\x80\x99t you,\r\nBartels?\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was nothing,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bartels. \xe2\x80\x9cBut the North Sea is no place for your\r\nlittle boat, captain. So I have told you many times. How did you like\r\nFlensburg? A fine town, is it not? Did you find Herr Krank, the\r\ncarpenter? I see you have placed a little mizzen-mast. The rudder was\r\nnothing much, but it was well that it held to the Eider. But she is\r\nstrong and good, your little ship, and\xe2\x80\x94Heaven!\xe2\x80\x94she had need be so.\xe2\x80\x9d He\r\nchuckled, and shook his head at Davies as at a wayward child.\r\n\r\nThis is all the conversation that I need record. For my part I merely\r\nwaited for its end, determined on my course, which was to know the\r\ntruth once and for all, and make an end of these distracting\r\nmystifications. Davies plied his friend with coffee, and kept up the\r\ntalk gallantly; but affectionate as he was, his manner plainly showed\r\nthat he wanted to be alone with me.\r\n\r\nThe gist of the little skipper\xe2\x80\x99s talk was a parental warning that,\r\nthough we were well enough here in the \xe2\x80\x9cOst-See\xe2\x80\x9d, it was time for\r\nlittle boats to be looking for winter quarters. That he himself was\r\ngoing by the Kiel Canal to Hamburg to spend a cosy winter as a decent\r\ncitizen at his warm fireside, and that we should follow his example. He\r\nended with an invitation to us to visit him on the _Johannes_, and with\r\nsuave farewells disappeared into the fog. Davies saw him into his boat,\r\nreturned without wasting a moment, and sat down on the sofa opposite\r\nme.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat did he mean?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you the whole thing. As far as\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99re concerned it\xe2\x80\x99s partly a confession. Last night I had made up my\r\nmind to say nothing, but when Bartels turned up I knew it must all come\r\nout. It\xe2\x80\x99s been fearfully on my mind, and perhaps you\xe2\x80\x99ll be able to help\r\nme. But it\xe2\x80\x99s for you to decide.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFire away!\xe2\x80\x9d I said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou know what I was saying about the Frisian Islands the other day? A\r\nthing happened there which I never told you, when you were asking about\r\nmy cruise.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt began near Norderney,\xe2\x80\x9d I put in.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow did you guess that?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re a bad hand at duplicity,\xe2\x80\x9d I replied. \xe2\x80\x9cGo on.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, you\xe2\x80\x99re quite right, it was there, on September 9. I told you the\r\nsort of thing I was doing at that time, but I don\xe2\x80\x99t think I said that I\r\nmade inquiries from one or two people about duck-shooting, and had been\r\ntold by some fishermen at Borkum that there was a big sailing-yacht in\r\nthose waters, whose owner, a German of the name of Dollmann, shot a\r\ngood deal, and might give me some tips. Well, I found this yacht one\r\nevening, knowing it must be her from the description I had. She was\r\nwhat is called a \xe2\x80\x98barge-yacht\xe2\x80\x99, of fifty or sixty tons, built for\r\nshallow water on the lines of a Dutch galliot, with lee-boards and\r\nthose queer round bows and square stern. She\xe2\x80\x99s something like those\r\ngalliots anchored near us now. You sometimes see the same sort of yacht\r\nin English waters, only there they copy the Thames barges. She looked a\r\nclipper of her sort, and very smart; varnished all over and shining\r\nlike gold. I came on her about sunset, after a long day of exploring\r\nround the Ems estuary. She was lying in\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWait a bit, let\xe2\x80\x99s have the chart,\xe2\x80\x9d I interrupted.\r\n\r\nDavies found it and spread it on the table between us, first pushing\r\nback the cloth and the breakfast things to one end, where they lay in a\r\nslovenly litter. This was one of the only two occasions on which I ever\r\nsaw him postpone the rite of washing up, and it spoke volumes for the\r\nurgency of the matter in hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere it is,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies _[See Map A]_ and I looked with a new and\r\nstrange interest at the long string of slender islands, the parallel\r\nline of coast, and the confusion of shoals, banks, and channels which\r\nlay between. \xe2\x80\x9cHere\xe2\x80\x99s Norderney, you see. By the way, there\xe2\x80\x99s a harbour\r\nthere at the west end of the island, the only real harbour on the whole\r\nline of islands, Dutch or German, except at Terschelling. There\xe2\x80\x99s quite\r\na big town there, too, a watering place, where Germans go for\r\nsea-bathing in the summer. Well, the _Medusa_, that was her name, was\r\nlying in the Riff Gat roadstead, flying the German ensign, and I\r\nanchored for the night pretty near her. I meant to visit her owner\r\nlater on, but I very nearly changed my mind, as I always feel rather a\r\nfool on smart yachts, and my German isn\xe2\x80\x99t very good. However, I thought\r\nI might as well; so, after dinner, when it was dark, I sculled over in\r\nthe dinghy, hailed a sailor on deck, said who I was, and asked if I\r\ncould see the owner. The sailor was a surly sort of chap, and there was\r\na good long delay while I waited on deck, feeling more and more\r\nuncomfortable. Presently a steward came up and showed me down the\r\ncompanion and into the saloon, which, after _this_, looked\xe2\x80\x94well,\r\nhorribly gorgeous\xe2\x80\x94you know what I mean, plush lounges, silk cushions,\r\nand that sort of thing. Dinner seemed to be just over, and wine and\r\nfruit were on the table. Herr Dollmann was there at his coffee. I\r\nintroduced myself somehow\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStop a moment,\xe2\x80\x9d I said; \xe2\x80\x9cwhat was he like?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, a tall, thin chap, in evening dress; about fifty I suppose, with\r\ngreyish hair and a short beard. I\xe2\x80\x99m not good at describing people. He\r\nhad a high, bulging forehead, and there was something about him\xe2\x80\x94but I\r\nthink I\xe2\x80\x99d better tell you the bare facts first. I can\xe2\x80\x99t say he seemed\r\npleased to see me, and he couldn\xe2\x80\x99t speak English, and, in fact, I felt\r\ninfernally awkward. Still, I had an object in coming, and as I was\r\nthere I thought I might as well gain it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe notion of Davies in his Norfolk jacket and rusty flannels\r\nharanguing a frigid German in evening dress in a \xe2\x80\x9cgorgeous\xe2\x80\x9d saloon\r\ntickled my fancy greatly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe seemed very much astonished to see me; had evidently seen the\r\n_Dulcibella_ arrive, and had wondered what she was. I began as soon as\r\nI could about the ducks, but he shut me up at once, said I could do\r\nnothing hereabouts. I put it down to sportsman\xe2\x80\x99s jealousy\xe2\x80\x94you know what\r\nthat is. But I saw I had come to the wrong shop, and was just going to\r\nback out and end this unpleasant interview, when he thawed a bit,\r\noffered me some wine, and began talking in quite a friendly way, taking\r\na great interest in my cruise and my plans for the future. In the end\r\nwe sat up quite late, though I never felt really at my ease. He seemed\r\nto be taking stock of me all the time, as though I were some new\r\nanimal.\xe2\x80\x9d (How I sympathised with that German!) \xe2\x80\x9cWe parted civilly\r\nenough, and I rowed back and turned in, meaning to potter on eastwards\r\nearly next day.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I was knocked up at dawn by a sailor with a message from Dollmann\r\nasking if he could come to breakfast with me. I was rather\r\nflabbergasted, but didn\xe2\x80\x99t like to be rude, so I said, \xe2\x80\x98Yes.\xe2\x80\x99 Well, he\r\ncame, and I returned the call\xe2\x80\x94and\xe2\x80\x94well, the end of it was that I stayed\r\nat anchor there for three days.\xe2\x80\x9d This was rather abrupt.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow did you spend the time?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked. Stopping three days anywhere was\r\nan unusual event for him, as I knew from his log.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I lunched or dined with him once or twice\xe2\x80\x94with _them_, I ought to\r\nsay,\xe2\x80\x9d he added, hurriedly. \xe2\x80\x9cHis daughter was with him. She didn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nappear the evening I first called.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what was she like?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked, promptly, before he could hurry on.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, she seemed a very nice girl,\xe2\x80\x9d was the guarded reply, delivered\r\nwith particular unconcern, \xe2\x80\x9cand\xe2\x80\x94the end of it was that I and the\r\n_Medusa_ sailed away in company. I must tell you how it came about,\r\njust in a few words for the present.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was his suggestion. He said he had to sail to Hamburg, and proposed\r\nthat I should go with him in the _Dulcibella_ as far as the Elbe, and\r\nthen, if I liked, I could take the ship canal at Brunsb\xc3\xbcttel through to\r\nKiel and the Baltic. I had no very fixed plans of my own, though I had\r\nmeant to go on exploring eastwards between the islands and the coast,\r\nand so reach the Elbe in a much slower way. He dissuaded me from this,\r\nsticking to it that I should have no chance of ducks, and urging other\r\nreasons. Anyway, we settled to sail in company direct to Cuxhaven, in\r\nthe Elbe. With a fair wind and an early start it should be only one\r\nday\xe2\x80\x99s sail of about sixty miles.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe plan only came to a head on the evening of the third day, on the\r\n12th of September.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI told you, I think, that the weather had broken after a long spell of\r\nheat. That very day it had been blowing pretty hard from the west, and\r\nthe glass was falling still. I said, of course, that I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t go with\r\nhim if the weather was too bad, but he prophesied a good day, said it\r\nwas an easy sail, and altogether put me on my mettle. You can guess how\r\nit was. Perhaps I had talked about single-handed cruising as though it\r\nwere easier than it was, though I never meant it in a boasting way, for\r\nI hate that sort of thing, and besides there _is_ no danger if you\xe2\x80\x99re\r\ncareful\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, go on,\xe2\x80\x9d I said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnyway, we went next morning at six. It was a dirty-looking day, wind\r\nW.N.W., but his sails were going up and mine followed. I took two reefs\r\nin, and we sailed out into the open and steered E.N.E. along the coast\r\nfor the Outer Elbe Lightship about fifty knots off. Here it all is, you\r\nsee.\xe2\x80\x9d (He showed me the course on the chart.) \xe2\x80\x9cThe trip was nothing for\r\nhis boat, of course, a safe, powerful old tub, forging through the sea\r\nas steady as a house. I kept up with her easily at first. My hands were\r\npretty full, for there was a hard wind on my quarter and a troublesome\r\nsea; but as long as nothing worse came I knew I should be all right,\r\nthough I also knew that I was a fool to have come.\r\n\r\n\r\nIllustration: Chart A to Illustrate the Stranding of the Dulcibella,\r\netc.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll went well till we were off Wangeroog, the last of the\r\nislands\xe2\x80\x94_here_\xe2\x80\x94and then it began to blow really hard. I had half a mind\r\nto chuck it and cut into the Jade River, _down there_,\xe2\x80\x9d but I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nthe face to, so I hove to and took in my last reef.\xe2\x80\x9d (Simple words,\r\nsimply uttered; but I had seen the operation in calm water and\r\nshuddered at the present picture.) \xe2\x80\x9cWe had been about level till then,\r\nbut with my shortened canvas I fell behind. Not that that mattered in\r\nthe least. I knew my course, had read up my tides, and, thick as the\r\nweather was, I had no doubt of being able to pick up the lightship. No\r\nchange of plan was possible now. The Weser estuary was on my starboard\r\nhand, but the whole place was a lee-shore and a mass of unknown\r\nbanks\xe2\x80\x94just look at them. I ran on, the _Dulcibella_ doing her level\r\nbest, but we had some narrow shaves of being pooped. I was about\r\n_here_, say six miles south-west of the lightship, _[See Chart A]_ when\r\nI suddenly saw that the _Medusa_ had hove to right ahead, as though\r\nwaiting till I came up. She wore round again on the course as I drew\r\nlevel, and we were alongside for a bit. Dollmann lashed the wheel,\r\nleaned over her quarter, and shouted, very slowly and distinctly so\r\nthat I could understand; \xe2\x80\x98Follow me\xe2\x80\x94sea too bad for you outside\xe2\x80\x94short\r\ncut through sands\xe2\x80\x94save six miles.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was taking me all my time to manage the tiller, but I knew what he\r\nmeant at once, for I had been over the chart carefully the night\r\nbefore. _[See Map A]_ You see, the whole bay between Wangeroog and the\r\nElbe is encumbered with sand. A great jagged chunk of it runs out from\r\nCuxhaven in a north-westerly direction for fifteen miles or so, ending\r\nin a pointed spit, called the _Scharhorn_. To reach the Elbe from the\r\nwest you have to go right outside this, round the lightship, which is\r\noff the Scharhorn, and double back. Of course, that\xe2\x80\x99s what all big\r\nvessels do. But, as you see, these sands are intersected here and there\r\nby channels, very shallow and winding, exactly like those behind the\r\nFrisian Islands. Now look at this one, which cuts right through the big\r\nchunk of sand and comes out near Cuxhaven. The _Telte_ _[See Chart A]_\r\nit\xe2\x80\x99s called. It\xe2\x80\x99s miles wide, you see, at the entrance, but later on it\r\nis split into two by the Hohenh\xc3\xb6rn bank: then it gets shallow and very\r\ncomplicated, and ends in a mere tidal driblet with another name. It\xe2\x80\x99s\r\njust the sort of channel I should like to worry into on a fine day or\r\nwith an off-shore wind. Alone, in thick weather and a heavy sea, it\r\nwould have been folly to attempt it, except as a desperate resource.\r\nBut, as I said I knew at once that Dollmann was proposing to run for it\r\nand guide me in.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t like the idea, because I like doing things for myself, and,\r\nsilly as it sounds, I believe I resented being told the sea was too bad\r\nfor me, which it certainly was. Yet the short cut did save several\r\nmiles and a devil of a tumble off the Scharhorn, where two tides meet.\r\nI had complete faith in Dollmann, and I suppose I decided that I should\r\nbe a fool not to take a good chance. I hesitated. I know; but in the\r\nend I nodded, and held up my arm as she forged ahead again. Soon after,\r\nshe shifted her course and I followed. You asked me once if I ever took\r\na pilot. That was the only time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe spoke with bitter gravity, flung himself back, and felt his pocket\r\nfor his pipe. It was not meant for a dramatic pause, but it certainly\r\nwas one. I had just a glimpse of still another Davies\xe2\x80\x94a Davies five\r\nyears older throbbing with deep emotions, scorn, passion, and stubborn\r\npurpose; a being above my plane, of sterner stuff, wider scope. Intense\r\nas my interest had become, I waited almost timidly while he\r\nmechanically rammed tobacco into his pipe and struck ineffectual\r\nmatches. I felt that whatever the riddle to be solved, it was no mean\r\none. He repressed himself with an effort, half rose, and made his\r\ncircular glance at the clock, barometer, and skylight, and then\r\nresumed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe soon came to what I knew must be the beginning of the Telte\r\nchannel. All round you could hear the breakers on the sands, though it\r\nwas too thick to see them yet. As the water shoaled, the sea, of\r\ncourse, got shorter and steeper. There was more wind\xe2\x80\x94a whole gale I\r\nshould say.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI kept dead in the wake of the _Medusa_, but to my disgust I found she\r\nwas gaining on me very fast. Of course I had taken for granted, when he\r\nsaid he would lead me in, that he would slow down and keep close to me.\r\nHe could easily have done so by getting his men up to check his sheets\r\nor drop his peak. Instead of that he was busting on for all he was\r\nworth. Once, in a rain-squall, I lost sight of him altogether; got him\r\nfaintly again, but had enough to do with my own tiller not to want to\r\nbe peering through the scud after a runaway pilot. I was all right so\r\nfar, but we were fast approaching the worst part of the whole passage,\r\nwhere the Hohenh\xc3\xb6rn bank blocks the road, and the channel divides. I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t know what it looks like to you on the chart\xe2\x80\x94perhaps fairly\r\nsimple, because you can follow the twists of the channels, as on a\r\nground-plan; but a stranger coming to a place like that (where there\r\nare no buoys, mind you) can tell nothing certain by the eye\xe2\x80\x94unless\r\nperhaps at dead low water, when the banks are high and dry, and in very\r\nclear weather\xe2\x80\x94he must trust to the lead and the compass, and feel his\r\nway step by step. I knew perfectly well that what I should soon see\r\nwould be a wall of surf stretching right across and on both sides. To\r\n_feel_ one\xe2\x80\x99s way in that sort of weather is impossible. You must _know_\r\nyour way, or else have a pilot. I had one, but he was playing his own\r\ngame.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWith a second hand on board to steer while I conned I should have felt\r\nless of an ass. As it was, I knew I ought to be facing the music in the\r\noffing, and cursed myself for having broken my rule and gone blundering\r\ninto this confounded short cut. It was giving myself away, doing just\r\nthe very thing that you can\xe2\x80\x99t do in single-handed sailing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy the time I realised the danger it was far too late to turn and\r\nhammer out to the open. I was deep in the bottle-neck bight of the\r\nsands, jammed on a lee shore, and a strong flood tide sweeping me on.\r\nThat tide, by the way, gave just the ghost of a chance. I had the hours\r\nin my head, and knew it was about two-thirds flood, with two hours more\r\nof rising water. That meant the banks would be all covering when I\r\nreached them, and harder than ever to locate; but it also meant that I\r\n_might_ float right over the worst of them if I hit off a lucky place.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nDavies thumped the table in disgust. \xe2\x80\x9cPah! It makes me sick to think of\r\nhaving to trust to an accident like that, like a lubberly cockney out\r\nfor a boozy Bank Holiday sail. Well, just as I foresaw, the wall of\r\nsurf appeared clean across the horizon, and curling back to shut me in,\r\nbooming like thunder. When I last saw the _Medusa_ she seemed to be\r\ncharging it like a horse at a fence, and I took a rough bearing of her\r\nposition by a hurried glance at the compass. At that very moment I\r\n_thought_ she seemed to luff and show some of her broadside; but a\r\nsquall blotted her out and gave me hell with the tiller. After that she\r\nwas lost in the white mist that hung over the line of breakers. I kept\r\non my bearing as well as I could, but I was already out of the channel.\r\nI knew that by the look of the water, and as we neared the bank I saw\r\nit was all awash and without the vestige of an opening. I wasn\xe2\x80\x99t going\r\nto chuck her on to it without an effort; so, more by instinct than with\r\nany particular hope, I put the helm down, meaning to work her along the\r\nedge on the chance of spotting a way over. She was buried at once by\r\nthe beam sea, and the jib flew to blazes; but the reefed stays\xe2\x80\x99l stood,\r\nshe recovered gamely, and I held on, though I knew it could only be for\r\na few minutes, as the centre-plate was up, and she made frightful\r\nleeway towards the bank.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was half-blinded by scud, but suddenly I noticed what looked like a\r\ngap, behind a spit which curled out right ahead. I luffed still more to\r\nclear this spit, but she couldn\xe2\x80\x99t weather it. Before you could say\r\nknife she was driving across it, bumped heavily, bucked forward again,\r\nbumped again, and\xe2\x80\x94ripped on in deeper water! I can\xe2\x80\x99t describe the next\r\nfew minutes. I was in some sort of channel, but a very narrow one, and\r\nthe sea broke everywhere. I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t proper command either; for the\r\nrudder had crocked up somehow at the last bump. I was like a drunken\r\nman running for his life down a dark alley, barking himself at every\r\ncorner. It couldn\xe2\x80\x99t last long, and finally we went crash on to\r\nsomething and stopped there, grinding and banging. So ended that little\r\ntrip under a pilot.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it was like this\xe2\x80\x94there was really no danger\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94I opened my eyes at\r\nthe characteristic phrase. \xe2\x80\x9cI mean, that lucky stumble into a channel\r\nwas my salvation. Since then I had struggled through a mile of sands,\r\nall of which lay behind me like a breakwater against the gale. They\r\nwere covered, of course, and seething like soapsuds; but the force of\r\nthe sea was deadened. The _Dulce_ was bumping, but not too heavily. It\r\nwas nearing high tide, and at half ebb she would be high and dry.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn the ordinary way I should have run out a kedge with the dinghy, and\r\nat the next high water sailed farther in and anchored where I could lie\r\nafloat. The trouble was now that my hand was hurt and my dinghy stove\r\nin, not to mention the rudder business. It was the first bump on the\r\nouter edge that did the damage. There was a heavy swell there, and when\r\nwe struck, the dinghy, which was towing astern, came home on her\r\npainter and down with a crash on the yacht\xe2\x80\x99s weather quarter. I stuck\r\nout one hand to ward it off and got it nipped on the gunwale. She was\r\nbadly stove in and useless, so I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t run out the kedge\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94this was\r\nGreek to me, but I let him go on\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cand for the present my hand was too\r\npainful even to stow the boom and sails, which were whipping and\r\nracketing about anyhow. There was the rudder, too, to be mended; and we\r\nwere several miles from the nearest land. Of course, if the wind fell,\r\nit was all easy enough; but if it held or increased it was a poor\r\nlook-out. There\xe2\x80\x99s a limit to strain of that sort\xe2\x80\x94and other things might\r\nhave happened.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn fact, it was precious lucky that Bartels turned up. His galliot was\r\nat anchor a mile away, up a branch of the channel. In a clear between\r\nsqualls he saw us, and, like a brick, rowed his boat out\xe2\x80\x94he and his\r\nboy, and a devil of a pull they must have had. I was glad enough to see\r\nthem\xe2\x80\x94no, that\xe2\x80\x99s not true; I was in such a fury of disgust and shame\r\nthat I believe I should have been idiot enough to say I didn\xe2\x80\x99t want\r\nhelp, if he hadn\xe2\x80\x99t just nipped on board and started work. He\xe2\x80\x99s a terror\r\nto work, that little mouse of a chap. In half an hour he had stowed the\r\nsails, unshackled the big anchor, run out fifty fathoms of warp, and\r\nhauled her off there and then into deep water. Then they towed her up\r\nthe channel\xe2\x80\x94it was dead to leeward and an easy job\xe2\x80\x94and berthed her near\r\ntheir own vessel.
