It was dark by that time, so I gave them a drink, and\r\nsaid good-night. It blew a howling gale that night, but the place was\r\nsafe enough, with good ground-tackle.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe whole affair was over; and after supper I thought hard about it\r\nall.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VIII.\r\nThe Theory\r\n\r\n\r\nDavies leaned back and gave a deep sigh, as though he still felt the\r\nrelief from some tension. I did the same, and felt the same relief. The\r\nchart, freed from the pressure of our fingers, rolled up with a flip,\r\nas though to say, \xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you think of that?\xe2\x80\x9d I have straightened out\r\nhis sentences a little, for in the excitement of his story they had\r\ngrown more and more jerky and elliptical.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat about Dollmann?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat about him? I didn\xe2\x80\x99t get at much that\r\nnight. It was all so sudden. The only thing I could have sworn to from\r\nthe first was that he had purposely left me in the lurch that day. I\r\npieced out the rest in the next few days, which I\xe2\x80\x99ll just finish with\r\nas shortly as I can. Bartels came aboard next morning, and though it\r\nwas blowing hard still we managed to shift the _Dulcibella_ to a place\r\nwhere she dried safely at the midday low water, and we could get at her\r\nrudder. The lower screw-plate on the stern post had wrenched out, and\r\nwe botched it up roughly as a makeshift. There were other little\r\nbreakages, but nothing to matter, and the loss of the jib was nothing,\r\nas I had two spare ones. The dinghy was past repair just then, and I\r\nlashed it on deck.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt turned out that Bartels was carrying apples from Bremen to Kappeln\r\n(in this fiord), and had run into that channel in the sands for shelter\r\nfrom the weather. To-day he was bound for the Eider River, whence, as I\r\ntold you, you can get through (by river and canal) into the Baltic. Of\r\ncourse the Elbe route, by the new Kaiser Wilhelm ship canal, is the\r\nshortest. The Eider route is the old one, but he hoped to get rid of\r\nsome of his apples at T\xc3\xb6nning, the town at its mouth. Both routes touch\r\nthe Baltic at Kiel. As you know, I had been running for the Elbe, but\r\nyesterday\xe2\x80\x99s muck-up put me off, and I changed my mind\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you why\r\npresently\xe2\x80\x94and decided to sail to the Eider along with the _Johannes_\r\nand get through that way. It cleared from the east next day, and I\r\nraced him there, winning hands down, left him at T\xc3\xb6nning, and in three\r\ndays was in the Baltic. It was just a week after I ran ashore that I\r\nwired to you. You see, I had come to the conclusion that _that chap was\r\na spy_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn the end it came out quite quietly and suddenly, and left me in\r\nprofound amazement. \xe2\x80\x9cI wired to you\xe2\x80\x94that chap was a spy.\xe2\x80\x9d It was the\r\nclose association of these two ideas that hit me hardest at the moment.\r\nFor a second I was back in the dreary splendour of the London\r\nclub-room, spelling out that crabbed scrawl from Davies, and\r\nfastidiously criticising its proposal in the light of a holiday.\r\nHoliday! What was to be its issue? Chilling and opaque as the fog that\r\nfiltered through the skylight there flooded my imagination a mist of\r\ndoubt and fear.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA spy!\xe2\x80\x9d I repeated blankly. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you mean? Why did you wire to me?\r\nA spy of what\xe2\x80\x94of whom?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you how I worked it out,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think \xe2\x80\x98spy\xe2\x80\x99\r\nis the right word; but I mean something pretty bad.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe purposely put me ashore. I don\xe2\x80\x99t think I\xe2\x80\x99m suspicious by nature,\r\nbut I know something about boats and the sea. I know he could have kept\r\nclose to me if he had chosen, and I saw the whole place at low water\r\nwhen we left those sands on the second day. Look at the chart again.\r\nHere\xe2\x80\x99s the Hohenh\xc3\xb6rn bank that I showed you as blocking the road. _[See\r\nChart A]_ It\xe2\x80\x99s in two pieces\xe2\x80\x94first the west and then the east. You see\r\nthe Telte channel dividing into two branches and curving round it. Both\r\nbranches are broad and deep, as channels go in those waters. Now, in\r\nsailing in I was nowhere near either of them. When I last saw Dollmann\r\nhe must have been steering straight for the bank itself, at a point\r\nsomewhere _here_, quite a mile from the northern arm of the channel,\r\nand two from the southern. I followed by compass, as you know, and\r\nfound nothing but breakers ahead. How did I get through? That\xe2\x80\x99s where\r\nthe luck came in. I spoke of only two channels, that is, _round_ the\r\nbank\xe2\x80\x94one to the north, the other to the south. But look closely and\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99ll see that right through the centre of the West Hohenh\xc3\xb6rn runs\r\nanother, a very narrow and winding one, so small that I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t even\r\nnoticed it the night before, when I was going over the chart. That was\r\nthe one I stumbled into in that tailor\xe2\x80\x99s fashion, as I was groping\r\nalong the edge of the surf in a desperate effort to gain time. I bolted\r\ndown it blindly, came out into this strip of open water, crossed that\r\naimlessly, and brought up on the edge of the _East_ Hohenh\xc3\xb6rn, _here_.\r\nIt was more than I deserved. I can see now that it was a hundred to one\r\nin favour of my striking on a bad place outside, where I should have\r\ngone to pieces in three minutes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd how did Dollmann go?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s as clear as possible,\xe2\x80\x9d Davies answered. \xe2\x80\x9cHe doubled back into the\r\nnorthern channel when he had misled me enough. Do you remember my\r\nsaying that when I last saw him I _thought_ he had luffed and showed\r\nhis broadside? I had another bit of luck in that. He was luffing\r\ntowards the north\xe2\x80\x94so it struck me through the blur\xe2\x80\x94and when I in my\r\nturn came up to the bank, and had to turn one way or the other to avoid\r\nit, I think I should naturally have turned north too, as he had done.\r\nIn that case I should have been done for, for I should have had a mile\r\nof the bank to skirt before reaching the north channel, and should have\r\ndriven ashore long before I got there. But as a matter of fact I turned\r\nsouth.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCouldn\xe2\x80\x99t help it. I was running on the starboard tack\xe2\x80\x94boom over to\r\nport; to turn north would have meant a jibe, and as things were I\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t risk one. It was blowing like fits; if anything had carried\r\naway I should have been on shore in a jiffy. I scarcely thought about\r\nit at all, but put the helm down and turned her south. Though I knew\r\nnothing about it, that little central channel was now on my port hand,\r\ndistant about two cables. The whole thing was luck from beginning to\r\nend.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHelped by pluck, I thought to myself, as I tried with my landsman\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nfancy to conjure up that perilous scene. As to the truth of the affair,\r\nthe chart and Davies\xe2\x80\x99s version were easy enough to follow, but I felt\r\nonly half convinced. The \xe2\x80\x9cspy\xe2\x80\x9d, as Davies strangely called his pilot,\r\nmight have honestly mistaken the course himself, outstripped his convoy\r\ninadvertently, and escaped disaster as narrowly as she did. I suggested\r\nthis on the spur of the moment, but Davies was impatient.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWait till you hear the whole thing,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI must go back to when\r\nI first met him. I told you that on that first evening he began by\r\nbeing as rude as a bear and as cold as stone, and then became suddenly\r\nfriendly. I can see now that in the talk that followed he was pumping\r\nme hard. It was an easy game to play, for I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t seen a gentleman\r\nsince Morrison left me, I was tremendously keen about my voyage, and I\r\nthought the chap was a good sportsman, even if he was a bit dark about\r\nthe ducks. I talked quite freely\xe2\x80\x94at least, as freely as I could with my\r\nbad German\xe2\x80\x94about my last fortnight\xe2\x80\x99s sailing; how I had been smelling\r\nout all the channels in and out of the islands, how interested I had\r\nbeen in the whole business, puzzling out the effect of the winds on the\r\ntides, the set of the currents, and so on. I talked about my\r\ndifficulties, too; the changes in the buoys, the prehistoric rottenness\r\nof the English charts. He drew me out as much as he could, and in the\r\nlight of what followed I can see the point of scores of his questions.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe next day and the next I saw a good deal of him, and the same thing\r\nwent on. And then there were my plans for the future. My idea was, as I\r\ntold you, to go on exploring the German coast just as I had the Dutch.\r\nHis idea\xe2\x80\x94Heavens, how plainly I see it now!\xe2\x80\x94was to choke me off, get me\r\nto clear out altogether from that part of the coast. That was why he\r\nsaid there were no ducks. That was why he cracked up the Baltic as a\r\ncruising-ground and shooting-ground. And that was why he broached and\r\nstuck to that plan of sailing in company direct to the Elbe. It was to\r\n_see_ me clear.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe improved on that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, but after that, it\xe2\x80\x99s guess-work. I mean that I can\xe2\x80\x99t tell when he\r\nfirst decided to go one better and drown me. He couldn\xe2\x80\x99t count for\r\ncertain on bad weather, though he held my nose to it when it came. But,\r\ngranted that he wanted to get rid of me altogether, he got a\r\nmagnificent chance on that trip to the Elbe lightship. I expect it\r\nstruck him suddenly, and he acted on the impulse. Left to myself I was\r\nall right; but the short cut was a grand idea of his. Everything was in\r\nits favour\xe2\x80\x94wind, sea, sand, tide. He thinks I\xe2\x80\x99m dead.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut the crew?\xe2\x80\x9d I said; \xe2\x80\x9cwhat about the crew?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s another thing. When he first hove to, waiting for me, of course\r\nthey were on deck (two of them, I think) hauling at sheets. But by the\r\ntime I had drawn up level the _Medusa_ had worn round again on her\r\ncourse, and no one was on deck but Dollmann at the wheel. No one\r\noverheard what he said.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWouldn\xe2\x80\x99t they have _seen_ you again?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery likely not; the weather was very thick, and the _Dulce_ is very\r\nsmall.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe incongruity of the whole business was striking me. Why should\r\nanyone want to kill Davies, and why should Davies, the soul of modesty\r\nand simplicity, imagine that anyone wanted to kill him? He must have\r\ncogent reasons, for he was the last man to give way to a morbid fancy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo on,\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat was his motive? A German finds an Englishman\r\nexploring a bit of German coast, determines to stop him, and even to\r\nget rid of him. It looks so far as if you were thought to be the spy.\r\n\r\nDavies winced. \xe2\x80\x9c_But he\xe2\x80\x99s not a German_,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, hotly. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s an\r\nEnglishman.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAn Englishman?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I\xe2\x80\x99m sure of it. Not that I\xe2\x80\x99ve much to go on. He professed to know\r\nvery little English, and never spoke it, except a word or two now and\r\nthen to help me out of a sentence; and as to his German, he seemed to\r\nme to speak it like a native; but, of course, I\xe2\x80\x99m no judge.\xe2\x80\x9d Davies\r\nsighed. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s where I wanted someone like you. You would have spotted\r\nhim at once, if he wasn\xe2\x80\x99t German. I go more by a\xe2\x80\x94what do you call\r\nit?\xe2\x80\x94a\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGeneral impression,\xe2\x80\x9d I suggested.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, that\xe2\x80\x99s what I mean. It was something in his looks and manner; you\r\nknow how different we are from foreigners. And it wasn\xe2\x80\x99t only himself,\r\nit was the way he talked\xe2\x80\x94I mean about cruising and the sea, especially.\r\nIt\xe2\x80\x99s true he let me do most of the talking; but, all the same\xe2\x80\x94how can I\r\nexplain it? I felt we understood one another, in a way that two\r\nforeigners wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe pretended to think me a bit crazy for coming so far in a small\r\nboat, but I could swear he knew as much about the game as I did; for\r\nlots of little questions he asked had the right ring in them. Mind you,\r\nall this is an afterthought. I should never have bothered about it\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99m\r\nnot cut out for a Sherlock Holmes\xe2\x80\x94if it hadn\xe2\x80\x99t been for what followed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s rather vague,\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9cHave you no more definite reason for\r\nthinking him English?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere were one or two things rather more definite,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies,\r\nslowly. \xe2\x80\x9cYou know when he hove to and hailed me, proposing the short\r\ncut, I told you roughly what he said. I forget the exact words, but\r\n\xe2\x80\x98abschneiden\xe2\x80\x99 came in\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x98durch Watten\xe2\x80\x99 and \xe2\x80\x98abschneiden\xe2\x80\x99 (they call the\r\nbanks \xe2\x80\x98watts\xe2\x80\x99, you know); they were simple words, and he shouted them\r\nloud, so as to carry through the wind. I understood what he meant, but,\r\nas I told you, I hesitated before consenting. I suppose he thought I\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t understand, for just as he was drawing ahead again he pointed to\r\nthe suth\xe2\x80\x99ard, and then shouted through his hands as a trumpet\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Verstehen Sie? short-cut through sands: follow me!\xe2\x80\x99 the last two\r\nsentences in downright English. I can hear those words now, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nswear they were in his native tongue. Of course I thought nothing of it\r\nat the time. I was quite aware that he knew a few English words, though\r\nhe had always mispronounced them; an easy trick when your hearer\r\nsuspects nothing. But I needn\xe2\x80\x99t say that just then I was observant of\r\ntrifles. I don\xe2\x80\x99t pretend to be able to unravel a plot and steer a small\r\nboat before a heavy sea at the same moment.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd if he was piloting you into the next world he could afford to\r\ncommit himself before you parted! Was there anything else? By the way,\r\nhow did the daughter strike you? Did she look English too?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTwo men cannot discuss a woman freely without a deep foundation of\r\nintimacy, and, until this day, the subject had never arisen between us\r\nin any form. It was the last that was likely to, for I could have\r\ndivined that Davies would have met it with an armour of reserve. He was\r\nbusy putting on this armour now; yet I could not help feeling a little\r\nbrutal as I saw how badly he jointed his clumsy suit of mail. Our ages\r\nwere the same, but I laugh now to think how old and _blas\xc3\xa9_ I felt as\r\nthe flush warmed his brown skin, and he slowly propounded the verdict,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I think she did.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe _talked_ nothing but German, I suppose?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, of course.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you see much of her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA good deal.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWas she\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94,\xe2\x80\x9d (how frame it?) \xe2\x80\x9cDid she want you to sail to the Elbe with\r\nthem?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe seemed to,\xe2\x80\x9d admitted Davies, reluctantly, clutching at his ally,\r\nthe match-box. \xe2\x80\x9cBut, hang it, don\xe2\x80\x99t dream that she knew what was\r\ncoming,\xe2\x80\x9d he added, with sudden fire.\r\n\r\nI pondered and wondered, shrinking from further inquisition, easy as it\r\nwould have been with so truthful a victim, and banishing all thought of\r\nill-timed chaff. There was a cross-current in this strange affair,\r\nwhose depth and strength I was beginning to gauge with increasing\r\nseriousness. I did not know my man yet, and I did not know myself. A\r\nconviction that events in the near future would force us into complete\r\nmutual confidence withheld me from pressing him too far. I returned to\r\nthe main question; who was Dollmann, and what was his motive? Davies\r\nstruggled out of his armour.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m convinced,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cthat he\xe2\x80\x99s an Englishman in German service.\r\nHe must be in German service, for he had evidently been in those waters\r\na long time, and knew every inch of them; of course, it\xe2\x80\x99s a very lonely\r\npart of the world, but he has a house on Norderney Island; and he, and\r\nall about him, must be well known to a certain number of people. One of\r\nhis friends I happened to meet; what do you think he was? A naval\r\nofficer. It was on the afternoon of the third day, and we were having\r\ncoffee on the deck of the _Medusa_, and talking about next day\xe2\x80\x99s trip,\r\nwhen a little launch came buzzing up from seaward, drew alongside, and\r\nthis chap I\xe2\x80\x99m speaking of came on board, shook hands with Dollmann, and\r\nstared hard at me. Dollmann introduced us, calling him Commander von\r\nBr\xc3\xbcning, in command of the torpedo gunboat _Blitz_. He pointed towards\r\nNorderney, and I saw her\xe2\x80\x94a low, grey rat of a vessel\xe2\x80\x94anchored in the\r\nRoads about two miles away. It turned out that she was doing the work\r\nof fishery guardship on that part of the coast.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI must say I took to him at once. He looked a real good sort, and a\r\nsplendid officer, too\xe2\x80\x94just the sort of chap I should have liked to be.\r\nYou know I always wanted\xe2\x80\x94but that\xe2\x80\x99s an old story, and can wait. I had\r\nsome talk with him, and we got on capitally as far as we went, but that\r\nwasn\xe2\x80\x99t far, for I left pretty soon, guessing that they wanted to be\r\nalone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Were_ they alone then?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked, innocently.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Fr\xc3\xa4ulein Dollmann was there, of course,\xe2\x80\x9d explained Davies, feeling\r\nfor his armour again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid he seem to know them well?\xe2\x80\x9d I pursued, inconsequently.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes, very well.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nScenting a faint clue, I felt the need of feminine weapons for my\r\nsensitive antagonist. But the opportunity passed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat was the last I saw of him,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cWe sailed, as I told you,\r\nat daybreak next morning. Now, have you got any idea what I\xe2\x80\x99m driving\r\nat?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA rough idea,\xe2\x80\x9d I answered. \xe2\x80\x9cGo ahead.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDavies sat up to the table, unrolled the chart with a vigorous sweep of\r\nhis two hands, and took up his parable with new zest.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI start with two certainties,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cOne is that I was \xe2\x80\x98moved on\xe2\x80\x99\r\nfrom that coast, because I was too inquisitive. The other is that\r\nDollmann is at some devil\xe2\x80\x99s work there which is worth finding out.\r\nNow\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94he paused in a gasping effort to be logical and articulate.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow\xe2\x80\x94well, look at the chart. No, better still, look first at this map\r\nof Germany. It\xe2\x80\x99s on a small scale, and you can see the whole thing.\xe2\x80\x9d He\r\nsnatched down a pocket-map from the shelf and unfolded it. _[See Map\r\nA]_ \xe2\x80\x9cHere\xe2\x80\x99s this huge empire, stretching half over central Europe\xe2\x80\x94an\r\nempire growing like wildfire, I believe, in people, and wealth, and\r\neverything. They\xe2\x80\x99ve licked the French, and the Austrians, and are the\r\ngreatest military power in Europe. I wish I knew more about all that,\r\nbut what I\xe2\x80\x99m concerned with is their sea-power. It\xe2\x80\x99s a new thing with\r\nthem, but it\xe2\x80\x99s going strong, and that Emperor of theirs is running it\r\nfor all it\xe2\x80\x99s worth. He\xe2\x80\x99s a splendid chap, and anyone can see he\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nright. They\xe2\x80\x99ve got no colonies to speak of, and _must_ have them, like\r\nus. They can\xe2\x80\x99t get them and keep them, and they can\xe2\x80\x99t protect their\r\nhuge commerce without naval strength. The command of the sea is _the_\r\nthing nowadays, isn\xe2\x80\x99t it? I say, don\xe2\x80\x99t think these are my ideas,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nadded, na\xc3\xafvely. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s all out of Mahan and those fellows. Well, the\r\nGermans have got a small fleet at present, but it\xe2\x80\x99s a thundering good\r\none, and they\xe2\x80\x99re building hard. There\xe2\x80\x99s the\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94and the\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94.\xe2\x80\x9d He broke off\r\ninto a digression on armaments and speeds in which I could not follow\r\nhim. He seemed to know every ship by heart. I had to recall him to the\r\npoint. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, think of Germany as a new sea-power,\xe2\x80\x9d he resumed. \xe2\x80\x9cThe\r\nnext thing is, what is her coast-line? It\xe2\x80\x99s a very queer one, as you\r\nknow, split clean in two by Denmark, most of it lying east of that and\r\nlooking on the Baltic, which is practically an inland sea, with its\r\nentrance blocked by Danish islands. It was to evade that block that\r\nWilliam built the ship canal from Kiel to the Elbe, but that could be\r\neasily smashed in war-time. Far the most important bit of coast-line is\r\nthat which lies _west_ of Denmark and looks on the North Sea. It\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nthere that Germany gets her head out into the open, so to speak. It\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nthere that she fronts us and France, the two great sea-powers of\r\nWestern Europe, and it\xe2\x80\x99s there that her greatest ports are and her\r\nrichest commerce.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow it must strike you at once that it\xe2\x80\x99s ridiculously short compared\r\nwith the huge country behind it. From Borkum to the Elbe, as the crow\r\nflies, is only seventy miles. Add to that the west coast of Schleswig,\r\nsay 120 miles. Total, say, two hundred. Compare that with the seaboard\r\nof France and England. Doesn\xe2\x80\x99t it stand to reason that every inch of it\r\nis important? Now what _sort_ of coast is it? Even on this small map\r\nyou can see at once, by all those wavy lines, shoals and sand\r\neverywhere, blocking nine-tenths of the land altogether, and doing\r\ntheir best to block the other tenth where the great rivers run in. Now\r\nlet\xe2\x80\x99s take it bit by bit. You see it divides itself into three.\r\nBeginning from the west the _first piece_ is from Borkum to\r\nWangeroog\xe2\x80\x94fifty odd miles. What\xe2\x80\x99s that like? A string of sandy islands\r\nbacked by sand; the Ems river at the western end, on the Dutch border,\r\nleading to Emden\xe2\x80\x94not much of a place. Otherwise, no coast towns at all.\r\n_Second piece:_ a deep sort of bay consisting of the three great\r\nestuaries\xe2\x80\x94the Jade, the Weser and the Elbe\xe2\x80\x94leading to Wilhelmshaven\r\n(their North Sea naval base), Bremen, and Hamburg; total breadth of\r\nbay, twenty odd miles only; sandbanks littered about all through it.\r\n_Third piece:_ the Schleswig coast, hopelessly fenced in behind a six\r\nto eight mile fringe of sand. No big towns; one moderate river, the\r\nEider. Let\xe2\x80\x99s leave that third piece aside. I may be wrong, but, in\r\nthinking this business out, I\xe2\x80\x99ve pegged away chiefly at the other two,\r\nthe seventy-mile stretch from Borkum to the Elbe\xe2\x80\x94half of it estuaries,\r\nand half islands. It was there that I found the _Medusa_, and it\xe2\x80\x99s that\r\nstretch that, thanks to him, I missed exploring.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI made an obvious conjecture. \xe2\x80\x9cI suppose there are forts and coast\r\ndefences? Perhaps he thought you would see too much. By the way, he saw\r\nyour naval books, of course?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExactly. Of course that was my first idea; but it can\xe2\x80\x99t be that. It\r\ndoesn\xe2\x80\x99t explain things in the least. To begin with, there _are_ no\r\nforts and can be none in that first division, where the islands are.\r\nThere might be something on Borkum to defend the Ems; but it\xe2\x80\x99s very\r\nunlikely, and, anyway, I had passed Borkum and was at Norderney.\r\nThere\xe2\x80\x99s nothing else to defend. Of course it\xe2\x80\x99s different in the second\r\ndivision, where the big rivers are. There are probably hosts of forts\r\nand mines round Wilhelmshaven and Bremerhaven, and at Cuxhaven just at\r\nthe mouth of the Elbe. Not that I should ever dream of bothering about\r\nthem; every steamer that goes in would see as much as me. Personally, I\r\nmuch prefer to stay on board, and don\xe2\x80\x99t often go on shore. And, good\r\nHeavens!\xe2\x80\x9d (Davies leant back and laughed joyously) \xe2\x80\x9cdo I _look_ like\r\nthat kind of spy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI figured to myself one of those romantic gentlemen that one reads of\r\nin sixpenny magazines, with a Kodak in his tie-pin, a sketch-book in\r\nthe lining of his coat, and a selection of disguises in his hand\r\nluggage. Little disposed for merriment as I was, I could not help\r\nsmiling, too.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAbout this coast,\xe2\x80\x9d resumed Davies. \xe2\x80\x9cIn the event of war it seems to me\r\nthat every inch of it would be important, _sand and all._ Take the big\r\nestuaries first, which, of course, might be attacked or blockaded by an\r\nenemy. At first sight you would say that their main channels were the\r\nonly things that mattered. Now, in time of peace there\xe2\x80\x99s no secrecy\r\nabout the navigation of these. They\xe2\x80\x99re buoyed and lighted like streets,\r\nopen to the whole world, and taking an immense traffic; well charted,\r\ntoo, as millions of pounds in commerce depend on them. But now look at\r\nthe sands they run through, intersected, as I showed you, by threads of\r\nchannels, tidal for the most part, and probably only known to smacks\r\nand shallow coasters, like that galliot of Bartels.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt strikes me that in a war a lot might depend on these, both in\r\ndefence and attack, for there\xe2\x80\x99s plenty of water in them at the right\r\ntide for patrol-boats and small torpedo craft, though I can see they\r\ntake a lot of knowing. Now, say _we_ were at war with Germany\xe2\x80\x94both\r\nsides could use them as lines between the three estuaries; and to take\r\nour own case, a small torpedo-boat (not a destroyer, mind you) could on\r\na dark night cut clean through from the Jade to the Elbe and play the\r\ndeuce with the shipping there. But the trouble is that I doubt if\r\nthere\xe2\x80\x99s a soul in our fleet who knows those channels. _We_ haven\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ncoasters there; and, as to yachts, it\xe2\x80\x99s a most unlikely game for an\r\nEnglish yacht to play at; but it does so happen that I have a fancy for\r\nthat sort of thing and would have explored those channels in the\r\nordinary course.\xe2\x80\x9d I began to see his drift.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow for the islands. I was rather stumped there at first, I grant,\r\nbecause, though there are lashings of sand behind them, and the same\r\nsort of intersecting channels, yet there seems nothing important to\r\nguard or attack.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t a stranger ramble as he pleases through them? Still\r\nDollmann had his headquarters there, and I was sure that had some\r\nmeaning. Then it struck me that the same point held good, for that\r\nstrip of Frisian coast adjoins the estuaries, and would also form a\r\nsplendid base for raiding midgets, which could travel unseen right\r\nthrough from the Ems to the Jade, and so to the Elbe, as by a covered\r\nway between a line of forts.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow here again it\xe2\x80\x99s an unknown land to us. Plenty of local galliots\r\ntravel it, but strangers never, I should say. Perhaps at the most an\r\noccasional foreign yacht gropes in at one of the gaps between the\r\nislands for shelter from bad weather, and is precious lucky to get in\r\nsafe. Once again, it was my fad to like such places, and Dollmann\r\ncleared me out. He\xe2\x80\x99s not a German, but he\xe2\x80\x99s in with Germans, and naval\r\nGermans too. He\xe2\x80\x99s established on that coast, and knows it by heart. And\r\nhe tried to drown me. Now what do you think?\xe2\x80\x9d He gazed at me long and\r\nanxiously.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER IX.\r\nI Sign Articles\r\n\r\n\r\nIt was not an easy question to answer, for the affair was utterly\r\noutside all my experience; its background the sea, and its actual scene\r\na region of the sea of which I was blankly ignorant. There were other\r\ndifficulties that I could see perhaps better than Davies, an enthusiast\r\nwith hobbies, who had been brooding in solitude over his dangerous\r\nadventure. Yet both narrative and theory (which have lost, I fear, in\r\ninterpretation to the reader) had strongly affected me; his forcible\r\nroughnesses, tricks of manner, sudden bursts of ardour, sudden retreats\r\ninto shyness, making up a charm I cannot render. I found myself\r\ncontinually trying to see the man through the boy, to distinguish sober\r\njudgement from the hot-headed vagaries of youth. Not that I dreamed for\r\na moment of dismissing the story of his wreck as an hallucination. His\r\nclear blue eyes and sane simplicity threw ridicule on such treatment.\r\n\r\nEvidently, too, he wanted my help, a matter that might well have\r\ninfluenced my opinion on the facts, had he been other than he was. But\r\nit would have taken a \xe2\x80\x9cfinished and finite clod\xe2\x80\x9d to resist the\r\nattraction of the man and the enterprise; and I take no credit whatever\r\nfor deciding to follow him, right or wrong. So, when I stated my\r\ndifficulties, I knew very well that we should go.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere are two main points that I don\xe2\x80\x99t understand,\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9cFirst,\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99ve never explained why an _Englishman_ should be watching those\r\nwaters and ejecting intruders; secondly, your theory doesn\xe2\x80\x99t supply\r\nsufficient motive. You say he wanted to\r\ndrown you\xe2\x80\x94a big charge, requiring a big motive to support it. But I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t deny that you\xe2\x80\x99ve got a strong case.\xe2\x80\x9d Davies lighted up. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m\r\nwilling to take a good deal for granted\xe2\x80\x94until we find out more.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe jumped up, and did a thing I never saw him do before or since\xe2\x80\x94bumped\r\nhis head against the cabin roof.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou mean that you\xe2\x80\x99ll come?\xe2\x80\x9d he exclaimed. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t even asked\r\nyou! I know now\r\nthat I want to; telling it all to you has been such an immense relief.\r\nAnd a lot depended on you, too, and that\xe2\x80\x99s why I\xe2\x80\x99ve been feeling such\r\nan absolute hypocrite. You\xe2\x80\x99re clever, and I knew you had lived in Germany\r\nand knew German, and I knew,\xe2\x80\x9d he added, with a little awkwardness,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cthat you had done a good deal of yachting; but of course I ought to\r\nhave told you what you were in for\xe2\x80\x94roughing it in a small boat with no\r\ncrew. I felt ashamed of myself when you wired back so promptly, and\r\nwhen you came\xe2\x80\x94er\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d Davies stammered and hesitated in the humane\r\nresolve not to wound my feelings. \xe2\x80\x9cOf course I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t help noticing\r\nthat it wasn\xe2\x80\x99t what you expected,\xe2\x80\x9d was the delicate summary he arrived\r\nat. \xe2\x80\x9cBut you took it splendidly,\xe2\x80\x9d he hastened to add. \xe2\x80\x9cOnly, somehow, I\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t bring myself to talk about the plan. There\r\nwere reasons, there are reasons still\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94he looked nervously at\r\nme\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cwhich\xe2\x80\x94well, which make it a tangled business.\xe2\x80\x9d I had thought a\r\nconfidence was coming, and was disappointed. \xe2\x80\x9cI was in an idiotic state\r\nof uncertainty,\xe2\x80\x9d he hurried on; \xe2\x80\x9cbut the plan grew on me more and more,\r\nwhen I saw how you were taking to the life and beginning to enjoy\r\nyourself. However,\r\nyou quite naturally objected, and last night I meant to chuck the whole\r\nthing up and give you the best time here I could. Then Bartels turned\r\nup\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStop,\xe2\x80\x9d I put in. \xe2\x80\x9cDid you know he might turn up when you sailed here?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, guiltily. \xe2\x80\x9cI knew he might; and now it\xe2\x80\x99s all come\r\nout, and you\xe2\x80\x99ll come! What a fool I\xe2\x80\x99ve been!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLong before he had finished I had grasped the whole meaning of the last\r\nfew days, and had read their meaning into scores of little incidents\r\nwhich had puzzled me.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor goodness\xe2\x80\x99 sake, don\xe2\x80\x99t apologise,\xe2\x80\x9d I protested. \xe2\x80\x9cI could make\r\nconfessions, too, if I liked. It was jolly\r\ndecent of you to\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever mind! What\xe2\x80\x99s your plan of action?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s this,\xe2\x80\x9d was the prompt reply: \xe2\x80\x9cto get back to the North Sea, _via_\r\nKiel and the ship canal. The two things\r\nmay overlap, we can\xe2\x80\x99t tell yet. I don\xe2\x80\x99t even know where he and his\r\nyacht are; but I\xe2\x80\x99ll be bound they\xe2\x80\x99re somewhere in those same waters,\r\nand probably back at Norderney.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a delicate matter,\xe2\x80\x9d I mused, dubiously, \xe2\x80\x9cif your theory\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ncorrect. Spying on a spy\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s not like that,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, indignantly. \xe2\x80\x9cAnyone who likes can\r\nsail about there and explore those waters. I say, you don\xe2\x80\x99t really\r\nthink it\xe2\x80\x99s like that, do you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think you\xe2\x80\x99re likely to do anything dishonourable,\xe2\x80\x9d I hastened\r\nto explain. \xe2\x80\x9cI grant you the sea\xe2\x80\x99s public property in your sense. I\r\nonly mean that developments are possible, which you don\xe2\x80\x99t reckon on.\r\nThere _must_ be more to find out than the mere navigation of those\r\nchannels, and if that\xe2\x80\x99s so, mightn\xe2\x80\x99t we come to be genuine spies\r\nourselves?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd, after all, hang it!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Davies, \xe2\x80\x9cif it comes to that, why\r\nshouldn\xe2\x80\x99t we? I look at it like this. The man\xe2\x80\x99s an Englishman, and if\r\nhe\xe2\x80\x99s in with Germany he\xe2\x80\x99s a traitor to us, and we as Englishmen have a\r\nright to expose him. If we can\xe2\x80\x99t do it without spying we\xe2\x80\x99ve a right to\r\nspy, at our own risk\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s a stronger argument than that. He tried to take your life.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t care a rap about that. I\xe2\x80\x99m not such an ass as to thirst for\r\nrevenge and all that, like some chap in a shilling shocker. But it\r\nmakes me wild to think of that fellow masquerading as a German, and up\r\nto who knows what mischief\xe2\x80\x94mischief enough to make him want to get rid\r\nof _any_ one. I\xe2\x80\x99m keen about the sea, and I think they\xe2\x80\x99re apt to be a\r\nbit slack at home,\xe2\x80\x9d he continued inconsequently. \xe2\x80\x9cThose Admiralty chaps\r\nwant waking up. Anyway, as far as I\xe2\x80\x99m concerned, it\xe2\x80\x99s quite natural\r\nthat I should look him up again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuite,\xe2\x80\x9d I agreed; \xe2\x80\x9cyou parted friends, and they may be delighted to\r\nsee you. You\xe2\x80\x99ll have plenty to talk about.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cH\xe2\x80\x99m,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, withered into silence by the \xe2\x80\x9cthey\xe2\x80\x9d. \xe2\x80\x9cHullo! I say,\r\ndo you know it\xe2\x80\x99s three o\xe2\x80\x99clock? How the time has gone! And, by Jove! I\r\nbelieve the fog\xe2\x80\x99s lifting.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI returned, with a shock, to the present, to the weeping walls, the\r\ndiscoloured deal table, the ghastly breakfast litter\xe2\x80\x94all the visible\r\nsymbols of the life I had pledged myself to. Disillusionment was making\r\nrapid headway when Davies returned, and said, with energy:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you say to starting for Kiel at once? The fog\xe2\x80\x99s going, and\r\nthere\xe2\x80\x99s a breeze from the sou\xe2\x80\x99-west.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow?\xe2\x80\x9d I protested. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, it\xe2\x80\x99ll mean sailing all night, won\xe2\x80\x99t it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, no,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies. \xe2\x80\x9cNot with luck.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, it\xe2\x80\x99s dark at seven!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, but it\xe2\x80\x99s only twenty-five miles. I know it\xe2\x80\x99s not exactly a fair\r\nwind, but we shall lie closehauled most of the way. The glass is\r\nfalling, and we ought to take this chance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTo argue about winds with Davies was hopeless, and the upshot was that\r\nwe started lunchless. A pale sun was flickering out of masses of racing\r\nvapour, and through delicate vistas between them the fair land of\r\nSchleswig now revealed and now withdrew her pretty face, as though\r\nsmiling _adieux_ to her faithless courtiers.\r\n\r\nThe clank of our chain brought up Bartels to the deck of the\r\n_Johannes_, rubbing his eyes and pulling round his throat a grey shawl,\r\nwhich gave him a comical likeness to a lodging-house landlady receiving\r\nthe milk in morning _d\xc3\xa9shabill\xc3\xa9._\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99re off, Bartels,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, without looking up from his work.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSee you at Kiel, I hope.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are always in a hurry, captain,\xe2\x80\x9d bleated the old man, shaking his\r\nhead. \xe2\x80\x9cYou should wait till to-morrow. The sky is not good, and it will\r\nbe dark before you are off Eckenf\xc3\xb6rde.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDavies laughed, and very soon his mentor\xe2\x80\x99s sad little figure was lost\r\nin haze.\r\n\r\nThat was a curious evening. Dusk soon fell, and the devil made a\r\ndetermined effort to unman me; first, with the scrambled tea which was\r\nthe tardy substitute for an orderly lunch, then with the new and\r\nnauseous duty of filling the side-lights, which meant squatting in the\r\nfo\xe2\x80\x99c\xe2\x80\x99sle to inhale paraffin and dabble in lamp-black; lastly, with an\r\nall-round attack on my nerves as the night fell on our frail little\r\nvessel, pitching on her precarious way through driving mist. In a sense\r\nI think I went through the same sort of mental crisis as when I sat\r\nupon my portmanteau at Flensburg. The main issue was not seriously in\r\nquestion, for I had signed on in the _Dulcibella_ for good or ill; but\r\nin doing so I had outrun myself, and still wanted an outlook, a mood\r\nsuited to the enterprise, proof against petty discouragements. Not for\r\nthe first time a sense of the ludicrous came to my assistance, as I saw\r\nmyself fretting in London under my burden of self-imposed woes, nicely\r\nweighing that insidious invitation, and stepping finally into the snare\r\nwith the dignity due to my importance; kidnapped as neatly as ever a\r\npeaceful clerk was kidnapped by a lawless press-gang, and, in the end,\r\nfinding as the arch-conspirator a guileless and warm-hearted friend,\r\nwho called me clever, lodged me in a cell, and blandly invited me to\r\ntalk German to the purpose, as he was aiming at a little secret service\r\non the high seas. Close in the train of Humour came Romance, veiling\r\nher face, but I knew it was the rustle of her robes that I heard in the\r\nfoam beneath me; I knew that it was she who handed me the cup of\r\nsparkling wine and bade me drink and be merry. Strange to me though it\r\nwas, I knew the taste when it touched my lips. It was not that bastard\r\nconcoction I had tasted in the pseudo-Bohemias of Soho; it was not the\r\nshowy but insipid beverage I should have drunk my fill of at Morven\r\nLodge; it was the purest of her pure vintages, instilling the ancient\r\ninspiration which, under many guises, quickens thousands of better\r\nbrains than mine, but whose essence is always the same; the gay pursuit\r\nof a perilous quest. Then and there I tried to clinch the matter and\r\nkeep that mood. In the main I think I succeeded, though I had many\r\nlapses.\r\n\r\nFor the present my veins tingled with the draught. The wind humming\r\ninto the mainsail, the ghostly wave-crests riding up out of the void,\r\nwhispered a low thrilling chorus in praise of adventure. Potent indeed\r\nmust the spell have been, for, in reality, that first night sail teemed\r\nwith terrors for me. It is true that it began well, for the haze\r\ndispersed, as Davies had prophesied, and Bulk Point Lighthouse guided\r\nus safely to the mouth of Kiel Fiord. It was during this stage that,\r\ncrouching together aft, our pipe-bowls glowing sympathetically, we\r\nreturned to the problem before us; for we had shot out on our quest\r\nwith volcanic precipitation, leaving much to be discussed. I gleaned a\r\nfew more facts, though I dispelled no doubts. Davies had only seen the\r\nDollmanns on their yacht, where father and daughter were living for the\r\ntime. Their villa at Norderney, and their home life there, were unknown\r\nto him, though he had landed once at the harbour himself. Further, he\r\nhad heard vaguely of a stepmother, absent at Hamburg. They were to have\r\njoined her on their arrival at that city, which, be it noted, stands a\r\nlong way up the Elbe, forty miles and more above Cuxhaven, the town at\r\nthe mouth.\r\n\r\nThe exact arrangement made on the day before the fatal voyage was that\r\nthe two yachts should meet in the evening at Cuxhaven and proceed up\r\nthe river together. Then, in the ordinary course, Davies would have\r\nparted company at Brunsb\xc3\xbcttel (fifteen miles up), which is the western\r\nterminus of the ship canal to the Baltic. Such at least had been his\r\noriginal intention; but, putting two and two together, I gathered that\r\nlatterly, and perhaps unconfessed to himself, his resolve had weakened,\r\nand that he would have followed the _Medusa_ to Hamburg, or indeed the\r\nend of the world, impelled by the same motive that, contrary to all his\r\ntastes and principles, had induced him to abandon his life in the\r\nislands and undertake the voyage at all. But on that point he was\r\nimmovably reticent, and all I could conclude was that the strange\r\ncross-current connected with Dollmann\xe2\x80\x99s daughter had given him cruel\r\npain and had clouded his judgement to distraction, but that he now was\r\nprepared to forget or ignore it, and steer a settled course.\r\n\r\nThe facts I elicited raised several important questions. Was it not\r\nknown by this time that he and his yacht had survived? Davies was\r\nconvinced that it was not. \xe2\x80\x9cHe may have waited at Cuxhaven, or inquired\r\nat the lock at Brunsb\xc3\xbcttel,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cBut there was no need, for I\r\ntell you the thing was a certainty. If I had struck and _stuck_ on that\r\nouter bank, as it was a hundred to one I should do, the yacht would\r\nhave broken up in three minutes. Bartels would never have seen me, and\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have got to me if he had. No one would have seen me. And\r\nnothing whatever has happened since to show that they know I\xe2\x80\x99m alive.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey,\xe2\x80\x9d I suggested. \xe2\x80\x9cWho are \xe2\x80\x98they\xe2\x80\x99? Who are our adversaries?\xe2\x80\x9d If\r\nDollmann were an accredited agent of the German Admiralty\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94 But, no, it\r\nwas incredible that the murder of a young Englishman should be connived\r\nat in modern days by a friendly and civilised government! Yet, if he\r\nwere not such an agent, the whole theory fell to the ground.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI believe,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, \xe2\x80\x9cthat Dollmann did it off his own bat, and\r\nbeyond that I can\xe2\x80\x99t see. And I don\xe2\x80\x99t know that it matters at present.\r\nAlive or dead we\xe2\x80\x99re doing nothing wrong, and have nothing to be ashamed\r\nof.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think it matters a good deal,\xe2\x80\x9d I objected. \xe2\x80\x9cWho will be interested\r\nin our resurrection, and how are we to go to work, openly or secretly?\r\nI suppose we shall keep out of the way as much as we can?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs for keeping out of the way,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, jerkily, as he peered to\r\nwindward under the foresail, \xe2\x80\x9cwe _must_ pass the ship canal; that\xe2\x80\x99s a\r\npublic highway, where anyone can see you. After that there won\xe2\x80\x99t be\r\nmuch difficulty. Wait till you see the place!\xe2\x80\x9d He gave a low, contented\r\nlaugh, which would have frozen my marrow yesterday. \xe2\x80\x9cBy the way, that\r\nreminds me,\xe2\x80\x9d he added; \xe2\x80\x9cwe must stop at Kiel for the inside of a day\r\nand lay in a lot of stores. We want to be independent of the shore.\xe2\x80\x9d I\r\nsaid nothing. Independence of the shore in a seven-tonner in October!\r\nWhat an end to aim at!\r\n\r\nAbout nine o\xe2\x80\x99clock we weathered the point, entered Kiel Fiord, and\r\nbegan a dead beat to windward of seven miles to the head of it where\r\nKiel lies. Hitherto, save for the latent qualms concerning my total\r\nhelplessness if anything happened to Davies, interest and excitement\r\nhad upheld me well. My alarms only began when I thought them nearly\r\nover. Davies had frequently urged me to turn in and sleep, and I went\r\nso far as to go below and coil myself up on the lee sofa with my pencil\r\nand diary. Suddenly there was a flapping and rattling on deck, and I\r\nbegan to slide on to the floor. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s happened?\xe2\x80\x9d I cried, in a panic,\r\nfor there was Davies stooping in at the cabin door.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, chafing his hands for warmth; \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m only going\r\nabout. Hand me the glasses, will you? There\xe2\x80\x99s a steamer ahead. I say,\r\nif you really don\xe2\x80\x99t want to turn in, you might make some soup. Just\r\nlet\xe2\x80\x99s look at the chart.\xe2\x80\x9d He studied it with maddening deliberation,\r\nwhile I wondered how near the steamer was, and what the yacht was doing\r\nmeanwhile.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose it\xe2\x80\x99s not really necessary for anyone to be at the helm?\xe2\x80\x9d I\r\nremarked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, she\xe2\x80\x99s all right for a minute,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, without looking up.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTwo\xe2\x80\x94one and a half\xe2\x80\x94one\xe2\x80\x94lights in line sou\xe2\x80\x99-west by west\xe2\x80\x94got a match?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nHe expended two, and tumbled upstairs again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t want me, do you?\xe2\x80\x9d I shouted after him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, but come up when you\xe2\x80\x99ve put the kettle on. It\xe2\x80\x99s a pretty beat up\r\nthe fiord. Lovely breeze.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHis legs disappeared. A sort of buoyant fatalism possessed me as I\r\nfinished my notes and pored over the stove. It upheld me, too, when I\r\nwent on deck and watched the \xe2\x80\x9cpretty beat\xe2\x80\x9d, whose prettiness was mainly\r\ndue to the crowd of fog-bound shipping\xe2\x80\x94steamers, smacks, and\r\nsailing-vessels\xe2\x80\x94now once more on the move in the confined fairway of\r\nthe fiord, their baleful eyes of red, green, or yellow, opening and\r\nshutting, brightening and fading; while shore-lights and anchor-lights\r\nadded to my bewilderment, and a throbbing of screws filled the air like\r\nthe distant roar of London streets. In fact, every time we spun round\r\nfor our dart across the fiord I felt like a rustic matron gathering her\r\nskirts for the transit of the Strand on a busy night. Davies, however,\r\nwas the street arab who zigzags under the horses\xe2\x80\x99 feet unscathed; and\r\nall the time he discoursed placidly on the simplicity and safety of\r\nnight-sailing if only you are careful, obeying rules, and burnt good\r\nlights. As we were nearing the hot glow in the sky that denoted Kiel we\r\npassed a huge scintillating bulk moored in mid-stream. \xe2\x80\x9cWarships,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nmurmured, ecstatically.\r\n\r\nAt one o\xe2\x80\x99clock we anchored off the town.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER X.\r\nHis Chance\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI say, Davies,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, \xe2\x80\x9chow long do you think this trip will last?\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ve only got a month\xe2\x80\x99s leave.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe were standing at slanting desks in the Kiel post-office, Davies\r\nscratching diligently at his letter-card, and I staring feebly at mine.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy Jove!\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, with a start of dismay; \xe2\x80\x9cthat\xe2\x80\x99s only three\r\nweeks more; I never thought of that. You couldn\xe2\x80\x99t manage to get an\r\nextension, could you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can write to the chief,\xe2\x80\x9d I admitted; \xe2\x80\x9cbut where\xe2\x80\x99s the answer to come\r\nto? We\xe2\x80\x99re better without an address, I suppose.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s Cuxhaven,\xe2\x80\x9d reflected Davies; \xe2\x80\x9cbut that\xe2\x80\x99s too near, and\r\nthere\xe2\x80\x99s\xe2\x80\x94but we don\xe2\x80\x99t want to be tied down to landing anywhere. I tell\r\nyou what: say \xe2\x80\x98Post Office, Norderney\xe2\x80\x99, just your name, not the\r\nyacht\xe2\x80\x99s. We _may_ get there and be able to call for letters.\xe2\x80\x9d The\r\ncasual character of our adventure never struck me more strongly than\r\nthen.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs that what _you\xe2\x80\x99re_ doing?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I shan\xe2\x80\x99t be having important letters like you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut what are you saying?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, just that we\xe2\x80\x99re having a splendid cruise, and are on our way\r\nhome.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe notion tickled me, and I said the same in my home letter, adding\r\nthat we were looking for a friend of Davies\xe2\x80\x99s who would be able to show\r\nus some sport. I wrote a line, too, to my chief (unaware of the gravity\r\nof the step I was taking) saying it was possible that I might have to\r\napply for longer leave, as I had important business to transact in\r\nGermany, and asking him kindly to write to the same address. Then we\r\nshouldered our parcels and resumed our business.\r\n\r\nTwo full dinghy-loads of stores we ferried to the _Dulcibella_, chief\r\namong which were two immense cans of petroleum, constituting our\r\nreserves of heat and light, and a sack of flour. There were spare ropes\r\nand blocks, too; German charts of excellent quality; cigars and many\r\nweird brands of sausage and tinned meats, besides a miscellany of\r\noddments, some of which only served in the end to slake my companion\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ncraving for jettison. Clothes were my own chief care, for, freely as I\r\nhad purged it at Flensburg, my wardrobe was still very unsuitable, and\r\nI had already irretrievably damaged two faultless pairs of white\r\nflannels. (\xe2\x80\x9cWe shall be able to throw them overboard,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies,\r\nhopefully.) So I bought a great pair of seaboots of the country,\r\nfelt-lined and wooden-soled, and both of us got a number of rough\r\nwoollen garments (as worn by the local fishermen), breeches, jerseys,\r\nhelmets, gloves; all of a colour chosen to harmonise with paraffin\r\nstains and anchor mud.\r\n\r\nThe same evening we were taking our last look at the Baltic, sailing\r\npast warships and groups of idle yachts battened down for their\r\nwinter\xe2\x80\x99s sleep; while the noble shores of the fiord, with its villas\r\nembowered in copper foliage, grew dark and dim above us.\r\n\r\nWe rounded the last headland, steered for a galaxy of coloured lights,\r\ntumbled down our sails, and came to under the colossal gates of the\r\nHoltenau lock. That these would open to such an infinitesimal suppliant\r\nseemed inconceivable. But open they did, with ponderous majesty, and\r\nour tiny hull was lost in the womb of a lock designed to float the\r\nlargest battleships. I thought of Boulter\xe2\x80\x99s on a hot August Sunday, and\r\nwondered if I really was the same peevish dandy who had jostled and\r\nsweltered there with the noisy cockney throng a month ago. There was a\r\nblaze of electricity overhead, but utter silence till a solitary\r\ncloaked figure hailed us and called for the captain. Davies ran up a\r\nladder, disappeared with the cloaked figure, and returned crumpling a\r\npaper into his pocket. It lies before me now, and sets forth, under the\r\nstamp of the K\xc3\xb6nigliches Zollamt, that, in consideration of the sum of\r\nten marks for dues and four for tonnage, an imperial tug would tow the\r\nvessel _Dulcibella_ (master A. H. Davies) through the Kaiser Wilhelm\r\ncanal from Holtenau to Brunsb\xc3\xbcttel. Magnificent condescension! I blush\r\nwhen I look at this yellow document and remember the stately courtesy\r\nof the great lock-gates; for the sleepy officials of the K\xc3\xb6nigliches\r\nZollamt little knew what an insidious little viper they were admitting\r\ninto the imperial bosom at the light toll of fourteen shillings.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSeems cheap,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, joining me, \xe2\x80\x9cdoesn\xe2\x80\x99t it? They\xe2\x80\x99ve a regular\r\ntariff on tonnage, same for yachts as for liners. We start at four\r\nto-morrow with a lot of other boats. I wonder if Bartels is here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe same silence reigned, but invisible forces were at work. The inner\r\ngates opened and we prised ourselves through into a capacious basin,\r\nwhere lay moored side by side a flotilla of sailing vessels of various\r\nsizes. Having made fast alongside a vacant space of quay, we had our\r\ndinner, and then strolled out with cigars to look for the _Johannes_.\r\nWe found her wedged among a stack of galliots, and her skipper sitting\r\nprimly below before a blazing stove, reading his Bible through\r\nspectacles. He produced a bottle of schnapps and some very small and\r\nhard pears, while Davies twitted him mercilessly about his false\r\npredictions.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe sky was not good,\xe2\x80\x9d was all he said, beaming indulgently at his\r\nincorrigible young friend.\r\n\r\nBefore parting for the night it was arranged that next morning we\r\nshould lash alongside the _Johannes_ when the flotilla was marshalled\r\nfor the tow through the canal.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cKarl shall steer for us both,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cand we will stay warm in the\r\ncabin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe scheme was carried out, not without much confusion and loss of\r\npaint, in the small hours of a dark and drizzling morning. Boisterous\r\nlittle tugs sorted us into parties, and half lost under the massive\r\nbulwarks of the _Johannes_ we were carried off into a black inane. If\r\nany doubt remained as to the significance of our change of\r\ncruising-grounds, dawn dispelled it. View there was none from the deck\r\nof the _Dulcibella_; it was only by standing on the mainboom that you\r\ncould see over the embankments to the vast plain of Holstein, grey and\r\nmonotonous under a pall of mist. The soft scenery of the Schleswig\r\ncoast was a baseless dream of the past, and a cold penetrating rain\r\nadded the last touch of dramatic completeness to the staging of the new\r\nact.\r\n\r\nFor two days we travelled slowly up the mighty waterway that is the\r\nstrategic link between the two seas of Germany. Broad and straight,\r\nmassively embanked, lit by electricity at night till it is lighter than\r\nmany a great London street; traversed by great war vessels, rich\r\nmerchantmen, and humble coasters alike, it is a symbol of the new and\r\nmighty force which, controlled by the genius of statesmen and\r\nengineers, is thrusting the empire irresistibly forward to the goal of\r\nmaritime greatness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIsn\xe2\x80\x99t it splendid?\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s a fine fellow, that emperor.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKarl was the shock-headed, stout-limbed boy of about sixteen, who\r\nconstituted the whole crew of the _Johannes_, and was as dirty as his\r\nmaster was clean. I felt a certain envious reverence for this\r\nunprepossessing youth, seeing in him a much more efficient counterpart\r\nof myself; but how he and his little master ever managed to work their\r\nungainly vessel was a miracle I never understood. Phlegmatically\r\nimpervious to rain and cold, he steered the _Johannes_ down the long\r\ngrey reaches in the wake of the tug, while we and Bartels held snug\r\ngatherings down below, sometimes in his cabin, sometimes in ours. The\r\nheating arrangements of the latter began to be a subject of serious\r\nconcern. We finally did the only logical thing, and brought the\r\nkitchen-range into the parlour, fixing the Rippingille stove on the\r\nforward end of the cabin table, where it could warm as well as cook for\r\nus. As an ornament it was monstrous, and the taint of oil which it\r\nintroduced was a disgusting drawback; but, after all, the great\r\nthing\xe2\x80\x94as Davies said\xe2\x80\x94is to be comfortable, and after that to be clean.\r\n\r\nDavies held long consultations with Bartels, who was thoroughly at home\r\nin the navigation of the sands we were bound for, his own boat being a\r\ntype of the very craft which ply in them. I shall not forget the moment\r\nwhen it first dawned on him that his young friend\xe2\x80\x99s curiosity was\r\npractical; for he had thought that our goal was his own beloved\r\nHamburg, queen of cities, a place to see and die.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is too late,\xe2\x80\x9d he wailed. \xe2\x80\x9cYou do not know the Nord See as I do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, nonsense, Bartels, it\xe2\x80\x99s quite safe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSafe! And have I not found you fast on Hohenh\xc3\xb6rn, in a storm, with\r\nyour rudder broken? God was good to you then, my son.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, but it wasn\xe2\x80\x99t my f\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d Davies checked himself. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99re going home.\r\nThere\xe2\x80\x99s nothing in that.\xe2\x80\x9d Bartels became sadly resigned.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is good that you have a friend,\xe2\x80\x9d was his last word on the subject;\r\nbut all the same he always glanced at me with a rather doubtful eye. As\r\nto Davies and myself, our friendship developed quickly on certain\r\nlimited lines, the chief obstacle, as I well know now, being his\r\nreluctance to talk about the personal side of our quest.\r\n\r\nOn the other hand, I spoke about my own life and interests, with an\r\nunsparing discernment, of which I should have been incapable a month\r\nago, and in return I gained the key to his own character. It was\r\ndevotion to the sea, wedded to a fire of pent-up patriotism struggling\r\nincessantly for an outlet in strenuous physical expression; a humanity,\r\nborn of acute sensitiveness to his own limitations, only adding fuel to\r\nthe flame. I learnt for the first time now that in early youth he had\r\nfailed for the navy, the first of several failures in his career. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd\r\nI can\xe2\x80\x99t settle down to anything else,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI read no end about\r\nit, and yet I am a useless outsider. All I\xe2\x80\x99ve been able to do is to\r\npotter about in small boats; but it\xe2\x80\x99s all been _wasted_ till this\r\nchance came. I\xe2\x80\x99m afraid you\xe2\x80\x99ll not understand how I feel about it; but\r\nat last, for once in a way, I see a chance of being useful.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere ought to be chances for chaps like you,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, \xe2\x80\x9cwithout the\r\naccident of a job such as this.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, as long as I get it, what matter? But I know what you mean. There\r\nmust be hundreds of chaps like me\xe2\x80\x94I know a good many myself\xe2\x80\x94who know\r\nour coasts like a book\xe2\x80\x94shoals, creeks, tides, rocks; there\xe2\x80\x99s nothing in\r\nit, it\xe2\x80\x99s only practice. They ought to make some use of us as a naval\r\nreserve. They tried to once, but it fizzled out, and nobody really\r\ncares. And what\xe2\x80\x99s the result? Using every man of what reserves we\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\ngot, there\xe2\x80\x99s about enough to man the fleet on a war footing, and no\r\nmore. They\xe2\x80\x99ve tinkered with fishermen, and merchant sailors, and\r\nyachting hands, but everyone of them ought to be got hold of; and the\r\ncolonies, too. Is there the ghost of a doubt that if war broke out\r\nthere\xe2\x80\x99d be wild appeals for volunteers, aimless cadging, hurry,\r\nconfusion, waste? My own idea is that we ought to go much further, and\r\ntrain every able-bodied man for a couple of years as a sailor. Army?\r\nOh, I suppose you\xe2\x80\x99d have to give them the choice. Not that I know or\r\ncare much about the Army, though to listen to people talk you\xe2\x80\x99d think\r\nit really mattered as the Navy matters. We\xe2\x80\x99re a maritime nation\xe2\x80\x94we\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\ngrown by the sea and live by it; if we lose command of it we starve.\r\nWe\xe2\x80\x99re unique in that way, just as our huge empire, only linked by the\r\nsea, is unique. And yet, read Brassey, Dilke, and those _Naval\r\nAnnuals_, and see what mountains of apathy and conceit have had to be\r\ntackled. It\xe2\x80\x99s not the people\xe2\x80\x99s fault. We\xe2\x80\x99ve been safe so long, and\r\ngrown so rich, that we\xe2\x80\x99ve forgotten what we owe it to. But there\xe2\x80\x99s no\r\nexcuse for those blockheads of statesmen, as they call themselves, who\r\nare paid to see things as they are. They have to go to an American to\r\nlearn their A B C, and it\xe2\x80\x99s only when kicked and punched by civilian\r\nagitators, a mere handful of men who get sneered at for their pains,\r\nthat they wake up, do some work, point proudly to it, and go to sleep\r\nagain, till they get another kick. By Jove! we want a man like this\r\nKaiser, who doesn\xe2\x80\x99t wait to be kicked, but works like a nigger for his\r\ncountry, and sees ahead.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99re improving, aren\xe2\x80\x99t we?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, of course, we are! But it\xe2\x80\x99s a constant uphill fight; and we aren\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nready. They talk of a two-power standard\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d He plunged away into\r\nregions where space forbids me to follow him. This is only a sample of\r\nmany similar conversations that we afterwards held, always culminating\r\nin the burning question of Germany. Far from including me and the\r\nForeign Office among his targets for vague invective, he had a profound\r\nrespect for my sagacity and experience as a member of that institution;\r\na respect which embarrassed me not a little when I thought of my\r\n_pr\xc3\xa9cis_ writing and cigarette-smoking, my dancing, and my dining. But\r\nI did know something of Germany, and could satisfy his tireless\r\nquestioning with a certain authority. He used to listen rapt while I\r\ndescribed her marvellous awakening in the last generation, under the\r\nstrength and wisdom of her rulers; her intense patriotic ardour; her\r\nseething industrial activity, and, most potent of all, the forces that\r\nare moulding modern Europe, her dream of a colonial empire, entailing\r\nher transformation from a land-power to a sea-power. Impregnably based\r\non vast territorial resources which we cannot molest, the dim instincts\r\nof her people, not merely directed but anticipated by the genius of her\r\nruling house, our great trade rivals of the present, our great naval\r\nrival of the future, she grows, and strengthens, and waits, an ever\r\nmore formidable factor in the future of our delicate network of empire,\r\nsensitive as gossamer to external shocks, and radiating from an island\r\nwhose commerce is its life, and which depends even for its daily ration\r\nof bread on the free passage of the seas.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd we aren\xe2\x80\x99t ready for her,\xe2\x80\x9d Davies would say; \xe2\x80\x9cwe don\xe2\x80\x99t look her\r\nway. We have no naval base in the North Sea, and no North Sea Fleet.\r\nOur best battleships are too deep in draught for North Sea work. And,\r\nto crown all, we were asses enough to give her Heligoland, which\r\ncommands her North Sea coast. And supposing she collars Holland; isn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nthere some talk of that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThat would lead me to describe the swollen ambitions of the\r\nPan-Germanic party, and its ceaseless intrigues to promote the\r\nabsorption of Austria, Switzerland, and\xe2\x80\x94a direct and flagrant menace to\r\nourselves\xe2\x80\x94of Holland.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t blame them,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, who, for all his patriotism, had not\r\na particle of racial spleen in his composition. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t blame them;\r\ntheir Rhine ceases to be German just when it begins to be most\r\nvaluable. The mouth is Dutch, and would give them magnificent ports\r\njust opposite British shores. _We_ can\xe2\x80\x99t talk about conquest and\r\ngrabbing. We\xe2\x80\x99ve collared a fine share of the world, and they\xe2\x80\x99ve every\r\nright to be jealous. Let them hate us, and say so; it\xe2\x80\x99ll teach us to\r\nbuck up; and that\xe2\x80\x99s what really matters.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn these talks there occurred a singular contact of minds. It was very\r\nwell for me to spin sonorous generalities, but I had never till now\r\ndreamed of being so vulgar as to translate them into practice. I had\r\nalways detested the meddlesome alarmist, who veils ignorance under\r\nnoisiness, and for ever wails his chant of lugubrious pessimism. To be\r\nthrown with Davies was to receive a shock of enlightenment; for here,\r\nat least, was a specimen of the breed who exacted respect. It is true\r\nhe made use of the usual jargon, interlarding his stammering sentences\r\n(sometimes, when he was excited, with the oddest effect) with the\r\nconventional catchwords of the journalist and platform speaker. But\r\nthese were but accidents; for he seemed to have caught his innermost\r\nconviction from the very soul of the sea itself. An armchair critic is\r\none thing, but a sunburnt, brine-burnt zealot smarting under a personal\r\ndiscontent, athirst for a means, however tortuous, of contributing his\r\neffort to the great cause, the maritime supremacy of Britain, that was\r\nquite another thing. He drew inspiration from the very wind and spray.\r\nHe communed with his tiller, I believe, and marshalled his figures with\r\nits help. To hear him talk was to feel a current of clarifying air\r\nblustering into a close club-room, where men bandy ineffectual\r\nplatitudes, and mumble old shibboleths, and go away and do nothing.\r\n\r\nIn our talk about policy and strategy we were Bismarcks and Rodneys,\r\nwielding nations and navies; and, indeed, I have no doubt that our\r\nfancy took extravagant flights sometimes. In plain fact we were merely\r\ntwo young gentlemen in a seven-ton pleasure boat, with a taste for\r\namateur hydrography and police duty combined. Not that Davies ever\r\ndoubted. Once set on the road he gripped his purpose with child-like\r\nfaith and tenacity. It was his \xe2\x80\x9cchance\xe2\x80\x9d.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XI.\r\nThe Pathfinders\r\n\r\n\r\nIn the late afternoon of the second day our flotilla reached the Elbe\r\nat Brunsb\xc3\xbcttel and ranged up in the inner basin, while a big liner,\r\nwhimpering like a fretful baby, was tenderly nursed into the lock.\r\nDuring the delay Davies left me in charge, and bolted off with an\r\noil-can and a milk-jug. An official in uniform was passing along the\r\nquay from vessel to vessel countersigning papers. I went up to meet him\r\nwith our receipt for dues, which he signed carelessly. Then he paused\r\nand muttered _\xe2\x80\x9cDooltzhibella,\xe2\x80\x9d_ scratching his head, \xe2\x80\x9cthat was the\r\nname. English?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLittle _lust-cutter_, that is so; there was an inquiry for you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhom from?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA friend of yours from a big barge-yacht.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I know; she went on to Hamburg, I suppose?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo such luck, captain; she was outward bound.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhat did the man mean? He seemed to be vastly amused by something.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen was this\xe2\x80\x94about three weeks ago?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked, indifferently.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThree weeks? It was the day before yesterday. What a pity to miss him\r\nby so little!\xe2\x80\x9d He chuckled and winked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid he leave any message?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was a lady who inquired,\xe2\x80\x9d whispered the fellow, sniggering. \xe2\x80\x9cOh,\r\nreally,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, beginning to feel highly absurd, but keenly curious.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd she inquired about the _Dulcibella_?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHerrgott! she was difficult to satisfy! Stood over me while I searched\r\nthe books. \xe2\x80\x98A very little one,\xe2\x80\x99 she kept saying, and \xe2\x80\x98Are you sure all\r\nthe names are here?\xe2\x80\x99 I saw her into her kleine Boot, and she rowed away\r\nin the rain. No, she left no message. It was dirty weather for a young\r\nFr\xc3\xa4ulein to be out alone in. Ach! she was safe enough, though. To see\r\nher crossing the ebb in a chop of tide was a treat.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd the yacht went on down the river? Where was she bound to?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow do I know? Bremen, Wilhelmshaven, Emden\xe2\x80\x94somewhere in the North\r\nSea; too far for you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know about that,\xe2\x80\x9d said I, bravely.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAch! you will not follow in _that_? Are not you bound to Hamburg?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe can change our plans. It seems a pity to have missed them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThink twice, captain, there are plenty of pretty girls in Hamburg. But\r\nyou English will do anything. Well, viel Gl\xc3\xbcck!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe moved on, chuckling, to the next boat. Davies soon returned with his\r\ncans and an armful of dark, rye loaves, just in time, for, the liner\r\nbeing through, the flotilla was already beginning to jostle into the\r\nlock and Bartels was growing impatient.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99ll last ten days,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, as we followed the throng, still\r\nclinging like a barnacle to the side of the _Johannes_. We spent the\r\nfew minutes while the lock was emptied in a farewell talk to Bartels.\r\nKarl had hitched their main halyards on to the windlass and was\r\ngrinding at it in an _acharnement_ of industry, his shock head jerking\r\nand his grubby face perspiring. Then the lock-gates opened; and so, in\r\na Babel of shouting, whining of blocks, and creaking of spars, our\r\nwhole company was split out into the dingy bosom of the Elbe. The\r\n_Johannes_ gathered way under wind and tide and headed for midstream. A\r\nlast shake of the hand, and Bartels reluctantly slipped the head-rope\r\nand we drifted apart. \xe2\x80\x9cGute Reise! Gute Reise!\xe2\x80\x9d It was no time for\r\nregretful gazing, for the flood-tide was sweeping us up and out, and it\r\nwas not until we had set the foresail, edged into a shallow bight, and\r\nlet go our anchor, that we had leisure to think of him again; but by\r\nthat time his and the other craft were shades in the murky east.\r\n\r\nWe swung close to a _glacis_ of smooth blue mud which sloped up to a\r\nweed-grown dyke; behind lay the same flat country, colourless, humid;\r\nand opposite us, two miles away, scarcely visible in the deepening\r\ntwilight, ran the outline of a similar shore. Between rolled the turgid\r\nElbe. \xe2\x80\x9cThe Styx flowing through Tartarus,\xe2\x80\x9d I thought to myself,\r\nrecalling some of our Baltic anchorages.\r\n\r\nI told my news to Davies as soon as the anchor was down, instinctively\r\nleaving the sex of the inquirer to the last, as my informant had done.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe _Medusa_ called yesterday?\xe2\x80\x9d he interrupted. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd outward bound?\r\nThat\xe2\x80\x99s a rum thing. Why didn\xe2\x80\x99t he inquire when he was going _up_?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was a lady,\xe2\x80\x9d and I drily retailed the official\xe2\x80\x99s story, very busy\r\nwith a deck-broom the while. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99re all square now, aren\xe2\x80\x99t we?\xe2\x80\x9d I\r\nended. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll go below and light the stove.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDavies had been engaged in fixing up the riding-light. When I last saw\r\nhim he was still so engaged, but motionless, the lantern under his left\r\narm and his right hand grasping the forestay and the half-knotted\r\nlanyard; his eyes staring fixedly down the river, a strange look in his\r\nface, half exultant, half perplexed. When he joined me and spoke he\r\nseemed to be concluding a difficult argument.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnyway, it proves,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cthat the _Medusa_ has gone back to\r\nNorderney. That\xe2\x80\x99s the main thing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cProbably,\xe2\x80\x9d I agreed, \xe2\x80\x9cbut let\xe2\x80\x99s sum up all we know. First, it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ncertain that nobody we\xe2\x80\x99ve met as yet has any suspicion of _us_\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI told you he did it off his own bat,\xe2\x80\x9d threw in Davies.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOr, secondly, of _him._ If he\xe2\x80\x99s what you think it\xe2\x80\x99s not known here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t help that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThirdly, he inquires for you on his way _back_ from Hamburg, three\r\nweeks after the event. It doesn\xe2\x80\x99t look as if he thought he had disposed\r\nof you\xe2\x80\x94it doesn\xe2\x80\x99t look as if he had _meant_ to dispose of you. He sends\r\nhis daughter, too; a curious proceeding under the circumstances.\r\nPerhaps it\xe2\x80\x99s all a mistake.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s not a mistake,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, half to himself. \xe2\x80\x9cBut _did_ he send\r\nher? He\xe2\x80\x99d have sent one of his men. He can\xe2\x80\x99t be on board at all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis was a new light.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you mean?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe must have left the yacht when he got to Hamburg; some other devil\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nwork, I suppose. She\xe2\x80\x99s being sailed back now, and passing here\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I see! It\xe2\x80\x99s a private supplementary inquiry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s a long name to call it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWould the girl sail back alone with the crew?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s used to the sea\xe2\x80\x94and perhaps she isn\xe2\x80\x99t alone. There was that\r\nstepmother\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94 But it doesn\xe2\x80\x99t make a ha\xe2\x80\x99porth of difference to our plans;\r\nwe\xe2\x80\x99ll start on the ebb to-morrow morning.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe were busier than usual that night, reckoning stores, tidying\r\nlockers, and securing movables. \xe2\x80\x9cWe must economise,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, for\r\nall the world as though we were castaways on a raft. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a wretched\r\nthing to have to land somewhere to buy oil,\xe2\x80\x9d was a favourite\r\nobservation of his.\r\n\r\nBefore getting to sleep I was made to recognise a new factor in the\r\nconditions of navigation, now that the tideless Baltic was left behind\r\nus. A strong current was sluicing past our sides, and at the eleventh\r\nhour I was turned out, clad in pyjamas and oilskins (a horrible\r\ncombination), to assist in running out a kedge or spare anchor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s kedging-off?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked, when we were tucked up again. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nwhen you run aground; you have to\xe2\x80\x94but you\xe2\x80\x99ll soon learn all about it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nI steeled my heart for the morrow.\r\n\r\nSo behold us, then, at eight o\xe2\x80\x99clock on October 5, standing down the\r\nriver towards the field of our first labours. It is fifteen miles to\r\nthe mouth; drab, dreary miles like the dullest reaches of the lower\r\nThames; but scenery was of no concern to us, and a south-westerly\r\nbreeze blowing out of a grey sky kept us constantly on the verge of\r\nreefing. The tide as it gathered strength swept us down with a force\r\nattested by the speed with which buoys came in sight, nodded above us\r\nand passed, each boiling in its eddy of dirty foam. I scarcely noticed\r\nat first\xe2\x80\x94so calm was the water, and so regular were the buoys, like\r\nmilestones along a road\xe2\x80\x94that the northern line of coast was rapidly\r\nreceding and that the \xe2\x80\x9criver\xe2\x80\x9d was coming to be but a belt of deep water\r\nskirting a vast estuary, three\xe2\x80\x94seven\xe2\x80\x94ten miles broad, till it merged in\r\nopen sea.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, we\xe2\x80\x99re at sea!\xe2\x80\x9d I suddenly exclaimed, \xe2\x80\x9cafter an hour\xe2\x80\x99s sailing!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust discovered that?\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, laughing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou said it was fifteen miles,\xe2\x80\x9d I complained.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo it is, till we reach this coast at Cuxhaven; but I suppose you may\r\nsay we\xe2\x80\x99re at sea; of course that\xe2\x80\x99s all sand over there to starboard.\r\nLook! some of it\xe2\x80\x99s showing already.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe pointed into the north. Looking more attentively I noticed that\r\noutside the line of buoys patches of the surface heaved and worked; in\r\none or two places streaks and circles of white were forming; in the\r\nmidst of one such circle a sleek mauve hump had risen, like the back of\r\na sleeping whale. I saw that an old spell was enthralling Davies as his\r\neye travelled away to the blank horizon. He scanned it all with a\r\ncritical eagerness, too, as one who looks for a new meaning in an old\r\nfriend\xe2\x80\x99s face. Something of his zest was communicated to me, and\r\nstilled the shuddering thrill that had seized me. The protecting land\r\nwas still a comforting neighbour; but our severance with it came\r\nquickly.
