The tide whirled us down, and our straining canvas aiding it,\r\nwe were soon off Cuxhaven, which crouched so low behind its mighty\r\ndyke, that of some of its houses only the chimneys were visible. Then,\r\na mile or so on, the shore sharpened to a point like a claw, where the\r\ninnocent dyke became a long, low fort, with some great guns peeping\r\nover; then of a sudden it ceased, retreating into the far south in a\r\ndim perspective of groins and dunes.\r\n\r\nWe spun out into the open and leant heavily over to the now\r\nunobstructed wind. The yacht rose and sank to a little swell, but my\r\nfirst impression was one of wonder at the calmness of the sea, for the\r\nwind blew fresh and free from horizon to horizon.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, it\xe2\x80\x99s all sand _there_ now, and we\xe2\x80\x99re under the lee of it,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nDavies, with an enthusiastic sweep of his hand over the sea on our left\r\nor port hand. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s our hunting ground.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are we going to do?\xe2\x80\x9d I inquired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPick up Sticker\xe2\x80\x99s Gat,\xe2\x80\x9d was the reply. \xe2\x80\x9cIt ought to be near Buoy K.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA red buoy with a huge K on it soon came into view. Davies peered over\r\nto port.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust pull up the centreboard, will you?\xe2\x80\x9d he remarked abstractedly,\r\nadding, \xe2\x80\x9cand hand me up the glasses as you are down there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever mind the glasses. I\xe2\x80\x99ve got it now; come to the main-sheet,\xe2\x80\x9d was\r\nthe next remark.\r\n\r\nHe put down the helm and headed the yacht straight for the troubled and\r\ndiscoloured expanse which covered the submerged sands. A \xe2\x80\x9csleeping\r\nwhale\xe2\x80\x9d, with a light surf splashing on it, was right in our path.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStand by the lead, will you?\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, politely. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll manage the\r\nsheets, it\xe2\x80\x99s a dead beat in. Ready about!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe wind was in our teeth now, and for a crowded half-hour we wormed\r\nourselves forward by ever-shortening tacks into the sinuous recesses of\r\na channel which threaded the shallows westward. I knelt in a tangle of\r\nline, and, under the hazy impression that something very critical was\r\ngoing on, plied the lead furiously, bumping and splashing myself, and\r\nshouting out the depths, which lessened steadily, with a great sense of\r\nthe importance of my function. Davies never seemed to listen, but\r\ntacked on imperturbably, juggling with the tiller, the sheets, and the\r\nchart, in a way that made one giddy to look at. For all our zeal we\r\nseemed to be making very slow progress.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s no use, tide\xe2\x80\x99s too strong; we must chance it,\xe2\x80\x9d he said at last.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cChance what?\xe2\x80\x9d I wondered to myself. Our tacks suddenly began to grow\r\nlonger, and the depths, which I registered, shallower. All went well\r\nfor some time though, and we made better progress. Then came a longer\r\nreach than usual.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTwo and a half\xe2\x80\x94two\xe2\x80\x94one and a half\xe2\x80\x94one\xe2\x80\x94only five feet,\xe2\x80\x9d I gasped,\r\nreproachfully. The water was growing thick and frothy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt doesn\xe2\x80\x99t matter if we do,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, thinking aloud. \xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s an\r\neddy here, and it\xe2\x80\x99s a pity to waste it\xe2\x80\x94ready about! Back the jib!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut it was too late. The yacht answered but faintly to the helm,\r\nstopped, and heeled heavily over, wallowing and grinding. Davies had\r\nthe mainsail down in a twinkling; it half smothered me as I crouched on\r\nthe lee-side among my tangled skeins of line, scared and helpless. I\r\ncrawled out from the folds, and saw him standing by the mast in a\r\nreverie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s not much use,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9con a falling tide, but we\xe2\x80\x99ll try\r\nkedging-off. Pay that warp out while I run out the kedge.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLike lightning he had cast off the dinghy\xe2\x80\x99s painter, tumbled the\r\nkedge-anchor and himself into the dinghy, pulled out fifty yards into\r\nthe deeper water, and heaved out the anchor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow haul,\xe2\x80\x9d he shouted.\r\n\r\nI hauled, beginning to see what kedging-off meant.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSteady on! Don\xe2\x80\x99t sweat yourself,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, jumping aboard again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s coming,\xe2\x80\x9d I spluttered, triumphantly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe warp is, the yacht isn\xe2\x80\x99t; you\xe2\x80\x99re dragging the anchor home. Never\r\nmind, she\xe2\x80\x99ll lie well here. Let\xe2\x80\x99s have lunch.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe yacht was motionless, and the water round her visibly lower.\r\nPetulant waves slapped against her sides, but, scattered as my senses\r\nwere, I realised that there was no vestige of danger. Round us the\r\nwhole face of the waters was changing from moment to moment, whitening\r\nin some places, yellowing in others, where breadths of sand began to be\r\nexposed. Close on our right the channel we had left began to look like\r\na turbid little river; and I understood why our progress had been so\r\nslow when I saw its current racing back to meet the Elbe. Davies was\r\nalready below, laying out a more than usually elaborate lunch, in high\r\ncontent of mind.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLies quiet, doesn\xe2\x80\x99t she?\xe2\x80\x9d he remarked. \xe2\x80\x9cIf you _do_ want a sit-down\r\nlunch, there\xe2\x80\x99s nothing like running aground for it. And, anyhow, we\xe2\x80\x99re\r\nas handy for work here as anywhere else. You\xe2\x80\x99ll see.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLike most landsmen I had a wholesome prejudice against running aground,\r\nso that my mentor\xe2\x80\x99s turn for breezy paradox was at first rather\r\nexasperating. After lunch the large-scale chart of the estuaries was\r\nbrought down, and we pored over it together, mapping out work for the\r\nnext few days. There is no need to tire the general reader with its\r\nintricacies, nor is there space to reproduce it for the benefit of the\r\ninstructed reader. For both classes the general map should be\r\nsufficient, taken with the large-scale fragment _[See Chart A]_ which\r\ngives a fair example of the region in detail. It will be seen that the\r\nthree broad fairways of the Jade, Weser, and Elbe split up the sands\r\ninto two main groups. The westernmost of these is symmetrical in\r\noutline, an acute-angled triangle, very like a sharp steel-shod pike,\r\nif you imagine the peninsula from which it springs to be the wooden\r\nhaft. The other is a huge congeries of banks, its base resting on the\r\nHanover coast, two of its sides tolerably clean and even, and the\r\nthird, that facing the north-west, ribboned and lacerated by the fury\r\nof the sea, which has eaten out deep cavities and struck hungry\r\ntentacles far into the interior. The whole resembles an inverted E, or,\r\nbetter still, a rude fork, on whose three deadly prongs, the Scharhorn\r\nReef, the Knecht Sand, and the Tegeler Flat, as on the no less deadly\r\npoint of the pike, many a good ship splinters herself in northerly\r\ngales. Following this simile, the Hohenh\xc3\xb6rn bank, where Davies was\r\nwrecked, is one of those that lie between the upper and middle prongs.\r\n\r\nOur business was to explore the Pike and the Fork and the channels\r\nwhich ramify through them. I use the general word \xe2\x80\x9cchannel\xe2\x80\x9d, but in\r\nfact they differ widely in character, and are called in German by\r\nvarious names: Balje, Gat, Loch, Diep, Rinne. For my purpose I need\r\nonly divide them into two sorts\xe2\x80\x94those which have water in them at all\r\nstates of the tide, and those which have not, which dry off, that is,\r\neither wholly or partly at low-tide.\r\n\r\nDavies explained that the latter would take most learning, and were to\r\nbe our chief concern, because they were the \xe2\x80\x9cthrough-routes\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94the\r\nconnecting links between the estuaries. You can always detect them on\r\nthe chart by rows of little Y-shaped strokes denoting \xe2\x80\x9cbooms\xe2\x80\x9d, that is\r\nto say, poles or saplings fixed in the sand to mark the passage. The\r\nstrokes, of course, are only conventional signs, and do not correspond\r\nin the least to individual \xe2\x80\x9cbooms\xe2\x80\x9d, which are far too numerous and\r\ncomplex to be indicated accurately on a chart, even of the largest\r\nscale. The same applies to the course of the channels themselves, whose\r\nminor meanderings cannot be reproduced.\r\n\r\nIt was on the edge of one of these tidal swatchways that the yacht was\r\nnow lying. It is called Sticker\xe2\x80\x99s Gat, and you cannot miss it _[See\r\nChart A]_ if you carry your eye westward along our course from\r\nCuxhaven. It was, so Davies told me, the last and most intricate stage\r\nof the \xe2\x80\x9cshort cut\xe2\x80\x9d which the _Medusa_ had taken on that memorable day\xe2\x80\x94a\r\nstage he himself had never reached. Discussion ended, we went on deck,\r\nDavies arming himself with a notebook, binoculars, and the prismatic\r\ncompass, whose use\xe2\x80\x94to map the angles of the channels\xe2\x80\x94was at last\r\napparent. This is what I saw when we emerged.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XII.\r\nMy Initiation\r\n\r\n\r\nThe yacht lay with a very slight heel (thanks to a pair of small\r\nbilge-keels on her bottom) in a sort of trough she had dug for herself,\r\nso that she was still ringed with a few inches of water, as it were\r\nwith a moat.\r\n\r\nFor miles in every direction lay a desert of sand. To the north it\r\ntouched the horizon, and was only broken by the blue dot of Neuerk\r\nIsland and its lighthouse. To the east it seemed also to stretch to\r\ninfinity, but the smoke of a steamer showed where it was pierced by the\r\nstream of the Elbe. To the south it ran up to the pencil-line of the\r\nHanover shore. Only to the west was its outline broken by any vestiges\r\nof the sea it had risen from. There it was astir with crawling white\r\nfilaments, knotted confusedly at one spot in the north-west, whence\r\ncame a sibilant murmur like the hissing of many snakes. Desert as I\r\ncall it, it was not entirely featureless. Its colour varied from light\r\nfawn, where the highest levels had dried in the wind, to brown or deep\r\nviolet, where it was still wet, and slate-grey where patches of mud\r\nsoiled its clean bosom. Here and there were pools of water, smitten\r\ninto ripples by the impotent wind; here and there it was speckled by\r\nshells and seaweed. And close to us, beginning to bend away towards\r\nthat hissing knot in the north-west, wound our poor little channel,\r\nmercilessly exposed as a stagnant, muddy ditch with scarcely a foot of\r\nwater, not deep enough to hide our small kedge-anchor, which perked up\r\none fluke in impudent mockery. The dull, hard sky, the wind moaning in\r\nthe rigging as though crying in despair for a prey that had escaped it,\r\nmade the scene inexpressibly forlorn.\r\n\r\nDavies scanned it with gusto for a moment, climbed to a point of\r\nvantage on the boom, and swept his glasses to and fro along the course\r\nof the channel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFairly well boomed,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, meditatively, \xe2\x80\x9cbut one or two are very\r\nmuch out. By Jove! that\xe2\x80\x99s a tricky bend there.\xe2\x80\x9d He took a bearing with\r\nthe compass, made a note or two, and sprang with a vigorous leap down\r\non to the sand.\r\n\r\nThis, I may say, was the only way of \xe2\x80\x9cgoing ashore\xe2\x80\x9d that he really\r\nliked. We raced off as fast as our clumsy sea-boots would let us, and\r\nfollowed up the course of our channel to the west, reconnoitring the\r\nroad we should have to follow when the tide rose.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe only way to learn a place like this,\xe2\x80\x9d he shouted, \xe2\x80\x9cis to see it at\r\nlow water. The banks are dry then, and the channels are plain. Look at\r\nthat boom\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94he stopped and pointed contemptuously\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cit\xe2\x80\x99s all out of\r\nplace. I suppose the channel\xe2\x80\x99s shifted there. It\xe2\x80\x99s just at an important\r\nbend too. If you took it as a guide when the water was up you\xe2\x80\x99d run\r\naground.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhich would be very useful,\xe2\x80\x9d I observed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, hang it!\xe2\x80\x9d he laughed, \xe2\x80\x9cwe\xe2\x80\x99re exploring. I want to be able to run\r\nthrough this channel without a mistake. We will, next time.\xe2\x80\x9d He\r\nstopped, and plied compass and notebook. Then we raced on till the next\r\nhalt was called.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, the channel\xe2\x80\x99s getting deeper, it was nearly dry a\r\nmoment ago; see the current in it now? That\xe2\x80\x99s the flood tide coming\r\nup\xe2\x80\x94from the _west,_ mind you; that is, from the Weser side. That shows\r\nwe\xe2\x80\x99re past the watershed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWatershed?\xe2\x80\x9d I repeated, blankly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, that\xe2\x80\x99s what I call it. You see, a big sand such as this is like a\r\nrange of hills dividing two plains, it\xe2\x80\x99s never dead flat though it\r\nlooks it; there\xe2\x80\x99s always one point, one ridge, rather, where it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhighest. Now a channel cutting right through the sand is, of course,\r\nalways at its shallowest when it\xe2\x80\x99s crossing this ridge; at low water\r\nit\xe2\x80\x99s generally dry there, and it gradually deepens as it gets nearer to\r\nthe sea on either side. Now at high tide, when the whole sand is\r\ncovered, the water can travel where it likes; but directly the ebb sets\r\nin the water falls away on either side the ridge and the channel\r\nbecomes two rivers flowing in opposite directions _from_ the centre, or\r\nwatershed, as I call it. So, also, when the ebb has run out and the\r\nflood begins, the channel is fed by two currents flowing to the centre\r\nand meeting in the middle. Here the Elbe and the Weser are our two\r\nfeeders. Now this current here is going eastwards; we know by the time\r\nof day that the tide\xe2\x80\x99s rising, _therefore_ the watershed is between us\r\nand the yacht.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy is it so important to know that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause these currents are strong, and you want to know when you\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nlose a fair one and strike a foul one. Besides, the ridge is the\r\ncritical point when you\xe2\x80\x99re crossing on a falling tide, and you want to\r\nknow when you\xe2\x80\x99re past it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe pushed on till our path was barred by a big lagoon. It looked far\r\nmore imposing than the channel; but Davies, after a rapid scrutiny,\r\ntreated it to a grunt of contempt.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a _cul de sac_,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cSee that hump of sand it\xe2\x80\x99s making for,\r\nbeyond?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s boomed,\xe2\x80\x9d I remonstrated, pointing to a decrepit stem drooping\r\nover the bank, and shaking a palsied finger at the imposture.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, that\xe2\x80\x99s just where one goes wrong, it\xe2\x80\x99s an old cut that\xe2\x80\x99s silted\r\nup. That boom\xe2\x80\x99s a fraud; there\xe2\x80\x99s no time to go farther, the flood\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nmaking fast. I\xe2\x80\x99ll just take bearings of what we can see.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe false lagoon was the first of several that began to be visible in\r\nthe west, swelling and joining hands over the ribs of sand that divided\r\nthem. All the time the distant hissing grew nearer and louder, and a\r\ndeep, thunderous note began to sound beneath it. We turned our backs to\r\nthe wind and hastened back towards the _Dulcibella_, the stream in our\r\nchannel hurrying and rising alongside of us.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s just time to do the other side,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, when we reached\r\nher, and I was congratulating myself on having regained our base\r\nwithout finding our communications cut. And away we scurried in the\r\ndirection we had come that morning, splashing through pools and jumping\r\nthe infant runnels that were stealing out through rifts from the\r\nmother-channel as the tide rose. Our observations completed, back we\r\ntravelled, making a wide circuit over higher ground to avoid the\r\nencroaching flood, and wading shin-deep in the final approach to the\r\nyacht.\r\n\r\nAs I scrambled thankfully aboard, I seemed to hear a far-off voice\r\nsaying, in languid depreciation of yachting, that it did not give one\r\nenough exercise. It was mine, centuries ago, in another life. From east\r\nand west two sheets of water had overspread the desert, each pushing\r\nout tongues of surf that met and fused.\r\n\r\nI waited on deck and watched the death-throes of the suffocating sands\r\nunder the relentless onset of the sea. The last strongholds were\r\nbattered, stormed, and overwhelmed; the tumult of sounds sank and\r\nsteadied, and the sea swept victoriously over the whole expanse. The\r\n_Dulcibella_, hitherto contemptuously inert, began to wake and tremble\r\nunder the buffetings she received. Then, with an effort, she jerked\r\nherself on to an even keel and bumped and strained fretfully, impatient\r\nto vanquish this insolent invader and make him a slave for her own\r\nends. Soon her warp tightened and her nose swung slowly round; only her\r\nstern bumped now, and that with decreasing force. Suddenly she was free\r\nand drifting broadside to the wind till the anchor checked her and she\r\nbrought up to leeward of it, rocking easily and triumphantly.\r\nGood-humoured little person! At heart she was friends alike with sand\r\nand sea. It was only when the old love and the new love were in mortal\r\ncombat for her favours, and she was mauled in the _fracas_, that her\r\ntemper rose in revolt.\r\n\r\nWe swallowed a hasty cup of tea, ran up the sails, and started off west\r\nagain. Once across the \xe2\x80\x9cwatershed\xe2\x80\x9d we met a strong current, but the\r\ntrend of the passage was now more to the north-west, so that we could\r\nhold our course without tacking, and consequently could stem the tide.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGive her just a foot of the centre-plate,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies. \xe2\x80\x9cWe know the\r\nway here, and she\xe2\x80\x99ll make less leeway; but we shall generally have to\r\ndo without it always on a falling tide. If you run aground with the\r\nplate down you deserve to be drowned.\xe2\x80\x9d I now saw how valuable our walk\r\nhad been. The booms were on our right; but they were broken reeds,\r\ngiving no hint as to the breadth of the channel. A few had lost their\r\ntops, and were being engulfed altogether by the rising water. When we\r\ncame to the point where they ceased, and the false lagoon had lain, I\r\nshould have felt utterly lost. We had crossed the high and relatively\r\nlevel sands which form the base of the Fork, and were entering the\r\nlabyrinth of detached banks which obstruct the funnel-shaped cavity\r\nbetween the upper and middle prongs. This I knew from the chart. My\r\nunaided eye saw nothing but the open sea, growing dark green as the\r\ndepths increased; a dour, threatening sea, showing its white fangs. The\r\nwaves grew longer and steeper, for the channels, though still tortuous,\r\nnow begin to be broad and deep.\r\n\r\nDavies had his bearings, and struck on his course confidently. \xe2\x80\x9cNow for\r\nthe lead,\xe2\x80\x9d he said; \xe2\x80\x9cthe compass\xe2\x80\x99ll be little use soon. We must feel\r\nthe edge of the sands till we pick up more booms.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere are we going to anchor for the night?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUnder the Hohenh\xc3\xb6rn,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, \xe2\x80\x9cfor auld lang syne!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPartly by sight and mostly by touch we crept round the outermost alley\r\nof the hidden maze till a new clump of booms appeared, meaningless to\r\nme, but analysed by him into two groups. One we followed for some\r\ndistance, and then struck finally away and began another beat to\r\nwindward.\r\n\r\nDusk was falling. The Hanover coast-line, never very distinct, had\r\nutterly vanished; an ominous heave of swell was under-running the short\r\nsea. I ceased to attend to Davies imparting instruction on his beloved\r\nhobby, and sought to stifle in hard manual labour the dread that had\r\nbeen latent in me all day at the prospect of our first anchorage at\r\nsea.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSound, like blazes now!\xe2\x80\x9d he said at last. I came to a fathom and a\r\nhalf. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s the bank,\xe2\x80\x9d he said; \xe2\x80\x9cwe\xe2\x80\x99ll give it a bit of a berth and\r\nthen let go.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet go now!\xe2\x80\x9d was the order after a minute, and the chain ran out with\r\na long-drawn moan. The _Dulcibella_ snubbed up to it and jauntily faced\r\nthe North Sea and the growing night.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere we are!\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, as we finished stowing the mainsail, \xe2\x80\x9csafe\r\nand snug in four fathoms in a magnificent sand-harbour, with no one to\r\nbother us and the whole of it to ourselves. No dues, no stinks, no\r\ntraffic, no worries of any sort. It\xe2\x80\x99s better than a Baltic cove even,\r\nless beastly civilization about. We\xe2\x80\x99re seven miles from the nearest\r\ncoast, and five even from Neuerk\xe2\x80\x94look, they\xe2\x80\x99re lighting up.\xe2\x80\x9d There was\r\na tiny spark in the east.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose it\xe2\x80\x99s all right,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, \xe2\x80\x9cbut I\xe2\x80\x99d rather see a solid\r\nbreakwater somewhere; it\xe2\x80\x99s a dirty-looking night, and I don\xe2\x80\x99t like this\r\nswell.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe swell\xe2\x80\x99s nothing,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies; \xe2\x80\x9cit\xe2\x80\x99s only a stray drain from\r\noutside. As for breakwaters, you\xe2\x80\x99ve got them all round you, only\r\nthey\xe2\x80\x99re hidden. Ahead and to starboard is the West Hohenh\xc3\xb6rn, curling\r\nround to the sou\xe2\x80\x99-west for all the world like a stone pier. You can\r\nhear the surf battering on its outside over to the north. That\xe2\x80\x99s where\r\nI was nearly wrecked that day, and the little channel I stumbled into\r\nmust be quite near us somewhere. Half a mile away\xe2\x80\x94to port there\xe2\x80\x94is the\r\nEast Hohenh\xc3\xb6rn, where I brought up, after dashing across this lake\r\nwe\xe2\x80\x99re in. Another mile astern is the main body of the sands, the top\r\nprong of your fork. So you see we\xe2\x80\x99re shut in\xe2\x80\x94practically. Surely you\r\nremember the chart? Why, it\xe2\x80\x99s\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, confound the chart!\xe2\x80\x9d I broke out, finding this flow of plausible\r\ncomfort too dismally suggestive for my nerves. \xe2\x80\x9c_Look_ at it, man!\r\nSupposing anything happens\xe2\x80\x94supposing it blows a gale! But it\xe2\x80\x99s no good\r\nshivering here and staring at the view. I\xe2\x80\x99m going below.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was a _mauvais quart d\xe2\x80\x99heure_ below, during which, I am ashamed\r\nto say, I forgot the quest.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhich soup do you feel inclined for?\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, timidly, after a\r\nblack silence of some minutes.\r\n\r\nThat simple remark, more eloquent of security than a thousand technical\r\narguments, saved the situation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI say, Davies,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m a white-livered cur at the best, and you\r\nmustn\xe2\x80\x99t spare me. But you\xe2\x80\x99re not like any yachtsman I ever met before,\r\nor any sailor of any sort. You\xe2\x80\x99re so casual and quiet in the\r\nextraordinary things you do. I believe I should like you better if you\r\nlet fly a volley of deep-sea oaths sometimes, or threatened to put me\r\nin irons.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDavies opened wide eyes, and said it was all his fault for forgetting\r\nthat I was not as used to such anchorages as he was. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd, by the way,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nhe added, \xe2\x80\x9cas to its blowing a gale, I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t wonder if it did; the\r\nglass is falling hard; but it can\xe2\x80\x99t hurt us. You see, even at high\r\nwater the drift of the sea\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, for Heaven\xe2\x80\x99s sake, don\xe2\x80\x99t begin again. You\xe2\x80\x99ll prove soon that we\xe2\x80\x99re\r\nsafer here than in an hotel. Let\xe2\x80\x99s have dinner, and a thundering good\r\none!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDinner ran a smooth course, but just as coffee was being brewed the\r\nhull, from pitching regularly, began to roll.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI knew she would,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies. \xe2\x80\x9cI was going to warn you, only\xe2\x80\x94the ebb\r\nhas set in _against_ the wind. It\xe2\x80\x99s quite safe\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought you said it would get calmer when the tide fell?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo it will, but it may _seem_ rougher. Tides are queer things,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nadded, as though in defence of some not very respectable acquaintances.\r\n\r\nHe busied himself with his logbook, swaying easily to the motion of the\r\nboat; and I for my part tried to write up my diary, but I could not fix\r\nmy attention. Every loose article in the boat became audibly restless.\r\nCans clinked, cupboards rattled, lockers uttered hollow groans. Small\r\nthings sidled out of dark hiding-places, and danced grotesque drunken\r\nfigures on the floor, like goblins in a haunted glade. The mast whined\r\ndolorously at every heel, and the centreboard hiccoughed and choked.\r\nOverhead another horde of demons seemed to have been let loose. The\r\ndeck and mast were conductors which magnified every sound and made the\r\ntap-tap of every rope\xe2\x80\x99s end resemble the blows of a hammer, and the\r\nslapping of the halyards against the mast the rattle of a Maxim gun.\r\nThe whole tumult beat time to a rhythmical chorus which became\r\nmaddening.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe might turn in now,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies; \xe2\x80\x9cit\xe2\x80\x99s half-past ten.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat, sleep through this?\xe2\x80\x9d I exclaimed. \xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t stand this, I must\r\n_do_ something. Can\xe2\x80\x99t we go for another walk?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI spoke in bitter, half-delirious jest.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course we can,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, \xe2\x80\x9cif you don\xe2\x80\x99t mind a bit of a tumble\r\nin the dinghy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI reconsidered my rash suggestion, but it was too late now to turn\r\nback, and some desperate expedient was necessary. I found myself on\r\ndeck, gripping a backstay and looking giddily down and then up at the\r\ndinghy, as it bobbed like a cork in the trough of the sea alongside,\r\nwhile Davies settled the sculls and rowlocks.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJump!\xe2\x80\x9d he shouted, and before I could gather my wits and clutch the\r\nsides we were adrift in the night, reeling from hollow to hollow of the\r\nsteep curling waves. Davies nursed our walnut-shell tenderly over their\r\ncrests, edging her slantwise across their course. He used very little\r\nexertion, relying on the tide to carry us to our goal. Suddenly the\r\nmotion ceased. A dark slope loomed up out of the night, and the dinghy\r\nrested softly in a shallow eddy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe West Hohenh\xc3\xb6rn,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies. We jumped out and sank into soft\r\nmud, hauled up the dinghy a foot or two, then mounted the bank and were\r\non hard, wet sand. The wind leapt on us, and choked our voices.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet\xe2\x80\x99s find my channel,\xe2\x80\x9d bawled Davies. \xe2\x80\x9cThis way. Keep Neuerk light\r\nright astern of you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe set off with a long, stooping stride in the teeth of the wind, and\r\nstraight towards the roar of the breakers on the farther side of the\r\nsand. A line of Matthew Arnold\xe2\x80\x99s, \xe2\x80\x9cThe naked shingles of the world,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nwas running in my head. \xe2\x80\x9cSeven miles from land,\xe2\x80\x9d I thought, \xe2\x80\x9cscuttling\r\nlike sea-birds on a transient islet of sand, encircled by rushing tides\r\nand hammered by ocean, at midnight in a rising gale\xe2\x80\x94cut off even from\r\nour one dubious refuge.\xe2\x80\x9d It was the time, if ever, to conquer weakness.\r\nA mad gaiety surged through me as I drank the wind and pressed forward.\r\nIt seemed but a minute or two and Davies clutched me.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook out!\xe2\x80\x9d he shouted. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s my channel.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe ground sloped down, and a rushing river glimmered before us. We\r\nstruck off at a tangent and followed its course to the north, stumbling\r\nin muddy rifts, slipping on seaweed, beginning to be blinded by a fine\r\nsalt spray, and deafened by the thunder of the ocean surf. The river\r\nbroadened, whitened, roughened, gathered itself for the shock, was\r\nshattered, and dissolved in milky gloom. We wheeled away to the right,\r\nand splashed into yeasty froth. I turned my back to the wind, scooped\r\nthe brine out of my eyes, faced back and saw that our path was barred\r\nby a welter of surf. Davies\xe2\x80\x99s voice was in my ear and his arm was\r\npointing seaward.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis\xe2\x80\x94is\xe2\x80\x94about where\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x94bumped first\xe2\x80\x94worse then\xe2\x80\x94nor\xe2\x80\x99-west\r\nwind\xe2\x80\x94this\xe2\x80\x94is\xe2\x80\x94nothing. Let\xe2\x80\x99s\xe2\x80\x94go\xe2\x80\x94right\xe2\x80\x94round.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe galloped away with the wind behind us, skirting the line of surf. I\r\nlost all account of time and direction. Another sea barred our road,\r\nbecame another river as we slanted along its shore. Again we were in\r\nthe teeth of that intoxicating wind. Then a point of light was swaying\r\nand flickering away to the left, and now we were checking and circling.\r\nI stumbled against something sharp\xe2\x80\x94the dinghy\xe2\x80\x99s gunwale. So we had\r\ncompleted the circuit of our fugitive domain, that\r\ndream-island\xe2\x80\x94nightmare island as I always remember it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must scull, too,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s blowing hard now. Keep her\r\nnose _up_ a little\xe2\x80\x94all you know!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe lurched along, my scull sometimes buried to the thwart, sometimes\r\nstriking at the bubbles of a wave top. Davies, in the bows, said\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPull!\xe2\x80\x9d or \xe2\x80\x9cSteady!\xe2\x80\x9d at intervals. I heard the scud smacking against\r\nhis oilskin back. Then a wan, yellow light glanced over the waves.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEasy! Let her come!\xe2\x80\x9d and the bowsprit of the _Dulcibella_, swollen to\r\nspectral proportions, was stabbing the darkness above me. \xe2\x80\x9cBack a bit!\r\nTwo good strokes. Ship your scull! Now jump!\xe2\x80\x9d I clawed at the tossing\r\nhull and landed in a heap. Davies followed with the painter, and the\r\ndinghy swept astern.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s riding beautifully now,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, when he had secured the\r\npainter. \xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99ll be no rolling on the flood, and it\xe2\x80\x99s nearly low\r\nwater.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI don\xe2\x80\x99t think I should have cared, however much she had rolled. I was\r\nfinally cured of funk.\r\n\r\nIt was well that I was, for to be pitched out of your bunk on to wet\r\noil-cloth is a disheartening beginning to a day. This happened about\r\neight o\xe2\x80\x99clock. The yacht was pitching violently, and I crawled on all\r\nfours into the cabin, where Davies was setting out breakfast on the\r\nfloor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI let you sleep on,\xe2\x80\x9d he said; \xe2\x80\x9cwe can\xe2\x80\x99t do anything till the water\r\nfalls. We should never get the anchor up in this sea. Come and have a\r\nlook round. It\xe2\x80\x99s clearing now,\xe2\x80\x9d he went on, when we were crouching low\r\non deck, gripping cleats for safety. \xe2\x80\x9cWind\xe2\x80\x99s veered to nor\xe2\x80\x99-west. It\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nbeen blowing a full gale, and the sea is at its worst now\xe2\x80\x94near high\r\nwater. You\xe2\x80\x99ll never see worse than this.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI was prepared for what I saw\xe2\x80\x94the stormy sea for leagues around, and a\r\nchaos of breakers where our dream-island had stood\xe2\x80\x94and took it quietly,\r\neven with a sort of elation. The _Dulcibella_ faced the storm as\r\ndoggedly as ever, plunging her bowsprit into the sea and flinging green\r\nwater over her bows. A wave of confidence and affection for her welled\r\nthrough me. I had been used to resent the weight and bulk of her\r\nunwieldy anchor and cable, but I saw their use now; varnish, paint,\r\nspotless decks, and snowy sails were foppish absurdities of a hateful\r\npast.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat can we do to-day?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe must keep well inside the banks and be precious careful wherever\r\nthere\xe2\x80\x99s a swell. It\xe2\x80\x99s rampant in here, you see, in spite of the barrier\r\nof sand. But there\xe2\x80\x99s plenty we can do farther back.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe breakfasted in horrible discomfort; then smoked and talked till the\r\nroar of the breakers dwindled. At the first sign of bare sand we got\r\nunder way, under mizzen and headsails only, and I learned how to sail a\r\nreluctant anchor out of the ground. Pivoting round, we scudded east\r\nbefore the wind, over the ground we had traversed the evening before,\r\nwhile an archipelago of new banks slowly shouldered up above the fast\r\nweakening waves. We trod delicately among and around them, sounding and\r\nobserving; heaving to where space permitted, and sometimes using the\r\ndinghy. I began to see where the risks lay in this sort of navigation.\r\nWherever the ocean swell penetrated, or the wind blew straight down a\r\nlong deep channel, we had to be very cautious and leave good margins.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s the sort of place you mustn\xe2\x80\x99t ground on,\xe2\x80\x9d Davies used to say.\r\n\r\nIn the end we traversed the Steil Sand again, but by a different\r\nswatchway, and anchored, after an arduous day, in a notch on its\r\neastern limit, just clear of the swell that rolled in from the\r\nturbulent estuary of the Elbe. The night was fair, and when the tide\r\nreceded we lay perfectly still, the fresh wind only sending a lip-lip\r\nof ripples against our sides.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XIII.\r\nThe Meaning of our Work\r\n\r\n\r\nNothing happened during the next ten days to disturb us at our work.\r\nDuring every hour of daylight and many of darkness, sailing or\r\nanchored, aground or afloat, in rain and shine, wind and calm, we\r\nstudied the bed of the estuaries, and practised ourselves in threading\r\nthe network of channels; holding no communication with the land and\r\nrarely approaching it. It was a life of toil, exposure, and peril; a\r\nstruggle against odds, too; for wild autumnal weather was the rule,\r\nwith the wind backing and veering between the south-west and\r\nnorth-west, and only for two placid days blowing gently from the east,\r\nthe safe quarter for this region. Its force and direction determined\r\neach fresh choice of ground. If it was high and northerly we explored\r\nthe inner fastnesses; in moderate intervals the exterior fringe,\r\ndarting when surprised into whatever lair was most convenient.\r\n\r\nSometimes we were tramping vast solitudes of sand, sometimes scudding\r\nacross ephemeral tracts of shallow sea. Again, we were creeping\r\ngingerly round the deeper arteries that surround the Great Knecht,\r\nexamining their convolutions as it were the veins of a living tissue,\r\nand the circulation of the tide throbbing through them like blood.\r\nAgain, we would be staggering through the tide-rips and overfalls that\r\ninfest the open fairway of the Weser on our passage between the Fork\r\nand the Pike. On one of our fine days I saw the scene of Davies\xe2\x80\x99s\r\noriginal adventure by daylight with the banks dry and the channels\r\nmanifest. The reader has seen it on the chart, and can, up to a point,\r\nform his opinion; I can only add that I realised by ocular proof that\r\nno more fatal trap could have been devised for an innocent stranger;\r\nfor approaching it from the north-west under the easiest conditions it\r\nwas hard enough to verify our true course. In a period so full of new\r\nexcitements it is not easy for me to say when we were hardest put to\r\nit, especially as it was a rule with Davies never to admit that we were\r\nin any danger at all. But I think that our ugliest experience was on\r\nthe 10th, when, owing to some minute miscalculation, we stranded in a\r\ndangerous spot. Mere stranding, of course, was all in the day\xe2\x80\x99s work;\r\nthe constantly recurring question being when and where to court or risk\r\nit. This time we were so situated that when the rising tide came again\r\nwe were on a lee shore, broadside on to a gale of wind which was\r\nsending a nasty sea\xe2\x80\x94with a three-mile drift to give it force\xe2\x80\x94down\r\nRobin\xe2\x80\x99s Balje, which is one of the deeper arteries I spoke of above,\r\nand now lay dead to windward of us. The climax came about ten o\xe2\x80\x99clock\r\nat night. \xe2\x80\x9cWe can do nothing till she floats,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies; and I can\r\nsee him now quietly smoking and splicing a chafed warp while he\r\nexplained that her double skin of teak fitted her to stand anything in\r\nreason. She certainly had a terrific test that night, for the bottom\r\nwas hard, unyielding sand, on which she rose and fell with convulsive\r\nvehemence. The last half-hour was for me one of almost intolerable\r\ntension. I spent it on deck unable to bear the suspense below. Sheets\r\nof driven sea flew bodily over the hull, and a score of times I thought\r\nshe must succumb as she shivered to the blows of her keel on the sand.\r\nBut those stout skins knit by honest labour stood the trial. One final\r\nthud and she wrenched herself bodily free, found her anchor, and rode\r\nclear.\r\n\r\nOn the whole I think we made few mistakes. Davies had a supreme\r\naptitude for the work. Every hour, sometimes every minute, brought its\r\nproblem, and his resource never failed. The stiffer it was the cooler\r\nhe became. He had, too, that intuition which is independent of acquired\r\nskill, and is at the root of all genius; which, to take cases analogous\r\nto his own, is the last quality of the perfect guide or scout. I\r\nbelieve he could _smell_ sand where he could not see or touch it.\r\n\r\nAs for me, the sea has never been my element, and never will be;\r\nnevertheless, I hardened to the life, grew salt, tough, and tolerably\r\nalert. As a soldier learns more in a week of war than in years of\r\nparades and pipeclay, so, cut off from all distractions, moving from\r\nbivouac to precarious bivouac, and depending, to some extent, for my\r\nlife on my muscles and wits, I rapidly learnt my work and gained a\r\ncertain dexterity. I knew my ropes in the dark, could beat economically\r\nto windward through squalls, take bearings, and estimate the\r\ninteraction of wind and tide.\r\n\r\nWe were generally in solitude, but occasionally we met galliots like\r\nthe _Johannes_ tacking through the sands, and once or twice we found a\r\nfleet of such boats anchored in a gut, waiting for water. Their\r\ndraught, loaded, was from six to seven feet, our own only four, without\r\nour centre-plate, but we took their mean draught as the standard of all\r\nour observations. That is, we set ourselves to ascertain when and how a\r\nvessel drawing six and a half feet could navigate the sands.\r\n\r\nA word more as to our motive. It was Davies\xe2\x80\x99s conviction, as I have\r\nsaid, that the whole region would in war be an ideal hunting-ground for\r\nsmall free-lance marauders, and I began to know he was right; for look\r\nat the three sea-roads through the sands to Hamburg, Bremen,\r\nWilhelmshaven, and the heart of commercial Germany. They are like\r\nhighways piercing a mountainous district by defiles, where a handful of\r\ndesperate men can arrest an army.\r\n\r\nFollow the parallel of a war on land. People your mountains with a\r\ndaring and resourceful race, who possess an intimate knowledge of every\r\ntrack and bridle-path, who operate in small bands, travel light, and\r\nmove rapidly. See what an immense advantage such guerillas possess over\r\nan enemy which clings to beaten tracks, moves in large bodies, slowly,\r\nand does not \xe2\x80\x9cknow the country\xe2\x80\x9d. See how they can not only inflict\r\ndisasters on a foe who vastly overmatches them in strength, but can\r\nprolong a semi-passive resistance long after all decisive battles have\r\nbeen fought. See, too, how the strong invader can only conquer his\r\nelusive antagonists by learning their methods, studying the country,\r\nand matching them in mobility and cunning. The parallel must not be\r\npressed too far; but that this sort of warfare will have its\r\ncounterpart on the sea is a truth which cannot be questioned.\r\n\r\nDavies in his enthusiasm set no limits to its importance. The small\r\nboat in shallow waters played a mighty _r\xc3\xb4le_ in his vision of a naval\r\nwar, a part that would grow in importance as the war developed and\r\nreach its height in the final stages.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe heavy battle fleets are all very well,\xe2\x80\x9d he used to say, \xe2\x80\x9cbut if\r\nthe sides are well matched there might be nothing left of them after a\r\nfew months of war. They might destroy one another mutually, leaving as\r\nnominal conqueror an admiral with scarcely a battleship to bless\r\nhimself with. It\xe2\x80\x99s then that the true struggle will set in; and it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nthen that anything that will float will be pressed into the service,\r\nand anybody who can steer a boat, knows his waters, and doesn\xe2\x80\x99t care\r\nthe toss of a coin for his life, will have magnificent opportunities.\r\nIt cuts both ways. What small boats can do in these waters is plain\r\nenough; but take our own case. Say we\xe2\x80\x99re beaten on the high seas by a\r\ncoalition. There\xe2\x80\x99s then a risk of starvation or invasion. It\xe2\x80\x99s all rot\r\nwhat they talk about instant surrender. We can live on half rations,\r\nrecuperate, and build; but we must have time. Meanwhile our coast and\r\nports are in danger, for the millions we sink in forts and mines won\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ncarry us far. They\xe2\x80\x99re fixed\xe2\x80\x94pure passive defence. What you want is\r\n_boats_\xe2\x80\x94mosquitoes with stings\xe2\x80\x94swarms of them\xe2\x80\x94patrol-boats,\r\nscout-boats, torpedo-boats; intelligent irregulars manned by local men,\r\nwith a pretty free hand to play their own game. And what a splendid\r\ngame to play! There are places very like this over there\xe2\x80\x94nothing half\r\nso good, but similar\xe2\x80\x94the Mersey estuary, the Dee, the Severn, the Wash,\r\nand, best of all, the Thames, with all the Kent, Essex, and Suffolk\r\nbanks round it. But as for defending our coasts in the way I mean\xe2\x80\x94we\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\nnothing ready\xe2\x80\x94nothing whatsoever! We don\xe2\x80\x99t even build or use small\r\ntorpedo-boats. These fast \xe2\x80\x98destroyers\xe2\x80\x99 are no good for _this_ work\xe2\x80\x94too\r\nlong and unmanageable, and most of them too deep. What you want is\r\nsomething strong and simple, of light draught, and with only a\r\nspar-torpedo, if it came to that. Tugs, launches, small yachts\xe2\x80\x94anything\r\nwould do at a pinch, for success would depend on intelligence, not on\r\nbrute force or complicated mechanism. They\xe2\x80\x99d get wiped out often, but\r\nwhat matter? There\xe2\x80\x99d be no lack of the right sort of men for them if\r\nthe thing was _organised._ But where are the men?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOr, suppose we have the best of it on the high seas, and have to\r\nattack or blockade a coast like this, which is sand from end to end.\r\nYou can\xe2\x80\x99t improvise people who are at home in such waters. The navy\r\nchaps don\xe2\x80\x99t learn it, though, by Jove! they\xe2\x80\x99re the most magnificent\r\nservice in the world\xe2\x80\x94in pluck, and nerve, and everything else. They\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\n_try_ anything, and often do the impossible. But their boats are deep,\r\nand they get little practice in this sort of thing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDavies never pushed home his argument here; but I know that it was the\r\npassionate wish of his heart, somehow and somewhere, to get a chance of\r\nturning his knowledge of this coast to practical account in the war\r\nthat he felt was bound to come, to play that \xe2\x80\x9csplendid game\xe2\x80\x9d in this,\r\nthe most fascinating field for it.\r\n\r\nI can do no more than sketch his views. Hearing them as I did, with the\r\nvery splash of the surf and the bubble of the tides in my ears, they\r\nmade a profound impression on me, and gave me the very zeal for our\r\nwork he, by temperament, possessed.\r\n\r\nBut as the days passed and nothing occurred to disturb us, I felt more\r\nand more strongly that, as regards our quest, we were on the wrong\r\ntack. We found nothing suspicious, nothing that suggested a really\r\nadequate motive for Dollmann\xe2\x80\x99s treachery. I became impatient, and was\r\nfor pushing on more quickly westward. Davies still clung to his theory,\r\nbut the same feeling influenced him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s something to do with these channels in the sand,\xe2\x80\x9d he persisted,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cbut I\xe2\x80\x99m afraid, as you say, we haven\xe2\x80\x99t got at the heart of the\r\nmystery. Nobody seems to care a rap what we do. We haven\xe2\x80\x99t done the\r\nestuaries as well as I should like, but we\xe2\x80\x99d better push on to the\r\nislands. It\xe2\x80\x99s exactly the same sort of work, and just as important, I\r\nbelieve. We\xe2\x80\x99re bound to get a clue soon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was also the question of time, for me at least. I was due to be\r\nback in London, unless I obtained an extension, on the 28th, and our\r\npresent rate of progress was slow. But I cannot conscientiously say\r\nthat I made a serious point of this. If there was any value in our\r\nenterprise at all, official duty pales beside it. The machinery of\r\nState would not suffer from my absence; excuses would have to be made,\r\nand the results braved.\r\n\r\nAll the time our sturdy little craft grew shabbier and more\r\nweather-worn, the varnish thinner, the decks greyer, the sails dingier,\r\nand the cabin roof more murky where stove-fumes stained it. But the\r\nonly beauty she ever possessed, that of perfect fitness for her\r\nfunctions, remained. With nothing to compare her to she became a home\r\nto me. My joints adapted themselves to her crabbed limits, my tastes\r\nand habits to her plain domestic economy.\r\n\r\nBut oil and water were running low, and the time had come for us to be\r\nforced to land and renew our stock.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XIV.\r\nThe First Night in the Islands\r\n\r\n\r\nA low line of sandhills, pink and fawn in the setting sun, at one end\r\nof them a little white village huddled round the base of a massive\r\nfour-square lighthouse\xe2\x80\x94such was Wangeroog, the easternmost of the\r\nFrisian Islands, as I saw it on the evening of October 15. We had\r\ndecided to make it our first landing-place; and since it possesses no\r\nharbour, and is hedged by a mile of sand at low water, we had run in on\r\nthe rising tide till the yacht grounded, in order to save ourselves as\r\nmuch labour as possible in the carriage to and fro of the heavy\r\nwater-breakers and oil-cans which we had to replenish. In faint outline\r\nthree miles to the south of us was the flat plain of Friesland, broken\r\nonly by some trees, a windmill or two, and a church spire. Between, the\r\nshallow expanse of sea was already beginning to shrink away into\r\nlagoons, chief among which was the narrow passage by which we had\r\napproached from the east. This continued its course west, directly\r\nparallel to the island, and in it, at a distance of half a mile from\r\nus, three galliots lay at anchor.\r\n\r\nBefore supper was over the yacht was high and dry, and when we had\r\neaten, Davies loaded himself with cans and breakers. I was for taking\r\nmy share, but he induced me to stay aboard; for I was dead tired after\r\nan unusually long and trying day, which had begun at 2 a.m., when,\r\nusing a precious instalment of east wind, we had started on a complete\r\npassage of the sands from the Elbe to the Jade. It was a barely\r\npossible feat for a boat of our low speed to perform in only two tides;\r\nand though we just succeeded, it was only by dint of tireless vigilance\r\nand severe physical strain.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLay out the anchor when you\xe2\x80\x99ve had a smoke,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, \xe2\x80\x9cand keep an\r\neye on the riding-light; it\xe2\x80\x99s my only guide back.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe lowered himself, and I heard the scrunch of his sea-boots as he\r\ndisappeared in the darkness. It was a fine starry night, with a touch\r\nof frost in the air. I lit a cigar, and stretched myself on a sofa\r\nclose to the glow of the stove. The cigar soon languished and dropped,\r\nand I dozed uneasily, for the riding-light was on my mind. I got up\r\nonce and squinted at it through the half-raised skylight, saw it\r\nburning steadily, and lay down again. The cabin lamp wanted oil and was\r\ndying down to a red-hot wick, but I was too drowsy to attend to it, and\r\nit went out. I lit my cigar stump again, and tried to keep awake by\r\nthinking. It was the first time I and Davies had been separated for so\r\nlong; yet so used had we grown to freedom from interference that this\r\nwould not have disturbed me in the least were it not for a sudden\r\npresentiment that on this first night of the second stage of our\r\nlabours something would happen. All at once I heard a sound outside, a\r\nsplashing footstep as of a man stepping in a puddle. I was wide awake\r\nin an instant, but never thought of shouting \xe2\x80\x9cIs that you, Davies?\xe2\x80\x9d for\r\nI knew in a flash that it was not he. It was the slip of a stealthy\r\nman. Presently I heard another footstep\xe2\x80\x94the pad of a boot on the\r\nsand\xe2\x80\x94this time close to my ear, just outside the hull; then some more,\r\nfainter and farther aft. I gently rose and peered aft through the\r\nskylight. A glimmer of light, reflected from below, was wavering over\r\nthe mizzen-mast and bumpkin; it had nothing to do with the\r\nriding-light, which hung on the forestay. My prowler, I understood, had\r\nstruck a match and was reading the name on the stern. How much farther\r\nwould his curiosity carry him? The match went out, and footsteps were\r\naudible again. Then a strong, guttural voice called in German, \xe2\x80\x9cYacht\r\nahoy!\xe2\x80\x9d I kept silence. \xe2\x80\x9cYacht ahoy!\xe2\x80\x9d a little louder this time. A\r\npause, and then a vibration of the hull as boots scraped on it and\r\nhands grasped the gunwale. My visitor was on deck. I bobbed down, sat\r\non the sofa, and I heard him moving along the deck, quickly and\r\nconfidently, first forward to the bows, where he stopped, then back to\r\nthe companion amidships. Inside the cabin it was pitch dark, but I\r\nheard his boots on the ladder, feeling for the steps. In another moment\r\nhe would be in the doorway lighting his second match. Surely it was\r\ndarker than before? There had been a little glow from the riding-lamp\r\nreflected on to the skylight, but it had disappeared. I looked up,\r\nrealised, and made a fool of myself. In a few seconds more I should\r\nhave seen my visitor face to face, perhaps had an interview: but I was\r\nnew to this sort of work and lost my head. All I thought of was\r\nDavies\xe2\x80\x99s last words, and saw him astray on the sands, with no light to\r\nguide him back, the tide rising, and a heavy load. I started up\r\ninvoluntarily, bumped against the table, and set the stove jingling. A\r\nlong step and a grab at the ladder, but just too late! I grasped\r\nsomething damp and greasy, there was tugging and hard breathing, and I\r\nwas left clasping a big sea-boot, whose owner I heard jump on to the\r\nsand and run. I scrambled out, vaulted overboard, and followed blindly\r\nby the sound. He had doubled round the bows of the yacht, and I did the\r\nsame, ducked under the bowsprit, forgetting the bobstay, and fell\r\nviolently on my head, with all the wind knocked out of me by a wire\r\nrope and block whose strength and bulk was one of the glories of the\r\n_Dulcibella_. I struggled on as soon as I got some breath, but my\r\ninvisible quarry was far ahead. I pulled off my heavy boots, carried\r\nthem, and ran in my stockings, promptly cutting my foot on some\r\ncockle-shells. Pursuit was hopeless, and a final stumble over a bit of\r\ndriftwood sent me sprawling with agony in my toes.\r\n\r\nLimping back, I decided that I had made a very poor beginning as an\r\nactive adventurer. I had gained nothing, and lost a great deal of\r\nbreath and skin, and did not even know for certain where I was. The\r\nyacht\xe2\x80\x99s light was extinguished, and, even with Wangeroog Lighthouse to\r\nguide me, I found it no easy matter to find her. She had no anchor out,\r\nif the tide rose. And how was Davies to find her? After much feeble\r\ncircling I took to lying flat at intervals in the hopes of seeing her\r\nsilhouetted against the starry sky. This plan succeeded at last, and\r\nwith relief and humility I boarded her, relit the riding-light, and\r\ncarried off the kedge anchor. The strange boot lay at the foot of the\r\nladder, but it told no tales when I examined it. It was eleven o\xe2\x80\x99clock,\r\npast low water. Davies was cutting it fine if he was to get aboard\r\nwithout the dinghy\xe2\x80\x99s help. But eventually he reappeared in the most\r\nprosaic way, exhausted with his heavy load, but full of talk about his\r\nvisit ashore. He began while we were still on deck.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook here, we ought to have settled more about what we\xe2\x80\x99re to say when\r\nwe\xe2\x80\x99re asked questions. I chose a quiet-looking shop, but it turned out\r\nto be a sort of inn, where they were drinking pink gin\xe2\x80\x94all very\r\nfriendly, as usual, and I found myself under a fire of questions. I\r\nsaid we were on our way back to England. There was the usual rot about\r\nthe smallness of the boat, etc. It struck me that we should want some\r\nother pretence for going so slow and stopping to explore, so I had to\r\nbring in the ducks, though goodness knows we don\xe2\x80\x99t want to waste time\r\nover _them._ The subject wasn\xe2\x80\x99t quite a success. They said it was too\r\nearly\xe2\x80\x94jealous, I suppose; but then two fellows spoke up, and asked to\r\nbe taken on to help. Said they would bring their punt; without local\r\nhelp we should do no good. All true enough, no doubt, but what a\r\nnuisance they\xe2\x80\x99d be. I got out of it\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s just as well you did,\xe2\x80\x9d I interposed. \xe2\x80\x9cWe shall never be able to\r\nleave the boat by herself. I believe we\xe2\x80\x99re watched,\xe2\x80\x9d and I related my\r\nexperience.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cH\xe2\x80\x99m! It\xe2\x80\x99s a pity you didn\xe2\x80\x99t see who it was. Confound that bobstay!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n(his tactful way of reflecting on my clumsiness); \xe2\x80\x9cwhich way did he\r\nrun?\xe2\x80\x9d I pointed vaguely into the west. \xe2\x80\x9cNot towards the island? I\r\nwonder if it\xe2\x80\x99s someone off one of those galliots. There are three\r\nanchored in the channel over there; you can see their lights. You\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t hear a boat pulling off?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI explained that I had been a miserable failure as a detective.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve done jolly well, I think,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies. \xe2\x80\x9cIf you had shouted\r\nwhen you first heard him we should know less still. And we\xe2\x80\x99ve got a\r\nboot, which may come in useful. Anchor out all right? Let\xe2\x80\x99s get below.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe smoked and talked till the new flood, lapping softly round the\r\n_Dulcibella_, raised her without a jar.\r\n\r\nOf course, I argued, there might be nothing in it. The visitor might\r\nhave been a commonplace thief; an apparently deserted yacht was a\r\ntempting bait. Davies scouted this possibility from the first.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99re not like that in Germany,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cIn Holland, if you like,\r\nthey\xe2\x80\x99ll do anything. And I don\xe2\x80\x99t like that turning out of the lantern\r\nto gain time, if we were away.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNor did I. In spite of my blundering in details, I welcomed the\r\nincident as the first concrete proof that the object of our quest was\r\nno mare\xe2\x80\x99s nest. The next point was what was the visitor\xe2\x80\x99s object? If to\r\nsearch, what would he have found?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe charts, of course, with all our corrections and notes, and the\r\nlog. They\xe2\x80\x99d give us away,\xe2\x80\x9d was Davies\xe2\x80\x99s instant conclusion. Not having\r\nhis faith in the channel theory, I was lukewarm about his precious\r\ncharts.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAfter all, we\xe2\x80\x99re doing nothing wrong, as you\xe2\x80\x99ve often said yourself,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nI said.\r\n\r\nStill, as a true index to our mode of life they were the only things on\r\nboard that could possibly compromise us or suggest that we were\r\nanything more than eccentric young Englishmen cruising for sport\r\n(witness the duck-guns) and pleasure. We had two sets of charts, German\r\nand English. The former we decided to use in practice, and to hide,\r\ntogether with the log, if occasion demanded. My diary, I resolved,\r\nshould never leave my person. Then there were the naval books. Davies\r\nscanned them with a look I knew well.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere are too many of them,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, in the tone of a cook fixing the\r\nfate of superfluous kittens. \xe2\x80\x9cLet\xe2\x80\x99s throw them overboard. They\xe2\x80\x99re very\r\nold anyhow, and I know them by heart.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, not here!\xe2\x80\x9d I protested, for he was laying greedy hands on the\r\nshelf; \xe2\x80\x9cthey\xe2\x80\x99ll be found at low water. In fact, I should leave them as\r\nthey are. You had them when you were here before, and Dollmann knows\r\nyou had them. If you return without them, it will look queer.\xe2\x80\x9d They\r\nwere spared.\r\n\r\nThe English charts, being relatively useless, though more suitable to\r\nour _r\xc3\xb4le_ as English yachtsmen, were to be left in evidence, as\r\nshining proofs of our innocence. It was all delightfully casual, I\r\ncould not help thinking. A seven-ton yacht does not abound in (dry)\r\nhiding-places, and we were helpless against a drastic search. If there\r\n_were_ secrets on this coast to guard, and we were suspected as spies,\r\nthere was nothing to prevent an official visit and warning. There need\r\nbe no prowlers scuttling off when alarmed, unless indeed it was thought\r\nwisest to let well alone, if we _were_ harmless, and not to arouse\r\nsuspicions where there were none. Here we lost ourselves in conjecture.\r\nWhose agent was the prowler? If Dollmann\xe2\x80\x99s, did Dollmann know now that\r\nthe _Dulcibella_ was safe, and back in the region he had expelled her\r\nfrom? If so, was he likely to return to the policy of violence? We\r\nfound ourselves both glancing at the duck-guns strung up under the\r\nracks, and then we both laughed and looked foolish. \xe2\x80\x9cA war of wits, and\r\nnot of duck-guns,\xe2\x80\x9d I opined. \xe2\x80\x9cLet\xe2\x80\x99s look at the chart.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nIllustration: Map B of East Friesland. Map B\r\n\r\n\r\nThe reader is already familiar with the general aspect of this singular\r\nregion, and I need only remind him that the mainland is that district\r\nof Prussia which is known as East Friesland. It is a _[See Map B]_\r\nshort, flat-topped peninsula, bounded on the west by the Ems estuary\r\nand beyond that by Holland, and on the east by the Jade estuary; a\r\nlow-lying country, containing great tracts of marsh and heath, and few\r\ntowns of any size; on the north side none. Seven islands lie off the\r\ncoast. All, except Borkum, which is round, are attenuated strips,\r\nslightly crescent-shaped, rarely more than a mile broad, and tapering\r\nat the ends; in length averaging about six miles, from Norderney and\r\nJuist, which are seven and nine respectively, to little Baltrum, which\r\nis only two and a half.\r\n\r\nOf the shoal spaces which lie between them and the mainland, two-thirds\r\ndry at low-water, and the remaining third becomes a system of lagoons\r\nwhose distribution is controlled by the natural drift of the North Sea\r\nas it forces its way through the intervals between the islands. Each of\r\nthese intervals resembles the bar of a river, and is obstructed by\r\ndangerous banks, over which the sea pours at every tide scooping out a\r\ndeep pool. This fans out and ramifies to east and west as the pent-up\r\ncurrent frees itself, encircles the islands, and spreads over the\r\nintervening flats. But the farther it penetrates the less coursing\r\nforce it has, and as a result no island is girt completely by a\r\nlow-water channel. About midway at the back of each of them is a\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cwatershed\xe2\x80\x9d, only covered for five or six hours out of the twelve. A\r\nboat, even of the lightest draught, navigating behind the islands must\r\nchoose its moment for passing these. As to navigability, the _North Sea\r\nPilot_ sums up the matter in these dry terms: \xe2\x80\x9cThe channels dividing\r\nthese islands from each other and the shore afford to the small craft\r\nof the country the means of communication between the Ems and the Jade,\r\nto which description of vessels only they are available.\xe2\x80\x9d The islands\r\nare dismissed with a brief note or two about beacons and lights.\r\n\r\nThe more I looked at the chart the more puzzled I became. The islands\r\nwere evidently mere sandbanks, with a cluster of houses and a church on\r\neach, the only hint of animation in their desolate _ensemble_ being the\r\noccasional word \xe2\x80\x9cBade-strand\xe2\x80\x9d, suggesting that they were visited in the\r\nsummer months by a handful of townsfolk for the sea-bathing. Norderney,\r\nof course, was conspicuous in this respect; but even its town, which I\r\nknow by repute as a gay and fashionable watering-place, would be dead\r\nand empty for some months in the year, and could have no commercial\r\nimportance. No man could do anything on the mainland coast\xe2\x80\x94a monotonous\r\nline of dyke punctuated at intervals by an infinitesimal village.\r\nGlancing idly at the names of these villages, I noticed that they most\r\nof them ended in siel\xe2\x80\x94a repulsive termination, that seemed appropriate\r\nto the whole region. There were Carolinensiel, Bensersiel, etc. Siel\r\nmeans either a sewer or a sluice, the latter probably in this case, for\r\nI noticed that each village stood at the outlet of a little stream\r\nwhich evidently carried off the drainage of the lowlands behind. A\r\nsluice, or lock, would be necessary at the mouth, for at high tide the\r\nland is below the level of the sea. Looking next at the sands outside,\r\nI noticed that across them and towards each outlet a line of booms was\r\nmarked, showing that there was some sort of tidal approach to the\r\nvillage, evidently formed by the scour of the little stream.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre we going to explore those?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked Davies.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t see the use,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered; \xe2\x80\x9cthey only lead to those potty\r\nlittle places. I suppose local galliots use them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow about your torpedo-boats and patrol-boats?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey _might,_ at certain tides. But I can\xe2\x80\x99t see what value they\xe2\x80\x99d be,\r\nunless as a refuge for a German boat in the last resort. They lead to\r\nno harbours. Wait! There\xe2\x80\x99s a little notch in the dyke at\r\nNeuharlingersiel and Dornumersiel, which may mean some sort of a quay\r\narrangement, but what\xe2\x80\x99s the use of that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe may as well visit one or two, I suppose?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose so; but we don\xe2\x80\x99t want to be playing round villages. There\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nheaps of really important work to do, farther out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, what _do_ you make of this coast?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDavies had nothing but the same old theory, but he urged it with a\r\nforce and keenness that impressed me more deeply than ever.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook at those islands!\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99re clearly the old line of\r\ncoast, hammered into breaches by the sea. The space behind them is like\r\nan immense tidal harbour, thirty miles by five, and they screen it\r\nimpenetrably. It\xe2\x80\x99s absolutely _made_ for shallow war-boats under\r\nskilled pilotage. They can nip in and out of the gaps, and dodge about\r\nfrom end to end. On one side is the Ems, on the other the big\r\nestuaries. It\xe2\x80\x99s a perfect base for torpedo-craft.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI agreed (and agree still), but still I shrugged my shoulders.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe go on exploring, then, in the same way?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; keeping a sharp look-out, though. Remember, we shall always be in\r\nsight of land now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the glass doing?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHigher than for a long time. I hope it won\xe2\x80\x99t bring fog. I know this\r\ndistrict is famous for fogs, and fine weather at this time of the year\r\nis bad for them anywhere. I would rather it blew, if it wasn\xe2\x80\x99t for\r\nexploring those gaps, where an on-shore wind would be nasty. Six-thirty\r\nto-morrow; not later. I think I\xe2\x80\x99ll sleep in the saloon for the future,\r\nafter what happened to-night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XV.\r\nBensersiel\r\n\r\n\r\n[For this chapter see Map B.]\r\n\r\nThe decisive incidents of our cruise were now fast approaching. Looking\r\nback on the steps that led to them, and anxious that the reader should\r\nbe wholly with us in our point of view, I think I cannot do better than\r\ngive extracts from my diary of the next three days:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Oct._ 16 (up at 6.30, yacht high and dry). Of the three galliots out\r\nat anchor in the channel yesterday, only one is left.... I took my turn\r\nwith the breakers this morning and walked to Wangeroog, whose village I\r\nfound half lost in sand drifts, which are planted with tufts of\r\nmarram-grass in mathematical rows, to give stability and prevent a\r\ncatastrophe like that at Pompeii. A friendly grocer told me all there\r\nis to know, which is little. The islands are what we thought\r\nthem\xe2\x80\x94barren for the most part, with a small fishing population, and a\r\nscanty accession of summer visitors for bathing. The season is over\r\nnow, and business slack for him. There is still, however, a little\r\ntrade with the mainland in galliots and lighters, a few of which come\r\nfrom the \xe2\x80\x98siels\xe2\x80\x99 on the mainland. \xe2\x80\x98Had these harbours?\xe2\x80\x99 I asked.\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Mud-holes!\xe2\x80\x99 he replied, with a contemptuous laugh. (He is a settler in\r\nthese wilds, not a native.) Said he had heard of schemes for improving\r\nthem, so as to develop the islands as health-resorts, but thought it\r\nwas only a wild speculation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA heavy tramp back to the yacht, nearly crushed by impedimenta. While\r\nDavies made yet another trip, I stalked some birds with a gun, and\r\nobtained what resembled a specimen of the smallest variety of\r\njack-snipe, and small at that; but I made a great noise, which I hope\r\npersuaded somebody of the purity of our motives.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe weighed anchor at one o\xe2\x80\x99clock, and in passing the anchored galliot\r\ntook a good look at her. _Kormoran_ was on her stern; otherwise she was\r\njust like a hundred others. Nobody was on deck.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe spent the whole afternoon till dark exploring the Harle, or gap\r\nbetween Wangeroog and Spiekeroog; the sea breaking heavily on the banks\r\noutside.... Fine as the day was, the scene from the offing was desolate\r\nto the last degree. The naked spots of the two islands are hideous in\r\ntheir sterility: melancholy bits of wreck-wood their only relief, save\r\nfor one or two grotesque beacons, and, most _bizarre_ of all, a great\r\nchurch-tower, standing actually _in_ the water, on the north side of\r\nWangeroog, a striking witness to the encroachment of the sea. On the\r\nmainland, which was barely visible, there was one very prominent\r\nlandmark, a spire, which from the chart we took to be that of _Esens,_\r\na town four miles inland.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe days are growing short. Sunset is soon after five, and an hour\r\nlater it is too dark to see booms and buoys distinctly. The tides also\r\nare awkward just now.[1] High-water at morning and evening is between\r\nfive and six\xe2\x80\x94just at twilight. For the night, we groped with the lead\r\ninto the Muschel Balge, the tributary channel which laps round the\r\ninside of Spiekeroog, and lay in two fathoms, clear of the outer swell,\r\nbut rolling a little when the ebb set in strong against the wind.\r\n\r\n [1] I exclude all the technicalities that I can, but the reader should\r\n take note that the tide-table is very important henceforward.\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA galliot passed us, going west, just as we were stowing sails; too\r\ndark to see her name. Later, we saw her anchor-light higher up our\r\nchannel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe great event of the day has been the sighting of a small German\r\ngunboat, steaming slowly west along the coast. That was about half-past\r\nfour, when we were sounding along the Harle.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDavies identified her at once as the _Blitz_, Commander von Br\xc3\xbcning\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ngunboat. We wondered if he recognised the _Dulcibella_, but, anyway,\r\nshe seemed to take no notice of us and steamed slowly on. We quite\r\nexpected to fall in with her when we came to the islands, but the\r\nactual sight of her has excited us a good deal. She is an ugly, cranky\r\nlittle vessel, painted grey, with one funnel. Davis is contemptuous\r\nabout her low freeboard forward; says he would rather go to sea in the\r\n_Dulce_. He has her dimensions and armament (learnt from Brassey) at\r\nhis fingers\xe2\x80\x99 ends: one hundred and forty feet by twenty-five, one 4.9\r\ngun, one 3.4, and four maxims\xe2\x80\x94an old type. Just going to bed; a\r\nbitterly cold night.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Oct._ 17.\xe2\x80\x94Glass falling heavily this morning, to our great disgust.\r\nWind back in the SW and much warmer. Starting at 5.30 we tacked on the\r\ntide over the \xe2\x80\x98watershed\xe2\x80\x99 behind Spiekeroog. So did the galliot we had\r\nseen last night, but we again missed identifying her, as she weighed\r\nanchor before we came up to her berth. Davies, however, swore she was\r\nthe _Kormoran_. We lost sight of her altogether for the greater part of\r\nthe day, which we spent in exploring the Otzumer Ee (the gap between\r\nLangeoog and Spiekeroog), now and then firing some perfunctory shots at\r\nseals and sea-birds . . . (nautical details omitted). . . . In the\r\nevening we were hurrying back to an inside anchorage, when we made a\r\nbad mistake; did, in fact, what we had never done before, ran aground\r\non the very top of high water, and are now sitting hard and fast on the\r\nedge of the Rute Flat, south of the east spit of Langeoog. The light\r\nwas bad, and a misplaced boom tricked us; kedging-off failed, and at 8\r\np.m. we were left on a perfect Ararat of sand, and only a yard or two\r\nfrom that accursed boom, which is perched on the very summit, as a lure\r\nto the unwary. It is going to blow hard too, though that is no great\r\nmatter, as we are sheltered by banks on the sou\xe2\x80\x99-west and nor\xe2\x80\x99-west\r\nsides, the likely quarters. We hope to float at 6.15 to-morrow morning,\r\nbut to make sure of being able to get her off, we have been\r\ntransferring some ballast to the dinghy, by way of lightening the\r\nyacht\xe2\x80\x94a horrid business handling the pigs of lead, heavy, greasy, and\r\nblack. The saloon is an inferno, the deck like a collier\xe2\x80\x99s, and\r\nourselves like sweeps.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe anchors are laid out, and there is nothing more to be done.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Oct._ 18\xe2\x80\x94Half a gale from the sou\xe2\x80\x99-west when we turned out, but it\r\nhelped us to float off safely at six. The dinghy was very nearly\r\nswamped with the weight of lead in it, and getting the ballast back\r\ninto the yacht was the toughest job of all. We got the dinghy\r\nalongside, and Davies jumped in (nearly sinking it for good), balanced\r\nhimself, fended off, and, whenever he got a chance, attached the pigs\r\none by one on to a bight of rope, secured to the peak halyards, on\r\nwhich I hoisted from the deck. It was touch and go for a few minutes,\r\nand then easier.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was nine before we had finished replacing the pigs in the hold, a\r\nfilthy but delicate operation, as they fit like a puzzle, and if one is\r\nout of place the floor-boards won\xe2\x80\x99t shut down. Coming on deck after it,\r\nwe saw to our surprise the _Blitz_, lying at anchor in the Schill\r\nBalje, inside Spiekeroog, about a mile and a half off. She must have\r\nentered the Otzumer Ee at high-water for shelter from the gale; a neat\r\nbit of work for a vessel of her size, as Davies says she draws\r\nnine-foot-ten, and there can\xe2\x80\x99t be more than twelve on the bar at\r\nhigh-water neaps. Several smacks had run in too, and there were two\r\ngalliots farther up our channel, but we couldn\xe2\x80\x99t make out if the\r\n_Kormoran_ was one.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen the banks uncovered we lay more quietly, so landed and took a\r\nlong, tempestuous walk over the Rute, with compass and notebooks.\r\nReturning at two, we found the glass tumbling down almost visibly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suggested running for Bensersiel, one of the mainland villages\r\nsouth-west of us, on the evening flood, as it seemed just the right\r\nopportunity, if we were to visit one of those \xe2\x80\x98siels\xe2\x80\x99 at all. Davies\r\nwas very lukewarm, but events overcame him. At 3.30 a black, ragged\r\ncloud, appearing to trail into the very sea, brought up a terrific\r\nsquall. This passed, and there was a deathly pause of ten minutes while\r\nthe whole sky eddied as with smoke-wreaths. Then an icy puff struck us\r\nfrom the north-west, rapidly veering till it reached north-east; there\r\nit settled and grew harder every moment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Sou\xe2\x80\x99-west to north-east\xe2\x80\x94only the worst sort do that,\xe2\x80\x99 said Davies.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe shift to the east changed the whole situation (as shifts often\r\nhave before), making the Rute Flats a lee shore, while to windward lay\r\nthe deep lagoons of the Otzumer Ee, bounded indeed by Spiekeroog, but\r\nstill offering a big drift for wind and sea. We had to clear out sharp,\r\nto set the mizzen. It was out of the question to beat to windward, for\r\nit was blowing a hurricane in a few minutes. We must go to leeward, and\r\nDavies was for running farther in well behind the Jans sand, and not\r\nrisking Bensersiel. A blunder of mine, when I went to the winch to get\r\nup anchor, settled the question. Thirty out of our forty fathoms of\r\nchain were out. Confused by the motion and a blinding sleet-shower that\r\nhad come on, and forgetting the tremendous strain on the cable, I cast\r\nthe slack off the bitts and left it loose. There was then only one turn\r\nof the chain round the drum, enough in ordinary weather to prevent it\r\nrunning out. But now my first heave on the winch-lever started it\r\nslipping, and in an instant it was whizzing out of the hawse-pipe and\r\noverboard. I tried to stop it with my foot, stumbled at a heavy plunge\r\nof the yacht, heard something snap below, and saw the last of it\r\ndisappear. The yacht fell off the wind, and drifted astern. I shouted,\r\nand had the sense to hoist the reefed foresail at once. Davies had her\r\nin hand in no time, and was steering south-west. Going aft I found him\r\ncool and characteristic.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Doesn\xe2\x80\x99t matter,\xe2\x80\x99 he said; \xe2\x80\x98anchor\xe2\x80\x99s buoyed. (Ever since leaving the\r\nElbe we had had a buoy-line on our anchor against the emergency of\r\nhaving to slip our cable and run. For the same reason the end of the\r\nchain was not made permanently fast below.) We\xe2\x80\x99ll come back to-morrow\r\nand get it. Can\xe2\x80\x99t now. Should have had to slip it anyhow; wind and sea\r\ntoo strong. We\xe2\x80\x99ll try for Bensersiel. Can\xe2\x80\x99t trust to a warp and kedge\r\nout here.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAn exciting run it was, across country, so to speak, over an unboomed\r\nwatershed; but we had bearings from our morning\xe2\x80\x99s walk. Shoal water all\r\nthe way and a hollow sea breaking everywhere. We soon made out the\r\nBensersiel booms, but even under mizzen and foresail only we travelled\r\ntoo fast, and had to heave to outside them, for the channel looked too\r\nshallow still. We lowered half the centreboard and kept her just\r\nholding her own to windward, through a most trying period. In the end\r\nhad to run for it sooner than we meant, as we were sagging to leeward\r\nin spite of all, and the light was failing. Bore up at 5.15, and raced\r\nup the channel with the booms on our left scarcely visible in the surf\r\nand rising water. Davies stood forward, signalling\xe2\x80\x94port, starboard, or\r\nsteady\xe2\x80\x94with his arms, while I wrestled with the helm, flung from side\r\nto side and flogged by wave-tops. Suddenly found a sort of dyke on our\r\nright just covering with sea. The shore appeared through scud, and men\r\non a quay shouting. Davies brandished his left arm furiously; I ported\r\nhard, and we were in smoother water. A few seconds more and we were\r\nwhizzing through a slit between two wood jetties. Inside a small square\r\nharbour showed, but there was no room to round up properly and no time\r\nto lower sails. Davies just threw the kedge over, and it just got a\r\ngrip in time to check our momentum and save our bowsprit from the\r\nquayside. A man threw us a rope and we brought up alongside, rather\r\nbewildered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot more so than the natives, who seemed to think we had dropped from\r\nthe sky. They were very friendly, with an undercurrent of\r\ndisappointment, having expected salvage work outside, I think.
