All\r\nshowed embarrassing helpfulness in stowing sails, etc. We were rescued\r\nby a fussy person in uniform and spectacles, who swept them aside and\r\nannounced himself as the Custom-house officer (fancy such a thing in\r\nthis absurd mud-hole!), marched down into the cabin, which was in a\r\nfearful mess and wringing wet, and producing ink, pen, and a huge\r\nprinted form, wanted to know our cargo, our crew, our last port, our\r\ndestination, our food, stores, and everything. No cargo (pleasure);\r\ncaptain, Davies; crew, me; last port, Brunsb\xc3\xbcttel; destination,\r\nEngland. What spirits had we? Whisky, produced. What salt? Tin of\r\nCerebos, produced, and a damp deposit in a saucer. What coffee? etc.\r\nLockers searched, guns fingered, bunks rifled. Meanwhile the German\r\ncharts and the log, the damning clues to our purpose, were in full\r\nevidence, crying for notice which they did not get. (We had forgotten\r\nour precautions in the hurry of our start from the Rute.) When the huge\r\nform was as full as he could make it, he suddenly became human,\r\ntalkative, and thirsty; and, when we treated him, patronising. It\r\nseemed to dawn on him that, under our rough clothes and crust of brine\r\nand grime, we were two mad and wealthy aristocrats, worthy _prot\xc3\xa9g\xc3\xa9s_\r\nof a high official. He insisted on our bringing our cushions to dry at\r\nhis house, and to get rid of him we consented, for we were wet, hungry,\r\nand longing to change and wash. He talked himself away at last, and we\r\nhid the log and charts; but he returned, in the postmaster\xe2\x80\x99s uniform\r\nthis time before we had finished supper, and haled us and our cushions\r\nup through dark and mud to his cottage near the quay. To reach it we\r\ncrossed a small bridge spanning what seemed to be a small river with\r\nsluice-gates, just as we had thought.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe showed his prizes to his wife, who was quite flustered by the\r\ndistinguished strangers, and received the cushions with awe; and next\r\nwe were carried off to the Gasthaus and exhibited to the village\r\ncircle, where we talked ducks and weather. (Nobody takes us seriously;\r\nI never felt less like a conspirator.) Our friend, who is a\r\nfeather-headed chatterbox, is enormously important about his ridiculous\r\nlittle port, whose principal customer seems to be the Langeoog\r\npost-boat, a galliot running to and fro according to tide. A few\r\nlighters also come down the stream with bricks and produce from the\r\ninterior, and are towed to the islands. The harbour has from five to\r\nseven feet in it for two hours out of twelve! Herr Schenkel talked us\r\nback to the yacht, which we found resting on the mud\xe2\x80\x94and here we are.\r\nDavies pretends there are harbour smells, and says he won\xe2\x80\x99t be able to\r\nsleep; is already worrying about how to get away from here. Ashore,\r\nthey were saying that it\xe2\x80\x99s impossible, under sail, in strong north-east\r\nwinds, the channel being too narrow to tack in. For my part I find it a\r\nhuge relief to be in any sort of harbour after a fortnight in the open.\r\nThere are no tides or anchors to think about, and no bumping or\r\nrolling. Fresh milk to-morrow!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XVI.\r\nCommander von Br\xc3\xbcning\r\n\r\n\r\nTo resume my story in narrative form.\r\n\r\nI was awakened at ten o\xe2\x80\x99clock on the 19th, after a long and delicious\r\nsleep, by Davies\xe2\x80\x99s voice outside, talking his unmistakable German.\r\nLooking out, in my pyjamas, I saw him on the quay above in conversation\r\nwith a man in a long mackintosh coat and a gold-laced navy cap. He had\r\na close-trimmed auburn beard, a keen, handsome face, and an animated\r\nmanner. It was raining in a raw air.\r\n\r\nThey saw me, and Davies said: \xe2\x80\x9cHullo, Carruthers! Here\xe2\x80\x99s Commander von\r\nBr\xc3\xbcning from the _Blitz_\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x98meiner Freund\xe2\x80\x99 Carruthers.\xe2\x80\x9d (Davies\r\nwas deplorably weak in terminations.)\r\n\r\nThe Commander smiled broadly at me, and I inclined an uncombed head,\r\nwhile, for a moment, the quest was a dream, and I myself felt\r\nunutterably squalid and foolish. I ducked down, heard them parting, and\r\nDavies came aboard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99re to meet him at the inn for a talk at twelve,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\nHis news was that the _Blitz_\xe2\x80\x99s steam-cutter had come in on the morning\r\ntide, and he had met von Br\xc3\xbcning when marketing at the inn. Secondly,\r\nthe _Kormoran_ had also come in, and was moored close by. It was as\r\nclear as possible, therefore, that the latter had watched us, and was\r\nin touch with the _Blitz_, and that both had seized the opportunity of\r\nour being cooped up in Bensersiel to take further stock of us. What had\r\npassed hitherto? Nothing much. Von Br\xc3\xbcning had greeted Davies with\r\ncordial surprise, and said he had wondered yesterday if it was the\r\n_Dulcibella_ that he had seen anchored behind Langeoog. Davies had\r\nexplained that we had left the Baltic and were on our way home; taking\r\nthe shelter of the islands.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSupposing he comes on board and asks to see our log?\xe2\x80\x9d I said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPull it out,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s rot, this hiding, after all, I say. I\r\nrather funk this interview; what are we to say? It\xe2\x80\x99s not in my line.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe resolved abruptly on an important change of plan, replaced the log\r\nand charts in the rack as the first logical step. They contained\r\nnothing but bearings, courses, and the bare data of navigation. To\r\nDavies they were hard-won secrets of vital import, to be lied for,\r\nhowever hard and distasteful lying was. I was cooler as to their value,\r\nbut in any case the same thing was now in both our minds. There would\r\nbe great difficulties in the coming interview if we tried to be too\r\nclever and conceal the fact that we had been exploring. We did not know\r\nhow much von Br\xc3\xbcning knew. When had our surveillance by the _Kormoran_\r\nbegun? Apparently at Wangeroog, but possibly in the estuaries, where we\r\nhad not fired a shot at duck. Perhaps he knew even more\xe2\x80\x94Dollmann\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ntreachery, Davies\xe2\x80\x99s escape, and our subsequent movements\xe2\x80\x94we could not\r\ntell. On the other hand, exploration was known to be a fad of Davies\xe2\x80\x99s,\r\nand in September he had made no secret of it.\r\n\r\nIt was safer to be consistent now. After breakfast we determined to\r\nfind out something about the _Kormoran_, which lay on the mud at the\r\nother side of the harbour, and accordingly addressed ourselves to two\r\nmighty sailors, whose jerseys bore the legend \xe2\x80\x9cPost\xe2\x80\x9d, and who towered\r\nconspicuous among a row of stolid Frisians on the quay, all gazing\r\ngravely down at us as at a curious bit of marine bric-\xc3\xa0-brac. The twins\r\n(for such they proved to be) were most benignant giants, and asked us\r\naboard the post-boat galliot for a chat. It was easy to bring the talk\r\nnaturally round to the point we wished, and we soon gained some most\r\ninteresting information, delivered in the broadest Frisian, but\r\nintelligible enough. They called the _Kormoran_ a Memmert boat, or\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cwreck-works\xe2\x80\x9d boat. It seemed that off the western end of _Juist_, the\r\nisland lying west of Norderney, there lay the bones of a French\r\nwar-vessel, wrecked ages ago. She carried bullion which has never been\r\nrecovered, in spite of many efforts. A salvage company was trying for\r\nit now, and had works on Memmert, an adjacent sandbank. \xe2\x80\x9cThat is Herr\r\nGrimm, the overseer himself,\xe2\x80\x9d they said, pointing to the bridge above\r\nthe sluice-gates. (I call him \xe2\x80\x9cGrimm\xe2\x80\x9d because it describes him\r\nexactly.) A man in a pilot jacket and peaked cap was leaning over the\r\nparapet.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s he doing here?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked.\r\n\r\nThey answered that he was often up and down the coast, work on the\r\nwreck being impossible in rough weather. They supposed he was bringing\r\ncargo in his galliot from Wilhelmshaven, all the company\xe2\x80\x99s plant and\r\nstores coming from that port. He was a local man from Aurich; an ex-tug\r\nskipper.\r\n\r\nWe discussed this information while walking out over the sands to see\r\nthe channel at low water.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you hear anything about this in September?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot a word. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t go to Juist. I would have, probably, if I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nmet Dollmann.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhat in the world did it mean? How did it affect our plans?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook at his boots if we pass him,\xe2\x80\x9d was all Davies had to suggest.\r\n\r\nThe channel was now a ditch, with a trickle in it, running north by\r\neast, roughly, and edged by a dyke of withies for the first quarter of\r\na mile. It was still blowing fresh from the north-east, and we saw that\r\nexit was impossible in such a wind.\r\n\r\nSo back to the village, a paltry, bleak little place. We passed friend\r\nGrimm on the bridge; a dark, clean-shaved, saturnine man, wearing\r\n_shoes._ Approaching the inn:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe haven\xe2\x80\x99t settled quite enough, have we?\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat about\r\nour future plans?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHeaven knows, we haven\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I don\xe2\x80\x99t see how we can. We\r\nmust see how things go. It\xe2\x80\x99s past twelve, and it won\xe2\x80\x99t do to be late.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I leave it to you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right, I\xe2\x80\x99ll do my best. All you\xe2\x80\x99ve got to do is to be yourself and\r\ntell one lie, if need be, about the trick Dollmann played you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe next scene: von Br\xc3\xbcning, Davies, and I, sitting over coffee and\r\nK\xc3\xbcmmel at a table in a dingy inn-parlour overlooking the harbour and\r\nthe sea, Davies with a full box of matches on the table before him. The\r\nCommander gave us a hearty welcome, and I am bound to say I liked him\r\nat once, as Davies had done; but I feared him, too, for he had honest\r\neyes, but abominably clever ones.\r\n\r\nI had impressed on Davies to talk and question as freely and naturally\r\nas though nothing uncommon had happened since he last saw von Br\xc3\xbcning\r\non the deck of the _Medusa_. He must ask about Dollmann\xe2\x80\x94the mutual\r\nfriend\xe2\x80\x94at the outset, and, if questioned about that voyage in his\r\ncompany to the Elbe, must lie like a trooper as to the danger he had\r\nbeen in. This was the one clear and essential necessity, where much was\r\ndifficult. Davies did his duty with precipitation, and blushed when he\r\nput his question, in a way that horrified me, till I remembered that\r\nhis embarrassment was due, and would be ascribed, to another cause.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHerr Dollmann is away still, I think,\xe2\x80\x9d said von Br\xc3\xbcning. (So Davies\r\nhad been right at Brunsb\xc3\xbcttel.) \xe2\x80\x9cWere you thinking of looking him up\r\nagain?\xe2\x80\x9d he added.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, shortly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I\xe2\x80\x99m sure he\xe2\x80\x99s away. But his yacht is back, I believe\xe2\x80\x94and\r\nFr\xc3\xa4ulein Dollmann, I suppose.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cH\xe2\x80\x99m!\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies; \xe2\x80\x9cshe\xe2\x80\x99s a very fine boat that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOur host smiled, gazing thoughtfully at Davies, who was miserable. I\r\nsaw a chance, and took it mercilessly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe can call on Fr\xc3\xa4ulein Dollmann, at least, Davies,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, with a\r\nmeaning smile at von Br\xc3\xbcning.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cH\xe2\x80\x99m!\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies; \xe2\x80\x9cwill he be back soon, do you think?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Commander had begun to light a cigar, and took his time in\r\nanswering. \xe2\x80\x9cProbably,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, after some puffing, \xe2\x80\x9che\xe2\x80\x99s never away\r\nvery long. But you\xe2\x80\x99ve seen them later than I have. Didn\xe2\x80\x99t you sail to\r\nthe Elbe together the day after I saw you last?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, part of the way,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, with great negligence. \xe2\x80\x9cI haven\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nseen him since. He got there first; outsailed me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGave you the slip, in fact?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course he beat me; I was close-reefed. Besides\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I remember; there was a heavy blow\xe2\x80\x94a devil of a heavy blow. I\r\nthought of you that day. How did you manage?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, it was a fair wind; it wasn\xe2\x80\x99t far, you see.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGrosse Gott! In _that_.\xe2\x80\x9d He nodded towards the window whence the\r\n_Dulcibella_\xe2\x80\x99s taper mast could be seen pointing demurely heavenwards.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s a splendid sea-boat,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, indignantly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA thousand pardons!\xe2\x80\x9d said von Br\xc3\xbcning, laughing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t shake my faith in her,\xe2\x80\x9d I put in. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve got to get to England in\r\nher.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHeaven forbid; I was only thinking that there must have been some sea\r\nround the Scharhorn that day; a tame affair, no doubt, Herr Davies?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cScharhorn?\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, who did not catch the idiom in the latter\r\nsentence. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, we didn\xe2\x80\x99t go that way. We cut through the sands\xe2\x80\x94by the\r\nTelte.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Telte! In a north-west gale!\xe2\x80\x9d The Commander started, ceased to\r\nsmile, and only stared. (It was genuine surprise; I could swear it. He\r\nhad heard nothing of this before.)\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHerr Dollmann knew the way,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, doggedly. \xe2\x80\x9cHe kindly offered\r\nto pilot me through, and I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have gone otherwise.\xe2\x80\x9d There was an\r\nawkward little pause.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe led you well, it seems?\xe2\x80\x9d said von Br\xc3\xbcning.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; there\xe2\x80\x99s a nasty surf there, though, isn\xe2\x80\x99t there? But it saves six\r\nmiles\xe2\x80\x94and the Scharhorn. Not that I saved distance. I was fool enough\r\nto run aground.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d said the other, with interest.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt didn\xe2\x80\x99t matter, because I was well inside then. Those sands are\r\ndifficult at high water. We\xe2\x80\x99ve come back that way, you know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n(\xe2\x80\x9cAnd we run aground every day,\xe2\x80\x9d I remarked, with resignation.)\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs that where the _Medusa_ gave you the slip?\xe2\x80\x9d asked von Br\xc3\xbcning,\r\nstill studying Davies with a strange look, which I strove anxiously to\r\nanalyse.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have noticed,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies. \xe2\x80\x9cIt was very thick and\r\nsqually\xe2\x80\x94and she had got some way ahead. There was no need for her to\r\nstop, anyway. I got off all right; the tide was rising still. But, of\r\ncourse, I anchored there for the night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cInside there, under the Hohenh\xc3\xb6rn,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, simply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUnder the _what_?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Hohenh\xc3\xb6rn.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo on\xe2\x80\x94didn\xe2\x80\x99t they wait for you at Cuxhaven?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know; I didn\xe2\x80\x99t go that way.\xe2\x80\x9d The Commander looked more and\r\nmore puzzled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot by the ship canal, I mean. I changed my mind about it, because the\r\nnext day the wind was easterly. It would have been a dead beat across\r\nthe sands to Cuxhaven, while it was a fair wind straight out to the\r\nEider River. So I sailed there, and reached the Baltic that way. It was\r\nall the same.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was another pause.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell done, Davies,\xe2\x80\x9d I thought. He had told his story well, using no\r\nsubtlety. I knew it was exactly how he would have told it to anyone\r\nelse, if he had not had irrefutable proof of foul play.\r\n\r\nThe Commander laughed, suddenly and heartily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnother liqueur?\xe2\x80\x9d he said. Then, to me: \xe2\x80\x9cUpon my word, your friend\r\namuses me. It\xe2\x80\x99s impossible to make him spin a yarn. I expect he had a\r\nbad time of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s nothing to him,\xe2\x80\x9d I said; \xe2\x80\x9che prefers it. He anchored me the\r\nother day behind the Hohenh\xc3\xb6rn in a gale of wind; said it was safer\r\nthan a harbour, and more sanitary.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wonder he brought you here last night. It was a fair wind for\r\nEngland; and not very far.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere was no pilot to follow, you see.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWith a charming daughter\xe2\x80\x94no.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDavies frowned and glared at me. I was merciful and changed the\r\nsubject.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBesides,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, \xe2\x80\x9cwe\xe2\x80\x99ve left our anchor and chain out there.\xe2\x80\x9d And I\r\nmade confession of my sin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, as it\xe2\x80\x99s buoyed, I should advise you to pick it up as soon as you\r\ncan,\xe2\x80\x9d said von Br\xc3\xbcning, carelessly; \xe2\x80\x9cor someone else will.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, by Jove! Carruthers,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, eagerly, \xe2\x80\x9cwe must get out on\r\nthis next tide.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, there\xe2\x80\x99s no hurry,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, partly from policy, partly because the\r\nease of the shore was on me. To sit on a chair upright is something of\r\na luxury, however good the cause in which you have crouched like a\r\nmonkey over a table at the level of your knees, with a reeking\r\noil-stove at your ear.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99re honest enough about here, aren\xe2\x80\x99t they?\xe2\x80\x9d I added. While the\r\nwords were on my lips I remembered the midnight visitor at Wangeroog,\r\nand guessed that von Br\xc3\xbcning was leading up to a test. Grimm (if he was\r\nthe visitor) would have told him of his narrow escape from detection,\r\nand reticence on our part would show we suspected something. I could\r\nhave kicked myself, but it was not too late. I took the bull by the\r\nhorns, and, before the Commander could answer, added:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy Jove! Davies, I forgot about that fellow at Wangeroog. The anchor\r\nmight be stolen, as he says.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDavies looked blank, but von Br\xc3\xbcning had turned to me.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe never dreamed there would be thieves among these islands,\xe2\x80\x9d I said,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cbut the other night I nearly caught a fellow in the act. He thought\r\nthe yacht was empty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI described the affair in detail, and with what humour I could. Our\r\nhost was amused, and apologetic for the islanders.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99re excellent folk,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cbut they\xe2\x80\x99re born with predatory\r\ninstincts. Their fathers made their living out of wrecks on this coast,\r\nand the children inherit a weakness for plunder. When Wangeroog\r\nlighthouse was built they petitioned the Government for compensation,\r\nin perfect good faith. The coast is well lighted now, and windfalls are\r\nrare, but the sight of a stranded yacht, with the owners ashore, would\r\ninflame the old passion; and, depend upon it, someone has seen that\r\nanchor-buoy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe word \xe2\x80\x9cwrecks\xe2\x80\x9d had set me tingling. Was it another test? Impossible\r\nto say; but audacity was safer than reserve, and might save trouble in\r\nthe future.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIsn\xe2\x80\x99t there the wreck of a treasure-ship somewhere farther west?\xe2\x80\x9d I\r\nasked. \xe2\x80\x9cWe heard of it at Wangeroog\xe2\x80\x9d (my first inaccuracy). \xe2\x80\x9cThey said\r\na company was exploiting it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuite right,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Commander, without a sign of embarrassment. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t wonder you heard of it. It\xe2\x80\x99s one of the few things folk have to\r\ntalk about in these parts. It lies on Juister Riff, a shoal off Juist.\r\n_[See Map B]_ She was a French frigate, the _Corinne_, bound from\r\nHamburg to Havre in 1811, when Napoleon held Hamburg as tight as Paris.\r\nShe carried a million and a half in gold bars, and was insured in\r\nHamburg; foundered in four fathoms, broke up, and there lies the\r\ntreasure.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever been raised?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. The underwriters failed and went bankrupt, and the wreck came into\r\nthe hands of your English Lloyd\xe2\x80\x99s. It remained their property till \xe2\x80\x9975,\r\nbut they never got at the bullion. In fact, for fifty years it was\r\nnever scratched at, and its very position grew doubtful, for the sand\r\nswallowed every stick. The rights passed through various hands, and in\r\n\xe2\x80\x9986 were held by an enterprising Swedish company, which brought modern\r\nappliances, dived, dredged, and dug, fished up a lot of timber and\r\nbric-\xc3\xa0-brac, and then broke. Since then, two Hamburg firms have tackled\r\nthe job and lost their capital. Scores of lives have been spent over\r\nit, all told, and probably a million of money. Still there are the\r\nbars, somewhere.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what\xe2\x80\x99s being done now?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, recently a small local company was formed. It has a dep\xc3\xb4t at\r\nMemmert, and is working with a good deal of perseverance. An engineer\r\nfrom Bremen was the principal mover, and a few men from Norderney and\r\nEmden subscribed the capital. By the way, our friend Dollmann is\r\nlargely interested in it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOut of the corner of my eye I saw Davies\xe2\x80\x99s tell-tale face growing\r\ntroubled with inward questionings.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe mustn\xe2\x80\x99t get back to him,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, laughing. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s not fair to my\r\nfriend. But all this is very interesting. Will they ever get those\r\nbars?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! that\xe2\x80\x99s the point,\xe2\x80\x9d said von Br\xc3\xbcning, with a mysterious twinkle.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s an undertaking of immense difficulty; for the wreck is wholly\r\ndisintegrated, and the gold, being the heaviest part of it, has, of\r\ncourse, sunk the deepest. Dredging is useless after a certain point;\r\nand the divers have to make excavations in the sand, and shore them up\r\nas best they can. Every gale nullifies half their labour, and weather\r\nlike this of the last fortnight plays the mischief with the work. Only\r\nthis morning I met the overseer, who happens to be ashore here. He was\r\nas black as thunder over prospects.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it\xe2\x80\x99s a romantic speculation,\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9cThey deserve a return for\r\ntheir money.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope they\xe2\x80\x99ll get it,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Commander. \xe2\x80\x9cThe fact is, I hold a few\r\nshares myself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I hope I haven\xe2\x80\x99t been asking indiscreet questions?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, dear no; all the world knows what I\xe2\x80\x99ve told you. But you\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nunderstand that one has to be reticent as to results in such a case.\r\nIt\xe2\x80\x99s a big stake, and the _title is none too sound._ There has been\r\nlitigation over it. Not that I worry much about my investment; for I\r\nshan\xe2\x80\x99t lose much by it at the worst. But it gives one an interest in\r\nthis abominable coast. I go and see how they\xe2\x80\x99re getting on sometimes,\r\nwhen I\xe2\x80\x99m down that way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt _is_ an abominable coast,\xe2\x80\x9d I agreed heartily, \xe2\x80\x9cthough you won\xe2\x80\x99t get\r\nDavies to agree.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a magnificent place for sailing,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, looking wistfully\r\nout over the storm-speckled grey of the North Sea.\r\n\r\nHe underwent some more chaff, and the talk passed to our cruising\r\nadventures in the Baltic and the estuaries. Von Br\xc3\xbcning cross-examined\r\nus with the most charming urbanity and skill. Nothing he asked could\r\ncause us the slightest offence; and a responsive frankness was our only\r\npossible course. So, date after date, and incident after incident, were\r\nelicited in the most natural way. As we talked I was astonished to find\r\nhow little there was that was worth concealing, and heartily thankful\r\nthat we had decided on candour. My fluency gave me the lead, and Davies\r\nfollowed me; but his own personality was really our tower of strength.\r\nI realised that as I watched the play of his eager features, and heard\r\nhim struggle for expression on his favourite hobby; all his pet phrases\r\ntranslated crudely into the most excruciating German. He was\r\nconvincing, because he was himself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre there many like you in England?\xe2\x80\x9d asked von Br\xc3\xbcning once.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLike me? Of course\xe2\x80\x94lots,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish there were more in Germany; they play at yachting over here\xe2\x80\x94on\r\nshore half the time, drinking and loafing; paid crews, clean hands,\r\nwhite trousers; laid up in the middle of September.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe haven\xe2\x80\x99t seen many yachts about, said Davies, politely.\r\n\r\nFor my part, I made no pretence of being a Davies. Faithful to my lower\r\nnature, I vowed the Germans were right, and, not without a secret zest,\r\ndrew a lurid picture of the horrors of crewless cruising, and the\r\ndrudgery that my remorseless skipper inflicted on me. It was delightful\r\nto see Davies wincing when I described my first night at Flensburg, for\r\nI had my revenge at last, and did not spare him. He bore up gallantly\r\nunder my jesting, but I knew very well by his manner that he had not\r\nforgiven me my banter about the \xe2\x80\x9ccharming daughter\xe2\x80\x9d.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou speak German well,\xe2\x80\x9d said von Br\xc3\xbcning.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have lived in Germany,\xe2\x80\x9d said I.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStudying for a profession, I suppose?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said I, thinking ahead. \xe2\x80\x9cCivil Service,\xe2\x80\x9d was my prepared answer\r\nto the next question, but again (morbidly, perhaps) I saw a pitfall.\r\nThat letter from my chief awaiting me at Norderney? My name was known,\r\nand we were watched. It might be opened. Lord, how casual we have been!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMay I ask what?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Foreign Office.\xe2\x80\x9d It sounded suspicious, but there it was.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed\xe2\x80\x94in the Government service? When do you have to be back?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThat was how the question of our future intentions was raised,\r\nprematurely by me; for two conflicting theories were clashing in my\r\nbrain. But the contents of the letter dogged me now, and \xe2\x80\x9cwhen at a\r\nloss, tell the truth\xe2\x80\x9d, was an axiom I was finding sound. So I answered,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPretty soon, in about a week. But I\xe2\x80\x99m expecting a letter at Norderney,\r\nwhich may give me an extension. Davies said it was a good address to\r\ngive,\xe2\x80\x9d I added, smiling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNaturally,\xe2\x80\x9d said von Br\xc3\xbcning, dryly; the joke had apparently ceased to\r\namuse him. \xe2\x80\x9cBut you haven\xe2\x80\x99t much time then, have you?\xe2\x80\x9d he added,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cunless you leave your skipper in the lurch. It\xe2\x80\x99s a long way to\r\nEngland, and the season is late for yachts.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI felt myself being hurried.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, you don\xe2\x80\x99t understand,\xe2\x80\x9d I explained; \xe2\x80\x9c_he\xe2\x80\x99s_ in no hurry. He\xe2\x80\x99s a\r\nman of leisure; aren\xe2\x80\x99t you, Davies?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat?\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies.\r\n\r\nI translated my cruel question.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, with simple pathos.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I have to leave him I shan\xe2\x80\x99t be missed\xe2\x80\x94as an able seaman, at least.\r\nHe\xe2\x80\x99ll just potter on down the islands, running aground and kedging-off,\r\nand arrive about Christmas.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOr take the first fair gale to Dover,\xe2\x80\x9d laughed the Commander.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOr that. So, you see, we\xe2\x80\x99re in no hurry; and we never make plans. And\r\nas for a passage to England straight, I\xe2\x80\x99m not such a coward as I was at\r\nfirst, but I draw the line at that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re a curious pair of shipmates; what\xe2\x80\x99s your point of view, Herr\r\nDavies?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI like this coast,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd\xe2\x80\x94we want to shoot some ducks.\xe2\x80\x9d He\r\nwas nervous, and forgot himself. I had already satirised our sporting\r\narmament and exploits, and hoped the subject was disposed of. Ducks\r\nwere pretexts, and might lead to complications. I particularly wanted a\r\nfree hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs to wild fowl,\xe2\x80\x9d said our friend, \xe2\x80\x9cI would like to give you gentlemen\r\nsome advice. There are plenty to be got, now that autumn weather has\r\nset in (you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have got a shot in September, Herr Davies; I\r\nremember your asking about them when I saw you last). And even now it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nearly for amateurs. In hard winter weather a child can pick them up;\r\nbut they\xe2\x80\x99re wild still, and want crafty hunting. You want a local punt,\r\nand above all a local man (you could stow him in your fo\xe2\x80\x99c\xe2\x80\x99sle), and to\r\ngo to work seriously. Now, if you really wish for sport, I could help\r\nyou. I could get you a trustworthy\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, it\xe2\x80\x99s too good of you,\xe2\x80\x9d stammered Davies, in a more unhappy accent\r\nthan usual. \xe2\x80\x9cWe can easily find one for ourselves. A man at Wangeroog\r\noffered\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, did he?\xe2\x80\x9d interrupted von Br\xc3\xbcning, laughing. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m not surprised.\r\nYou don\xe2\x80\x99t know the Frieslanders. They\xe2\x80\x99re guileless, as I said, but they\r\ncling to their little perquisites.\xe2\x80\x9d (I translated to Davies.) \xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\nbeen cheated out of wrecks, and they\xe2\x80\x99re all the more sensitive about\r\nducks, which are more lucrative than fish. A stranger is a poacher.\r\nYour man would have made slight errors as to time and place.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou said they were odd in their manner, didn\xe2\x80\x99t you, Davies?\xe2\x80\x9d I put in.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook here, this is very kind of Commander von Br\xc3\xbcning; but hadn\xe2\x80\x99t we\r\nbetter be certain of my plans before settling down to shoot? Let\xe2\x80\x99s push\r\non direct to Norderney and get that letter of mine, and then decide.\r\nBut we shan\xe2\x80\x99t see you again, I suppose, Commander?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy not? I am cruising westwards, and shall probably call at\r\nNorderney. Come aboard if you\xe2\x80\x99re there, won\xe2\x80\x99t you? I should like to\r\nshow you the _Blitz_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThanks, very much,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, uneasily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThanks, very much,\xe2\x80\x9d said I, as heartily as I could.\r\n\r\nOur party broke up soon after this.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, gentlemen, I must take leave of you,\xe2\x80\x9d said our friend. \xe2\x80\x9cI have\r\nto drive to Esens. I shall be going back to the _Blitz_ on the evening\r\ntide, but you\xe2\x80\x99ll be busy then with your own boat.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt had been a puzzling interview, but the greatest puzzle was still to\r\ncome. As we went towards the door, von Br\xc3\xbcning made a sign to me. We\r\nlet Davies pass out and remained standing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne word in confidence with you, Herr Carruthers,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, speaking\r\nlow. \xe2\x80\x9cYou won\xe2\x80\x99t think me officious, I hope. I only speak out of keen\r\nregard for your friend. It is about the Dollmanns\xe2\x80\x94you see how the land\r\nlies? I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t encourage him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThanks,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, \xe2\x80\x9cbut really\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s only a hint. He\xe2\x80\x99s a splendid young fellow, but if anything\xe2\x80\x94you\r\nunderstand\xe2\x80\x94too honest and simple. I take it you have influence with\r\nhim, and I should use it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was not in earnest,\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9cI have never seen the Dollmanns; I\r\nthought they were friends of yours,\xe2\x80\x9d I added, looking him straight in\r\nthe eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know them, but\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94he shrugged his shoulders\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cI know everybody.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s wrong with them?\xe2\x80\x9d I said, point-blank.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSoftly! Herr Carruthers. Remember, I speak out of pure friendliness to\r\nyou as strangers, foreigners, and young. You I take to have discretion,\r\nor I should not have said a word. Still, I will add this. We know very\r\nlittle of Herr Dollmann, of his origin, his antecedents. He is half a\r\nSwede, I believe, certainly not a Prussian; came to Norderney three\r\nyears ago, appears to be rich, and has joined in various commercial\r\nundertakings. Little scope about here? Oh, there is more enterprise\r\nthan you think\xe2\x80\x94development of bathing resorts, you know, speculation in\r\nland on these islands. Sharp practice? Oh, no! he\xe2\x80\x99s perfectly straight\r\nin that way. But he\xe2\x80\x99s a queer fellow, of eccentric habits, and\xe2\x80\x94and,\r\nwell, as I say, little is known of him. That\xe2\x80\x99s all, just a warning.\r\nCome along.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI saw that to press him further was useless.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThanks; I\xe2\x80\x99ll remember,\xe2\x80\x9d I said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd look here,\xe2\x80\x9d he added, as we walked down the passage, \xe2\x80\x9cif you take\r\nmy advice, you\xe2\x80\x99ll omit that visit to the _Medusa_ altogether.\xe2\x80\x9d He gave\r\nme a steady look, smiling gravely.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow much do you know, and what do you mean?\xe2\x80\x9d were the questions that\r\nthrobbed in my thoughts; but I could not utter them, so I said nothing\r\nand felt very young.\r\n\r\nOutside we joined Davies, who was knitting his brow over prospects.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt just comes of going into places like this,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to me. \xe2\x80\x9cWe may\r\nbe stuck here for days. Too much wind to tow out with the dinghy, and\r\ntoo narrow a channel to beat in.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nVon Br\xc3\xbcning was ready with a new proposal.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy didn\xe2\x80\x99t I think of it before?\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll tow you out in my\r\nlaunch. Be ready at 6.30; we shall have water enough then. My men will\r\nsend you a warp.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was impossible to refuse, but a sense of being personally conducted\r\nagain oppressed me; and the last hope of a bed in the inn vanished.\r\nDavies was none too effusive either. A tug meant a pilot, and he had\r\nhad enough of them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe objects to towage on principle,\xe2\x80\x9d I said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust like him!\xe2\x80\x9d laughed the other. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s settled, then!\xe2\x80\x9d A dogcart\r\nwas standing before the inn door in readiness for von Br\xc3\xbcning. I was\r\ncurious about Esens and his business there. Esens, he said, was the\r\nprincipal town of the district, four miles inland.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have to go there,\xe2\x80\x9d he volunteered, \xe2\x80\x9cabout a poaching case\xe2\x80\x94a Dutchman\r\ntrawling inside our limits. That\xe2\x80\x99s my work, you know\xe2\x80\x94police duty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHad the words a deeper meaning?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you ever catch an Englishman?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked, recklessly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, very rarely; your countrymen don\xe2\x80\x99t come so far as this\xe2\x80\x94except on\r\npleasure.\xe2\x80\x9d He bowed to us each and smiled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot much of that to be got in Bensersiel,\xe2\x80\x9d I laughed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m afraid you\xe2\x80\x99ll have a dull afternoon. Look here. I know you can\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nleave your boat altogether, and it\xe2\x80\x99s no use asking Herr Davies; but\r\nwill _you_ drive into Esens with me and see a Frisian town\xe2\x80\x94for what\r\nit\xe2\x80\x99s worth? You\xe2\x80\x99re getting a dismal impression of Friesland.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI excused myself, said I would stop with Davies; we would walk out over\r\nthe sands and prospect for the evening\xe2\x80\x99s sail.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, good-bye then,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9ctill the evening. Be ready for the\r\nwarp at 6.30.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe jumped up, and the cart rattled off through the mud, crossed the\r\nbridge, and disappeared into the dreary hinterland.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XVII.\r\nClearing the Air\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHas he gone to get the police, do you think?\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, grimly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think so,\xe2\x80\x9d said I. \xe2\x80\x9cLet\xe2\x80\x99s go aboard before that Customs fellow\r\nbuttonholes us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA diminished row of stolid Frisians still ruminated over the\r\n_Dulcibella_. Friend Grimm was visible smoking on his forecastle. We\r\nwent on board in silence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFirst of all, where exactly is Memmert?\xe2\x80\x9d I said.\r\n\r\nDavies pulled down the chart, said \xe2\x80\x9cThere,\xe2\x80\x9d and flung himself at full\r\nlength on a sofa.\r\n\r\nThe reader can see Memmert for himself. South of Juist, _[See Map B]_\r\nabutting on the Ems delta, lies an extensive sandbank called Nordland,\r\nwhose extreme western rim remains uncovered at the highest tides; the\r\neffect being to leave a C-shaped island, a mere paring of sand like a\r\nboomerang, nearly two miles long, but only 150 yards or so broad, of\r\ncuriously symmetrical outline, except at one spot, where it bulges to\r\nthe width of a quarter of a mile. On the English chart its nakedness\r\nwas absolute, save for a beacon at the south; but the German chart\r\nmarked a building at the point where the bulge occurs. This was\r\nevidently the dep\xc3\xb4t. \xe2\x80\x9cFancy living there!\xe2\x80\x9d I thought, for the very name\r\nstruck cold. No wonder Grimm was grim; and no wonder he was used to\r\nseek change of air. But the advantages of the site were obvious. It was\r\nremarkably isolated, even in a region where isolation is the rule; yet\r\nit was conveniently near the wreck, which, as we had heard, lay two\r\nmiles out on the Juister Reef. Lastly, it was clearly accessible at any\r\nstate of the tide, for the six-fathom channel of the Ems estuary runs\r\nhard up to it on the south, and thence sends off an eastward branch\r\nwhich closely borders the southern horn, thus offering an anchorage at\r\nonce handy, deep, and sheltered from seaward gales.\r\n\r\nSuch was Memmert, as I saw it on the chart, taking in its features\r\nmechanically, for while Davies lay there heedless and taciturn, a\r\npretence of interest was useless. I knew perfectly well what was\r\nbetween us, but I did not see why I should make the first move; for I\r\nhad a grievance too, an old one. So I sat back on my sofa and jotted\r\ndown in my notebook the heads of our conversation at the inn while it\r\nwas fresh in my memory, and strove to draw conclusions. But the silence\r\ncontinuing and becoming absurd, I threw my pride to the winds, and my\r\nnotebook on the table.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI say, Davies,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m awfully sorry I chaffed you about\r\nFr\xc3\xa4ulein Dollmann.\xe2\x80\x9d (No answer.) \xe2\x80\x9cDidn\xe2\x80\x99t you see I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t help it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish to Heaven we had never come in here,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, in a hard voice;\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cit comes of landing _ever_.\xe2\x80\x9d (I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t help smiling at this, but he\r\nwasn\xe2\x80\x99t looking at me.) \xe2\x80\x9cHere we are, given away, moved on, taken in\r\ncharge, arranged for like Cook\xe2\x80\x99s tourists. I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t follow your\r\ngame\xe2\x80\x94too infernally deep for me, but\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d That stung me.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook here,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, \xe2\x80\x9cI did my best. It was you that muddled it. Why\r\ndid you harp on ducks?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe could have got out of that. Why did you harp on everything\r\nidiotic\xe2\x80\x94your letter, the Foreign Office, the _Kormoran_, the wreck,\r\nthe\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re utterly unreasonable. Didn\xe2\x80\x99t you see what traps there were? I\r\nwas driven the way I went. We started unprepared, and we\xe2\x80\x99re jolly well\r\nout of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDavies drove on blindly. \xe2\x80\x9cIt was bad enough telling all about the\r\nchannels and exploring\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, you agreed to that yourself!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI gave in to you. We can\xe2\x80\x99t explore any more now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s the wreck, though.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, hang the wreck! It\xe2\x80\x99s all a blind, or he wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have made so much\r\nof it. There are all these channels to be\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, hang the channels! I know we wanted a free hand, but we\xe2\x80\x99ve got to\r\ngo to Norderney some time, and if Dollmann\xe2\x80\x99s away\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy did you harp on Miss Dollmann?\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies.\r\n\r\nWe had worked round, through idle recrimination, to the real point of\r\ndeparture. I knew Davies was not himself, and would not return to\r\nhimself till the heart of the matter was reached.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook here,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, \xe2\x80\x9cyou brought me out here to help you, because, as\r\nyou say, I was clever, talked German, and\xe2\x80\x94liked yachting (I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nresist adding this). But directly you really _want_ me you turn round\r\nand go for me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I didn\xe2\x80\x99t mean all that, really,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies; \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m sorry\xe2\x80\x94I was\r\nworried.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know; but it\xe2\x80\x99s your own fault. You haven\xe2\x80\x99t been fair with me.\r\nThere\xe2\x80\x99s a complication in this business that you\xe2\x80\x99ve never talked about.\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ve never pressed you because I thought you would confide in me.\r\nYou\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know I haven\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, you see the result. Our hand was forced. To have said nothing\r\nabout Dollmann was folly\xe2\x80\x94to have said he tried to wreck you was equal\r\nfolly. The story we agreed on was the best and safest, and you told it\r\nsplendidly. But for two reasons I had to harp on the daughter\xe2\x80\x94one\r\nbecause your manner when they were mentioned was so confused as to\r\nimperil our whole position. Two, because your story, though the safest,\r\nwas, at the best, suspicious. Even on your own showing Dollmann treated\r\nyou badly\xe2\x80\x94discourteously, say: though you pretended not to have seen\r\nit. You want a motive to neutralise that, and induce you to revisit him\r\nin a friendly way. I supplied it, or rather I only encouraged von\r\nBr\xc3\xbcning to supply it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy revisit him, after all?\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, come\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut don\xe2\x80\x99t you see what a hideous fix you\xe2\x80\x99ve put me in? How caddish I\r\nfeel about it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI did see, and I felt a cad myself, as his full distress came home to\r\nme. But I felt, too, that, whosesoever the fault, we had drifted into a\r\nridiculous situation, and were like characters in one of those tiresome\r\nplays where misunderstandings are manufactured and so carefully\r\nsustained that the audience are too bored to wait for the _d\xc3\xa9nouement._\r\nYou can do that on the stage; but we wanted our _d\xc3\xa9nouement._\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m very sorry,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, \xe2\x80\x9cbut I wish you had told me all about it.\r\nWon\xe2\x80\x99t you now? Just the bare, matter-of-fact truth. I hate sentiment,\r\nand so do you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI find it very difficult to tell people things,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, \xe2\x80\x9cthings\r\nlike this.\xe2\x80\x9d I waited. \xe2\x80\x9cI did like her\xe2\x80\x94very much.\xe2\x80\x9d Our eyes met for a\r\nsecond, in which all was said that need be said, as between two of our\r\nphlegmatic race. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd she\xe2\x80\x99s\xe2\x80\x94separate from him. That was the reason of\r\nall my indecisions.\xe2\x80\x9d he hurried on. \xe2\x80\x9cI only told you half at Schlei. I\r\nknow I ought to have been open, and asked your advice. But I let it\r\nslide. I\xe2\x80\x99ve been hoping all along that we might find what we want and\r\nwin the game without coming to close quarters again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI no longer wondered at his devotion to the channel theory, since,\r\nbuilt on conviction, it was thus doubly fortified.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYet you always knew what might happen,\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9cAt Schlei you spoke\r\nof \xe2\x80\x98settling with\xe2\x80\x99 Dollmann.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know. When I thought of him I was mad. I made myself forget the\r\nother part.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhich recurred at Brunsb\xc3\xbcttel?\xe2\x80\x9d I thought of the news we had there.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDavies, we must have no more secrets. I\xe2\x80\x99m going to speak out. Are you\r\nsure you\xe2\x80\x99ve not misunderstood her? You say\xe2\x80\x94and I\xe2\x80\x99m willing to assume\r\nit\xe2\x80\x94that Dollmann\xe2\x80\x99s a traitor and a murderer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, hang the murder part!\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, impatiently. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat does _that_\r\nmatter?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, traitor. Very good; but in that case I suspect his daughter. No!\r\nlet me go on. She was useful, to say the least. She encouraged\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x94you\xe2\x80\x99ve told me that\xe2\x80\x94to make that passage with them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStop, Carruthers,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, firmly. \xe2\x80\x9cI know you mean kindly; but\r\nit\xe2\x80\x99s no use. I believe in her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI thought for a moment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn that case,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve something to propose. When we get out of\r\nthis place let\xe2\x80\x99s sail straight away to England.\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9c(There, Commander von\r\nBr\xc3\xbcning,\xe2\x80\x9d I thought, \xe2\x80\x9cyou never can say I neglected your advice.\xe2\x80\x9d)\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Davies, starting up and facing me. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m hanged if we\r\nwill. Think what\xe2\x80\x99s at stake. Think of that traitor\xe2\x80\x94plotting with\r\nGermans. My God!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery good,\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m with you for going on. But let\xe2\x80\x99s face facts.\r\nWe _must_ scotch Dollmann. We can\xe2\x80\x99t do so without hurting _her_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan\xe2\x80\x99t we _possibly_?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course not; be sensible, man. Face that. Next point; it\xe2\x80\x99s absurd to\r\nhope that we need not revisit them\xe2\x80\x94it\xe2\x80\x99s ten to one that we must, if\r\nwe\xe2\x80\x99re to succeed. His attempt on you is the whole foundation of our\r\nsuspicions. And we don\xe2\x80\x99t even know for certain who he _is_ yet. We\xe2\x80\x99re\r\ncommitted, I know, to going straight to Norderney now; but even if we\r\nweren\xe2\x80\x99t, should we do any good by exploring and prying? It\xe2\x80\x99s very\r\ndoubtful. We know we\xe2\x80\x99re watched, if not suspected, and that disposes of\r\nnine-tenths of our power. The channels? Yes, but is it likely they\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nlet us learn them by heart, if they\xe2\x80\x99re of such vital importance, even\r\nif we are thought to be _bona fide_ yachtsmen? And, seriously, apart\r\nfrom their value in war, which I don\xe2\x80\x99t deny, are they at the root of\r\nthis business? But we\xe2\x80\x99ll talk about that in a moment. The point now is,\r\nwhat shall we do if we meet the Dollmanns?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBeads of sweat stood on Davies\xe2\x80\x99s brow. I felt like a torturer, but it\r\ncould not be helped. \xe2\x80\x9cTax him with having wrecked you? Our quest would\r\nbe at an end! We must be friendly. You must tell the story you told\r\nto-day, and chance his believing it. If he does, so much the better; if\r\nhe doesn\xe2\x80\x99t, he won\xe2\x80\x99t dare say so, and we still have chances. We gain\r\ntime, and have a tremendous hold on him\xe2\x80\x94_if_ we\xe2\x80\x99re friendly.\xe2\x80\x9d Davies\r\nwinced. I gave another turn to the screw. \xe2\x80\x9cFriendly with them _both,_\r\nof course. You were before, you know; you liked her very much\xe2\x80\x94you must\r\nseem to still.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, stop your infernal logic.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShall we chuck it and go to England?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked again, as an inquisitor\r\nmight say, \xe2\x80\x9cHave you had enough?\xe2\x80\x9d No answer. I went on: \xe2\x80\x9cTo make it\r\neasier, you _do_ like her still.\xe2\x80\x9d I had roused my victim at last.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat the devil do you mean, Carruthers? That I\xe2\x80\x99m to trade on my liking\r\nfor her\xe2\x80\x94on her innocence, to\xe2\x80\x94good God! what _do_ you mean?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no, not that. I\xe2\x80\x99m not such a cad, or such a fool, or so ignorant\r\nof you. If she knows nothing of her father\xe2\x80\x99s character and likes\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x94and you like her\xe2\x80\x94and you are what you are\xe2\x80\x94oh Heavens! man, face it,\r\nrealise it! But what I mean is this: is she, _can_ she be, what you\r\nthink? Imagine his position if we\xe2\x80\x99re right about him; the vilest\r\ncreature on God\xe2\x80\x99s earth\xe2\x80\x94a disgraceful past to have been driven to\r\nthis\xe2\x80\x94in the pay of Germany. I want to spare you misery.\xe2\x80\x9d I was going to\r\nadd: \xe2\x80\x9cAnd if you\xe2\x80\x99re on your guard, to increase our chances.\xe2\x80\x9d But the\r\nutter futility of such suggestions silenced me. What a plan I had\r\nforeshadowed! An enticing plan and a fair one, too, as against such\r\nadversaries; turning this baffling cross-current to advantage as many a\r\ntime we had worked eddies of an adverse tide in these difficult seas.\r\nBut Davies was Davies, and there was an end of it; his faith and\r\nsimplicity shamed me. And the pity of it, the cruelty of it, was that\r\nhis very qualities were his last torture, raising to the acutest pitch\r\nthe conflict between love and patriotism. Remember that the latter was\r\nhis dominant life-motive, and that here and now was his chance\xe2\x80\x94if you\r\nwould gauge the bitterness of that conflict.\r\n\r\nIt was in its last throes now. His elbows were on the table, and his\r\ntwitching hands pressed on his forehead. He took them away.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course we must go on. It can\xe2\x80\x99t be helped, that\xe2\x80\x99s all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you believe in her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll remember what you\xe2\x80\x99ve said. There may be some way out. And\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99d\r\nrather not talk about that any more. What about the wreck?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFurther argument was futile. Davies by an effort seemed to sweep the\r\nsubject from his thoughts, and I did my best to do the same. At any\r\nrate the air was cleared\xe2\x80\x94we were friends; and it only remained to\r\ngrapple with the main problem in the light of the morning\xe2\x80\x99s interview.\r\n\r\nEvery word that I could recollect of that critical conversation I\r\nreviewed with Davies, who had imperfectly understood what he had not\r\nbeen directly concerned in; and, as I did so, I began to see with what\r\ncleverness each succeeding sentence of von Br\xc3\xbcning\xe2\x80\x99s was designed to\r\nsuit both of two contingencies. If we were innocent travellers, he was\r\nthe genial host, communicative and helpful. If we were spies, his\r\ntactics had been equally applicable. He had outdone us in apparent\r\ncandour, hiding nothing which he knew we would discover for ourselves,\r\nand contriving at the same time both to gain knowledge and control of\r\nour movements, and to convey us warnings, which would only be\r\nunderstood if we were guilty, that we were playing an idle and perilous\r\ngame, and had better desist. But in one respect we had had the\r\nadvantage, and that was in the version Davies had given of his\r\nstranding on the Hohenh\xc3\xb6rn. Inscrutable as our questioner was, he let\r\nit appear not only that the incident was new to him, but that he\r\nconjectured at its sinister significance. A little cross-examination on\r\ndetail would have been fatal to Davies\xe2\x80\x99s version; but that was where\r\nour strength lay; he dared not cross-examine for fear of suggesting to\r\nDavies suspicions which he might never have felt. Indeed, I thought I\r\ndetected that fear underlying his whole attitude towards us, and it\r\nstrengthened a conviction which had been growing in me since Grimm\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nfurtive midnight visit, that the secret of this coast was of so\r\nimportant and delicate a nature that rather than attract attention to\r\nit at all, overt action against intruders would be taken only in the\r\nlast resort, and on irrefragable proofs of guilty intention.\r\n\r\nNow for our clues. I had come away with two, each the germ of a\r\ndistinct theory, and both obscured by the prevailing ambiguity. Now,\r\nhowever, as we thumbed the chart and I gave full rein to my fancy, one\r\nof them, the idea of Memmert, gained precision and vigour every moment.\r\nTrue, such information as we had about the French wreck and his own\r\nconnection with it was placed most readily at our disposal by von\r\nBr\xc3\xbcning; but I took it to be information calculated only to forestall\r\nsuspicion, since he was aware that we already associated him with\r\nDollmann, possibly also with Grimm, and it was only likely that in the\r\nordinary course we should learn that the trio were jointly concerned in\r\nMemmert. So much for the facts; as for the construction he wished us to\r\nput on them, I felt sure it was absolutely false. He wished to give us\r\nthe impression that the buried treasure itself was at the root of any\r\nmystery we might have scented. I do not know if the reader fully\r\nappreciated that astute suggestion\xe2\x80\x94the hint that secrecy as to results\r\nwas necessary owing both to the great sum at stake and the flaw in the\r\ntitle, which he had been careful to inform us had passed through\r\nBritish hands. What he meant to imply was, \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t be surprised if you\r\nhave midnight visitors; Englishmen prowling along this coast are\r\nsuspected of being Lloyd\xe2\x80\x99s agents.\xe2\x80\x9d An ingenious insinuation, which, at\r\nthe time it was made, had caused me to contemplate a new and much more\r\ncommonplace solution of our enigma than had ever occurred to us; but it\r\nwas only a passing doubt, and I dismissed it altogether now.\r\n\r\nThe fact was, it either explained everything or nothing. As long as we\r\nheld to our fundamental assumption\xe2\x80\x94that Davies had been decoyed into a\r\ndeath-trap in September\xe2\x80\x94it explained nothing. It was too fantastic to\r\nsuppose that the exigencies of a commercial speculation would lead to\r\nsuch extremities as that. We were not in the South Sea Islands; nor\r\nwere we the puppets of a romance. We were in Europe, dealing not only\r\nwith a Dollmann, but with an officer of the German Imperial Navy, who\r\nwould scarcely be connected with a commercial enterprise which could\r\nconceivably be reduced to forwarding its objects in such a fashion. It\r\nwas shocking enough to find him in relations with such a scoundrel at\r\nall, but it was explicable if the motive were imperial\xe2\x80\x94not so if it\r\nwere financial. No; to accept the suggestion we must declare the whole\r\nquest a mare\xe2\x80\x99s nest from beginning to end; the attempt on Davies a\r\ndelusion of his own fancy, the whole structure we had built on it,\r\nbaseless.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d I can hear the reader saying, \xe2\x80\x9cwhy not? You, at any rate, were\r\nalways a little sceptical.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGranted; yet I can truthfully say I scarcely faltered for a moment.\r\nMuch had happened since Schlei Fiord. I had seen the mechanism of the\r\ndeath-trap; I had lived with Davies for a stormy fortnight, every hour\r\nof which had increased my reliance on his seamanship, and also,\r\ntherefore, on his account of an event which depended largely for its\r\ncorrect interpretation on a balanced nautical judgement. Finally, I had\r\nbeen unconsciously realising, and knew from his mouth to-day, that he\r\nhad exercised and acted on that judgement in the teeth of personal\r\nconsiderations, which his loyal nature made overwhelming in their\r\nforce.\r\n\r\nWhat, then, was the meaning of Memmert? At the outset it riveted my\r\nattention on the Ems estuary, whose mouth it adjoins. We had always\r\nrather neglected the Ems in our calculations; with some excuse, too,\r\nfor at first sight its importance bears no proportion to that of the\r\nthree greater estuaries. The latter bear vessels of the largest tonnage\r\nand deepest draught to the very quays of Hamburg, Bremerhaven, and the\r\nnaval dockyard of Wilhelmshaven; while two of them, the Elbe and the\r\nWeser, are commerce carriers on the vastest scale for the whole empire.\r\nThe Ems, on the other hand, only serves towns of the second class. A\r\nglance at the chart explains this. You see a most imposing estuary on a\r\ngrander scale than any of the other three taken singly, with a length\r\nof thirty miles and a frontage on the North Sea of ten miles, or\r\none-seventieth, roughly, of the whole seaboard; encumbered by outlying\r\nshoals, and blocked in the centre by the island of Borkum, but\r\npresenting two fine deep-water channels to the incoming vessel. These\r\nroll superbly through enormous sheets of sand, unite and approach the\r\nmainland in one stately stream three miles in breadth. But then comes a\r\nsad falling off. The navigable fairway shoals and shrinks, middle\r\ngrounds obstruct it, and shelving foreshores persistently deny it that\r\neasy access to the land that alone can create great seaboard cities.\r\nAll the ports of the Ems are tidal; the harbour of Delfzyl, on the\r\nDutch side, dries at low water, and Emden, the principal German port,\r\ncan only be reached by a lock and a mile of canal.\r\n\r\nBut this depreciation is only relative. Judged on its merits, and not\r\nby the standard of the Elbe, it is a very important river. Emden is a\r\nflourishing and growing port. For shallow craft the stream is navigable\r\nfar into the interior, where, aided by tributaries and allied canals\r\n(notably the connection with the Rhine at Dortmund, then approaching\r\ncompletion), it taps the resources of a great area. Strategically there\r\nwas still less reason for underrating it. It is one of the great\r\nmaritime gates of Germany; and it is the westernmost gate, the nearest\r\nto Great Britain and France, contiguous to Holland. Its great forked\r\ndelta presents two yawning breaches in that singular rampart of islets\r\nand shoals which masks the German seaboard\xe2\x80\x94a seaboard itself so short\r\nin proportion to the empire\xe2\x80\x99s bulk, that, as Davies used to say, \xe2\x80\x9cevery\r\ninch of it must be important.\xe2\x80\x9d Warships could force these breaches, and\r\nso threaten the mainland at one of its few vulnerable points. Quay\r\naccommodation is no object to such visitors; intricate navigation no\r\ndeterrent. Even the heaviest battleships could approach within striking\r\ndistance of the land, while cruisers and military transports could\r\npenetrate to the level of Emden itself. Emden, as Davies had often\r\npointed out, is connected by canal with Wilhelmshaven on the Jade, a\r\nstrategic canal, designed to carry gunboats as well as merchandise.\r\n\r\nNow Memmert was part of the outer rampart; its tapering sickle of sand\r\ndirectly commanded the eastern breach; it _must_ be connected with the\r\ndefence of this breach. No more admirable base could be imagined;\r\nself-contained and isolated, yet sheltered, accessible\xe2\x80\x94better than\r\nJuist and Borkum. And supposing it were desired to shroud the nature of\r\nthe work in absolute secrecy, what a pretext lay to hand in the wreck\r\nand its buried bullion, which lay in the offing opposite the fairway!\r\n\r\nOn Memmert was the dep\xc3\xb4t for the salvage operations. Salvage work, with\r\nits dredging and diving, offered precisely the disguise that was\r\nneeded. It was submarine, and so are some of the most important\r\ndefences of ports, mines, and dirigible torpedoes. All the details of\r\nthe story were suggestive: the \xe2\x80\x9csmall local company\xe2\x80\x9d; the \xe2\x80\x9cengineer\r\nfrom Bremen\xe2\x80\x9d (who, I wondered, was he?); the few shares held by von\r\nBr\xc3\xbcning, enough to explain his visits; the stores and gear coming from\r\nWilhelmshaven, a naval dockyard.\r\n\r\nTry as I would I could not stir Davies\xe2\x80\x99s imagination as mine was\r\nstirred. He was bent on only seeing the objections, which, of course,\r\nwere numerous enough. Could secrecy be ensured under pretext of salving\r\na wreck? It must be a secret shared by many\xe2\x80\x94divers, crews of tugs,\r\nemployees of all sorts. I answered that trade secrets are often\r\npreserved under no less difficult conditions, and why not imperial\r\nsecrets?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy the Ems and not the Elbe?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps,\xe2\x80\x9d I replied, \xe2\x80\x9cthe Elbe, too, holds similar mysteries.\xe2\x80\x9d Neuerk\r\nIsland might, for all we knew, be another Memmert; when cruising in\r\nthat region we had had no eyes for such things, absorbed in a\r\npreconceived theory of our own. Besides, we must not take ourselves too\r\nseriously. We were amateurs, not experts in coast defence, and on such\r\nvague grounds to fastidiously reject a clue which went so far as this\r\none was to quarrel with our luck. There was a disheartening corollary\r\nto this latter argument that in my new-born zeal I shut my eyes to. As\r\namateurs, were we capable of using our clue and gaining exact knowledge\r\nof the defences in question? Davies, I knew, felt this strongly, and I\r\nthink it accounted for his lukewarm view of Memmert more than he was\r\naware. He clung more obstinately than ever to his \xe2\x80\x9cchannel theory\xe2\x80\x9d,\r\nconscious that it offered the one sort of opportunity of which with his\r\npeculiar gifts he was able to take advantage. He admitted, however,\r\nthat it was under a cloud at present, for if knowledge of the coastwise\r\nnavigation were a crime in itself we should scarcely be sitting here\r\nnow. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s something to do with it, anyhow!\xe2\x80\x9d he persisted.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XVIII.\r\nImperial Escort\r\n\r\n\r\nMemmert gripped me, then, to the exclusion of a rival notion which had\r\ngiven me no little perplexity during the conversation with von Br\xc3\xbcning.\r\nHis reiterated advice that we should lose no time in picking up our\r\nanchor and chain had ended by giving me the idea that he was anxious to\r\nget us away from Bensersiel and the mainland. At first I had taken the\r\nadvice partly as a test of our veracity (as I gave the reader to\r\nunderstand), and partly as an indirect method of lulling any suspicions\r\nwhich Grimm\xe2\x80\x99s midnight visit may have caused. Then it struck me that\r\nthis might be over-subtlety on my part, and the idea recurred when the\r\nquestion of our future plans cropped up, and hampered me in deciding on\r\na course. It returned again when von Br\xc3\xbcning offered to tow us out in\r\nthe evening. It was in my mind when I questioned him as to his business\r\nashore, for it occurred to me that perhaps his landing here was not\r\nsolely due to a wish to inspect the crew of the _Dulcibella_. Then came\r\nhis perfectly frank explanation (with its sinister _double entente_ for\r\nus), coupled with an invitation to me to accompany him to Esens. But,\r\non the principle of _timeo Danaos_ etc., I instantly smelt a ruse, not\r\nthat I dreamt that I was to be decoyed into captivity; but if there was\r\nanything here which we two might discover in the few hours left to us,\r\nit was an ingenious plan to remove the most observant of the two till\r\nthe hour of departure.\r\n\r\nDavies scorned them, and I had felt only a faint curiosity in these\r\ninsignificant hamlets, influenced, I am afraid, chiefly by a hankering\r\nafter _terra firma_ which the pitiless rigour of his training had been\r\nunable to cure.\r\n\r\nBut it was imprudent to neglect the slightest chance. It was three\r\no\xe2\x80\x99clock, and I think both our brains were beginning to be addled with\r\nthinking in close confinement. I suggested that we should finish our\r\ncouncil of war in the open, and we both donned oilskins and turned out.\r\nThe sky had hardened and banked into an even canopy of lead, and the\r\nwind drove before it a fine cold rain. You could hear the murmur of the\r\nrising flood on the sands outside, but the harbour was high above it\r\nstill, and the _Dulcibella_ and the other boats squatted low in a bed\r\nof black slime. Native interest seemed to be at last assuaged, for not\r\na soul was visible on the bank (I cannot call it a quay); but the top\r\nof a black sou\xe2\x80\x99wester with a feather of smoke curling round it showed\r\nabove the forehatch of the _Kormoran_.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish I could get a look at your cargo, my friend,\xe2\x80\x9d I thought to\r\nmyself.\r\n\r\nWe gazed at Bensersiel in silence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere can\xe2\x80\x99t be anything _here_?\xe2\x80\x9d I said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat _can_ there be?\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat about that dyke?\xe2\x80\x9d I said, with a sudden inspiration.\r\n\r\nFrom the bank we could see all along the coast-line, which is dyked\r\ncontinuously, as I have already said. The dyke was here a substantial\r\nbrick-faced embankment, very similar, though on a smaller scale, to\r\nthat which had bordered the Elbe near Cuxhaven, and over whose summit\r\nwe had seen the snouts of guns.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI say, Davies,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, \xe2\x80\x9cdo you think this coast could be invaded?\r\nAlong here, I mean, behind these islands?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDavies shook his head. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve thought of that,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nnothing in it. It\xe2\x80\x99s just the very last place on earth where a landing\r\nwould be possible. No transport could get nearer than where the _Blitz_\r\nis lying, four miles out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, you say every inch of this coast is important?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, but it\xe2\x80\x99s the _water_ I mean.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I want to see that dyke. Let\xe2\x80\x99s walk along it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMy mushroom theory died directly I set foot on it. It was the most\r\ninnocent structure in the world\xe2\x80\x94like a thousand others in Essex and\r\nHolland\xe2\x80\x94topped by a narrow path, where we walked in single file with\r\narms akimbo to keep our balance in the gusts of wind. Below us lay the\r\nsands on one side and rank fens on the other, interspersed with squares\r\nof pasture ringed in with ditches. After half a mile we dropped down\r\nand came back by a short circuit inland, following a mazy path\xe2\x80\x94which\r\nwas mostly right angles and minute plank bridges, till we came to the\r\nEsens road. We crossed this and soon after found our way barred by the\r\nstream I spoke of. This involved a _d\xc3\xa9tour_ to the bridge in the\r\nvillage, and a stealthy avoidance of the post-office, for dread of its\r\ngarrulous occupant. Then we followed the dyke in the other direction,\r\nand ended by a circuit over the sands, which were fast being covered by\r\nthe tide, and so back to the yacht.\r\n\r\nNobody appeared to have taken the slightest notice of our movements.\r\n\r\nAs we walked we had tackled the last question, \xe2\x80\x9cWhat are we to do?\xe2\x80\x9d and\r\nfound very little to say on it. We were to leave to-night (unless the\r\nEsens police appeared on the scene), and were committed to sailing\r\ndirect to Norderney, as the only alternative to duck-shooting under the\r\nespionage of a \xe2\x80\x9ctrustworthy\xe2\x80\x9d nominee of von Br\xc3\xbcning\xe2\x80\x99s. Beyond\r\nthat\xe2\x80\x94vagueness and difficulty of every sort.\r\n\r\nAt Norderney I should be fettered by my letter. If it seemed to have\r\nbeen opened and it ordered my return, I was limited to a week, or must\r\nrisk suspicion by staying. Dollmann was away (according to von\r\nBr\xc3\xbcning), \xe2\x80\x9cwould probably be back soon\xe2\x80\x9d; but how soon? Beyond Norderney\r\nlay Memmert. How to probe its secret? The ardour it had roused in me\r\nwas giving way to a mortifying sense of impotence. The sight of the\r\n_Kormoran_, with her crew preparing for sea, was a pointed comment on\r\nmy diplomacy, and most of all on my ridiculous survey of the dykes.\r\nWhen all was said and done we were _prot\xc3\xa9g\xc3\xa9s_ of von Br\xc3\xbcning, and\r\ndogged by Grimm. Was it likely they would let us succeed?\r\n\r\nThe tide was swirling into the harbour in whorls of chocolate froth,\r\nand as it rose all Bensersiel, dominated as before by Herr Schenkel,\r\nstraggled down to the quay to watch the movements of shipping during\r\nthe transient but momentous hour when the mud-hole was a seaport. The\r\ncaptain\xe2\x80\x99s steam-cutter was already afloat, and her sailors busy with\r\nsidelights and engines. When it became known that we, too, were to\r\nsail, and under such distinguished escort, the excitement intensified.\r\n\r\nAgain our friend of the Customs was spreading out papers to sign, while\r\na throng of helpful Frisians, headed by the twin giants of the\r\npost-boat, thronged our decks and made us ready for sea in their own\r\nconfused fashion. Again we were carried up to the inn and overwhelmed\r\nwith advice, and warnings, and farewell toasts. Then back again to find\r\nthe _Dulcibella_ afloat, and von Br\xc3\xbcning just arrived, cursing the\r\nweather and the mud, chaffing Davies, genial and _d\xc3\xa9bonnaire_ as ever.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStow that mainsail, you won\xe2\x80\x99t want it,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll tow you right\r\nout to Spiekeroog. It\xe2\x80\x99s your only anchorage for the night in this\r\nwind\xe2\x80\x94under the island, near the _Blitz_, and that would mean a dead\r\nbeat for you in the dark.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe fact was so true, and the offer so timely, that Davies\xe2\x80\x99s faint\r\nprotests were swept aside in a torrent of ridicule.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd now I think of it,\xe2\x80\x9d the Commander ended, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll make the trip with\r\nyou, if I may. It\xe2\x80\x99ll be pleasanter and drier.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe all three boarded the _Dulcibella_, and then the end came. Our\r\ntow-rope was attached, and at half-past six the little launch jumped\r\ninto the collar, and amidst a demonstration that could not have been\r\nmore hearty if we had been ambassadors on a visit to a friendly power,\r\nwe sidled out through the jetties.\r\n\r\nIt took us more than an hour to cover the five miles to Spiekeroog, for\r\nthe _Dulcibella_ was a heavy load in the stiff head wind, and Davies,\r\nthough he said nothing, showed undisguised distrust of our tug\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ncapacities. He at once left the helm to me and flung himself on the\r\ngear, not resting till every rope was ready to hand, the mainsail\r\nreefed, the binnacle lighted, and all ready for setting sail or\r\nanchoring at a moment\xe2\x80\x99s notice. Our guest watched these precautions\r\nwith infinite amusement. He was in the highest and most mischievous\r\nhumour, raining banter on Davies and mock sympathy on me, laughing at\r\nour huge compass, heaving the lead himself, startling us with imaginary\r\nsoundings, and doubting if his men were sober. I offered entertainment\r\nand warmth below, but he declined on the ground that Davies would be\r\ntempted to cut the tow-rope and make us pass the night on a safe\r\nsandbank. Davies took the raillery unmoved. His work done, he took the\r\ntiller and sat bareheaded, intent on the launch, the course, the\r\ndetails, and chances of the present. I brought up cigars and we settled\r\nourselves facing him, our backs to the wind and spray. And so we made\r\nthe rest of the passage, von Br\xc3\xbcning cuddled against me and the\r\ncabin-hatch, alternately shouting a jest to Davies and talking to me in\r\na light and charming vein, with just that shade of patronage that the\r\ndisparity in our ages warranted, about my time in Germany, places,\r\npeople, and books I knew, and about life, especially young men\xe2\x80\x99s life,\r\nin England, a country he had never visited, but hoped to; I responding\r\nas well as I could, striving to meet his mood, acquit myself like a\r\nman, draw zest instead of humiliation from the irony of our position,\r\nbut scarcely able to make headway against a numbing sense of defeat and\r\nincapacity. A queer thought was haunting me, too, that such skill and\r\njudgement as I possessed was slipping from me as we left the land and\r\nfaced again the rigours of this exacting sea. Davies, I very well knew,\r\nwas under exactly the opposite spell\xe2\x80\x94a spell which even the reproach of\r\nthe tow-rope could not annul. His face, in the glow of the binnacle,\r\nwas beginning to wear that same look of contentment and resolve that I\r\nhad seen on it that night we had sailed to Kiel from Schlei Fiord.\r\nHeaven knows he had more cause for worry than I\xe2\x80\x94a casual comrade in an\r\nadventure which was peculiarly his, which meant everything on earth to\r\nhim; but there he was, washing away perplexity in the salt wind,\r\ndrawing counsel and confidence from the unfailing source of all his\r\ninspirations\xe2\x80\x94the sea.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLooks happy, doesn\xe2\x80\x99t he?\xe2\x80\x9d said the captain once. I grunted that he\r\ndid, ashamed to find how irritated the remark made me.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll remember what I said,\xe2\x80\x9d he added in my ear.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I should like to see her. What _is_ she like?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDangerous.\xe2\x80\x9d I could well believe it.\r\n\r\nThe hull of the _Blitz_ loomed up, and a minute later our kedge was\r\nsplashing overboard and the launch was backing alongside.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood-night, gentlemen,\xe2\x80\x9d said our passenger. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re safe enough here,\r\nand you can run across in ten minutes in the morning and pick up your\r\nanchor, if it\xe2\x80\x99s there still. Then you\xe2\x80\x99ve a fair wind west\xe2\x80\x94to England if\r\nyou like. If you decide to stay a little longer in these parts, and I\xe2\x80\x99m\r\nin reach, count on me to help you, to sport or anything else.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe thanked him, shook hands, and he was gone.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s a thundering good chap, anyhow,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies; and I heartily\r\nagreed.\r\n\r\nThe narrow vigilant life began again at once. We were \xe2\x80\x9csafe enough\xe2\x80\x9d in\r\na sense, but a warp and a twenty-pound anchor were poor security if the\r\nwind backed or increased. Plans for contingencies had to be made, and\r\ndeck-watches kept till midnight, when the weather seemed to improve,\r\nand stars appeared. The glass was rising, so we turned in and slept\r\nunder the very wing, so to speak, of the Imperial Government.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDavies,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, when we were settled in our bunks, \xe2\x80\x9cit\xe2\x80\x99s only a day\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nsail to Norderney, isn\xe2\x80\x99t it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWith a fair wind, less, if we go outside the islands direct.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it\xe2\x80\x99s settled that we do that to-morrow?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose so. We\xe2\x80\x99ve got to get the anchor first. Good-night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XIX.\r\nThe Rubicon\r\n\r\n\r\nIt was a cold, vaporous dawn, the glass rising, and the wind fallen to\r\na light air still from the north-east. Our creased and sodden sails\r\nscarcely answered to it as we crept across the oily swell to Langeoog.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFogs and calms,\xe2\x80\x9d Davies prophesied. The _Blitz_ was astir when we\r\npassed her, and soon after steamed out to sea. Once over the bar, she\r\nturned westward and was lost to view in the haze. I should be sorry to\r\nhave to explain how we found that tiny anchor-buoy, on the\r\nexpressionless waste of grey. I only know that I hove the lead\r\nincessantly while Davies conned, till at last he was grabbing overside\r\nwith the boathook, and there was the buoy on deck. The cable was soon\r\nfollowing it, and finally the rusty monster himself, more loathsome\r\nthan usual, after his long sojourn in the slime.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s all right,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies. \xe2\x80\x9cNow we can go anywhere.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it\xe2\x80\x99s Norderney, isn\xe2\x80\x99t it? We\xe2\x80\x99ve settled that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I suppose we have. I was wondering whether it wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be\r\nshortest to go inside Langeoog after all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSurely not,\xe2\x80\x9d I urged. \xe2\x80\x9cThe tide\xe2\x80\x99s ebbing now, and the light\xe2\x80\x99s bad;\r\nit\xe2\x80\x99s new ground, with a \xe2\x80\x98watershed\xe2\x80\x99 to cross, and we\xe2\x80\x99re safe to get\r\naground.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right\xe2\x80\x94outside. Ready about.\xe2\x80\x9d We swung lazily round and headed for\r\nthe open sea.
