I record the fact, but in truth Davies might have taken\r\nme where he liked, for no land was visible, only a couple of ghostly\r\nbooms.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt seems a pity to miss over that channel,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies with a sigh;\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cjust when the _Kormoran_ can\xe2\x80\x99t watch us.\xe2\x80\x9d (We had not seen her at all\r\nthis morning.)\r\n\r\nI set myself to the lead again, averse to reopening a barren argument.\r\nGrimm had done his work for the present, I felt certain, and was on his\r\nway by the shortest road to Norderney and Memmert.\r\n\r\nWe were soon outside and heading west, our boom squared away and the\r\nisland sand-dunes just apparent under our lee. Then the breeze died to\r\nthe merest draught, and left us rolling inert in a long swell. Consumed\r\nwith impatience to get on I saw fatality in this failure of wind, after\r\na fortnight of unprofitable meanderings, when we had generally had too\r\nmuch of it, and always enough for our purpose. I tried to read below,\r\nbut the vile squirting of the centreboard drove me up.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan\xe2\x80\x99t we go any faster?\xe2\x80\x9d I burst out once. I felt that there ought to\r\nbe a pyramid of gauzy canvas aloft, spinnakers, flying jibs and what\r\nnot.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t go in for speed,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, shortly. He loyally did his\r\nbest to \xe2\x80\x9cshove her\xe2\x80\x9d along, but puffs and calms were the rule all day,\r\nand it was only by towing in the dinghy for two hours in the afternoon\r\nthat we covered the length of Langeoog, and crept before dark to an\r\nanchorage behind Baltrum, its slug-shaped neighbour on the west.\r\nStrictly, I believe, we should have kept the sea all night; but I had\r\nnot the grit to suggest that course, and Davies was only too glad of an\r\nexcuse for threading the shoals of the Accumer Ee on a rising tide. The\r\natmosphere had been slowly clearing as the day wore on; but we had\r\nscarcely anchored ten minutes before a blanket of white fog, rolling in\r\nfrom seaward, swallowed us up. Davies was already afield in the dinghy,\r\nand I had to guide him back with a foghorn, whose music roused hosts of\r\nsea birds from the surrounding flats, and brought them wheeling and\r\ncomplaining round us, a weird invisible chorus to my mournful solo.\r\n\r\nThe fog hung heavy still at daybreak on the 20th, but dispersed\r\npartially under a catspaw from the south about eight o\xe2\x80\x99clock, in time\r\nfor us to traverse the boomed channel behind Baltrum, before the tide\r\nleft the watershed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe shan\xe2\x80\x99t get far to-day,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, with philosophy. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd this\r\nsort of thing may go on for any time. It\xe2\x80\x99s a regular autumn\r\nanti-cyclone\xe2\x80\x94glass thirty point five and steady. That gale was the last\r\nof a stormy equinox.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe took the inside route as a matter of course to-day. It was now the\r\nshortest to Norderney harbour, and scarcely less intricate than the\r\nWichter Ee, which appeared to be almost totally blocked by banks, and\r\nis, in fact, the most impassable of all these outlets to the North Sea.\r\nBut, as I say, this sort of navigation, always puzzling to me, was\r\nutterly bewildering in hazy weather. Any attempt at orientation made me\r\ngiddy. So I slaved at the lead, varying my labour with a fierce bout of\r\nkedge-work when we grounded somewhere. I had two rests before two\r\no\xe2\x80\x99clock, one of an hour, when we ran into a patch of windless fog;\r\nanother of a few moments, when Davies said, \xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s Norderney!\xe2\x80\x9d and I\r\nsaw, surmounting a long slope of weedy sand, still wet with the\r\nreceding sea, a cluster of sandhills exactly like a hundred others I\r\nhad seen of late, but fraught with a new and unique interest.\r\n\r\nThe usual formula, \xe2\x80\x9cWhat have you got now?\xe2\x80\x9d checked my reverie, and\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHelm\xe2\x80\x99s a-lee,\xe2\x80\x9d ended it for the time. We tacked on (for the wind had\r\nheaded us) in very shoal water.\r\n\r\nSuddenly Davies said: \xe2\x80\x9cIs that a boat ahead?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you mean that galliot?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked. I could plainly distinguish one of\r\nthose familiar craft about half a mile away, just within the limit of\r\nvision.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe _Kormoran_, do you think?\xe2\x80\x9d I added. Davies said nothing, but grew\r\ninattentive to his work. \xe2\x80\x9cBarely four,\xe2\x80\x9d from me passed unnoticed, and\r\nwe touched once, but swung off under some play of the current. Then\r\ncame abruptly, \xe2\x80\x9cStand by the anchor. Let go,\xe2\x80\x9d and we brought up in\r\nmid-stream of the narrow creek we were following. I triced up the\r\nmain-tack, and stowed the headsails unaided. When I had done Davies was\r\nstill gazing to windward through his binoculars, and, to my\r\nastonishment, I noticed that his hands were trembling violently. I had\r\nnever seen this happen before, even at moments when a false turn of the\r\nwrist meant death on a surf-battered bank.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked; \xe2\x80\x9care you cold?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat little boat,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. I gazed to windward, too, and now saw a\r\nscrap of white in the distance, in sharp relief.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSmall standing lug and jib; it\xe2\x80\x99s her, right enough,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies to\r\nhimself, in a sort of nervous stammer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho? What?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Medusa_\xe2\x80\x99s dinghy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe handed, or rather pushed, me the glasses, still gazing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDollmann?\xe2\x80\x9d I exclaimed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, it\xe2\x80\x99s _hers_\xe2\x80\x94the one she always sails. She\xe2\x80\x99s come to meet m\xe2\x80\x94, us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThrough the glasses the white scrap became a graceful little sail,\r\nsquared away for the light following breeze. An angle of the creek hid\r\nthe hull, then it glided into view. Someone was sitting aft steering,\r\nman or woman I could not say, for the sail hid most of the figure. For\r\nfull two minutes\xe2\x80\x94two long, pregnant minutes\xe2\x80\x94we watched it in silence.\r\nThe damp air was fogging the lenses, but I kept them to my eyes; for I\r\ndid not want to look at Davies. At last I heard him draw a deep breath,\r\nstraighten himself up, and give one of his characteristic \xe2\x80\x9ch\xe2\x80\x99ms\xe2\x80\x9d. Then\r\nhe turned briskly aft, cast off the dinghy\xe2\x80\x99s painter, and pulled her up\r\nalongside.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou come too,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, jumping in, and fixing the rowlocks. (His\r\nhands were steady again.) I laughed, and shoved the dinghy off.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99d rather you did,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, defiantly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99d rather stay. I\xe2\x80\x99ll tidy up, and put the kettle on.\xe2\x80\x9d Davies had\r\ntaken a half stroke, but paused.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe oughtn\xe2\x80\x99t to come aboard.\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe might like to,\xe2\x80\x9d I suggested. \xe2\x80\x9cChilly day, long way from home,\r\ncommon courtesy\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCarruthers,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, \xe2\x80\x9cif she comes aboard, please remember that\r\nshe\xe2\x80\x99s outside this business. There are no clues to be got from _her_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA little lecture which would have nettled me more if I had not been\r\nexultantly telling myself that, once and for all, for good or ill, the\r\nRubicon was passed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s your affair this time,\xe2\x80\x9d I said; \xe2\x80\x9crun it as you please.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe sculled away with vigorous strokes. \xe2\x80\x9cJust as he is,\xe2\x80\x9d I thought to\r\nmyself: bare head, beaded with fog-dew, ancient oilskin coat (only one\r\nbutton); grey jersey; grey woollen trousers (like a deep-sea\r\nfisherman\xe2\x80\x99s) stuffed into long boots. A vision of his antitype, the\r\nCowes Philanderer, crossed me for a second. As to his face\xe2\x80\x94well, I\r\ncould only judge by it, and marvel, that he was gripping his dilemma by\r\neither horn, as firmly as he gripped his sculls.\r\n\r\nI watched the two boats converging. They would meet in the natural\r\ncourse about three hundred yards away, but a hitch occurred. First, the\r\nsail-boat checked and slewed; \xe2\x80\x9caground,\xe2\x80\x9d I concluded. The rowboat leapt\r\nforward still; then checked, too. From both a great splashing of sculls\r\nfloated across the still air, then silence. The summit of the\r\nwatershed, a physical Rubicon, prosaic and slimy, had still to be\r\ncrossed, it seemed. But it could be evaded. Both boats headed for the\r\nnorthern side of the creek: two figures were out on the brink, hauling\r\non two painters. Then Davies was striding over the sand, and a girl\xe2\x80\x94I\r\ncould see her now\xe2\x80\x94was coming to meet him. And then I thought it was\r\ntime to go below and tidy up.\r\n\r\nNothing on earth could have made the _Dulcibella_\xe2\x80\x99s saloon a worthy\r\nreception-room for a lady. I could only use hurried efforts to make it\r\nlook its best by plying a bunch of cotton-waste and a floor-brush; by\r\npitching into racks and lockers the litter of pipes, charts, oddments\r\nof apparel, and so on, that had a way of collecting afresh, however\r\nrecently we had tidied up; by neatly arranging our demoralised library,\r\nand by lighting the stove and veiling the table under a clean white\r\ncloth.\r\n\r\nI suppose about twenty minutes had elapsed, and I was scrubbing\r\nfruitlessly at the smoky patch on the ceiling, when I heard the sound\r\nof oars and voices outside. I threw the cotton-waste into the fo\xe2\x80\x99c\xe2\x80\x99sle,\r\nmade an onslaught on my hands, and then mounted the companion ladder.\r\nOur own dinghy was just rounding up alongside, Davies sculling in the\r\nbows, facing him in the stern a young girl in a grey tam-o\xe2\x80\x99-shanter,\r\nloose waterproof jacket and dark serge skirt, the latter, to be\r\nfrigidly accurate, disclosing a pair of workman-like rubber boots\r\nwhich, _mutatis mutandis,_ were very like those Davies was wearing. Her\r\nhair, like his, was spangled with moisture, and her rose-brown skin\r\nstruck a note of delicious colour against the sullen Stygian\r\nbackground.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere he is,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies. Never did his \xe2\x80\x9cmeiner Freund, Carruthers,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsound so pleasantly in my ears; never so discordantly the \xe2\x80\x9cFr\xc3\xa4ulein\r\nDollmann\xe2\x80\x9d that followed it. Every syllable of the four was a lie. Two\r\nhonest English eyes were looking up into mine; an honest English\r\nhand\xe2\x80\x94is this insular nonsense? Perhaps so, but I stick to it\xe2\x80\x94a brown,\r\nfirm hand\xe2\x80\x94no, not so very small, my sentimental reader\xe2\x80\x94was clasping\r\nmine. Of course I had strong reasons, apart from the racial instinct,\r\nfor thinking her to be English, but I believe that if I had had none at\r\nall I should at any rate have congratulated Germany on a clever bit of\r\nplagiarism. By her voice, when she spoke, I knew that she must have\r\ntalked German habitually from childhood; diction and accent were\r\nfaultless, at least to my English ear; but the native constitutional\r\nring was wanting.\r\n\r\nShe came on board. There was a hollow discussion first about time and\r\nweather, but it ended as we all in our hearts wished it to end. None of\r\nus uttered our real scruples. Mine, indeed, were too new and\r\nrudimentary to be worth uttering, so I said common-sense things about\r\ntea and warmth; but I began to think about my compact with Davies.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust for a few minutes, then,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\nI held out my hand and swung her up. She gazed round the deck and\r\nrigging with profound interest\xe2\x80\x94a breathless, hungry interest\xe2\x80\x94touching\r\nto see.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve seen her before, haven\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x9d I said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve not been on board before,\xe2\x80\x9d she answered.\r\n\r\nThis struck me in passing as odd; but then I had only too few details\r\nfrom Davies about his days at Norderney in September.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course, _that_ is what puzzled me,\xe2\x80\x9d she exclaimed, suddenly,\r\npointing to the mizzen. \xe2\x80\x9cI knew there was something different.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDavies had belayed the painter, and now had to explain the origin of\r\nthe mizzen. This was a cumbrous process, and his hearer\xe2\x80\x99s attention\r\nsoon wandered from the subject and became centred in him\xe2\x80\x94his was\r\nalready more than half in her\xe2\x80\x94and the result was a golden opportunity\r\nfor the discerning onlooker. It was very brief, but I made the most of\r\nit; buried deep a few regrets, did a little heartfelt penance, told\r\nmyself I had been a cynical fool not to have foreseen this, and faced\r\nthe new situation with a sinking heart; I am not ashamed to admit that,\r\nfor I was fond of Davies, and I was keen about the quest.\r\n\r\nShe had never been a guilty agent in that attempt on Davies. Had she\r\nbeen an unconscious tool or only an unwilling one? If the latter, did\r\nshe know the secret we were seeking? In the last degree unlikely, I\r\ndecided. But, true to the compact, whose importance I now fully\r\nappreciated, I flung aside my diplomatic weapons, recoiling, as\r\nstrongly, or nearly as strongly, let us say, from any effort direct or\r\nindirect to gain information from such a source. It was not our fault\r\nif by her own conversation and behaviour she gave us some idea of how\r\nmatters stood. Davies already knew more than I did.\r\n\r\nWe spent a few minutes on deck while she asked eager questions about\r\nour build and gear and seaworthiness, with a quaint mixture of\r\nprofessional acumen and personal curiosity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow _did_ you manage alone that day?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked Davies, suddenly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, it was quite safe,\xe2\x80\x9d was the reply. \xe2\x80\x9cBut it\xe2\x80\x99s much better to have a\r\nfriend.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe looked at me; and\xe2\x80\x94well, I would have died for Davies there and\r\nthen.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFather said you would be safe,\xe2\x80\x9d she remarked, with decision\xe2\x80\x94a slight\r\nexcess of decision, I thought. And at that turned to some rope or block\r\nand pursued her questioning. She found the compass impressive, and the\r\ntrappings of that hateful centreboard had a peculiar fascination for\r\nher. Was this the way we did it in England? was her constant query.\r\n\r\nYet, in spite of a superficial freedom, we were all shy and\r\nconstrained. The descent below was a welcome diversion, for we should\r\nhave been less than human if we had not extracted some spontaneous fun\r\nfrom the humours of the saloon. I went down first to see about the tea,\r\nleaving them struggling for mutual comprehension over the theory of an\r\nEnglish lifeboat. They soon followed, and I can see her now stooping in\r\nat the doorway, treading delicately, like a kitten, past the\r\nobstructive centreboard to a place on the starboard sofa, then taking\r\nin her surroundings with a timid rapture that broke into delight at all\r\nthe primitive arrangements and dingy amenities of our den. She explored\r\nthe cavernous recesses of the Rippingille, fingered the duck-guns and\r\nthe miscellany in the racks, and peeped into the fo\xe2\x80\x99c\xe2\x80\x99sle with dainty\r\nawe. Everything was a source of merriment, from our cramped attitudes\r\nto the painful deficiency of spoons and the \xe2\x80\x9cyachtiness\xe2\x80\x9d (there is no\r\nother word to describe it) of the bread, which had been bought at\r\nBensersiel, and had suffered from incarceration and the climate. This\r\nfact came out, and led to some questions, while we waited for the water\r\nto boil, about the gale and our visit there. The topic, a pregnant one\r\nfor us, appeared to have no special significance to her. At the mention\r\nof von Br\xc3\xbcning she showed no emotion of any sort; on the contrary, she\r\nwent out of her way, from an innocent motive that anyone could have\r\nguessed, to show that she could talk about him with dispassionate\r\ndetachment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe came to see us when you were here last, didn\xe2\x80\x99t he?\xe2\x80\x9d she said to\r\nDavies. \xe2\x80\x9cHe often comes. He goes with father to Memmert sometimes. You\r\nknow about Memmert? They are diving for money out of an old wreck.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, we had heard about it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course you have. Father is a director of the company, and Commander\r\nvon Br\xc3\xbcning takes great interest in it; they took me down in a\r\ndiving-bell once.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI murmured, \xe2\x80\x9cIndeed!\xe2\x80\x9d and Davies sawed laboriously at the bread. She\r\nmust have misconstrued our sheepish silence, for she stopped and drew\r\nherself up with just a touch of momentary _hauteur_, utterly lost on\r\nDavies. I could have laughed aloud at this transient little comedy of\r\nerrors.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you see any gold?\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies at last, with husky solemnity.\r\nSomething had to be said or we should defeat our own end; but I let him\r\nsay it. He had not my faith in Memmert.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, only mud and timber\xe2\x80\x94oh, I forgot\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou mustn\xe2\x80\x99t betray the company\xe2\x80\x99s secrets,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, laughing;\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCommander von Br\xc3\xbcning wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t tell us a word about the gold.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n(\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s self-denial!\xe2\x80\x9d I said to myself.)\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I don\xe2\x80\x99t think it matters much,\xe2\x80\x9d she answered, laughing too. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\r\nare only visitors.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s all,\xe2\x80\x9d I remarked, demurely. \xe2\x80\x9cJust passing travellers.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will stop at Norderney?\xe2\x80\x9d she said, with na\xc3\xafve anxiety. \xe2\x80\x9cHerr\r\nDavies said\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI looked to Davies; it was his affair. Fair and square came his answer,\r\nin blunt dog-German.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, of course, we shall. I should like to see your father again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nUp to this moment I had been doubtful of his final decision; for ever\r\nsince our explanation at Bensersiel I had had the feeling that I was\r\nholding his nose to a very cruel grindstone. This straight word, clear\r\nand direct, beyond anything I had hoped for, brought me to my senses\r\nand showed me that his mind had been working far in advance of mine;\r\nand more, shaping a double purpose that I had never dreamt of.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy father?\xe2\x80\x9d said Fr\xc3\xa4ulein Dollmann; \xe2\x80\x9cyes, I am sure he will be very\r\nglad to see you.\r\n\r\nThere was no conviction in her tone, and her eyes were distant and\r\ntroubled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s not at home now, is he?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow did you know?\xe2\x80\x9d (a little maidenly confusion). \xe2\x80\x9cOh, Commander von\r\nBr\xc3\xbcning.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI might have added that it had been clear as daylight all along that\r\nthis visit was in the nature of an escapade of which her father might\r\nnot approve. I tried to say \xe2\x80\x9cI won\xe2\x80\x99t tell,\xe2\x80\x9d without words, and may have\r\nsucceeded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI told Mr Davies when we first met,\xe2\x80\x9d she went on. \xe2\x80\x9cI expect him back\r\nvery soon\xe2\x80\x94to-morrow in fact; he wrote from Amsterdam. He left me at\r\nHamburg and has been away since. Of course, he will not know your yacht\r\nis back again. I think he expected Mr Davies would stay in the Baltic,\r\nas the season was so late. But\xe2\x80\x94but I am sure he will be glad to see\r\nyou.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs the _Medusa_ in harbour?\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; but we are not living on her now. We are at our villa in the\r\nSchwannall\xc3\xa9e\xe2\x80\x94my stepmother and I, that is.\xe2\x80\x9d She added some details, and\r\nDavies gravely pencilled down the address on a leaf of the log-book; a\r\nformality which somehow seemed to regularise the present position.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe shall be at Norderney to-morrow,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\nMeanwhile the kettle was boiling merrily, and I made the tea\xe2\x80\x94cocoa, I\r\nshould say, for the menu was changed in deference to our visitor\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ntastes. \xe2\x80\x9cThis _is_ fun!\xe2\x80\x9d she said. And by common consent we abandoned\r\nourselves, three youthful, hungry mariners, to the enjoyment of this\r\nimpromptu picnic. Such a chance might never occur again\xe2\x80\x94_carpamus\r\ndiem._\r\n\r\nBut the banquet was never celebrated. As at Belshazzar\xe2\x80\x99s feast, there\r\nwas a writing on the wall; no supernatural inscription, but just a\r\nprinted name; an English surname with title and initials, in cheap gilt\r\nlettering on the back of an old book; a silent, sneering witness of our\r\nsnug party. The catastrophe came and passed so suddenly that at the\r\ntime I had scarcely even an inkling of what caused it; but I know now\r\nthat this is how it happened. Our visitor was sitting at the forward\r\nend of the starboard sofa, close to the bulkhead. Davies and I were\r\nopposite her. Across the bulkhead, on a level with our heads, ran the\r\nbookshelf, whose contents, remember, I had carefully straightened only\r\nhalf an hour ago, little dreaming of the consequence. Some trifle,\r\nprobably the logbook which Davies had reached down from the shelf,\r\ncalled her attention to the rest of our library. While busied with the\r\ncocoa I heard her spelling out some titles, fingering leaves, and\r\ntwitting Davies with the little care he took of his books. Suddenly\r\nthere was a silence which made me look up, to see a startled and\r\npitiful change in her. She was staring at Davies with wide eyes and\r\nparted lips, a burning flush mounting on her forehead, and such an\r\nexpression on her face as a sleep-walker might wear, who wakes in fear\r\nhe knows not where.\r\n\r\nHalf her mind was far away, labouring to construe some hideous dream of\r\nthe past; half was in the present, cringing before some sickening\r\nreality. She remained so for perhaps ten seconds, and then\xe2\x80\x94plucky girl\r\nthat she was\xe2\x80\x94she mastered herself, looked deliberately round and up\r\nwith a circular glance, strangely in the manner of Davies himself, and\r\nspoke. How late it was, she must be going\xe2\x80\x94her boat was not safe. At the\r\nsame time she rose to go, or rather slid herself along the sofa, for\r\nrising was impossible. We sat like mannerless louts, in blank\r\namazement. Davies at the outset had said, \xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the matter?\xe2\x80\x9d in plain\r\nEnglish, and then relapsed into stupefaction. I recovered myself the\r\nfirst, and protested in some awkward fashion about the cocoa, the time,\r\nthe absence of fog. In trying to answer, her self-possession broke\r\ndown, poor child, and her retreat became a blind flight, like that of a\r\nwounded animal, while every sordid circumstance seemed to accentuate\r\nher panic.\r\n\r\nShe tilted the corner of the table in leaving the sofa and spilt cocoa\r\nover her skirt; she knocked her head with painful force against the\r\nsharp lintel of the doorway, and stumbled on the steps of the ladder. I\r\nwas close behind, but when I reached the deck she was already on the\r\ncounter hauling up the dinghy. She had even jumped in and laid hands on\r\nthe sculls before any check came in her precipitate movements. Now\r\nthere occurred to her the patent fact that the dinghy was ours, and\r\nthat someone must accompany her to bring it back.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDavies will row you over,\xe2\x80\x9d I said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no, thank you,\xe2\x80\x9d she stammered. \xe2\x80\x9cIf you will be so kind, Herr\r\nCarruthers. It is your turn. No, I mean, I want\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo on,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies to me in English.\r\n\r\nI stepped into the dinghy and motioned to take the sculls from her. She\r\nseemed not to see me, and pushed off while Davies handed down her\r\njacket, which she had left in the cabin. Neither of us tried to better\r\nthe situation by conventional apologies. It was left to her, at the\r\nlast moment, to make a show of excusing herself, an attempt so brave\r\nand yet so wretchedly lame that I tingled all over with hot shame. She\r\nonly made matters worse, and Davies interrupted her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Auf Wiedersehen_,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, simply.\r\n\r\nShe shook her head, did not even offer her hand, and pulled away;\r\nDavies turned sharp round and went below.\r\n\r\nThere was now no muddy Rubicon to obstruct us, for the tide had risen a\r\ngood deal, and the sands were covering. I offered again to take the\r\nsculls, but she took no notice and rowed on, so that I was a silent\r\npassenger on the stern seat till we reached her boat, a spruce little\r\nyacht\xe2\x80\x99s gig, built to the native model, with a spoon-bow and tiny\r\nlee-boards. It was already afloat, but riding quite safely to a rope\r\nand a little grapnel, which she proceeded to haul in.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was quite safe after all, you see,\xe2\x80\x9d I said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, but I could not stay. Herr Carruthers, I want to say something to\r\nyou.\xe2\x80\x9d (I knew it was coming; von Br\xc3\xbcning\xe2\x80\x99s warning over again.) \xe2\x80\x9cI made\r\na mistake just now; it is no use your calling on us to-morrow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy not?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will not see my father.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought you said he was coming back?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, by the morning steamer; but he will be very busy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe can wait. We have several days to spare, and we have to call for\r\nletters anyhow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must not delay on our account. The weather is very fine at last.\r\nIt would be a pity to lose a chance of a smooth voyage to England. The\r\nseason\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe have no fixed plans. Davies wants to get some shooting.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy father will be much occupied.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe can see _you_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI insisted on being obtuse, for though this fencing with an unstrung\r\ngirl was hateful work, the quest was at stake. We were going to\r\nNorderney, come what might, and sooner or later we must see Dollmann.\r\nIt was no use promising not to. I had given no pledge to von Br\xc3\xbcning,\r\nand I would give none to her. The only alternative was to violate the\r\ncompact (which the present fiasco had surely weakened), speak out, and\r\ntry and make an ally of her. Against her own father? I shrank from the\r\nresponsibility and counted the cost of failure\xe2\x80\x94certain failure, to\r\njudge by her conduct. She began to hoist her lugsail in a dazed,\r\nshiftless fashion, while our two boats drifted slowly to leeward.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFather might not like it,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, so low and from such tremulous\r\nlips that I scarcely caught her words. \xe2\x80\x9cHe does not like foreigners\r\nmuch. I am afraid . . . he did not want to see Herr Davies again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I thought\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was wrong of me to come aboard\xe2\x80\x94I suddenly remembered; but I could\r\nnot tell Herr Davies.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI see,\xe2\x80\x9d I answered. \xe2\x80\x9cI will tell him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, that he must not come near us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe will understand. I know he will be very sorry, but,\xe2\x80\x9d I added,\r\nfirmly, \xe2\x80\x9cyou can trust him implicitly to do the right thing.\xe2\x80\x9d And how I\r\nprayed that this would content her! Thank Heaven, it did.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cI am afraid I did not say good-bye to him. You will\r\ndo so?\xe2\x80\x9d She gave me her hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne thing more,\xe2\x80\x9d I added, holding it, \xe2\x80\x9cnothing had better be said\r\nabout this meeting?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no, nothing. It must never be known.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI let go the gig\xe2\x80\x99s gunwale and watched her tighten her sheet and make a\r\ntack or two to windward. Then I rowed back to the _Dulcibella_ as hard\r\nas I could.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XX.\r\nThe Little Drab Book\r\n\r\n\r\nI found Davies at the cabin table, surrounded with a litter of books.\r\nThe shelf was empty, and its contents were tossed about among the cups\r\nand on the floor. We both spoke together.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, what was it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, what did she say?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI gave way, and told my story briefly. He listened in silence, drumming\r\non the table with a book which he held.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s not good-bye,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I don\xe2\x80\x99t wonder; look here!\xe2\x80\x9d and he\r\nheld out to me a small volume, whose appearance was quite familiar to\r\nme, if its contents were less so. As I noted in an early chapter,\r\nDavies\xe2\x80\x99s library, excluding tide-tables, \xe2\x80\x9cpilots\xe2\x80\x9d, etc., was limited to\r\ntwo classes of books, those on naval warfare, and those on his own\r\nhobby, cruising in small yachts. This\r\nparticular volume was\xe2\x80\x94no, I had better not describe it too fully; but I\r\nwill say that it was old and unpretentious, bound in cheap cloth of a\r\nrather antiquated style, with a title which showed it to be a guide for\r\nyachtsmen to a certain British estuary. A white label partly scratched\r\naway bore the legend \xe2\x80\x9c3_d_.\xe2\x80\x9d I had glanced at it once or twice with no\r\nspecial interest.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell?\xe2\x80\x9d I said, turning over some yellow pages.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDollmann!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Davies. \xe2\x80\x9cDollmann wrote it.\xe2\x80\x9d I turned to the\r\ntitle-page, and read: \xe2\x80\x9cBy Lieut. Davies went on: \xe2\x80\x9cThe name\xe2\x80\x99s\r\non the back, too\xe2\x80\x94and I\xe2\x80\x99m certain it\xe2\x80\x99s the last she looked at.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut how do you know?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd there\xe2\x80\x99s the man himself. Ass that I am not to have seen it before!\r\nLook at the frontispiece.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was a sorry piece of illustration of the old-fashioned sort, lacking\r\ndefinition and finish, but effective notwithstanding; for it was\r\nevidently the reproduction, though a cheap and imperfect process, of a\r\nphotograph. It represented a small yacht at anchor below some woods,\r\nwith the owner standing on deck in his shirt sleeves: a well-knit,\r\npowerful man, young, of middle height, clean-shaved. The\r\npublisher\xe2\x80\x99s date on the title-page was the same.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSixteen years ago,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies. \xe2\x80\x9cHe looks thirty odd in that, doesn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nhe? So I said\r\nsomething vague, and then she asked the very question von Br\xc3\xbcning did,\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Wasn\xe2\x80\x99t there a _schrecklich_ sea round the Scharhorn?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe didn\xe2\x80\x99t know you took the short cut, then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; he hadn\xe2\x80\x99t dared to tell her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe knew that _they_ took it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. Now\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut _she\xe2\x80\x99ll_ tell him we\xe2\x80\x99re back,\xe2\x80\x9d interrupted Davies.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think so. There\xe2\x80\x99s a railway\r\nthere from Norden, and a steam ferry crosses to the island.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt what time?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour Bradshaw will tell us\xe2\x80\x94here it is: \xe2\x80\x98Winter Service, 8.30 a.m., due\r\nat 9.5.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet\xe2\x80\x99s get away at once.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe had a tussle with the tide at first, but once over the watershed the\r\nchannel improved, and the haze lightened gradually. A lighthouse\r\nappeared among the sand-dunes on the island shore, and before darkness\r\nfell we dimly saw the spires and roofs of a town, and two long black\r\npiers stretching out southwards. We were scarcely a mile away when we\r\nlost our wind altogether, and had to anchor. Determined to reach our\r\ndestination that night we waited till the ebb stream made, and then\r\ntowed the yacht with the dinghy. In the course of this a fog dropped on\r\nus suddenly, just as it had yesterday. I was towing at the time, and,\r\nof course, stopped short; but Davies shouted to me from the tiller to\r\ngo on, that he could manage with the lead and compass. And the end of\r\nit was that, at about nine o\xe2\x80\x99clock, we anchored safely in the\r\nfive-fathom roadstead, close to the eastern pier, as a short\r\nreconnaissance proved to us. It had been a little masterpiece of adroit\r\nseamanship.\r\n\r\nThere was utter stillness till our chain rattled down, when a muffled\r\nshout came from the direction of the pier, and soon we heard a boat\r\ngroping out to us. It was a polite but sleepy port-officer, who asked\r\nin a perfunctory way for our particulars, and when he heard them,\r\nremembered the _Dulcibella_\xe2\x80\x99s previous visit.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere are you bound to?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEngland\xe2\x80\x94sooner or later,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies.\r\n\r\nThe man laughed derisively. \xe2\x80\x9cNot this year,\xe2\x80\x9d he said; \xe2\x80\x9cthere will be\r\nfogs for another week; it is always so, and then storms. Better leave\r\nyour yawl here. Dues will be only sixpence a month for you.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll think about it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies. \xe2\x80\x9cGood-night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe man vanished like a ghost in the thick night.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs the post-office open?\xe2\x80\x9d I called after him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; eight to-morrow,\xe2\x80\x9d came back out of the fog.\r\n\r\nWe were too excited to sup in comfort, or sleep in peace, or to do\r\nanything but plan and speculate. Never till this night had we talked\r\nwith absolute mutual confidence, for Davies broke down the last\r\nbarriers of reserve and let me see his whole mind. He loved this girl\r\nand he loved his country, two simple passions which for the time\r\nabsorbed his whole moral capacity. There was no room left for\r\ncasuistry. To weigh one passion against the other, with the discordant\r\nvoices of honour and expediency dinning in his ears, had too long\r\ninvolved him in fruitless torture. Both were right; neither could be\r\nsurrendered. If the facts showed them irreconcilable, _tant pis pour\r\nles faits._ A way must be found to satisfy both or neither.\r\n\r\nI should have been a spiritless dog if I had not risen to his mood. But\r\nin truth his cutting of the knot was at this juncture exactly what\r\nappealed to me. I, too, was tired of vicarious casuistry, and the\r\nfascination of our enterprise, intensified by the discovery of that\r\nafternoon, had never been so strong in me. Not to be insincere, I\r\ncannot pretend that I viewed the situation with his single mind. My\r\nphilosophy when I left London was of a very worldly sort, and no one\r\ncan change his temperament in three weeks. I plainly said as much to\r\nDavies, and indeed took perverse satisfaction in stating with brutal\r\nemphasis some social truths which bore on this attachment of his to the\r\ndaughter of an outlaw. Truths I call them, but I uttered them more by\r\nrote than by conviction, and he heard them unmoved. And meanwhile I\r\nsnatched recklessly at his own solution. If it imparted into our\r\nadventure a strain of crazy chivalry more suited to knights-errant of\r\nthe Middle Ages than to sober modern youths\xe2\x80\x94well, thank Heaven, I was\r\nnot too sober, and still young enough to snatch at that fancy with an\r\nardour of imagination, if not of character; perhaps, too, of character,\r\nfor Galahads are not so common but that ordinary folk must needs draw\r\ncourage from their example and put something of a blind trust in their\r\ntenfold strength.\r\n\r\nTo reduce a romantic ideal to a working plan is a very difficult thing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe shall have to argue backwards,\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat is to be the final\r\nstage? Because that must govern the others.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was only one answer\xe2\x80\x94to get Dollmann, secrets and all, daughter\r\nand all, away from Germany altogether. So only could we satisfy the\r\ndouble aim we had set before us. What a joy it is, when beset with\r\ndoubts, to find a bed-rock necessity, however unattainable! We fastened\r\non this one and reasoned back from it. The first lesson was that,\r\nhowever many and strong were the enemies we had to contend with, our\r\nsole overt foe must be Dollmann. The issue of the struggle must be\r\nknown only to ourselves and him. If we won, and found out \xe2\x80\x9cwhat he was\r\nat\xe2\x80\x9d, we must at all costs conceal our success from his German friends,\r\nand detach him from them before he was compromised. (You will remark\r\nthat to blithely accept this limitation showed a very sanguine spirit\r\nin us.) The next question, how to find out what he was at, was a deal\r\nmore thorny. If it had not been for the discovery of Dollmann\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nidentity, we should have found it as hard a nut to crack as ever. But\r\nthis discovery was illuminating. It threw into relief two methods of\r\naction which hitherto we had been hazily seeking to combine, seesawing\r\nbetween one and the other, each of us influenced at different times by\r\ndifferent motives. One was to rely on independent research; the other\r\nto extort the secret from Dollmann direct, by craft or threats. The\r\nmoral of to-day was to abandon the first and embrace the second.\r\n\r\nThe prospects of independent research were not a whit better than\r\nbefore. There were only two theories in the field, the channel theory\r\nand the Memmert theory. The former languished for lack of\r\ncorroboration; the latter also appeared to be weakened. To Fr\xc3\xa4ulein\r\nDollmann the wreck-works were evidently what they purported to be, and\r\nnothing more. This fact in itself was unimportant, for it was clear as\r\ncrystal that she was no party to her father\xe2\x80\x99s treacherous intrigues, if\r\nhe was engaged in such. But if Memmert was his sphere for them, it was\r\ndisconcerting to find her so familiar with that sphere, lightly talking\r\nof a descent in a diving-bell\xe2\x80\x94hinting, too, that the mystery as to\r\nresults was only for local consumption. Nevertheless, the charm of\r\nMemmert as the place we had traced Grimm to, and as the only tangible\r\nclue we had obtained, was still very great. The really cogent objection\r\nwas the insuperable difficulty, known and watched as we were, of\r\nlearning its significance. If there was anything important to see there\r\nwe should never be allowed to see it, while by trying and failing we\r\nrisked everything. It was on this point that the last of all\r\nmisunderstandings between me and Davies was dissipated. At Bensersiel\r\nhe had been influenced more than he owned by my arguments about\r\nMemmert; but at that time (as I hinted) he was biased by a radical\r\nprejudice. The channel theory had become a sort of religion with him,\r\npromising double salvation\xe2\x80\x94not only avoidance of the Dollmanns, but\r\nsuccess in the quest by methods in which he was past master. To have to\r\ndesert it and resort to spying on naval defences was an idea he dreaded\r\nand distrusted. It was not the morality of the course that bothered\r\nhim. He was far too clear-headed to blink at the essential fact that at\r\nheart we were spies on a foreign power in time of peace, or to salve\r\nhis conscience by specious distinctions as to our mode of operation.\r\nThe foreign power to him was Dollmann, a traitor. There was his final\r\njustification, fearlessly adopted and held to the last. It was rather\r\nthat, knowing his own limitations, his whole nature shrank from the\r\nsort of action entailed by the Memmert theory. And there was strong\r\ncommon sense in his antipathy.\r\n\r\nSo much for independent research.\r\n\r\nOn the other hand the road was now clear for the other method. Davies\r\nno longer feared to face the imbroglio at Norderney; and that day\r\nfortune had given us a new and potent weapon against Dollmann;\r\nprecisely how potent we could not tell, for we had only a glimpse of\r\nhis past, and his exact relations with the Government were unknown to\r\nus. But we knew who he was. Using this knowledge with address, could we\r\nnot wring the rest from him? Feel our way, of course, be guided by his\r\nown conduct, but in the end strike hard and stake everything on the\r\nstroke? Such at any rate was our scheme to-night. Later, tossing in my\r\nbunk, I bethought me of the little drab book, lit a candle, and fetched\r\nit. A preface explained that it had been written during a spell of two\r\nmonths\xe2\x80\x99 leave from naval duty, and expressed a hope that it might be of\r\nservice to Corinthian sailors. The style was unadorned, but scholarly\r\nand pithy. There was no trace of the writer\xe2\x80\x99s individuality, save a\r\ncertain subdued relish in describing banks and shoals, which reminded\r\nme of Davies himself. For the rest, I found the book dull, and, in\r\nfact, it sent me to sleep.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXI.\r\nBlindfold to Memmert\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere she comes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies. It was nine o\xe2\x80\x99clock on the next day,\r\nOctober 22, and we were on deck waiting for the arrival of the steamer\r\nfrom Norddeich. There was no change in the weather\xe2\x80\x94still the same\r\nstringent cold, with a high barometer, and only fickle flaws of air;\r\nbut the morning was gloriously clear, except for a wreath or two of\r\nmist curling like smoke from the sea, and an attenuated belt of opaque\r\nfog on the northern horizon. The harbour lay open before us, and very\r\ncommodious and civilised it looked, enclosed between two long piers\r\nwhich ran quite half a mile out from the land to the roadstead (Riff\r\nGat by name) where we lay. A stranger might have taken it for a deep\r\nand spacious haven; but this, of course, was an illusion, due to the\r\nhigh water. Davies knew that three-quarters of it was mud, the\r\nremainder being a dredged-out channel along the western pier. A couple\r\nof tugs, a dredger, and a ferry packet with steam up, were moored on\r\nthat side\xe2\x80\x94a small stack of galliots on the other. Beyond these was\r\nanother vessel, a galliot in build, but radiant as a queen among sluts;\r\nher varnished sides and spars flashing orange in the sun. These, and\r\nher snow-white sailcovers and the twinkle of brass and gun-metal,\r\nproclaimed her to be a yacht. I had already studied her through the\r\nglasses and read on her stern _Medusa_. A couple of sailors were\r\nswabbing her decks; you could hear the slush of the water and the\r\nscratching of the deck-brooms. \xe2\x80\x9c_They_ can see us anyway,\xe2\x80\x9d Davies had\r\nsaid.\r\n\r\nFor that matter all the world could see us\xe2\x80\x94certainly the incoming\r\nsteamer must; for we lay as near to the pier as safety permitted,\r\nabreast of the berth she would occupy, as we knew by a gangway and a\r\nknot of sailors.\r\n\r\nA packet boat, not bigger than a big tug, was approaching from the\r\nsouth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRemember, we\xe2\x80\x99re not supposed to know he\xe2\x80\x99s coming,\xe2\x80\x9d I said; \xe2\x80\x9clet\xe2\x80\x99s go\r\nbelow.\xe2\x80\x9d Besides the skylight, our \xe2\x80\x9ccoach-house\xe2\x80\x9d cabin top had little\r\noblong side windows. We wiped clean those on the port side and watched\r\nevents from them, kneeling on the sofa.\r\n\r\nThe steamer backed her paddles, flinging out a wash that set us rolling\r\nto our scuppers. There seemed to be very few passengers aboard, but all\r\nof them were gazing at the _Dulcibella_ while the packet was warped\r\nalongside. On the forward deck there were some market-women with\r\nbaskets, a postman, and a weedy youth who might be an hotel-waiter; on\r\nthe after-deck, standing close together, were two men in ulsters and\r\nsoft felt hats.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere he is!\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, in a tense whisper; \xe2\x80\x9cthe tall one.\xe2\x80\x9d But the\r\ntall one turned abruptly as Davies spoke and strode away behind the\r\ndeck-house, leaving me just a lightning impression of a grey beard and\r\na steep tanned forehead, behind a cloud of cigar-smoke. It was perverse\r\nof me, but, to tell the truth, I hardly missed him, so occupied was I\r\nby the short one, who remained leaning on the rail, thoughtfully\r\ncontemplating the _Dulcibella_ through gold-rimmed pince-nez: a sallow,\r\nwizened old fellow, beetlebrowed, with a bush of grizzled moustache and\r\na jet-black tuft of beard on his chin. The most remarkable feature was\r\nthe nose, which was broad and flat, merging almost imperceptibly in the\r\nwrinkled cheeks. Lightly beaked at the nether extremity, it drooped\r\ntowards an enormous cigar which was pointing at us like a gun just\r\ndischarged. He looked wise as Satan, and you would say he was smiling\r\ninwardly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho\xe2\x80\x99s that?\xe2\x80\x9d I whispered to Davies. (There was no need to talk in\r\nwhispers, but we did so instinctively.)\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan\xe2\x80\x99t think,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies. \xe2\x80\x9cHullo! she\xe2\x80\x99s backing off, and they\xe2\x80\x99ve not\r\nlanded.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSome parcels and mail-bags had been thrown up, and the weedy waiter and\r\ntwo market-women had gone up the gangway, which was now being hauled\r\nup, and were standing on the quay. I think one or two other persons had\r\nfirst come aboard unnoticed by us, but at the last moment a man we had\r\nnot seen before jumped down to the forward deck. \xe2\x80\x9cGrimm!\xe2\x80\x9d we both\r\nejaculated at once.\r\n\r\nThe steamer whistled sharply, circled backwards into the roadstead, and\r\nthen steamed away. The pier soon hid her, but her smoke showed she was\r\nsteering towards the North Sea.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat does this mean?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere must be some other quay to stop at nearer the town,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nDavies. \xe2\x80\x9cLet\xe2\x80\x99s go ashore and get your letters.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe had made a long and painful toilette that morning, and felt quite\r\nshy of one another as we sculled towards the pier, in much-creased blue\r\nsuits, conventional collars, and brown boots. It was the first time for\r\ntwo years that I had seen Davies in anything approaching a respectable\r\ngarb; but a fashionable watering-place, even in the dead season, exacts\r\nrespect; and, besides, we had friends to visit.\r\n\r\nWe tied up the dinghy to an iron ladder, and on the pier found our\r\ninquisitor of the night before smoking in the doorway of a shed marked\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHarbour Master\xe2\x80\x9d. After some civilities we inquired about the steamer.\r\nThe answer was that it was Saturday, and she had, therefore, gone on to\r\nJuist. Did we want a good hotel? The \xe2\x80\x9cVier Jahreszeiten\xe2\x80\x9d was still\r\nopen, etc.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJuist, by Jove!\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, as we walked on. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy are those three\r\ngoing to Juist?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should have thought it was pretty clear. They\xe2\x80\x99re on their way to\r\nMemmert.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDavies agreed, and we both looked longingly westward at a\r\nstraw-coloured streak on the sea.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it some meeting, do you think?\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLooks like it. We shall probably find the _Kormoran_ here,\r\nwind-bound.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd find her we did soon after, the outermost of the stack of galliots,\r\non the farther side of the harbour. Two men, whose faces we took a good\r\nlook at, were sitting on her hatch, mending a sail.\r\n\r\nFlooded with sun, yet still as the grave, the town was like a dead\r\nbutterfly for whom the healing rays had come too late. We crossed some\r\ndeserted public gardens commanded by a gorgeous casino, its porticos\r\nheaped with chairs and tables; so past kiosques and _caf\xc3\xa9s,_ great\r\nwhite hotels with boarded windows, bazaars and booths, and all the\r\nstale lees of vulgar frivolity, to the post-office, which at least was\r\nalive. I received a packet of letters and purchased a local time-table,\r\nfrom which we learned that the steamer sailed daily to Borkum _via_\r\nNorderney, touching three times a week at Juist (weather permitting).\r\nOn the return journey to-day it was due at Norderney at 7.30 p.m. Then\r\nI inquired the way to the \xe2\x80\x9cVier Jahreszeiten\xe2\x80\x9d. \xe2\x80\x9cFor whatever your\r\nprinciples, Davies,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, \xe2\x80\x9cwe are going to have the best breakfast\r\nmoney can buy! We\xe2\x80\x99ve got the whole day before us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe \xe2\x80\x9cFour Seasons\xe2\x80\x9d Hotel was on the esplanade facing the northern\r\nbeach. Living up to its name, it announced on an illuminated signboard,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cInclusive terms for winter visitors; special attention to invalids,\r\netc.\xe2\x80\x9d Here in a great glass restaurant, with the unruffled blue of\r\nocean spread out before us, we ate the king of breakfasts, dismissed\r\nthe waiter, and over long and fragrant Havanas examined my mail at\r\nleisure.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat a waste of good diplomacy!\xe2\x80\x9d was my first thought, for nothing had\r\nbeen tampered with, so far as we could judge from the minutest\r\nscrutiny, directed, of course, in particular to the franked official\r\nletters (for to my surprise there were two) from Whitehall.\r\n\r\nThe first in order of date (October 6) ran: \xe2\x80\x9cDear Carruthers.\xe2\x80\x94Take\r\nanother week by all means.\xe2\x80\x94Yours, etc.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe second (marked \xe2\x80\x9curgent\xe2\x80\x9d) had been sent to my home address and\r\nforwarded. It was dated October 15, and cancelled the previous letter,\r\nrequesting me to return to London without delay\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cI am sorry to abridge\r\nyour holiday, but we are very busy, and, at present,\r\nshort-handed.\xe2\x80\x94Yours, etc.\xe2\x80\x9d There was a dry postscript to the effect\r\nthat another time I was to be good enough to leave more regular and\r\ndefinite information as to my whereabouts when absent.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m afraid I never got this!\xe2\x80\x9d I said, handing it to Davies.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou won\xe2\x80\x99t go, will you?\xe2\x80\x9d said he, looking, nevertheless, with\r\nunconcealed awe at the great man\xe2\x80\x99s handwriting under the haughty\r\nofficial crest. Meanwhile I discovered an endorsement on a corner of\r\nthe envelope: \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t worry; it\xe2\x80\x99s only the chief\xe2\x80\x99s fuss.\xe2\x80\x94M\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d I promptly\r\ntore up the envelope. There are domestic mysteries which it would be\r\nindecent and disloyal to reveal, even to one\xe2\x80\x99s best friend. The rest of\r\nmy letters need no remark; I smiled over some and blushed over\r\nothers\xe2\x80\x94all were voices from a life which was infinitely far away.\r\nDavies, meanwhile, was deep in the foreign intelligence of a newspaper,\r\nspelling it out line by line, and referring impatiently to me for the\r\nmeaning of words.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHullo!\xe2\x80\x9d he said, suddenly; \xe2\x80\x9csame old game! Hear that siren?\xe2\x80\x9d A curtain\r\nof fog had grown on the northern horizon and was drawing shorewards\r\nslowly but surely.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt doesn\xe2\x80\x99t matter, does it?\xe2\x80\x9d I said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, we must get back to the yacht. We can\xe2\x80\x99t leave her alone in the\r\nfog.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was some marketing to be done on the way back, and in the course\r\nof looking for the shops we wanted we came on the Schwannall\xc3\xa9e and\r\nnoted its position. Before we reached the harbour the fog was on us,\r\ncharging up the streets in dense masses. Happily a tramline led right\r\nup to the pier-head, or we should have lost our way and wasted time,\r\nwhich, in the event, was of priceless value. Presently we stumbled up\r\nagainst the Harbour Office, which was our landmark for the steps where\r\nwe had tied up the dinghy. The same official appeared and\r\ngood-naturedly held the painter while we handed in our parcels. He\r\nwanted to know why we had left the flesh-pots of the \xe2\x80\x9cVier\r\nJahreszeiten\xe2\x80\x9d. To look after our yacht, of course. There was no need,\r\nhe objected; there would be no traffic moving while the fog lasted, and\r\nthe fog, having come on at that hour, had come to stay. If it did clear\r\nhe would keep an eye on the yacht for us. We thanked him, but thought\r\nwe would go aboard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll have a job to find her now,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\nThe distance was eighty yards at the most, but we had to use a\r\nscientific method, the same one, in fact, that Davies had used last\r\nnight in the approach to the eastern pier.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRow straight out at right angles to the pier,\xe2\x80\x9d he said now. I did so,\r\nDavies sounding with his scull between the strokes. He found the bottom\r\nafter twenty yards, that being the width of the dredged-out channel at\r\nthis point. Then we turned to the right, and moved gently forward,\r\nkeeping touch with the edge of the mud-bank (for all the world like\r\nblind men tapping along a kerbstone) and taking short excursions from\r\nit, till the _Dulcibella_ hove in view. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s partly luck,\xe2\x80\x9d Davies\r\ncommented; \xe2\x80\x9cwe ought to have had the compass as well.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe exchanged shouts with the man on the pier to show we had arrived.\r\n\r\n\r\nIllustration: Chart B of Juist, Memmert, and Part of Norderney\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s very good practice, that sort of thing,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, when we had\r\ndisembarked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve got a sixth sense,\xe2\x80\x9d I observed. \xe2\x80\x9cHow far could you go like\r\nthat?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t know. Let\xe2\x80\x99s have another try. I can\xe2\x80\x99t sit still all day. Let\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nexplore this channel.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Why not go to Memmert?\xe2\x80\x9d_ I said, in fun.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo Memmert?\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, slowly; \xe2\x80\x9cby Jove! that\xe2\x80\x99s an idea!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood Heavens, man! I was joking. Why, it\xe2\x80\x99s ten mortal miles.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMore,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, absently. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s not so much the distance\xe2\x80\x94what\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nthe time? Ten fifteen; quarter ebb\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94 What am I talking about? We made\r\nour plans last night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut seeing him, to my amazement, serious, I was stung by the splendour\r\nof the idea I had awakened. Confidence in his skill was second nature\r\nto me. I swept straight on to the logic of the thing, the greatness,\r\nthe completeness of the opportunity, if by a miracle it could be seized\r\nand used. Something was going on at Memmert to-day; our men had gone\r\nthere; here were we, ten miles away, in a smothering, blinding fog. It\r\nwas known we were here\xe2\x80\x94Dollmann and Grimm knew it; the crew of the\r\n_Medusa_ knew it; the crew of the _Kormoran_ knew it; the man on the\r\npier, whether he cared or not, knew it. But none of them knew Davies as\r\nI knew him. Would anyone dream for an instant\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStop a second,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies; \xe2\x80\x9cgive me two minutes.\xe2\x80\x9d He whipped out the\r\nGerman chart. \xe2\x80\x9cWhere exactly should we go?\xe2\x80\x9d (\xe2\x80\x9cExactly!\xe2\x80\x9d The word\r\ntickled me hugely.)\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo the dep\xc3\xb4t, of course; it\xe2\x80\x99s our only chance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cListen then\xe2\x80\x94there are two routes: the outside one by the open sea,\r\nright round Juist, and doubling south\xe2\x80\x94the simplest, but the longest;\r\nthe dep\xc3\xb4t\xe2\x80\x99s at the south point of Memmert, and Memmert\xe2\x80\x99s nearly two\r\nmiles long.\xe2\x80\x9d _[See Chart B]_\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow far would that way be?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSixteen miles good. And we should have to row in a breaking swell most\r\nof the way, close to land.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOut of the question; it\xe2\x80\x99s too public, too, if it clears. The steamer\r\nwent that way, and will come back that way. We must go inside over the\r\nsands. Am I dreaming, though? Can you possibly find the way?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t wonder. But I don\xe2\x80\x99t believe you see the hitch. It\xe2\x80\x99s the\r\n_time_ and the falling tide. High water was about 8.15: it\xe2\x80\x99s now 10.15,\r\nand all those sands are drying off. We must cross the See Gat and\r\nstrike that boomed channel, the Memmert Balje; strike it, freeze on to\r\nit\xe2\x80\x94can\xe2\x80\x99t cut off an inch\xe2\x80\x94and pass that \xe2\x80\x98watershed\xe2\x80\x99 you see there before\r\nit\xe2\x80\x99s too late. It\xe2\x80\x99s an infernally bad one, I can see. Not even a dinghy\r\nwill cross it for an hour each side of low water.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, how far is the \xe2\x80\x98watershed\xe2\x80\x99?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood Lord! What are we talking for? Change, man, change! Talk while\r\nwe\xe2\x80\x99re changing.\xe2\x80\x9d (He began flinging off his shore clothes, and I did\r\nthe same.) \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s at least five miles to the end of it; six, allowing\r\nfor bends; hour and a half hard pulling; two, allowing for checks. Are\r\nyou fit? You\xe2\x80\x99ll have to pull the most. Then there are six or seven more\r\nmiles\xe2\x80\x94easier ones. And then\xe2\x80\x94What are we to do when we get there?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLeave that to me,\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9cYou get me there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSupposing it clears?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAfter we get there? Bad; but we must risk that. If it clears on the\r\nway there it doesn\xe2\x80\x99t matter by this route; we shall be miles from\r\nland.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat about getting back?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe shall have a rising tide, anyway. If the fog lasts\xe2\x80\x94can you manage\r\nin a fog _and_ dark?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe dark makes it no more difficult, if we\xe2\x80\x99ve a light to see the\r\ncompass and chart by. You trim the binnacle lamp\xe2\x80\x94no, the riding-light.\r\nNow give me the scissors, and don\xe2\x80\x99t speak a word for ten minutes.\r\nMeanwhile, think it out, and load the dinghy\xe2\x80\x94(by Jove! though, don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nmake a sound)\xe2\x80\x94some grub and whisky, the boat-compass, lead,\r\nriding-light, matches, _small_ boathook, grapnel and line.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFoghorn?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, and the whistle too.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA gun?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat for?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99re after ducks.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right. And muffle the rowlocks with cotton-waste.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI left Davies absorbed in the charts, and softly went about my own\r\nfunctions. In ten minutes he was on the ladder, beckoning.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve done,\xe2\x80\x9d he whispered. \xe2\x80\x9cNow _shall_ we go?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve thought it out. Yes,\xe2\x80\x9d I answered.\r\n\r\nThis was only roughly true, for I could not have stated in words all\r\nthe pros and cons that I had balanced. It was an impulse that drove me\r\nforward; but an impulse founded on reason, with just a tinge, perhaps,\r\nof superstition; for the quest had begun in a fog and might fitly end\r\nin one.\r\n\r\nIt was twenty-five minutes to eleven when we noiselessly pushed off.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet her drift,\xe2\x80\x9d whispered Davies, \xe2\x80\x9cthe ebb\xe2\x80\x99ll carry her past the\r\npier.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe slid by the _Dulcibella_, and she disappeared. Then we sat without\r\nspeech or movement for about five minutes, while the gurgle of tide\r\nthrough piles approached and passed. The dinghy appeared to be\r\nmotionless, just as a balloon in the clouds may appear to its occupants\r\nto be motionless, though urged by a current of air. In reality we were\r\ndriving out of the Riff Gat into the See Gat. The dinghy swayed to a\r\nlight swell.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, pull,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, under his breath; \xe2\x80\x9ckeep it long and steady,\r\nabove all steady\xe2\x80\x94both arms with equal force.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI was on the bow-thwart; he _vis-\xc3\xa0-vis_ to me on the stern seat, his\r\nleft hand behind him on the tiller, his right forefinger on a small\r\nsquare of paper which lay on his knees; this was a section cut out from\r\nthe big German chart. _[See Chart B]_ On the midship-thwart between us\r\nlay the compass and a watch. Between these three objects\xe2\x80\x94compass,\r\nwatch, and chart\xe2\x80\x94his eyes darted constantly, never looking up or out,\r\nsave occasionally for a sharp glance over the side at the flying\r\nbubbles, to see if I was sustaining a regular speed. My duty was to be\r\nhis automaton, the human equivalent of a marine engine whose\r\nrevolutions can be counted and used as data by the navigator. My arms\r\nmust be regular as twin pistons; the energy that drove them as\r\ncontrollable as steam. It was a hard ideal to reach, for the complex\r\nmortal tends to rely on all the senses God has given him, so unfitting\r\nhimself for mechanical exactitude when a sense (eyesight, in my case)\r\nfails him. At first it was constantly \xe2\x80\x9cleft\xe2\x80\x9d or \xe2\x80\x9cright\xe2\x80\x9d from Davies,\r\naccompanied by a bubbling from the rudder.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis won\xe2\x80\x99t do, too much helm,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, without looking up. \xe2\x80\x9cKeep\r\nyour stroke, but listen to me. Can you see the compass card?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen I come forward.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTake your time, and don\xe2\x80\x99t get flurried, but each time you come forward\r\nhave a good look at it. The course is sou\xe2\x80\x99-west half-west. You take the\r\nopposite, north-east half-east, and keep her _stern_ on that. It\xe2\x80\x99ll be\r\nrough, but it\xe2\x80\x99ll save some helm, and give me a hand free if I want it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI did as he said, not without effort, and our progress gradually became\r\nsmoother, till he had no need to speak at all. The only sound now was\r\none like the gentle simmer of a saucepan away to port\xe2\x80\x94the lisp of surf\r\nI knew it to be\xe2\x80\x94and the muffled grunt of the rowlocks. I broke the\r\nsilence once to say \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s very shallow.\xe2\x80\x9d I had touched sand with my\r\nright scull.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t talk,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies.\r\n\r\nAbout half an hour passed, and then he added sounding to his other\r\noccupations. \xe2\x80\x9cPlump\xe2\x80\x9d went the lead at regular intervals, and he steered\r\nwith his hip while pulling in the line. Very little of it went out at\r\nfirst, then less still. Again I struck bottom, and, glancing aside, saw\r\nweeds. Suddenly he got a deep cast, and the dinghy, freed from the\r\nslight drag which shallow water always inflicts on a small boat, leapt\r\nbuoyantly forward. At the same time, I knew by boils on the smooth\r\nsurface that we were in a strong tideway.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Buse Tief,\xe2\x80\x9d _[See Chart B]_ muttered Davies. \xe2\x80\x9cRow hard now, and\r\nsteady as a clock.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFor a hundred yards or more I bent to my sculls and made her fly.\r\nDavies was getting six fathom casts, till, just as suddenly as it had\r\ndeepened, the water shoaled\xe2\x80\x94ten feet, six, three, one\xe2\x80\x94the dinghy\r\ngrounded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood!\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies. \xe2\x80\x9cBack her off! Pull your right only.\xe2\x80\x9d The dinghy\r\nspun round with her bow to N.N.W. \xe2\x80\x9cBoth arms together! Don\xe2\x80\x99t you worry\r\nabout the compass now; just pull, and listen for orders. There\xe2\x80\x99s a\r\ntricky bit coming.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe put aside the chart, kicked the lead under the seat, and, kneeling\r\non the dripping coils of line, sounded continuously with the butt-end\r\nof the boathook, a stumpy little implement, notched at intervals of a\r\nfoot, and often before used for the same purpose. All at once I was\r\naware that a check had come, for the dinghy swerved and doubled like a\r\nhound ranging after scent.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStop her,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, suddenly, \xe2\x80\x9cand throw out the grapnel.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI obeyed and we brought up, swinging to a slight current, whose\r\ndirection Davies verified by the compass. Then for half a minute he\r\ngave himself up to concentrated thought. What struck me most about him\r\nwas that he never for a moment strained his eyes through the fog; a\r\nuseless exercise (for five yards or so was the radius of our vision)\r\nwhich, however, I could not help indulging in, while I rested. He made\r\nup his mind, and we were off again, straight and swift as an arrow this\r\ntime, and in water deeper than the boathook. I could see by his face\r\nthat he was taking some bold expedient whose issue hung in the\r\nbalance.... Again we touched mud, and the artist\xe2\x80\x99s joy of achievement\r\nshone in his eyes. Backing away, we headed west, and for the first time\r\nhe began to gaze into the fog.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s one!\xe2\x80\x9d he snapped at last. \xe2\x80\x9cEasy all!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA boom, one of the usual upright saplings, glided out of the mist. He\r\ncaught hold of it, and we brought up.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRest for three minutes now,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99re in fairly good time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was 11.10. I ate some biscuits and took a nip of whisky while Davies\r\nprepared for the next stage.\r\n\r\nWe had reached the eastern outlet of Memmert Balje, the channel which\r\nruns east and west behind Juist Island, direct to the south point of\r\nMemmert. How we had reached it was incomprehensible to me at the time,\r\nbut the reader will understand by comparing my narrative with the\r\ndotted line on the chart. I add this brief explanation, that Davies\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nmethod had been to cross the channel called the Buse Tief, and strike\r\nthe other side of it at a point well _south_ of the outlet of the\r\nMemmert Balje (in view of the northward set of the ebb-tide), and then\r\nto drop back north and feel his way to the outlet. The check was caused\r\nby a deep indentation in the Itzendorf Flat; a _cul-de-sac,_ with a\r\nwide mouth, which Davies was very near mistaking for the Balje itself.\r\nWe had no time to skirt dents so deep as that; hence the dash across\r\nits mouth with the chance of missing the upper lip altogether, and of\r\neither being carried out to sea (for the slightest error was\r\ncumulative) or straying fruitlessly along the edge.\r\n\r\nThe next three miles were the most critical of all. They included the\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cwatershed\xe2\x80\x9d, whose length and depth were doubtful; they included, too,\r\nthe crux of the whole passage, a spot where the channel forks, our own\r\nbranch continuing west, and another branch diverging from it\r\nnorth-westward. We must row against time, and yet we must negotiate\r\nthat crux. Add to this that the current was against us till the\r\nwatershed was crossed; that the tide was just at its most baffling\r\nstage, too low to allow us to risk short cuts, and too high to give\r\ndefinition to the banks of the channel; and that the compass was no aid\r\nwhatever for the minor bends. \xe2\x80\x9cTime\xe2\x80\x99s up,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, and on we went.\r\nI was hugging the comfortable thought that we should now have booms on\r\nour starboard for the whole distance; on our starboard, I say, for\r\nexperience had taught us that all channels running parallel with the\r\ncoast and islands were uniformly boomed on the northern side. Anyone\r\nless confident than Davies would have succumbed to the temptation of\r\nslavishly relying on these marks, creeping from one to the other, and\r\nwasting precious time. But Davies knew our friend the \xe2\x80\x9cboom\xe2\x80\x9d and his\r\neccentricities too well; and preferred to trust to his sense of touch,\r\nwhich no fog in the world could impair. If we happened to sight one,\r\nwell and good, we should know which side of the channel we were on. But\r\neven this contingent advantage he deliberately sacrificed after a short\r\ndistance, for he crossed over to the _south_ or unboomed side and\r\nsteered and sounded along it, using the ltzendorf Flat as his handrail,\r\nso to speak. He was compelled to do this, he told me afterwards, in\r\nview of the crux, where the converging lines of booms would have\r\ninvolved us in irremediable confusion. Our branch was the southern one,\r\nand it followed that we must use the southern bank, and defer obtaining\r\nany help from booms until sure we were past that critical spot.\r\n\r\nFor an hour we were at the extreme strain, I of physical exertion, he\r\nof mental. I could not get into a steady swing, for little checks were\r\nconstant. My right scull was for ever skidding on mud or weeds, and the\r\nbackward suck of shoal water clogged our progress. Once we were both of\r\nus out in the slime tugging at the dinghy\xe2\x80\x99s sides; then in again,\r\nblundering on. I found the fog bemusing, lost all idea of time and\r\nspace, and felt like a senseless marionette kicking and jerking to a\r\nmad music without tune or time. The misty form of Davies as he sat with\r\nhis right arm swinging rhythmically forward and back, was a clockwork\r\nfigure as mad as myself, but didactic and gibbering in his madness.\r\nThen the boathook he wielded with a circular sweep began to take\r\ngrotesque shapes in my heated fancy; now it was the antenna of a\r\ngroping insect, now the crank of a cripple\xe2\x80\x99s self-propelled\r\nperambulator, now the alpenstock of a lunatic mountaineer, who sits in\r\nhis chair and climbs and climbs to some phantom \xe2\x80\x9cwatershed\xe2\x80\x9d. At the\r\nback of such mind as was left me lodged two insistent thoughts: \xe2\x80\x9cwe\r\nmust hurry on,\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cwe are going wrong.\xe2\x80\x9d As to the latter, take a link-boy\r\nthrough a London fog and you will experience the same thing: he always\r\ngoes the way you think is wrong. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99re rowing _back_!\xe2\x80\x9d I remember\r\nshouting to Davies once, having become aware that it was now my left\r\nscull which splashed against obstructions. \xe2\x80\x9cRubbish,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve crossed over\xe2\x80\x9d; and I relapsed.\r\n\r\nBy degrees I returned to sanity, thanks to improved conditions. It is\r\nan ill wind that blows nobody good, and the state of the tide, though\r\nit threatened us with total failure, had the compensating advantage\r\nthat the lower it fell the more constricted and defined became our\r\nchannel; till the time came when the compass and boathook were alike\r\nunnecessary, because our handrail, the muddy brink of the channel, was\r\nvisible to the eye, close to us; on our right hand always now, for the\r\ncrux was far behind, and the northern side was now our guide. All that\r\nremained was to press on with might and main ere the bed of the creek\r\ndried.\r\n\r\nWhat a race it was! Homeric, in effect; a struggle of men with gods,\r\nfor what were the gods but forces of nature personified? If the God of\r\nthe Falling Tide did not figure in the Olympian circle he is none the\r\nless a mighty divinity. Davies left his post, and rowed stroke. Under\r\nour united efforts the dinghy advanced in strenuous leaps, hurling\r\nminiature rollers on the bank beside us. My palms, seasoned as they\r\nwere, were smarting with watery blisters. The pace was too hot for my\r\nstrength and breath.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI must have a rest,\xe2\x80\x9d I gasped.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I think we\xe2\x80\x99re over it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies.\r\n\r\nWe stopped the dinghy dead, and he stabbed over the side with the\r\nboathook. It passed gently astern of us, and even my bewildered brain\r\ntook in the meaning of that.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThree feet and the current with us. _Well_ over it,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\npaddle on while you rest and feed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was a few minutes past one and we still, as he calculated, had eight\r\nmiles before us, allowing for bends.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut it\xe2\x80\x99s a mere question of muscle,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\nI took his word for it, and munched at tongue and biscuits. As for\r\nmuscle, we were both in hard condition. He was fresh, and what distress\r\nI felt was mainly due to spasmodic exertion culminating in that\r\ndesperate spurt. As for the fog, it had more than once shown a faint\r\ntendency to lift, growing thinner and more luminous, in the manner of\r\nfogs, always to settle down again, heavy as a quilt.\r\n\r\nNote the spot marked \xe2\x80\x9csecond rest\xe2\x80\x9d (approximately correct, Davies says)\r\nand the course of the channel from that point westward. You will see it\r\nbroadening and deepening to the dimensions of a great river, and\r\nfinally merging in the estuary of the Ems. Note, too, that its northern\r\nboundary, the edge of the now uncovered Nordland Sand, leads, with one\r\ninterruption _(marked A),_ direct to Memmert, and is boomed throughout.\r\nYou will then understand why Davies made so light of the rest of his\r\nproblem. Compared with the feats he had performed, it was child\xe2\x80\x99s play,\r\nfor he always had that visible margin to keep touch with if he chose,\r\nor to return to in case of doubt. As a matter of fact\xe2\x80\x94observe our\r\ndotted line\xe2\x80\x94he made two daring departures from it, the first purely to\r\nsave time, the second partly to save time and partly to avoid the very\r\nawkward spot marked A, where a creek with booms and a little delta of\r\nits own interrupts the even bank. During the first of these\r\ndepartures\xe2\x80\x94the shortest but most brilliant\xe2\x80\x94he let me do the rowing, and\r\ndevoted himself to the niceties of the course; during the second, and\r\nthrough both the intermediate stages, he rowed himself, with occasional\r\npauses to inspect the chart. We fell into a long, measured stroke, and\r\ncovered the miles rapidly, scarcely exchanging a single word till, at\r\nthe end of a long pull through vacancy, Davies said suddenly:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow where are we to land?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA sandbank was looming over us crowned by a lonely boom.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere are we?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA quarter of a mile from Memmert.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat time is it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNearly three.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXII.\r\nThe Quartette\r\n\r\n\r\nHis _tour de force_ was achieved, and for the moment something like\r\ncollapse set in.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat in the world have we come here for?\xe2\x80\x9d he muttered; \xe2\x80\x9cI feel a bit\r\ngiddy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI made him drink some whisky, which revived him; and then, speaking in\r\nwhispers, we settled certain points.\r\n\r\nI alone was to land. Davies demurred to this out of loyalty, but common\r\nsense, coinciding with a strong aversion of his own, settled the\r\nmatter. Two were more liable to detection than one. I spoke the\r\nlanguage well, and if challenged could cover my retreat with a gruff\r\nword or two; in my woollen overalls, sea-boots, oilskin coat, with a\r\nsou\xe2\x80\x99-wester pulled well over my eyes, I should pass in a fog for a\r\nFrisian. Davies must mind the dinghy; but how was I to regain it? I\r\nhoped to do so without help, by using the edge of the sand; but if he\r\nheard a long whistle he was to blow the foghorn.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTake the pocket-compass,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cNever budge from the shore without\r\nusing it, and lay it on the ground for steadiness. Take this scrap of\r\nchart, too\xe2\x80\x94it may come in useful; but you can\xe2\x80\x99t miss the dep\xc3\xb4t, it\r\nlooks to be close to the shore. How long will you be?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow long have I got?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe young flood\xe2\x80\x99s making\xe2\x80\x94has been for nearly an hour\xe2\x80\x94that bank (he\r\nmeasured it with his eye) will be covering in an hour and a half.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat ought to be enough.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t run it too fine. It\xe2\x80\x99s steep here, but it may shelve farther on.\r\nIf you have to wade you\xe2\x80\x99ll never find me, and you\xe2\x80\x99ll make a deuce of a\r\nrow. Got your watch, matches, knife? No knife? Take mine; never go\r\nanywhere without a knife.\xe2\x80\x9d (It was his seaman\xe2\x80\x99s idea of efficiency.)\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWait a bit, we must settle a place to meet at in case I\xe2\x80\x99m late and\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t reach you here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Don\xe2\x80\x99t_ be late. We\xe2\x80\x99ve got to get back to the yacht before we\xe2\x80\x99re\r\nmissed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I may have to hide and wait till dark\xe2\x80\x94the fog may clear.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe were fools to come, I believe,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, gloomily. \xe2\x80\x9cThere _are_\r\nno meeting-places in a place like this. Here\xe2\x80\x99s the best I can see on\r\nthe chart\xe2\x80\x94a big triangular beacon marked on the very point of Memmert.\r\nYou\xe2\x80\x99ll pass it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right. I\xe2\x80\x99m off.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood luck,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, faintly.\r\n\r\nI stepped out, climbed a miry glacis of five or six feet, reached hard\r\nwet sand, and strode away with the sluggish ripple of the Balje on my\r\nleft hand. A curtain dropped between me and Davies, and I was\r\nalone\xe2\x80\x94alone, but how I thrilled to feel the firm sand rustle under my\r\nboots; to know that it led to dry land, where, whatever befell, I could\r\ngive my wits full play. I clove the fog briskly.\r\n\r\nGood Heavens! what was that? I stopped short and listened. From over\r\nthe water on my left there rang out, dulled by fog, but distinct to the\r\near, three double strokes on a bell or gong.
