I looked at my watch.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShip at anchor,\xe2\x80\x9d I said to myself. \xe2\x80\x9cSix bells in the afternoon watch.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nI knew the Balje was here a deep roadstead, where a vessel entering the\r\nEastern Ems might very well anchor to ride out a fog.\r\n\r\nI was just stepping forward when another sound followed from the same\r\nquarter, a bugle-call this time. Then I understood\xe2\x80\x94only men-of-war\r\nsound bugles\xe2\x80\x94the _Blitz_ was here then; and very natural, too, I\r\nthought, and strode on. The sand was growing drier, the water farther\r\nbeneath me; then came a thin black ribbon of weed\xe2\x80\x94high-water mark. A\r\nfew cautious steps to the right and I touched tufts of marram grass. It\r\nwas Memmert. I pulled out the chart and refreshed my memory. No! there\r\ncould be no mistake; keep the sea on my left and I must go right. I\r\nfollowed the ribbon of weed, keeping it just in view, but walking on\r\nthe verge of the grass for the sake of silence. All at once I almost\r\ntripped over a massive iron bar; others, a rusty network of them, grew\r\ninto being above and around me, like the arms of a ghostly polyp.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat infernal spider\xe2\x80\x99s web is this?\xe2\x80\x9d I thought, and stumbled clear. I\r\nhad strayed into the base of a gigantic tripod, its gaunt legs stayed\r\nand cross-stayed, its apex lost in fog; the beacon, I remembered. A\r\nhundred yards farther and I was down on my knees again, listening with\r\nmight and main; for several little sounds were in the air\xe2\x80\x94voices, the\r\nrasp of a boat\xe2\x80\x99s keel, the whistling of a tune. These were straight\r\nahead. More to the left, seaward, that is, I had aural evidence of the\r\npresence of a steamboat\xe2\x80\x94a small one, for the hiss of escaping steam was\r\nlow down. On my right front I as yet heard nothing, but the dep\xc3\xb4t must\r\nbe there.\r\n\r\nI prepared to strike away from my base, and laid the compass on the\r\nground\xe2\x80\x94NW. roughly I made the course. (\xe2\x80\x9cSouth-east\xe2\x80\x94south-east for\r\ncoming back,\xe2\x80\x9d I repeated inwardly, like a child learning a lesson.)\r\nThen of my two allies I abandoned one, the beach, and threw myself\r\nwholly on the fog.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlay the game,\xe2\x80\x9d I said to myself. \xe2\x80\x9cNobody expects you; nobody will\r\nrecognise you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI advanced in rapid stages of ten yards or so, while grass disappeared\r\nand soft sand took its place, pitted everywhere with footmarks. I trod\r\ncarefully, for obstructions began to show themselves\xe2\x80\x94an anchor, a heap\r\nof rusty cable; then a boat bottom upwards, and, lying on it, a foul\r\nold meerschaum pipe. I paused here and strained my ears, for there were\r\nsounds in many directions; the same whistling (behind me now), heavy\r\nfootsteps in front, and somewhere beyond\xe2\x80\x94fifty yards away, I reckoned\xe2\x80\x94a\r\nbuzz of guttural conversation; from the same quarter there drifted to\r\nmy nostrils the acrid odour of coarse tobacco. Then a door banged.\r\n\r\nI put the compass in my pocket (thinking \xe2\x80\x9csouth-east, south-east\xe2\x80\x9d),\r\nplaced the pipe between my teeth (ugh! the rank savour of it!) rammed\r\nmy sou\xe2\x80\x99-wester hard down, and slouched on in the direction of the door\r\nthat had banged. A voice in front called, \xe2\x80\x9cKarl Schicker\xe2\x80\x9d; a nearer\r\nvoice, that of the man whose footsteps I had heard approaching, took it\r\nup and called \xe2\x80\x9cKarl Schicker\xe2\x80\x9d: I, too, took it up, and, turning my\r\nback, called \xe2\x80\x9cKarl Schicker\xe2\x80\x9d as gruffly and gutturally as I could. The\r\nfootsteps passed quite close to me, and glancing over my shoulder I saw\r\na young man passing, dressed very like me, but wearing a sealskin cap\r\ninstead of a sou\xe2\x80\x99-wester. As he walked he seemed to be counting coins\r\nin his palm. A hail came back from the beach and the whistling stopped.\r\n\r\nI now became aware that I was on a beaten track. These meetings were\r\nhazardous, so I inclined aside, but not without misgivings, for the\r\npath led towards the buzz of talk and the banging door, and these were\r\nmy only guides to the dep\xc3\xb4t. Suddenly, and much before I expected it, I\r\nknew rather than saw that a wall was in front of me; now it was\r\nvisible, the side of a low building of corrugated iron. A pause to\r\nreconnoitre was absolutely necessary; but the knot of talkers might\r\nhave heard my footsteps, and I must at all costs not suggest the\r\ngroping of a stranger. I lit a match\xe2\x80\x94two\xe2\x80\x94and sucked heavily (as I had\r\nseen navvies do) at my pipe, studying the trend of the wall by\r\nreference to the sounds. There was a stale dottle wedged in the bowl,\r\nand loathsome fumes resulted. Just then the same door banged again;\r\nanother name, which I forget, was called out. I decided that I was at\r\nthe end of a rectangular building which I pictured as like an Aldershot\r\n\xe2\x80\x9chut\xe2\x80\x9d, and that the door I heard was round the corner to my left. A\r\nknot of men must be gathered there, entering it by turns. Having\r\nexpectorated noisily, I followed the tin wall to my _right,_ and\r\nturning a corner strolled leisurely on, passing signs of domesticity, a\r\nwashtub, a water-butt, then a tiled approach to an open door. I now was\r\naware of the corner of a second building, also of zinc, parallel to the\r\nfirst, but taller, for I could only just see the eave. I was just going\r\nto turn off to this as a more promising field for exploration, when I\r\nheard a window open ahead of me in my original building.\r\n\r\nI am afraid I am getting obscure, so I append a rough sketch of the\r\nscene, as I partly saw and chiefly imagined it. It was window (A) that\r\nI heard open. From it I could just distinguish through the fog a hand\r\nprotrude, and throw something out\xe2\x80\x94cigar-end? The hand, a clean one with\r\na gold signet ring, rested for an instant afterwards on the sash, and\r\nthen closed the window.\r\n\r\n\r\nIllustration: diagram, Memmert Salvage Depot\r\n\r\nMy geography was clear now in one respect. That window belonged to the\r\nsame room as the banging door (B); for I distinctly heard the latter\r\nopen and shut again, opposite me on the other side of the building. It\r\nstruck me that it might be interesting to see into that room. \xe2\x80\x9cPlay the\r\ngame,\xe2\x80\x9d I reminded myself, and retreated a few yards back on tiptoe,\r\nthen turned and sauntered coolly past the window, puffing my villainous\r\npipe and taking a long deliberate look into the interior as I\r\npassed\xe2\x80\x94the more deliberate that at the first instant I realised that\r\nnobody inside was disturbing himself about me. As I had expected (in\r\nview of the fog and the time) there was artificial light within. My\r\nmental photograph was as follows: a small room with varnished deal\r\nwalls and furnished like an office; in the far right-hand corner a\r\ncounting-house desk, Grimm sitting at it on a high stool, side-face to\r\nme, counting money; opposite him in an awkward attitude a burly fellow\r\nin seaman\xe2\x80\x99s dress holding a diver\xe2\x80\x99s helmet. In the middle of the room a\r\ndeal table, and on it something big and black. Lolling on chairs near\r\nit, their backs to me and their faces turned towards the desk and the\r\ndiver, two men\xe2\x80\x94von Br\xc3\xbcning and an older man with a bald yellow head\r\n(Dollmann\xe2\x80\x99s companion on the steamer, beyond a doubt). On another\r\nchair, with its back actually tilted against the window, Dollmann.\r\n\r\nSuch were the principal features of the scene; for details I had to\r\nmake another inspection. Stooping low, I crept back, quiet as a cat,\r\ntill I was beneath the window, and, as I calculated, directly behind\r\nDollmann\xe2\x80\x99s chair. Then with great caution I raised my head. There was\r\nonly one pair of eyes in the room that I feared in the least, and that\r\nwas Grimm\xe2\x80\x99s, who sat in profile to me, farthest away. I instantly put\r\nDollmann\xe2\x80\x99s back between Grimm and me, and then made my scrutiny. As I\r\nmade it, I could feel a cold sweat distilling on my forehead and\r\ntickling my spine; not from fear or excitement, but from pure ignominy.\r\nFor beyond all doubt I was present at the meeting of a _bona-fide_\r\nsalvage company. It was pay-day, and the directors appeared to be\r\ntaking stock of work done; that was all.\r\n\r\nOver the door was an old engraving of a two-decker under full sail;\r\npinned on the wall a chart and the plan of a ship. Relics of the\r\nwrecked frigate abounded. On a shelf above the stove was a small\r\npyramid of encrusted cannon-balls, and supported on nails at odd places\r\non the walls were corroded old pistols, and what I took to be the\r\nremains of a sextant. In a corner of the floor sat a hoary little\r\ncarronade, carriage and all. None of these things affected me so much\r\nas a pile of lumber on the floor, not firewood but unmistakable\r\nwreck-wood, black as bog-oak, still caked in places with the mud of\r\nages. Nor was it the mere sight of this lumber that dumbfounded me. It\r\nwas the fact that a fragment of it, a balk of curved timber garnished\r\nwith some massive bolts, lay on the table, and was evidently an object\r\nof earnest interest. The diver had turned and was arguing with gestures\r\nover it; von Br\xc3\xbcning and Grimm were pressing another view. The diver\r\nshook his head frequently, finally shrugged his shoulders, made a\r\nsalutation, and left the room. Their movements had kept me ducking my\r\nhead pretty frequently, but I now grew almost reckless as to whether I\r\nwas seen or not. All the weaknesses of my theory crowded on me\xe2\x80\x94the\r\narguments Davies had used at Bensersiel; Fr\xc3\xa4ulein Dollmann\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nthoughtless talk; the ease (comparatively) with which I had reached\r\nthis spot, not a barrier to cross or a lock to force; the publicity of\r\ntheir passage to Memmert by Dollmann, his friend, and Grimm; and now\r\nthis glimpse of business-like routine. In a few moments I sank from\r\ndepth to depth of scepticism. Where were my mines, torpedoes, and\r\nsubmarine boats, and where my imperial conspirators? Was gold after all\r\nat the bottom of this sordid mystery? Dollmann after all a commonplace\r\ncriminal? The ladder of proof I had mounted tottered and shook beneath\r\nme. \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t be a fool,\xe2\x80\x9d said the faint voice of reason. \xe2\x80\x9cThere are your\r\nfour men. Wait.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTwo more _employ\xc3\xa9s_ came into the room in quick succession and received\r\nwages; one looking like a fireman, the other of a superior type, the\r\nskipper of a tug, say. There was another discussion with this latter\r\nover the balk of wreck-wood, and this man, too, shrugged his shoulders.\r\nHis departure appeared to end the meeting. Grimm shut up a ledger, and\r\nI shrank down on my knees, for a general shifting of chairs began. At\r\nthe same time, from the other side of the building, I heard my knot of\r\nmen retreating beachwards, spitting and chatting as they went.\r\nPresently someone walked across the room towards my window. I sidled\r\naway on all fours, rose and flattened myself erect against the wall, a\r\nsickening despondency on me; my intention to slink away south-east as\r\nsoon as the coast was clear. But the sound that came next pricked me\r\nlike an electric shock; it was the tinkle and scrape of curtain-rings.\r\n\r\nQuick as thought I was back in my old position, to find my view barred\r\nby a cretonne curtain. It was in one piece, with no chink for my\r\nbenefit, but it did not hang straight, bulging towards me under the\r\npressure of something\xe2\x80\x94human shoulders by the shape. Dollmann, I\r\nconcluded, was still in his old place. I now was exasperated to find\r\nthat I could scarcely hear a word that was said, not even by pressing\r\nmy ear against the glass. It was not that the speakers were of set\r\npurpose hushing their voices\xe2\x80\x94they used an ordinary tone for intimate\r\ndiscussion\xe2\x80\x94but the glass and curtain deadened the actual words. Still,\r\nI was soon able to distinguish general characteristics. Von Br\xc3\xbcning\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nvoice\xe2\x80\x94the only one I had ever heard before\xe2\x80\x94I recognised at once; he was\r\non the left of the table, and Dollmann\xe2\x80\x99s I knew from his position. The\r\nthird was a harsh croak, belonging to the old gentleman whom, for\r\nconvenience, I shall prematurely begin to call Herr B\xc3\xb6hme. It was too\r\nold a voice to be Grimm\xe2\x80\x99s; besides, it had the ring of authority, and\r\nwas dealing at the moment in sharp interrogations. Three of its\r\nsentences I caught in their entirety. \xe2\x80\x9cWhen was that?\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cThey went no\r\nfarther?\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cToo long; out of the question.\xe2\x80\x9d Dollmann\xe2\x80\x99s voice, though\r\nnearest to me, was the least audible of all. It was a dogged monotone,\r\nand what was that odd movement of the curtain at his back? Yes, his\r\nhands were behind him clutching and kneading a fold of the cretonne.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are feeling uncomfortable, my friend,\xe2\x80\x9d was my comment. Suddenly he\r\nthrew back his head\xe2\x80\x94I saw the dent of it\xe2\x80\x94and spoke up so that I could\r\nnot miss a word. \xe2\x80\x9cVery well, sir, you shall see them at supper\r\nto-night; I will ask them both.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n(You will not be surprised to learn that I instantly looked at my\r\nwatch\xe2\x80\x94though it takes long to write what I have described\xe2\x80\x94but the time\r\nwas only a quarter to four.) He added something about the fog, and his\r\nchair creaked. Ducking promptly I heard the curtain-rings jar, and:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThick as ever.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour report, Herr Dollmann,\xe2\x80\x9d said B\xc3\xb6hme, curtly. Dollmann left the\r\nwindow and moved his chair up to the table; the other two drew in\r\ntheirs and settled themselves.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Chatham,_\xe2\x80\x9d said Dollmann, as if announcing a heading. It was an easy\r\nword to catch, rapped out sharp, and you can imagine how it startled\r\nme. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s where you\xe2\x80\x99ve been for the last month!\xe2\x80\x9d I said to myself. A\r\nmap crackled and I knew they were bending over it, while Dollmann\r\nexplained something. But now my exasperation became acute, for not a\r\nsyllable more reached me. Squatting back on my heels, I cast about for\r\nexpedients. Should I steal round and try the door? Too dangerous. Climb\r\nto the roof and listen down the stove-pipe? Too noisy, and generally\r\nhopeless. I tried for a downward purchase on the upper half of the\r\nwindow, which was of the simple sort in two sections, working\r\nvertically. No use; it resisted gentle pressure, would start with a\r\nsudden jar if I forced it. I pulled out Davies\xe2\x80\x99s knife and worked the\r\npoint of the blade between sash and frame to give it play\xe2\x80\x94no result;\r\nbut the knife was a nautical one, with a marlin-spike as well as a big\r\nblade.\r\n\r\nJust now the door within opened and shut again, and I heard steps\r\napproaching round the corner to my right. I had the presence of mind\r\nnot to lose a moment, but moved silently away (blessing the deep\r\nFrisian sand) round the corner of the big parallel building. Someone\r\nwhom I could not see walked past till his boots clattered on tiles,\r\nnext resounded on boards. \xe2\x80\x9cGrimm in his living-room,\xe2\x80\x9d I inferred. The\r\nprecious minutes ebbed away\xe2\x80\x94five, ten, fifteen. Had he gone for good? I\r\ndared not return otherwise. Eighteen\xe2\x80\x94he was coming out! This time I\r\nstole forward boldly when the man had just passed, dimly saw a figure,\r\nand clearly enough the glint of a white paper he was holding. He made\r\nhis circuit and re-entered the room.\r\n\r\nHere I felt and conquered a relapse to scepticism. \xe2\x80\x9cIf this is an\r\nimportant conclave why don\xe2\x80\x99t they set guards?\xe2\x80\x9d Answer, the only\r\npossible one, \xe2\x80\x9cBecause they stand alone. Their _employ\xc3\xa9s,_ like\r\n_everyone_ we had met hitherto, know nothing. The real object of this\r\nsalvage company (a poor speculation, I opined) is solely to afford a\r\npretext for the conclave.\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cWhy the curtain, even?\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cBecause there are\r\nmaps, stupid!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI was back again at the window, but as impotent as ever against that\r\neven stream of low confidential talk. But I would not give up. Fate and\r\nthe fog had brought me here, the one solitary soul perhaps who by the\r\nchain of circumstances had both the will and the opportunity to wrest\r\ntheir secret from these four men.\r\n\r\nThe marlin-spike! Where the lower half of the window met the sill it\r\nsank into a shallow groove. I thrust the point of the spike down into\r\nthe interstice between sash and frame and heaved with a slowly\r\nincreasing force, which I could regulate to the fraction of an ounce,\r\non this powerful lever. The sash gave, with the faintest possible\r\nprotest, and by imperceptible degrees I lifted it to the top of the\r\ngroove, and the least bit above it, say half an inch in all; but it\r\nmade an appreciable difference to the sounds within, as when you remove\r\nyour foot from a piano\xe2\x80\x99s soft pedal. I could do no more, for there was\r\nno further fulcrum for the spike, and I dared not gamble away what I\r\nhad won by using my hands.\r\n\r\nHope sank again when I placed my cheek on the damp sill, and my ear to\r\nthe chink. My men were close round the table referring to papers which\r\nI heard rustle. Dollmann\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9creport\xe2\x80\x9d was evidently over, and I rarely\r\nheard his voice; Grimm\xe2\x80\x99s occasionally, von Br\xc3\xbcning\xe2\x80\x99s and B\xc3\xb6hme\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nfrequently; but, as before, it was the latter only that I could ever\r\ncount on for an intelligible word. For, unfortunately, the villains of\r\nthe piece plotted without any regard to dramatic fitness or to my\r\ninterests. Immersed in a subject with which they were all familiar,\r\nthey were allusive, elliptic, and persistently technical. Many of the\r\nwords I did catch were unknown to me. The rest were, for the most part,\r\neither letters of the alphabet or statistical figures, of depth,\r\ndistance, and, once or twice, of time. The letters of the alphabet\r\nrecurred often, and seemed, as far as I could make out, to represent\r\nthe key to the cipher. The numbers clustering round them were mostly\r\nvery small, with decimals. What maddened me most was the scarcity of\r\nplain nouns.\r\n\r\nTo report what I heard to the reader would be impossible; so chaotic\r\nwas most of it that it left no impression on my own memory. All I can\r\ndo is to tell him what fragments stuck, and what nebulous\r\nclassification I involved. The letters ran from A to G, and my best\r\ncontinuous chance came when B\xc3\xb6hme, reading rapidly from a paper, I\r\nthink, went through the letters, backwards, from G, adding remarks to\r\neach; thus: \xe2\x80\x9cG. . . completed.\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cF. . . bad. . . 1.3 (metres?). . .2.5\r\n(kilometres?).\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cE . . . thirty-two. . . 1.2.\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cD. . . 3 weeks. . .\r\nthirty.\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cC. . .\xe2\x80\x9d and so on.\r\n\r\nAnother time he went through this list again, only naming each letter\r\nhimself, and receiving laconic answers from Grimm\xe2\x80\x94answers which seemed\r\nto be numbers, but I could not be sure. For minutes together I caught\r\nnothing but the scratching of pens and inarticulate mutterings. But out\r\nof the muck-heap I picked five pearls\xe2\x80\x94four sibilant nouns and a name\r\nthat I knew before. The nouns were \xe2\x80\x9cSchleppboote\xe2\x80\x9d (tugs); \xe2\x80\x9cWassertiefe\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n(depth of water); \xe2\x80\x9cEisenbahn\xe2\x80\x9d (railway); \xe2\x80\x9cLotsen\xe2\x80\x9d (pilots). The name,\r\nalso sibilant and thus easier to hear, was \xe2\x80\x9cEsens\xe2\x80\x9d.\r\n\r\nTwo or three times I had to stand back and ease my cramped neck, and on\r\neach occasion I looked at my watch, for I was listening against time,\r\njust as we had rowed against time. We were going to be asked to supper,\r\nand must be back aboard the yacht in time to receive the invitation.\r\nThe fog still brooded heavily and the light, always bad, was growing\r\nworse. How would _they_ get back? How had they come from Juist? Could\r\nwe forestall them? Questions of time, tide, distance\xe2\x80\x94just the odious\r\nsort of sums I was unfit to cope with\xe2\x80\x94were distracting my attention\r\nwhen it should have been wholly elsewhere. 4.20\xe2\x80\x944.25\xe2\x80\x94now it was past\r\n4.30 when Davies said the bank would cover. I should have to make for\r\nthe beacon; but it was fatally near that steamboat path, etc., and I\r\nstill at intervals heard voices from there. It must have been about\r\n4.35 when there was another shifting of chairs within. Then someone\r\nrose, collected papers, and went out; someone else, _without_ rising\r\n(therefore Grimm), followed him.\r\n\r\nThere was silence in the room for a minute, and after that, for the\r\nfirst time, I heard some plain colloquial German, with no accompaniment\r\nof scratching or rustling. \xe2\x80\x9cI must wait for this,\xe2\x80\x9d I thought, and\r\nwaited.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe insists on coming,\xe2\x80\x9d said B\xc3\xb6hme.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAch!\xe2\x80\x9d (an ejaculation of surprise and protest from von Br\xc3\xbcning).\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI said the 25th.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe tide serves well. The night-train, of course. Tell Grimm to be\r\nready\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d (An inaudible question from von Br\xc3\xbcning.)\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, any weather.\xe2\x80\x9d A laugh from von Br\xc3\xbcning and some words I could not\r\ncatch.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly one, with half a load.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c.....meet?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt the station.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo\xe2\x80\x94how\xe2\x80\x99s the fog?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis appeared to be really the end. Both men rose and steps came\r\ntowards the window. I leapt aside as I heard it thrown up, and covered\r\nby the noise backed into safety. Von Br\xc3\xbcning called \xe2\x80\x9cGrimm!\xe2\x80\x9d and that,\r\nand the open window, decided me that my line of advance was now too\r\ndangerous to retreat by. The only alternative was to make a circuit\r\nround the bigger of the two buildings\xe2\x80\x94and an interminable circuit it\r\nseemed\xe2\x80\x94and all the while I knew my compass-course \xe2\x80\x9csouth-east\xe2\x80\x9d was\r\ngrowing nugatory. I passed a padlocked door, two corners, and faced the\r\nvoid of fog. Out came the compass, and I steadied myself for the sum.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSouth-east before\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99m farther to the eastward now\xe2\x80\x94east will about do\xe2\x80\x9d;\r\nand off I went, with an error of four whole points, over tussocks and\r\ndeep sand. The beach seemed much farther off than I had thought, and I\r\nbegan to get alarmed, puzzled over the compass several times, and\r\nfinally realised that I had lost my way. I had the sense not to make\r\nmatters worse by trying to find it again, and, as the lesser of two\r\nevils, blew my whistle, softly at first, then louder. The bray of a\r\nfoghorn sounded right _behind_ me. I whistled again and then ran for my\r\nlife, the horn sounding at intervals. In three or four minutes I was on\r\nthe beach and in the dinghy.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXIII.\r\nA Change of Tactics\r\n\r\n\r\nWe pushed off without a word, and paddled out of sight of the beach. A\r\nvoice was approaching, hailing us. \xe2\x80\x9cHail back,\xe2\x80\x9d whispered Davies;\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cpretend we\xe2\x80\x99re a galliot.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHo-a,\xe2\x80\x9d I shouted, \xe2\x80\x9cwhere am I?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOff Memmert,\xe2\x80\x9d came back. \xe2\x80\x9cWhere are you bound?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDelfzyl,\xe2\x80\x9d whispered Davies.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDelf-zyl,\xe2\x80\x9d I bawled.\r\n\r\nA sentence ending with \xe2\x80\x9canchor\xe2\x80\x9d was returned.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe flood\xe2\x80\x99s tearing east,\xe2\x80\x9d whispered Davies; \xe2\x80\x9csit still.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe heard no more, and, after a few minutes\xe2\x80\x99 drifting, \xe2\x80\x9cWhat luck?\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nDavies.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne or two clues, and an invitation to supper.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe clues I left till later; the invitation was the thing, and I\r\nexplained its urgency.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow will _they_ get back?\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies; \xe2\x80\x9cif the fog lasts the\r\nsteamer\xe2\x80\x99s sure to be late.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe can count for nothing,\xe2\x80\x9d I answered. \xe2\x80\x9cThere was some little\r\nsteamboat off the dep\xc3\xb4t, and the fog may lift. Which is our quickest\r\nway?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt this tide, a bee-line to Norderney by compass; we shall have water\r\nover all the banks.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe had all his preparations made, the lamp lit in advance, the compass\r\nin position, and we started at once; he at the bow-oar where he had\r\nbetter control over the boat\xe2\x80\x99s nose; lamp and compass on the floor\r\nbetween us. Twilight thickened into darkness\xe2\x80\x94a choking, pasty\r\ndarkness\xe2\x80\x94and still we sped unfalteringly over that trackless waste,\r\nsitting and swinging in our little pool of stifled orange light. To\r\ndrown fatigue and suspense I conned over my clues, and tried to carve\r\ninto my memory every fugitive word I had overheard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are there seven of round here?\xe2\x80\x9d I called back to Davies once\r\n(thinking of A to G). \xe2\x80\x9cSorry,\xe2\x80\x9d I added, for no answer came.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI see a star,\xe2\x80\x9d was my next word, after a long interval. \xe2\x80\x9cNow it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ngone. There it is again! Right aft!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s Borkum light,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, presently; \xe2\x80\x9cthe fog\xe2\x80\x99s lifting.\xe2\x80\x9d A\r\nkeen wind from the west struck our faces, and as swiftly as it had come\r\nthe fog rolled away from us, in one mighty mass, stripping clean and\r\npure the starry dome of heaven, still bright with the western\r\nafter-glow, and beginning to redden in the east to the rising moon.\r\nNorderney light was flashing ahead, and Davies could take his tired\r\neyes from the pool of light.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDamn!\xe2\x80\x9d was all he uttered in the way of gratitude for this mercy, and\r\nI felt very much the same; for in a fog Davies in a dinghy was a match\r\nfor a steamer; in a clear he lost his handicap.\r\n\r\nIt was a quarter to seven. \xe2\x80\x9cAn hour\xe2\x80\x99ll do it, if we buck up,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\npronounced, after taking a rough bearing with the two lights. He\r\npointed out a star to me, which we were to keep exactly astern, and\r\nagain I applied to their labour my aching back and smarting palms.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat did you say about seven of something?\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are there seven of hereabouts?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIslands, of course,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies. \xe2\x80\x9cIs that the clue?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMaybe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen followed the most singular of all our confabulations. Two memories\r\nare better than one, and the sooner I carved the cipher into his memory\r\nas well as mine the better record we should have. So, with rigid\r\neconomy of breath, I snapped out all my story, and answered his\r\nbreathless questions. It saved me from being mesmerised by the star,\r\nand both of us from the consciousness of over-fatigue.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSpying at Chatham, the blackguard?\xe2\x80\x9d he hissed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you make of it?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing about battleships, mines, forts?\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing about the Ems, Emden, Wilhelmshaven?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing about transports?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI believe\xe2\x80\x94I was right\xe2\x80\x94after all\xe2\x80\x94something to do\xe2\x80\x94with the\r\nchannels\xe2\x80\x94behind islands.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd so that outworn creed took a new lease of life; though for my part\r\nthe words that clashed with it were those that had sunk the deepest.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEsens,\xe2\x80\x9d I protested; \xe2\x80\x9cthat town behind Bensersiel.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWassertiefe, Lotsen, Schleppboote,\xe2\x80\x9d spluttered Davies.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cKilometre\xe2\x80\x94Eisenbahn,\xe2\x80\x9d from me, and so on.\r\n\r\nI should earn the just execration of the reader if I continued to\r\nreport such a dialogue. Suffice to say that we realised very soon that\r\nthe substance of the plot was still a riddle. On the other hand, there\r\nwas fresh scent, abundance of it; and the question was already taking\r\nshape\xe2\x80\x94were we to follow it up or revert to last night\xe2\x80\x99s decision and\r\nstrike with what weapons we had? It was a pressing question, too, the\r\nlast of many\xe2\x80\x94was there to be no end to the emergencies of this crowded\r\nday?\xe2\x80\x94pressing for reasons I could not define, while convinced that we\r\nmust be ready with an answer by supper-time to-night.\r\n\r\nMeantime, we were nearing Norderney; the See Gat was crossed, and with\r\nthe last of the flood tide fair beneath us, and the red light on the\r\nwest pier burning ahead, we began insensibly to relax our efforts. But\r\nI dared not rest, for I was at that point of exhaustion when mechanical\r\nmovement was my only hope.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLight astern,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, thickly. \xe2\x80\x9cTwo\xe2\x80\x94white and red.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSteamer,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies; \xe2\x80\x9cgoing south though.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThree now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA neat triangle of gems\xe2\x80\x94topaz, ruby, and emerald\xe2\x80\x94hung steady behind us.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTurned east,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies. \xe2\x80\x9cBuck up\xe2\x80\x94steamer from Juist. No, by Jove!\r\ntoo small. What is it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOn we laboured, while the gems waxed in brilliancy as the steamer\r\noverhauled us.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEasy,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, \xe2\x80\x9cI seem to know those lights\xe2\x80\x94the _Blitz_\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nlaunch\xe2\x80\x94don\xe2\x80\x99t let\xe2\x80\x99s be caught rowing like madmen in a muck sweat. Paddle\r\ninshore a bit.\xe2\x80\x9d He was right, and, as in a dream, I saw hurrying and\r\npalpitating up the same little pinnace that had towed us out of\r\nBensersiel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99re done for now,\xe2\x80\x9d I remember thinking, for the guilt of the runaway\r\nwas strong in me; and an old remark of von Br\xc3\xbcning\xe2\x80\x99s about \xe2\x80\x9cpolice\xe2\x80\x9d was\r\nin my ears. But she was level with and past us before I could sink far\r\ninto despair.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThree of them behind the hood,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies: \xe2\x80\x9cwhat are we to do?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFollow,\xe2\x80\x9d I answered, and essayed a feeble stroke, but the blade\r\nscuttered over the surface.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet\xe2\x80\x99s wait about for a bit,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99re late anyhow. If they\r\ngo to the yacht they\xe2\x80\x99ll think we\xe2\x80\x99re ashore.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOur shore clothes\xe2\x80\x94lying about.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you up to talking?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; but we must. The least suspicion\xe2\x80\x99ll do for us now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGive me your scull, old chap, and put on your coat.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe extinguished the lantern, lit a pipe, and then rowed slowly on,\r\nwhile I sat on a slack heap in the stern and devoted my last resources\r\nof will to the emancipation of the spirit from the tired flesh.\r\n\r\nIn ten minutes or so we were rounding the pier, and there was the\r\nyacht\xe2\x80\x99s top-mast against the sky. I saw, too, that the launch was\r\nalongside of her, and told Davies so. Then I lit a cigarette, and made\r\na lamentable effort to whistle. Davies followed suit, and emitted a\r\nstrange melody which I took to be \xe2\x80\x9cHome, Sweet Home,\xe2\x80\x9d but he has not\r\nthe slightest ear for music.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, they\xe2\x80\x99re on board, I believe,\xe2\x80\x9d said I; \xe2\x80\x9cthe cabin\xe2\x80\x99s lighted. Ahoy\r\nthere!\xe2\x80\x9d I shouted as we came up. \xe2\x80\x9cWho\xe2\x80\x99s that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood evening, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d said a sailor, who was fending off the yacht with\r\na boathook. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s Commander von Br\xc3\xbcning\xe2\x80\x99s launch. I think the gentlemen\r\nwant to see you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBefore we could answer, an exclamation of: \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, here they are!\xe2\x80\x9d came\r\nfrom the deck of the _Dulcibella_, and the dim form of von Br\xc3\xbcning\r\nhimself emerged from the companionway. There was something of a scuffle\r\ndown below, which the Commander nearly succeeded in drowning by the\r\nbreeziness of his greeting. Meanwhile, the ladder creaked under fresh\r\nweight, and Dollmann appeared.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs that you, Herr Davies?\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHullo! Herr Dollmann,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies; \xe2\x80\x9chow are you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI must explain that we had floated up between the yacht and the launch,\r\nwhose sailors had passed her a little aside in order to give us room.\r\nHer starboard side-light was just behind and above us, pouring its\r\ngreen rays obliquely over the deck of the _Dulcibella_, while we and\r\nthe dinghy were in deep shadow between. The most studied calculation\r\ncould not have secured us more favourable conditions for a moment which\r\nI had always dreaded\xe2\x80\x94the meeting of Davies and Dollmann. The former,\r\nhaving shortened his sculls, just sat where he was, half turned towards\r\nthe yacht and looking up at his enemy. No lineament of his own face\r\ncould have been visible to the latter, while those pitiless green\r\nrays\xe2\x80\x94you know their ravaging effect on the human physiognomy\xe2\x80\x94struck\r\nfull on Dollmann\xe2\x80\x99s face. It was my first fair view of it at close\r\nquarters, and, secure in my background of gloom, I feasted with a\r\nluxury of superstitious abhorrence on the livid smiling mask that for a\r\nfew moments stooped peering down towards Davies. One of the caprices of\r\nthe crude light was to obliterate, or at any rate so penetrate, beard\r\nand moustache, as to reveal in outline lips and chin, the features in\r\nwhich defects of character are most surely betrayed, especially when\r\nyour victim smiles. Accuse me, if you will, of stooping to melodramatic\r\nembroidery; object that my own prejudiced fancy contributed to the\r\nresult; but I can, nevertheless, never efface the impression of\r\nmalignant perfidy and base passion, exaggerated to caricature, that I\r\nreceived in those few instants. Another caprice of the light was to\r\nidentify the man with the portrait of him when younger and\r\nclean-shaven, in the frontispiece of his own book; and another still,\r\nthe most repulsively whimsical of all, was to call forth a strong\r\nresemblance to the sweet young girl who had been with us yesterday.\r\n\r\nEnough! I shall never offend again in this way. In reality I am much\r\nmore inclined to laugh than shudder over this meeting; for meanwhile\r\nthe third of our self-invited guests had with stertorous puffing risen\r\nto the stage, for all the world like a demon out of a trapdoor,\r\nspecially when he entered the zone of that unearthly light. And there\r\nthey stood in a row, like delinquents at judgement, while we, the true\r\nculprits, had only passively to accept explanations. Of course these\r\nwere plausible enough. Dollmann having seen the yacht in port that\r\nmorning had called on his return from Memmert to ask us to supper.\r\nFinding no one aboard, and concluding we were ashore, he had meant to\r\nleave a note for Davies in the cabin. His friend, Herr B\xc3\xb6hme, _\xe2\x80\x9cthe\r\ndistinguished engineer,\xe2\x80\x9d_ was anxious to see over the little vessel\r\nthat had come so far, and he knew that Davies would not mind the\r\nintrusion. Not at all, said Davies; would not they stop and have\r\ndrinks? No, but would we come to supper at Dollmann\xe2\x80\x99s villa? With\r\npleasure, said Davies, but we had to change first. Up to this point we\r\nhad been masters of the situation; but here von Br\xc3\xbcning, who alone of\r\nthe three appeared to be entirely at his ease, made the _retour\r\noffensif_.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere have you been?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, rowing about since the fog cleared,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies.\r\n\r\nI suppose he thought that evasion would pass muster, but as he spoke, I\r\nnoticed to my horror that a stray beam of light was playing on the\r\nbunch of white cotton-waste that adorned one of the rowlocks: for we\r\nhad forgotten to remove these tell-tale appendages. So I added:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAfter ducks again\xe2\x80\x9d; and, lifting one of the guns, let the light flash\r\non its barrel. To my own ears my voice sounded husky and distant.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlways ducks,\xe2\x80\x9d laughed von Br\xc3\xbcning. \xe2\x80\x9cNo luck, I suppose?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies; \xe2\x80\x9cbut it ought to be a good time after sunset\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat, with a rising tide and the banks covered?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe saw some,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, sullenly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI tell you what, my zealous young sportsmen, you\xe2\x80\x99re rash to leave your\r\nboat at anchor here after dark without a light. I came aboard to find\r\nyour lamp and set it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, thanks,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies; \xe2\x80\x9cwe took it with us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo see to shoot by?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe laughed uncomfortably, and Davies compassed a wonderful German\r\nphrase to the effect that \xe2\x80\x9cit might come in useful\xe2\x80\x9d. Happily the matter\r\nwent no farther, for the position was a strained one at the best, and\r\nwould not bear lengthening. The launch went alongside, and the invaders\r\nevacuated British soil, looking, for all von Br\xc3\xbcning\xe2\x80\x99s flippant\r\nnonchalance, a rather crestfallen party. So much so, that, acute as was\r\nmy anxiety, I took courage to whisper to Davies, while the transhipment\r\nof Herr B\xc3\xb6hme was proceeding: \xe2\x80\x9cAsk Dollmann to stay while we dress.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy?\xe2\x80\x9d he whispered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo on.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI say, Herr Dollmann,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, \xe2\x80\x9cwon\xe2\x80\x99t you stay on board with us\r\nwhile we dress? There\xe2\x80\x99s a lot to tell you, and\xe2\x80\x94and we can follow on\r\nwith you when we\xe2\x80\x99re ready.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDollmann had not yet stepped into the launch. \xe2\x80\x9cWith pleasure,\xe2\x80\x9d he said;\r\nbut there followed an ominous silence, broken by von Br\xc3\xbcning.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, come along, Dollmann, and let them alone,\xe2\x80\x9d he said brusquely.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll be horribly in the way down there, and we shall never get any\r\nsupper if you keep them yarning.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd it\xe2\x80\x99s now a quarter-past eight o\xe2\x80\x99clock,\xe2\x80\x9d grumbled Herr B\xc3\xb6hme from\r\nhis corner behind the hood. Dollmann submitted, and excused himself,\r\nand the launch steamed away.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think I twig,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, as he helped, almost hoisted, me aboard.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRather risky though\xe2\x80\x94eh?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI knew they\xe2\x80\x99d object\xe2\x80\x94only wanted to make sure.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe cabin was just as we had left it, our shore clothes lying in\r\ndisorder on the bunks, a locker or two half open.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I wonder what they did down here,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies.\r\n\r\nFor my part I went straight to the bookshelf.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoes anything strike you about this?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked, kneeling on the sofa.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLogbook\xe2\x80\x99s shifted,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll swear it was at the end\r\nbefore.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat doesn\xe2\x80\x99t matter. Anything else?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy Jove!\xe2\x80\x94where\xe2\x80\x99s Dollmann\xe2\x80\x99s book?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s here all right, but not where it should be.\xe2\x80\x9d I had been reading\r\nit, you remember, overnight, and in the morning had replaced it in full\r\nview among the other books. I now found it behind them, in a wrenched\r\nattitude, which showed that someone who had no time to spare had pushed\r\nit roughly inwards.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you make of that?\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies.\r\n\r\nHe produced long drinks, and we allowed ourselves ten minutes of\r\nabsolute rest, stretched at full length on the sofas.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey don\xe2\x80\x99t trust Dollmann,\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9cI spotted that at Memmert even.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFirst, when they were talking about you and me. He was on his defence,\r\nand in a deuce of a funk, too. B\xc3\xb6hme was pressing him hard. Again, at\r\nthe end, when he left the room followed by Grimm, who I\xe2\x80\x99m certain was\r\nsent to watch him. It was while he was away that the other two arranged\r\nthat rendezvous for the night of the 25th. And again just now, when you\r\nasked him to stay. I believe it\xe2\x80\x99s working out as I thought it would.\r\nVon Br\xc3\xbcning, and through him B\xc3\xb6hme (who is the \xe2\x80\x9cengineer from Bremen\xe2\x80\x9d),\r\nknow the story of that short cut and suspect that it was an attempt on\r\nyour life. Dollmann daren\xe2\x80\x99t confess to that, because, morality apart,\r\nit could only have been prompted by extreme necessity\xe2\x80\x94that is, by the\r\nknowledge that you were really dangerous, and not merely an inquisitive\r\nstranger. Now _we_ know his motive; but they don\xe2\x80\x99t yet. The position of\r\nthat book proves it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe shoved it in?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo prevent them seeing it. There\xe2\x80\x99s no earthly reason why _they_ should\r\nhave hidden it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen we\xe2\x80\x99re getting on,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies. \xe2\x80\x9cThat shows they know his real\r\nname, or why should he shove the book in? But they don\xe2\x80\x99t know he wrote\r\na book, and that I have a copy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt any rate he _thinks_ they don\xe2\x80\x99t; we can\xe2\x80\x99t say more than that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what does he think about me\xe2\x80\x94and you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s the point. Ten to one he\xe2\x80\x99s in tortures of doubt, and would give\r\na fortune to have five minutes\xe2\x80\x99 talk alone with you to see how the land\r\nlies and get your version of the short cut incident. But they won\xe2\x80\x99t let\r\nhim. They want to watch him in our company and us in his; you see it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nan interesting reunion for you and him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, let\xe2\x80\x99s get into these beastly clothes for it,\xe2\x80\x9d groaned Davis. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nshall have a plunge overboard.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSomething drastic was required, and I followed his example, curious as\r\nthe hour was for bathing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI believe I know what happened just now,\xe2\x80\x9d said I, as we plied rough\r\ntowels in the warmth below. \xe2\x80\x9cThey steamed up and found nobody on board.\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I\xe2\x80\x99ll leave a note,\xe2\x80\x99 says Dollmann. \xe2\x80\x98No independent communications,\xe2\x80\x99\r\nsay they (or think they), \xe2\x80\x98we\xe2\x80\x99ll come too, and take the chance of\r\ninspecting this hornets\xe2\x80\x99 nest.\xe2\x80\x99 Down they go, and Dollmann, who knows\r\nwhat to look for first, sees that damning bit of evidence staring him\r\nin the face. They look casually at the shelf among other things\xe2\x80\x94examine\r\nthe logbook, say\xe2\x80\x94and he manages to push his own book out of sight. But\r\nhe couldn\xe2\x80\x99t replace it when the interruption came. The action would\r\nhave attracted attention _then,_ and B\xc3\xb6hme made him leave the cabin in\r\nadvance, you know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is all very well,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, pausing in his toilet, \xe2\x80\x9cbut do\r\nthey guess how we\xe2\x80\x99ve spent the day? By Jove, Carruthers, that chart\r\nwith the square cut out; there it is on the rack!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe must chance it, and bluff for all we\xe2\x80\x99re worth,\xe2\x80\x9d I said. The fact\r\nwas that Davies could not be brought to realise that he had done\r\nanything very remarkable that day; yet those fourteen sinuous miles\r\ntraversed blindfold, to say nothing of the return journey and my own\r\nexploits, made up an achievement audacious and improbable enough to\r\nout-distance suspicion. Nevertheless, von Br\xc3\xbcning\xe2\x80\x99s banter had been\r\ndisquieting, and if an inkling of our expedition had crossed his mind\r\nor theirs, there were ways of testing us which it would require all our\r\neffrontery to defeat.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you looking for?\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies. I was at the collar and stud\r\nstage, but had broken off to study the time-table which we had bought\r\nthat morning.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSomebody insists on coming by the night train to somewhere, on the\r\n25th,\xe2\x80\x9d I reminded him. \xe2\x80\x9cB\xc3\xb6hme, von Br\xc3\xbcning, and Grimm are to meet the\r\nSomebody.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt a railway station! I don\xe2\x80\x99t know where. They seemed to take it for\r\ngranted. But it must be somewhere on the sea, because B\xc3\xb6hme said, \xe2\x80\x98the\r\ntide serves.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt may be anywhere from Emden to Hamburg.\xe2\x80\x9d _[See Map B]_\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, there\xe2\x80\x99s a limit; it\xe2\x80\x99s probably somewhere near. Grimm was to come,\r\nand he\xe2\x80\x99s at Memmert.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere\xe2\x80\x99s the map.... Emden and Norddeich are the only coast stations\r\ntill you get to Wilhelmshaven\xe2\x80\x94no, to Carolinensiel; but those are a\r\nlong way east.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd Emden\xe2\x80\x99s a long way south. Say Norddeich then; but according to\r\nthis there\xe2\x80\x99s no train there after 6.15 p.m.; that\xe2\x80\x99s hardly \xe2\x80\x98night\xe2\x80\x99.\r\nWhen\xe2\x80\x99s high tide on the 25th?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet\xe2\x80\x99s see\xe2\x80\x948.30 here to-night\xe2\x80\x94Norddeich\xe2\x80\x99ll be the same. Somewhere\r\nbetween 10.30 and 11 on the 25th.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s a train at Emden at 9.22 from Leer and the south, and one at\r\n10.50 from the north.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you counting on another fog?\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, mockingly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; but I want to know what our plans are.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan\xe2\x80\x99t we wait till this cursed inspection\xe2\x80\x99s over?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, we can\xe2\x80\x99t; we should come to grief.\xe2\x80\x9d This was no barren truism, for\r\nI was ready with a plan of my own, though reluctant to broach it to\r\nDavies.\r\n\r\nMeanwhile, ready or not, we had to start. The cabin we left as it was,\r\nchanging nothing and hiding nothing; the safest course to take, we\r\nthought, in spite of the risk of further search. But, as usual, I\r\ntransferred my diary to my breast-pocket, and made sure that the two\r\nofficial letters from England were safe in a compartment of it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you propose?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked, when we were in the dinghy again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a case of \xe2\x80\x98as you were\xe2\x80\x99,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies. \xe2\x80\x9cTo-day\xe2\x80\x99s trip was a\r\nchance we shall never get again. We must go back to last night\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ndecision\xe2\x80\x94tell them that we\xe2\x80\x99re going to stay on here for a bit.\r\nShooting, I suppose we shall have to say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd courting?\xe2\x80\x9d I suggested.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, they know all about that. And then we must watch for a chance of\r\ntackling Dollmann privately. Not to-night, because we want time to\r\nconsider those clues of yours.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Consider\xe2\x80\x99?\xe2\x80\x9d I said: \xe2\x80\x9cthat\xe2\x80\x99s putting it mildly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe were at the ladder, and what a languid stiffness oppressed me I did\r\nnot know till I touched its freezing rungs, each one of which seared my\r\nsore palms like red-hot iron.\r\n\r\nThe overdue steamer was just arriving as we set foot on the quay. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd\r\nyet, by Jove! why not to-night?\xe2\x80\x9d pursued Davies, beginning to stride up\r\nthe pier at a pace I could not imitate.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSteady on,\xe2\x80\x9d I protested; \xe2\x80\x9cand, look here, I disagree altogether. I\r\nbelieve to-day has doubled our chances, but unless we alter our tactics\r\nit has doubled our risks. We\xe2\x80\x99ve involved ourselves in too tangled a\r\nweb. I don\xe2\x80\x99t like this inspection, and I fear that foxy old B\xc3\xb6hme who\r\nprompted it. The mere fact of their inviting us shows that we stand\r\nbadly; for it runs in the teeth of Br\xc3\xbcning\xe2\x80\x99s warning at Bensersiel, and\r\nsmells uncommonly like arrest. There\xe2\x80\x99s a rift between Dollmann and the\r\nothers, but it\xe2\x80\x99s a ticklish matter to drive our wedge in; as to\r\n_to-night,_ hopeless; they\xe2\x80\x99re on the watch, and won\xe2\x80\x99t give us a chance.\r\nAnd after all, do we know enough? We don\xe2\x80\x99t know why he fled from\r\nEngland and turned German. It may have been an extraditable crime, but\r\nit may not. Supposing he defies us? There\xe2\x80\x99s the girl, you see\xe2\x80\x94she ties\r\nour hands, and if he once gets wind of that, and trades on our\r\nweakness, the game\xe2\x80\x99s up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you driving at?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe want to detach him from Germany, but he\xe2\x80\x99ll probably go to any\r\nlengths rather than abandon his position here. His attempt on you is\r\nthe measure of his interest in it. Now, is to-day to be wasted?\xe2\x80\x9d We\r\nwere passing through the public gardens, and I dropped on to a seat for\r\na moment\xe2\x80\x99s rest, crackling dead leaves under me. Davies remained\r\nstanding, and pecked at the gravel with his toe.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe have got two valuable clues,\xe2\x80\x9d I went on; \xe2\x80\x9cthat rendezvous on the\r\n25th is one, and the name Esens is the other. We may consider them to\r\neternity; I vote we act on them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow?\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99re under a searchlight here; and if we\xe2\x80\x99re\r\ncaught\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour plan\xe2\x80\x94ugh!\xe2\x80\x94it\xe2\x80\x99s as risky as mine, and more so,\xe2\x80\x9d I replied, rising\r\nwith a jerk, for a spasm of cramp took me. \xe2\x80\x9cWe must separate,\xe2\x80\x9d I added,\r\nas we walked on. \xe2\x80\x9cWe want, at one stroke, to prove to them that we\xe2\x80\x99re\r\nharmless, and to get a fresh start. I go back to London.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo London!\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies. We were passing under an arc lamp, and, for\r\nthe dismay his face showed, I might have said Kamchatka.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, after all, it\xe2\x80\x99s where I ought to be at this moment,\xe2\x80\x9d I observed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I forgot. And me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou can\xe2\x80\x99t get on without me, so you lay up the yacht here\xe2\x80\x94taking your\r\ntime.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhile you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAfter making inquiries about Dollmann\xe2\x80\x99s past I double back as somebody\r\nelse, and follow up the clues.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll have to be quick,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, abstractedly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can just do it in time for the 25th.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen you say \xe2\x80\x98making inquiries\xe2\x80\x99,\xe2\x80\x9d he continued, looking straight\r\nbefore him, \xe2\x80\x9cI hope you don\xe2\x80\x99t mean setting other people on his track?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s fair game!\xe2\x80\x9d I could not help saying; for there were moments when\r\nI chafed under this scrupulous fidelity to our self-denying ordinance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s our game, or nobody\xe2\x80\x99s,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, sharply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I\xe2\x80\x99ll keep the secret,\xe2\x80\x9d I rejoined.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet\xe2\x80\x99s stick together,\xe2\x80\x9d he broke out. \xe2\x80\x9cI shall make a muck of it\r\nwithout you. And how are we to communicate\xe2\x80\x94meet?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSomehow\xe2\x80\x94that can wait. I know it\xe2\x80\x99s a leap in the dark, but there\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nsafety in darkness.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCarruthers! what are we talking about? If they have the ghost of a\r\nnotion where we have been to-day, you give us away by packing off to\r\nLondon. They\xe2\x80\x99ll think we know their secret and are clearing out to make\r\nuse of it. _That_ means arrest, if you like!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPessimist! Haven\xe2\x80\x99t I written proof of good faith in my pocket\xe2\x80\x94official\r\nletters of recall, received to-day? It\xe2\x80\x99s one deception the less, you\r\nsee; for those letters _may_ have been opened; skilfully done it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nimpossible to detect. When in doubt, tell the truth!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a rum thing how often it pays in this spying business,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nDavies thoughtfully.\r\n\r\nWe had been tramping through deserted streets under the glare of\r\nelectricity, I with my leaden shuffle, he with the purposeful forward\r\nstoop and swinging arms that always marked his gait ashore.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, what\xe2\x80\x99s it to be?\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9cHere\xe2\x80\x99s the Schwannall\xc3\xa9e.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t like it,\xe2\x80\x9d said he; \xe2\x80\x9cbut I trust your judgement.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe turned slowly down, running over a few last points where prior\r\nagreement was essential. As we stood at the very gate of the villa:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t commit yourself to dates,\xe2\x80\x9d I said; \xe2\x80\x9csay nothing that will\r\nprevent you from being here at least a week hence with the yacht still\r\nafloat.\xe2\x80\x9d And my final word, as we waited at the door for the bell to be\r\nanswered, was: \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t mind what _I_ say. If things look queer we may\r\nhave to lighten the ship.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLighten?\xe2\x80\x9d whispered Davies; \xe2\x80\x9coh, I hope I shan\xe2\x80\x99t bosh it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope I shan\xe2\x80\x99t get cramp,\xe2\x80\x9d I muttered between my teeth.\r\n\r\nIt will be remembered that Davies had never been to the villa before.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXIV.\r\nFinesse\r\n\r\n\r\nThe door of a room on the ground floor was opened to us by a\r\nman-servant. As we entered the rattle of a piano stopped, and a hot\r\nwave of mingled scent and cigar-smoke struck my nostrils. The first\r\nthing I noticed over Davies\xe2\x80\x99s shoulder, as he preceded me into the\r\nroom, was a woman\xe2\x80\x94the source of the perfume I decided\xe2\x80\x94turning round\r\nfrom the piano as he passed it and staring him up and down with a\r\ndisdainful familiarity that I at once hotly resented. She was in\r\nevening dress, pronounced in cut and colour; had a certain exuberant\r\nbeauty, not wholly ascribable to nature, and a notable lack of\r\nbreeding. Another glance showed me Dollmann putting down a liqueur\r\nglass of brandy, and rising from a low chair with something of a start;\r\nand another, von Br\xc3\xbcning, lying back in a corner of a sofa, smoking; on\r\nthe same sofa, _vis-\xc3\xa0-vis_ to him, was\xe2\x80\x94yes, of course it was\xe2\x80\x94Clara\r\nDollmann; but how their surroundings alter people, I caught myself\r\nthinking. For the rest, I was aware that the room was furnished with\r\nostentation, and was stuffy with stove-engendered warmth. Davies\r\nsteered a straight course for Dollmann, and shook his hand with\r\nbusinesslike resolution. Then he tacked across to the sofa, abandoning\r\nme in the face of the enemy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94?\xe2\x80\x9d said Dollmann.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCarruthers,\xe2\x80\x9d I answered, distinctly. \xe2\x80\x9cI was with Davies in the boat\r\njust now, but I don\xe2\x80\x99t think he introduced me. And now he has forgotten\r\nagain,\xe2\x80\x9d I added, dryly, turning towards Davies, who, having presented\r\nhimself to Fr\xc3\xa4ulein Dollmann, was looking feebly from her to von\r\nBr\xc3\xbcning, the picture of tongue-tied awkwardness. (The Commander nodded\r\nto me and stretched himself with a yawn.)\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVon Br\xc3\xbcning told me about you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dollmann, ignoring my allusion,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cbut I was not quite sure of the name. No; it was not an occasion for\r\nformalities, was it?\xe2\x80\x9d He gave a sudden, mirthless laugh. I thought him\r\nflushed and excitable; yet, seen in a normal light, he was in some\r\nrespects a pleasant surprise, the remarkable conformation of the head\r\ngiving an impression of intellectual power and restless, almost\r\ninsanely restless, energy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat need?\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9cI have heard so much about you from Davies\xe2\x80\x94and\r\nCommander von Br\xc3\xbcning\xe2\x80\x94that we seem to be old friends already.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe shot a doubtful look at me, and a diversion came from the piano.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd now, for Heaven\xe2\x80\x99s sake,\xe2\x80\x9d cried the lady of the perfume, \xe2\x80\x9clet us\r\njoin Herr B\xc3\xb6hme at supper!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet me present you to my wife,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dollmann.\r\n\r\nSo this was the stepmother; unmistakably German, I may add. I made my\r\nbow, and underwent much the same sort of frank scrutiny as Davies, only\r\nthat it was rather more favourable to me, and ended in a carmine smile.\r\n\r\nThere was a general movement and further introductions. Davies was led\r\nto the stepmother, and I found myself confronting the daughter with\r\nquickened pulses, and a sudden sense of added complexity in the issues.\r\nI had, of course, made up my mind to ignore our meeting of yesterday,\r\nand had assumed that she would do the same. And she did ignore it\xe2\x80\x94we\r\nmet as utter strangers; nor did I venture (for other eyes were upon us)\r\nto transmit any sign of intelligence to her. But the next moment I was\r\nwondering if I had not fallen into a trap. She had promised not to\r\ntell, but under what circumstances? I saw the scene again; the misty\r\nflats, the spruce little sail-boat and its sweet young mistress, fresh\r\nas a dewy flower, but blanched and demoralised by a horrid fear,\r\nappealing to my honour so to act that we three should never meet again,\r\npromising to be silent, but as much in her own interest as ours, and\r\nunder that implied condition which I had only equivocally refused. The\r\ncondition was violated, not by her fault or ours, but violated. She was\r\nfree to help her father against us, and was she helping him? What\r\ntroubled me was the change in her; that she\xe2\x80\x94how can I express it\r\nwithout offence?\xe2\x80\x94was less in discord with her surroundings than she\r\nshould have been; that in dress, pose and manner (as we exchanged some\r\ntrivialities) she was too near reflecting the style of the other woman;\r\nthat, in fact, she in some sort realised my original conception of her,\r\nso brutally avowed to Davies, so signally, as I had thought, falsified.\r\nIn the sick perplexity that this discovery caused me I dare say I\r\nlooked as foolish as Davies had done, and more so, for the close heat\r\nof the room and its tainted atmosphere, succeeding so abruptly to the\r\nwholesome nip of the outside air, were giving me a faintness which this\r\nmoral check lessened my power to combat. Von Br\xc3\xbcning\xe2\x80\x99s face wore a\r\nsneering smile that I winced under; and, turning, I found another pair\r\nof eyes fixed on me, those of Herr B\xc3\xb6hme, whose squat figure had\r\nappeared at a pair of folding doors leading to an adjoining room.\r\nNapkin in hand, he was taking in the scene before him with fat\r\nbenevolence, but exceeding shrewdness. I instantly noticed a faint red\r\nweal relieving the ivory of his bald head; and I had suffered too often\r\nin the same quarter myself to mistake its origin, namely, our cabin\r\ndoorway.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is the other young explorer, B\xc3\xb6hme,\xe2\x80\x9d said von Br\xc3\xbcning. \xe2\x80\x9cHerr\r\nDavies kidnapped him a month ago, and bullied and starved him into\r\nsubmission; they\xe2\x80\x99ll drown together yet. I believe his sufferings have\r\nbeen terrible.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHis sufferings are over,\xe2\x80\x9d I retorted. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve mutinied\xe2\x80\x94deserted\xe2\x80\x94haven\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nI, Davies?\xe2\x80\x9d I caught Davies gazing with solemn _gaucherie_ at Miss\r\nDollmann.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, what?\xe2\x80\x9d he stammered. I explained in English. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes, Carruthers\r\nhas to go home,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, in his vile lingo.\r\n\r\nNo one spoke for a moment, and even von Br\xc3\xbcning had no persiflage\r\nready.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, are we never going to have supper?\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame impatiently; and\r\nwith that we all moved towards the folding doors. There had been little\r\nformality in the proceedings so far, and there was less still in the\r\nsupper-room. B\xc3\xb6hme resumed his repast with appetite, and the rest of us\r\nsat down apparently at random, though an underlying method was\r\ndiscernible. As it worked out, Dollmann was at one end of the small\r\ntable, with Davies on his right and B\xc3\xb6hme on his left; Frau Dollmann at\r\nthe other, with me on her right and von Br\xc3\xbcning on her left. The\r\nseventh personage, Fr\xc3\xa4ulein Dollmann, was between the Commander and\r\nDavies on the side opposite to me. No servants appeared, and we waited\r\non ourselves. I have a vague recollection of various excellent dishes,\r\nand a distinct one of abundance of wine. Someone filled me a glass of\r\nchampagne, and I confess that I drained it with honest avidity,\r\nblessing the craftsman who coaxed forth the essence, the fruit that\r\nharboured it, the sun that warmed it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy are you going so suddenly?\xe2\x80\x9d said von Br\xc3\xbcning to me across the\r\ntable.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDidn\xe2\x80\x99t I tell you we had to call here for letters? I got mine this\r\nmorning, and among others a summons back to work. Of course I must\r\nobey.\xe2\x80\x9d (I found myself speaking in a frigid silence.) \xe2\x80\x9cThe annoying\r\nthing was that there were two letters, and if I had only come here two\r\ndays sooner I should have only got the first, which gave me an\r\nextension.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are very conscientious. How will they know?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, but the second\xe2\x80\x99s rather urgent.\xe2\x80\x9d There was another uncomfortable\r\nsilence, broken by Dollmann.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy the way, Herr Davies,\xe2\x80\x9d he began, \xe2\x80\x9cI ought to apologise to you\r\nfor\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis was no business of mine, and the less interest I took in it the\r\nbetter; so I turned to Frau Dollmann and abused the fog.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you been in the harbour all day?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked, \xe2\x80\x9cthen how was it you\r\ndid not visit us? Was Herr Davies so shy?\xe2\x80\x9d (Curiosity or malice?)\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuite the contrary; but I was,\xe2\x80\x9d I answered coldly; \xe2\x80\x9cyou see, we knew\r\nHerr Dollmann was away, and we really only called here to get my\r\nletters; besides, we did not know your address.\xe2\x80\x9d I looked at Clara and\r\nfound her talking gaily to von Br\xc3\xbcning, deaf seemingly to our little\r\ndialogue.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnyone would have told you it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame, raising her eyebrows.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI dare say; but directly after breakfast the fog came on, and\xe2\x80\x94well,\r\none cannot leave a yacht alone in a fog,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, with professional\r\nsolidity.\r\n\r\nVon Br\xc3\xbcning pricked up his ears at this. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll be hanged if that was\r\n_your_ maxim,\xe2\x80\x9d he laughed; \xe2\x80\x9cyou\xe2\x80\x99re too fond of the shore!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI sent him a glance of protest, as though to say: \xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the use of\r\nyour warning if you won\xe2\x80\x99t let me act on it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFor, of course, my excuses were meant chiefly for his consumption, and\r\nFr\xc3\xa4ulein Dollmann\xe2\x80\x99s. That the lady I addressed them to found them\r\nunpalatable was not my fault.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen you sat in your wretched little cabin all day?\xe2\x80\x9d she persisted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll day,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, brazenly; \xe2\x80\x9cit was the safest thing to do.\xe2\x80\x9d And I\r\nlooked again at Fr\xc3\xa4ulein Dollmann, frankly and squarely. Our eyes met,\r\nand she dropped hers instantly, but not before I had learnt something;\r\nfor if ever I saw misery under a mask it was on her face. No; she had\r\nnot told.\r\n\r\nI think I puzzled the stepmother, who shrugged her white shoulders, and\r\nsaid in that case she wondered we had dared to leave our precious boat\r\nand come to supper. If we knew Frisian fogs as well as she did\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\nOh, I explained, we were not so nervous as that; and as for supper on\r\nshore, if she only knew what a Spartan life we led\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, for mercy\xe2\x80\x99s sake, don\xe2\x80\x99t tell me about it!\xe2\x80\x9d she cried, with a\r\ngrimace; \xe2\x80\x9cI hate the mention of yachts. When I think of that dreadful\r\n_Medusa_ coming from Hamburg\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d I sympathised with half my attention,\r\nkeeping one strained ear open for developments on my right. Davies, I\r\nknew, was in the thick of it, and none too happy under B\xc3\xb6hme\xe2\x80\x99s eye, but\r\nworking manfully. \xe2\x80\x9cMy fault\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9csudden squall\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cquite safe\xe2\x80\x9d, were some of\r\nthe phrases I caught; while I was aware, to my alarm, that he was\r\nactually drawing a diagram of something with bread-crumbs and\r\ntable-knives. The subject seemed to gutter out to an awkward end, and\r\nsuddenly B\xc3\xb6hme, who was my right-hand neighbour, turned to me. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are\r\nstarting for England to-morrow morning?\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d I answered; \xe2\x80\x9cthere is a steamer at 8.15, I believe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is good. We shall be companions.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you going to England, too, sir?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked, with hot misgivings.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no! I am going to Bremen; but we shall travel together as far\r\nas\xe2\x80\x94you go by Amsterdam, I suppose?\xe2\x80\x94as far as Leer, then. That will be\r\nvery pleasant.\xe2\x80\x9d I fancied there was a ghoulish gusto in his tone.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery,\xe2\x80\x9d I assented. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are making a short stay here, then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs long as usual. I visit the work at Memmert once a month or so,\r\nspend a night with my friend Dollmann and his charming family\xe2\x80\x9d (he\r\nleered round him), \xe2\x80\x9cand return.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhether I was right or wrong in my next step I shall never know, but\r\nobeying a strong instinct, \xe2\x80\x9cMemmert,\xe2\x80\x9d I said; \xe2\x80\x9cdo tell me more about\r\nMemmert. We heard a good deal about it from Commander von Br\xc3\xbcning;\r\nbut\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe was discreet, I expect,\xe2\x80\x9d said B\xc3\xb6hme.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe left off at the most interesting part.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s that about me?\xe2\x80\x9d joined in von Br\xc3\xbcning.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was saying that we\xe2\x80\x99re dying to know more about Memmert, aren\xe2\x80\x99t we,\r\nDavies?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, evidently aghast at my temerity; but I\r\ndid not mind that. If he roughed my suit, so much the better; I\r\nintended to rough his.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou gave us plenty of history, Commander, but you did not bring it up\r\nto date.\xe2\x80\x9d The triple alliance laughed, Dollmann boisterously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said von Br\xc3\xbcning; \xe2\x80\x9cI gave you very good reasons, and you\r\nacquiesced.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd now he is trying to pump _me_,\xe2\x80\x9d said B\xc3\xb6hme, with his rasping\r\nchuckle.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWait a bit, sir; I have an excuse. The Commander was not only\r\nmysterious but inaccurate. I appeal to you, Herr Dollmann, for it was\r\n_\xc3\xa0 propos_ of you. When we fell in with him at Bensersiel, Davies asked\r\nhim if you were at home, and he said \xe2\x80\x98No.\xe2\x80\x99 When would you be back?\r\nProbably soon; _but he did not know when_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, he said that?\xe2\x80\x9d said Dollmann.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, only three days later we arrive at Norderney, and find you have\r\nreturned that very day, but have gone to Memmert. Again (by the way)\r\nthe mysterious Memmert! But more than ever mysterious now, for in the\r\nevening, not only you and Herr B\xc3\xb6hme\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat penetration!\xe2\x80\x9d laughed von Br\xc3\xbcning.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut also Commander von Br\xc3\xbcning, pay us a visit in _his_ launch, all\r\ncoming from Memmert!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you infer?\xe2\x80\x9d said von Br\xc3\xbcning.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, that you must have known at Bensersiel\xe2\x80\x94only three days\r\nago\xe2\x80\x94exactly when Herr Dollmann was coming back, having an appointment\r\nat Memmert with him for to-day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhich I wished to conceal from you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, and that\xe2\x80\x99s why I\xe2\x80\x99m so inquisitive; it\xe2\x80\x99s entirely your own fault.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo it seems,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, with mock humility; \xe2\x80\x9cbut fill your glass and go\r\non, young man. Why should I want to deceive you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s just what I want to know. Come, confess now; wasn\xe2\x80\x99t there\r\nsomething important afoot to-day at Memmert? Something to do with the\r\ngold? You were inspecting it, sorting it, weighing it? Or _I_ know! You\r\nwere transporting it secretly to the mainland?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot a very good day for that! But softly, Herr Carruthers; no fishing\r\nfor admissions. Who said we had found any gold?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, have you? There!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s better! Nothing like candour, my young investigator. But I am\r\nafraid, having no authority, I cannot assist you at all. Better try\r\nHerr B\xc3\xb6hme again. I\xe2\x80\x99m only a casual onlooker.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWith shares.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! you remember that? (He remembers everything!) With a few shares,\r\nthen; but with no expert knowledge. Now, B\xc3\xb6hme is the consulting\r\nengineer. Rescue me, B\xc3\xb6hme.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI cannot disclaim expert knowledge,\xe2\x80\x9d said B\xc3\xb6hme, with humorous\r\ngravity; \xe2\x80\x9cbut I disclaim responsibility. Now, Herr Dollmann is chairman\r\nof the company.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dollmann, with a noisy laugh, \xe2\x80\x9cmust fall back on the\r\nshareholders, whose interests I have to guard. One can\xe2\x80\x99t be too careful\r\nin these confidential matters.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere\xe2\x80\x99s one who gives his consent,\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9cCan\xe2\x80\x99t he represent the\r\nrest?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExtorted by torture,\xe2\x80\x9d said von Br\xc3\xbcning. \xe2\x80\x9cI retract.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t mind them, Herr Carruthers,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Frau Dollmann, \xe2\x80\x9cthey are\r\nmaking fun of you; but I will give you a hint; no woman can keep a\r\nsecret\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d I cried, triumphantly, \xe2\x80\x9cyou have been there?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI? Not I; I detest the sea! But Clara has.\xe2\x80\x9d Everyone looked at Clara,\r\nwho in her turn looked in na\xc3\xafve bewilderment from me to her father.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed?\xe2\x80\x9d I said, more soberly, \xe2\x80\x9cbut perhaps she is not a free agent.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerfectly free!\xe2\x80\x9d said Dollmann.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have only been there once, some time ago,\xe2\x80\x9d said she, \xe2\x80\x9cand I saw no\r\ngold at all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGuarded,\xe2\x80\x9d I observed. \xe2\x80\x9cI beg your pardon; I mean that perhaps you only\r\nsaw what you were allowed to see. And, in any case, the Fr\xc3\xa4ulein has no\r\nexpert knowledge and no responsibility, and, perhaps, no shares. Her\r\nprovince is to be charming, not to hold financial secrets.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have done my best to help you,\xe2\x80\x9d said the stepmother.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99re all against us, Davies.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, chuck it, Carruthers!\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, in English.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s insatiable,\xe2\x80\x9d said von Br\xc3\xbcning, and there was a pause; clearly,\r\nthey meant to elicit more.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I shall draw my own conclusions,\xe2\x80\x9d I said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is interesting,\xe2\x80\x9d said von Br\xc3\xbcning, \xe2\x80\x9cin what sense?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt begins to dawn on me that you made fools of us at Bensersiel. Don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nyou remember, Davies, what an interest he took in all our doings? I\r\nwonder if he feared our exploring propensities might possibly lead us\r\nto Memmert?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUpon my word, this is the blackest ingratitude. I thought I made\r\nmyself particularly agreeable to you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, indeed; especially about the duck-shooting! How useful your local\r\nman would have been\xe2\x80\x94both to us and to you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo on,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Commander, imperturbably.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWait a moment; I\xe2\x80\x99m thinking it out.\xe2\x80\x9d And thinking it out I was in\r\ndeadly earnest, for all my levity, as I pressed my hand on my burning\r\nforehead and asked myself where I was to stop in this seductive but\r\nperilous fraud. To carry it too far was to court complete exposure; to\r\nstop too soon was equally compromising.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is he talking about, and why go on with this ridiculous mystery?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Frau Dollmann.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was thinking about this supper party, and the way it came about,\xe2\x80\x9d I\r\npursued, slowly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing to complain of, I hope?\xe2\x80\x9d said Dollmann.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course not! Impromptu parties are always the pleasantest, and this\r\none was delightfully impromptu. Now I bet you I know its origin! Didn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nyou discuss us at Memmert? And didn\xe2\x80\x99t one of you suggest\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne would almost think you had been there,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dollmann.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou may thank your vile climate that we weren\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d I retorted,\r\nlaughing. \xe2\x80\x9cBut, as I was saying, didn\xe2\x80\x99t one of you suggest\xe2\x80\x94which of\r\nyou? Well, I\xe2\x80\x99m sure it wasn\xe2\x80\x99t the Commander\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy not?\xe2\x80\x9d said B\xc3\xb6hme.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s difficult to explain\xe2\x80\x94an intuition, say\xe2\x80\x94I am sure he stood up for\r\nus; and I don\xe2\x80\x99t think it was Herr Dollmann, because he knows Davies\r\nalready, and he\xe2\x80\x99s always on the spot; and, in short I\xe2\x80\x99ll swear it was\r\nHerr B\xc3\xb6hme, who is leaving early to-morrow, and had never seen either\r\nof us. It was you, sir, who proposed that we should be asked to supper\r\nto-night\xe2\x80\x94for inspection?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cInspection?\xe2\x80\x9d said B\xc3\xb6hme; \xe2\x80\x9cwhat an extraordinary idea!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou can\xe2\x80\x99t deny it, though! And one thing more; in the harbour just\r\nnow\xe2\x80\x94no\xe2\x80\x94this is going too far; I shall mortally offend you.\xe2\x80\x9d I gave way\r\nto hearty laughter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome, let\xe2\x80\x99s have it. Your hallucinations are diverting.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you insist; but this is rather a delicate matter. You know we were\r\na little surprised to find you _all_ on board; and you, Herr B\xc3\xb6hme, did\r\nyou always take such a deep interest in small yachts? I am afraid that\r\nit was at a certain sacrifice of comfort that you _inspected_ ours!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nAnd I glanced at the token he bore of his encounter with our lintel.\r\nThere was a burst of pent-up merriment, in which Dollmann took the\r\nloudest share.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI warned you, B\xc3\xb6hme,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\nThe engineer took the joke in the best possible part.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe owe you apologies,\xe2\x80\x9d he conceded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t mention it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_He_ doesn\xe2\x80\x99t mind,\xe2\x80\x9d I said; \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m the injured one. I\xe2\x80\x99m sure you never\r\nsuspected Davies, who could?\xe2\x80\x9d (Who indeed? I was on firm ground there.)\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe point is, what did you take _me_ for?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps we take you for it still,\xe2\x80\x9d said von Br\xc3\xbcning.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOho!
