Still suspicious? Don\xe2\x80\x99t drive me to extremities.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat extremities?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen I get back to London I shall go to Lloyd\xe2\x80\x99s! I haven\xe2\x80\x99t forgotten\r\nthat flaw in the title.\xe2\x80\x9d There was an impressive silence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGentlemen,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dollmann, with exaggerated solemnity, \xe2\x80\x9cwe must come\r\nto terms with this formidable young man. What do you say?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTake me to Memmert,\xe2\x80\x9d I exclaimed. \xe2\x80\x9cThose are my terms!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTake you to Memmert? But I thought you were starting for England\r\nto-morrow?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI ought to; but I\xe2\x80\x99ll stay for that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou said it was urgent. Your conscience is very elastic.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s my affair. Will you take me to Memmert?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you say, gentlemen?\xe2\x80\x9d B\xc3\xb6hme nodded. \xe2\x80\x9cI think we owe some\r\nreparation. Under promise of absolute secrecy, then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course, now that you trust me. But you\xe2\x80\x99ll show me everything\xe2\x80\x94honour\r\nbright\xe2\x80\x94wreck, dep\xc3\xb4t, and all?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEverything; if you don\xe2\x80\x99t object to a diver\xe2\x80\x99s dress.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVictory!\xe2\x80\x9d I cried, in triumph. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ve won our point, Davies. And now,\r\ngentlemen, I don\xe2\x80\x99t mind saying that as far as I am concerned the joke\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nat an end; and, in spite of your kind offer, I must start for England\r\nto-morrow\xe2\x80\x9d under the good Herr B\xc3\xb6hme\xe2\x80\x99s wing. And in case my elastic\r\nconscience troubles you (for I see you think me a weather-cock) here\r\nare the letters received this morning, establishing my identity as a\r\nhumble but respectable clerk in the British Civil Service, summoned\r\naway from his holiday by a tyrannical superior.\xe2\x80\x9d (I pulled out my\r\nletters and tossed them to Dollmann.) \xe2\x80\x9cAh, you don\xe2\x80\x99t read English\r\neasily, perhaps? I dare say Herr B\xc3\xb6hme does.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLeaving B\xc3\xb6hme to study dates, postmarks, and contents to his heart\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ncontent, and unobserved, I turned to sympathise with my fair neighbour,\r\nwho complained that her head was going round; and no wonder. But at\r\nthis juncture, and very much to my surprise, Davies struck in.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_I_ should like to go to Memmert,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou?\xe2\x80\x9d said von Br\xc3\xbcning. \xe2\x80\x9cNow I\xe2\x80\x99m surprised at that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you won\xe2\x80\x99t be staying here either, Davies,\xe2\x80\x9d I objected.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I shall,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, I told you I should. If you leave me\r\nin the lurch like this I must have time to look round.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou needn\xe2\x80\x99t pretend that you cannot sail alone,\xe2\x80\x9d said von Br\xc3\xbcning.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s much more fun with two; I think I shall wire for another friend.\r\nMeanwhile, I should like to see Memmert.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s only an excuse, I\xe2\x80\x99m afraid,\xe2\x80\x9d said I.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI want to shoot ducks too,\xe2\x80\x9d pursued Davies, reddening. \xe2\x80\x9cI always have\r\nwanted to; and you promised to help in that, Commander.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou can\xe2\x80\x99t get out of it now,\xe2\x80\x9d I laughed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly not,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, unmoved; \xe2\x80\x9cbut, honestly, I should advise Herr\r\nDavies, if he is ever going to get home this season, to make the best\r\nof this fine weather.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s too fine,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies; \xe2\x80\x9cI prefer wind. If I cannot get a friend\r\nI think I shall stop cruising, leave the yacht here, and come back for\r\nher next year.\r\n\r\nThere was some mute telegraphy between the allies.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou can leave her in my charge,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dollmann, \xe2\x80\x9cand start with your\r\nfriend to-morrow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThanks; but there is no hurry,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, growing redder than ever.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI like Norderney\xe2\x80\x94and we might have another sail in your dinghy,\r\nFr\xc3\xa4ulein,\xe2\x80\x9d he blurted out.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, in that low dry voice I had heard yesterday;\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cbut I think I shall not be sailing again\xe2\x80\x94it is getting too cold.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, no!\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, \xe2\x80\x9cit\xe2\x80\x99s splendid.\xe2\x80\x9d But she had turned to von\r\nBr\xc3\xbcning, and took no notice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, send me a report about Memmert, Davies,\xe2\x80\x9d I laughed, with the\r\nidea of drawing attention from his rebuff. But Davies, having once\r\ndelivered his soul, seemed to have lost his shyness, and only gazed at\r\nhis neighbour with the placid, dogged expression that I knew so well.\r\nThat was the end of those delicate topics; and conviviality grew apace.\r\n\r\nI am not indifferent at any time to good wine and good cheer, nor was\r\nit for lack of pressing that I drank as sparingly as I was able, and\r\npretended to a greater elation than I felt. Nor certainly was it from\r\nany fine scruples as to the character of the gentleman whose\r\nhospitality we were receiving\xe2\x80\x94scruples which I knew affected Davies,\r\nwho ate little and drank nothing. In any case he was adamant in such\r\nmatters, and I verily believe would at any time have preferred our own\r\nlittle paraffin-flavoured messes to the best dinner in the world. It\r\nwas a very wholesome caution that warned me not to abuse the finest\r\nbrain tonic ever invented by the wit of man. I had finessed Memmert, as\r\none finesses a low card when holding a higher; but I had too much\r\nrespect for our adversaries to trade on any fancied security we had won\r\nthereby. They had allowed me to win the trick, but I credited them with\r\na better knowledge of my hand than they chose to show. On the other\r\nhand I hugged the axiom that in all conflicts it is just as fatal to\r\nunderrate the difficulties of your enemy as to overrate your own. Their\r\nchief one\xe2\x80\x94and it multiplied a thousandfold the excitement of the\r\ncontest\xe2\x80\x94was, I felt sure, the fear of striking in error; of using a\r\nsledge-hammer to break a nut. In breaking it they risked publicity, and\r\npublicity, I felt convinced, was death to their secret. So, even\r\nsupposing they had detected the finesse, and guessed that we had in\r\nfact got wind of imperial designs; yet, even so, I counted on immunity\r\nso long as they thought we were on the wrong scent, with Memmert, and\r\nMemmert alone, as the source of our suspicions.\r\n\r\nHad it been necessary I was prepared to encourage such a view,\r\nadmitting that the cloth von Br\xc3\xbcning wore had made his connexion with\r\nMemmert curious, and had suggested to Davies, for I should have put it\r\non him, with his naval enthusiasms, that the wreck-works were really\r\nnaval-defence works. If they went farther, and suspected that we had\r\ntried to go to Memmert that very day, the position was worse, but not\r\ndesperate; for the fear that they would take the final step and suppose\r\nthat we had actually got there and overheard their talk, I flatly\r\nrefused to entertain, until I should find myself under arrest.\r\n\r\nPrecisely how near we came to it I shall never rightly know; but I have\r\ngood reason to believe that we trembled on the verge. The main issue\r\nwas fully enough for me, and it was only in passing flashes that I\r\nfollowed the play of the warring under-currents. And yet, looking back\r\non the scene, I would warrant there was no party of seven in Europe\r\nthat evening where a student of human documents would have found so\r\nrich a field, such noble and ignoble ambitions, such base and holy\r\nfears, aye, and such pitiful agonies of the spirit. Roughly divided\r\nthough we were into separate camps, no two of us were wholly at one.\r\nEach wore a mask in the grand imposture; excepting, I am inclined to\r\nthink, the lady on my left, who, outside her own well-being, which she\r\ncultivated without reserve, had, as far as I could see, but one axe to\r\ngrind\xe2\x80\x94the intimacy of von Br\xc3\xbcning and her stepdaughter\xe2\x80\x94and ground it\r\nopenly.\r\n\r\nNot even B\xc3\xb6hme and von Br\xc3\xbcning were wholly at one; and as moral\r\ndistances are reckoned, Davies and I were leagues apart. Sitting\r\nbetween Dollmann and Dollmann\xe2\x80\x99s daughter, the living and breathing\r\nsymbols of the two polar passions he had sworn to harmonise, he kept an\r\nequilibrium which, though his aims were nominally mine, I could not\r\nattain to. For me the man was the central figure; if I had attention to\r\nspare it was on him that I bestowed it; groping disgustfully after his\r\nhidden springs of action, noting the evidences of great gifts\r\nsquandered and prostituted; questioning where he was most vulnerable;\r\nwhom he feared most, us or his colleagues; whether he was open to\r\nremorse or shame; or whether he meditated further crime. The girl was\r\nincidental. After the first shock of surprise I had soon enough\r\ndiscovered that she, like the rest, had assumed a disguise; for she was\r\nfar too innocent to sustain the deception; and yesterday was fresh in\r\nmy memory. I was forced to continue turning her assumed character to\r\naccount; but it would be pharisaical in me to say that I rose to any\r\nmoral heights in her regard\xe2\x80\x94wine and excitement had deadened my better\r\nnature to that extent. I thought she looked prettier than ever, and, as\r\ntime passed, I fell into a cynical carelessness about her. This glimpse\r\nof her home life, and the desperate expedients to which she was driven\r\n(whether by compulsion or from her own regard for Davies) to repel and\r\ndismiss him, did not strike me as they might have done as the crowning\r\nargument in favour of the course we had adopted the night before, that\r\nof compassing our end without noise and scandal, disarming Dollmann,\r\nbut aiding him to escape from the allies he had betrayed. To Davies,\r\nthe man, if not a pure abstraction, was at most a noxious vermin to be\r\ntrampled on for the public good; while the girl, in her blackguardly\r\nsurroundings, and with her sinister future, had become the very source\r\nof his impulse.\r\n\r\nAnd the other players? B\xc3\xb6hme was _my_ abstraction, the fortress whose\r\nfoundations we were sapping, the embodiment of that systematised force\r\nwhich is congenital to the German people. In von Br\xc3\xbcning, the personal\r\nfactor was uppermost. Callous as I was this evening, I could not help\r\nwondering occasionally, as he talked and laughed with Clara Dollmann,\r\nwhat in his innermost thoughts, knowing her father, he felt and meant.\r\nIt is a point I cannot and would not pursue, and, thank Heaven, it does\r\nnot matter now; yet, with fuller knowledge of the facts, and, I trust,\r\na mellower judgement, I often return to the same debate, and, by I know\r\nnot what illogical bypaths, always arrive at the same conclusion, that\r\nI liked the man and like him still.\r\n\r\nWe behaved as sportsmen in the matter of time, giving them over two\r\nhours to make up their minds about us. It was only when tobacco-smoke\r\nand heat brought back my faintness, and a twinge of cramp warned me\r\nthat human strength has limits, that I rose and said we must go; that I\r\nhad to make an early start to-morrow. I am hazy about the farewells,\r\nbut I think that Dollmann was the most cordial, to me at any rate, and\r\nI augured good therefrom. B\xc3\xb6hme said he should see me again. Von\r\nBr\xc3\xbcning, though bound for the harbour also, considered it was far too\r\nearly to be going yet, and said good-bye.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou want to talk us over,\xe2\x80\x9d I remember saying, with the last flicker of\r\ngaiety I could muster.\r\n\r\nWe were in the streets again, under a silver, breathless night; dizzily\r\nfooting the greasy ladder again; in the cabin again, where I collapsed\r\non a sofa just as I was, and slept such a deep and stringent sleep that\r\nthe men of the _Blitz_\xe2\x80\x99s launch might have handcuffed and trussed and\r\ncarried me away, without incommoding me in the least.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXV.\r\nI Double Back\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood-bye, old chap,\xe2\x80\x9d called Davies.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood-bye,\xe2\x80\x9d the whistle blew and the ferry-steamer forged ahead,\r\nleaving Davies on the quay, bareheaded and wearing his old Norfolk\r\njacket and stained grey flannels, as at our first meeting in Flensburg\r\nstation. There was no bandaged hand this time, but he looked pinched\r\nand depressed; his eyes had black circles round them; and again I felt\r\nthat same indefinable pathos in him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour friend is in low spirits,\xe2\x80\x9d said B\xc3\xb6hme, who was installed on a\r\nseat beside me, voluminously caped and rugged against the biting air.\r\nIt was a still, sunless day.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo am I,\xe2\x80\x9d I grunted, and it was the literal truth. I was only half\r\nawake, felt unwashed and dissipated, heavy in head and limbs. But for\r\nDavies I should never have been where I was. It was he who had\r\npatiently coaxed me out of my bunk, packed my bag, fed me with tea and\r\nan omelette (to which I believe he had devoted peculiarly tender care),\r\nand generally mothered me for departure. While I swallowed my second\r\ncup he was brushing the mould and smoothing the dents from my felt hat,\r\nwhich had been entombed for a month in the sail-locker; working at it\r\nwith a remorseful concern in his face. The only initiative I am\r\nconscious of having shown was in the matter of my bag. \xe2\x80\x9cPut in my sea\r\nclothes, oils, and all,\xe2\x80\x9d I had said; \xe2\x80\x9cI may want them again.\xe2\x80\x9d There was\r\nmortal need of a thorough consultation, but this was out of the\r\nquestion. Davies did not badger or complain, but only timidly asked me\r\nhow we were to meet and communicate, a question on which my mind was an\r\nabsolute blank.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook out for me about the 26th,\xe2\x80\x9d I suggested feebly.\r\n\r\nBefore we left the cabin he gave me a scrap of pencilled paper and saw\r\nthat it went safely into my pocket-book. \xe2\x80\x9cLook at it in the train,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid.\r\n\r\nUnable to cope with B\xc3\xb6hme, I paced the deck aimlessly as we swung round\r\nthe See Gat into the Buse Tief, trying to identify the point where we\r\ncrossed it yesterday blindfold. But the tide was full, and the waters\r\nblank for miles round till they merged in haze. Soon I drifted down\r\ninto the saloon, and crouching over a stove pulled out that scrap of\r\npaper. In a crabbed, boyish hand, and much besmudged with\r\ntobacco-ashes, I found the following notes:\r\n\r\n(1) _Your journey_. [See Maps A and B.] Norddeich 8.58, Emden 10.32,\r\nLeer 11.16 (B\xc3\xb6hme changes for Bremen), Rheine 1.8 (change), Amsterdam\r\n7.17 p.m. Leave again _via_ Hook 8.52, London 9 am.\r\n\r\n(2) The coast-station\xe2\x80\x94_their_ rondezvous\xe2\x80\x94querry is it _Norden_? (You\r\npass it 9.13)\xe2\x80\x94there is a tidal creek up to it. High-water there on\r\n25th, say 10.30 to 11 p.m. It cannot be Norddeich, which I find has a\r\ndredged-out low-water channel for the steamer, so tide \xe2\x80\x9cserves\xe2\x80\x9d would\r\nnot apply.\r\n\r\n(3) _Your other clews_ (tugs, pilots, depths, railway, Esens, seven of\r\nsomething). Querry: Scheme of defence by land and sea for North Sea\r\nCoast?\r\n\r\n_Sea_\xe2\x80\x947 islands, 7 channels between (counting West Ems), very small\r\ndepths (what you said) in most of them. Tugs and pilots for patrol work\r\nbehind islands, as I always said. Querry: Rondezvous is for inspecting\r\nchannels?\r\n\r\n_Land_\xe2\x80\x94Look at railway (map in ulster pocket) running in a loop all\r\nround Friesland, a few miles from coast. Querry: To be used as line of\r\ncommunication for army corps. Troops could be quickly sent to any\r\nthreatened point. _Esens_ the base? It is in top centre of loop. Von\r\nBrooning dished us fairly over that at Bensersiel.\r\n\r\n_Chatham_\xe2\x80\x94D. was spying after our naval plans for war with Germany.\r\n\r\nVon Brooning runs naval part over here.\r\n\r\nWhere does Burmer come in? Querry\xe2\x80\x94you go to Bremen and find out about\r\nhim?\r\n\r\nI nodded stupidly over this document\xe2\x80\x94so stupidly that I found myself\r\nwondering whether Burmer was a place or a person. Then I dozed, to wake\r\nwith a violent start and find the paper on the floor. Panic-stricken, I\r\nhid it away, and went on deck, when I found we were close to Norddeich,\r\nrunning up to the bleakest of bleak jetties thrown out from the\r\ndyke-bound polders of the mainland. B\xc3\xb6hme and I landed together, and he\r\nwas at my elbow as I asked for a ticket for Amsterdam, and was given\r\none as far as Rheine, a junction near the Dutch frontier. He was\r\nensconced in an opposite corner to me in the railway carriage, looking\r\nlike an Indian idol. \xe2\x80\x9cWhere do you come in?\xe2\x80\x9d I pondered, dreamily. Too\r\nsleepy to talk, I could only blink at him, sitting bolt upright with my\r\narms folded over my precious pocket-book. Finally, I gave up the\r\nstruggle, buttoned my ulster tightly up, and turning my back upon him\r\nwith an apology, lay down to sleep, the precious pocket nethermost. He\r\nwas at liberty to rifle my bag if he chose, and I dare say he did. I\r\ncannot say, for from this point till Rheine, for the best part of four\r\nhours, that is, I had only two lucid intervals.\r\n\r\nThe first was at Emden, where we both had to change. Here, as we pushed\r\nour way down the crowded platform, B\xc3\xb6hme, after being greeted\r\nrespectfully by several persons, was at last buttonholed without means\r\nof escape by an obsequious gentleman, whose description is of no\r\nmoment, but whose conversation is. It was about a canal; what canal I\r\ndid not gather, though, from a name dropped, I afterwards identified it\r\nas one in course of construction as a feeder to the Ems. The point is\r\nthat the subject was canals. At the moment it was seed dropped in\r\nunreceptive soil, but it germinated later. I passed on, mingling with\r\nthe crowd, and was soon asleep again in another carriage where B\xc3\xb6hme\r\nthis time did not follow me.\r\n\r\nThe second occasion was at Leer, where I heard myself called by name,\r\nand woke to find him at the window. He had to change trains, and had\r\ncome to say good-bye. \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t forget to go to Lloyd\xe2\x80\x99s,\xe2\x80\x9d he grated in my\r\near. I expect it was a wan smile that I returned, for I was at a very\r\nlow ebb, and my fortress looked sarcastically impregnable. But the\r\nsapper was free; \xe2\x80\x9cfree\xe2\x80\x9d was my last conscious thought.\r\n\r\nEven after Rheine, where I changed for the last time, a brutish\r\ndrowsiness enchained me, and the afternoon was well advanced before my\r\nfaculties began to revive.\r\n\r\nThe train crept like a snail from station to station. I might, so a\r\nfellow-passenger told me, have waited three hours at Rheine for an\r\nexpress which would have brought me to Amsterdam at about the same\r\ntime; or, if I had chosen to break the journey farther back, two hours\r\nat either Emden or Leer would still have enabled me to catch the said\r\nexpress at Rheine. These alternatives had escaped Davies, and, I\r\nsurmised, had been suppressed by B\xc3\xb6hme, who doubtless did not want me\r\nbehind him, free either to double back or to follow him to Bremen.\r\n\r\nThe pace, then, was execrable, and there were delays; we were behind\r\ntime at Hengelo, thirty minutes late at Apeldoorn; so that I might well\r\nhave grown nervous about my connexions at Amsterdam, which were in some\r\njeopardy. But as I battled out of my lethargy and began to take account\r\nof our position and prospects, quite a different thought at the outset\r\naffected me. Anxiety to reach London was swamped in reluctance to quit\r\nGermany, so that I found myself grudging every mile that I placed\r\nbetween me and the frontier. It was the old question of urgency. To-day\r\nwas the 23rd. The visit to London meant a minimum absence of\r\nforty-eight hours, counting from Amsterdam; that is to say, that by\r\ntravelling for two nights and one day, and devoting the other day to\r\ninvestigating Dollmann\xe2\x80\x99s past, it was humanly possible for me to be\r\nback on the Frisian coast on the evening of the 25th. Yes, I could be\r\nat Norden, if that was the \xe2\x80\x9crendezvous\xe2\x80\x9d, at 7 p.m. But what a scramble!\r\nNo margin for delays, no physical respite. Some pasts take a deal of\r\nraking up\xe2\x80\x94other persons may be affected; men are cautious, they trip\r\nyou up with red tape; or the man who knows is out at lunch\xe2\x80\x94a protracted\r\nlunch; or in the country\xe2\x80\x94a protracted week-end. Will you see Mr\r\nSo-and-so, or leave a note? Oh! I know those public departments\xe2\x80\x94from\r\nthe inside! And the Admiralty!... I saw myself baffled and racing back\r\nthe same night to Germany, with two days wasted, arriving, good for\r\nnothing, at Norden, with no leisure to reconnoitre my ground; to be\r\nbaffled again there, probably, for you cannot always count on fogs (as\r\nDavies said). Esens was another clue, and \xe2\x80\x9cto follow Burmer\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94there was\r\nsomething in that notion. But I wanted time, and had I time? How long\r\ncould Davies maintain himself at Norderney? Not so very long, from what\r\nI remembered of last night. And was he even safe there? A feverish\r\ndream recurred to me\xe2\x80\x94a dream of Davies in a diving-dress; of a\r\nregrettable hitch in the air-supply\xe2\x80\x94Stop, that was nonsense!... Let us\r\nbe sane. What matter if he had to go? What matter if I took my time in\r\nLondon? Then with a flood of shame I saw Davies\xe2\x80\x99s wistful face on the\r\nquay, heard his grim ejaculation: \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s our game or no one\xe2\x80\x99s\xe2\x80\x9d; and my\r\nown sullen \xe2\x80\x9cOh, I\xe2\x80\x99ll keep the secret!\xe2\x80\x9d London was utterly impossible.\r\nIf I found my informant, what credentials had I, what claim to\r\nconfidences? None, unless I told the whole story. Why, my mere presence\r\nin Whitehall would imperil the secret; for, once on my native heath, I\r\nshould be recognised\xe2\x80\x94possibly haled to judgement; at the best should\r\nescape in a cloud of rumour\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9clast heard of at Norderney\xe2\x80\x9d; \xe2\x80\x9conly this\r\nmorning was raising Cain at the Admiralty about a mythical lieutenant.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nNo! Back to Friesland, was the word. One night\xe2\x80\x99s rest\xe2\x80\x94I must have\r\nthat\xe2\x80\x94between sheets, on a feather bed; one long, luxurious night, and\r\nthen back refreshed to Friesland, to finish our work in our own way,\r\nand with none but our own weapons.\r\n\r\nHaving reached this resolve, I was nearly putting it into instant\r\nexecution, by alighting at Amersfoort, but thought better of it. I had\r\na transformation to effect before I returned north, and the more\r\npopulous centre I made it in the less it was likely to attract notice.\r\nBesides, I had in my mind\xe2\x80\x99s eye a perfect bed in a perfect hostelry\r\nhard by the Amstel River. It was an economy in the end.\r\n\r\nSo, at half-past eight I was sipping my coffee in the aforesaid\r\nhostelry, with a London newspaper before me, which was unusually\r\ninteresting, and some German journals, which, \xe2\x80\x9cin hate of a wrong not\r\ntheirs\xe2\x80\x9d, were one and all seething with rancorous Anglophobia. At nine\r\nI was in the Jewish quarter, striking bargains in an infamous marine\r\nslop-shop. At half-past nine I was despatching this unscrupulous\r\ntelegram to my chief\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cVery sorry, could not call Norderney; hope\r\nextension all right; please write to H\xc3\xb4tel du Louvre, Paris.\xe2\x80\x9d At ten I\r\nwas in the perfect bed, rapturously flinging my limbs abroad in its\r\nglorious redundancies. And at 8.28 on the following morning, with a\r\nnovel chilliness about the upper lip, and a vast excess of strength and\r\nspirits, I was sitting in a third-class carriage, bound for Germany,\r\nand dressed as a young seaman, in a pea-jacket, peaked cap, and\r\ncomforter.\r\n\r\nThe transition had not been difficult. I had shaved off my moustache\r\nand breakfasted hastily in my bedroom, ready equipped for a journey in\r\nmy ulster and cloth cap. I had dismissed the hotel porter at the\r\nstation, and left my bag at the cloak-room, after taking out of it an\r\number bundle and substituting the ulster. The umber bundle, which\r\nconsisted of my oilskins, and within them my sea-boots and a few other\r\ngarments and necessaries, the whole tied up with a length of tarry\r\nrope, was now in the rack above me, and (with a stout stick)\r\nrepresented my luggage. Every article in it\xe2\x80\x94I shudder at their\r\norigin\xe2\x80\x94was in strict keeping with my humble _m\xc3\xa9tier,_ for I knew they\r\nwere liable to search at the frontier Custom-house; but there was a\r\nBaedeker of Northern Germany in my jacket pocket.\r\n\r\nFor the nonce, if questions were asked, I was an English seaman, going\r\nto Emden to join a ship, with a ticket as far as the frontier. Beyond\r\nthat a definite scheme of action had still to be thought out. One\r\nthing, however, was sure. I was determined to be at Norden to-morrow\r\nnight, the 25th. A word about Norden, which is a small town seven miles\r\nsouth of Norddeich. When hurriedly scanning the map for coast stations\r\nin the cabin yesterday, I had not thought of Norden, because it did not\r\nappear to be on the coast, but Davies had noticed it while I slept, and\r\nI now saw that his pencilled hint was a shrewd one. The creek he spoke\r\nof, though barely visible on the map, _[See Map B]_ flowed into the Ems\r\nEstuary in a south-westerly direction. The \xe2\x80\x9cnight train\xe2\x80\x9d tallied to\r\nperfection, for high tide in the creek would be, as Davies estimated,\r\nbetween 10.30 and 11 p.m. on the night of the 25th; and the time-table\r\nshowed that the only night train arriving at Norden was one from the\r\nsouth at 10.46 p.m. This looked promising. Emden, which I had inclined\r\nto on the spur of the moment, was out of court in comparison, for many\r\nreasons; not the least being that it was served by three trains between\r\n9 p.m. and 1 a.m., so that the phrase \xe2\x80\x9cnight train\xe2\x80\x9d would be ambiguous\r\nand not decisive as with Norden.\r\n\r\nSo far good; but how was I to spend the intervening time? Should I act\r\non Davies\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9cquerry\xe2\x80\x9d and go to Bremen after B\xc3\xb6hme? I soon dismissed\r\nthat idea. It was one to act upon if others failed; for the present it\r\nmeant another scramble. Bremen is six hours from Norden by rail. I\r\nshould spend a disproportionate amount of my limited time in trains,\r\nand I should want a different disguise. Besides, I had already learnt\r\nsomething fresh about B\xc3\xb6hme; for the seed dropped at Emden Station\r\nyesterday had come to life. A submarine engineer I knew him to be\r\nbefore; I now knew that canals were another branch of his labours\xe2\x80\x94not a\r\nvery illuminating fact; but could I pick up more in a single day?\r\n\r\nThere remained Esens, and it was thither I resolved to go to-night\xe2\x80\x94a\r\ntedious journey, lasting till past eight in the evening; but there I\r\nshould only be an hour from Norden by rail.\r\n\r\nAnd at Esens?\r\n\r\nAll day long I strove for light on the central mystery, collecting from\r\nmy diary, my memory, my imagination, from the map, the time-table, and\r\nDavies\xe2\x80\x99s grubby jottings, every elusive atom of material. Sometimes I\r\nissued from a reverie with a start, to find a phlegmatic Dutch peasant\r\nstaring strangely at me over his china pipe. I was more careful over\r\nthe German border. Davies\xe2\x80\x99s paper I soon knew by heart. I pictured him\r\nwriting it with his cramped fist in his corner by the stove, fighting\r\nagainst sleep, absently striking salvos of matches, while I snored in\r\nmy bunk; absently diverging into dreams, I knew, of a rose-brown face\r\nunder dewy hair and a grey tam-o\xe2\x80\x99-shanter; though not a word of her\r\ncame into the document. I smiled to see his undying faith in the\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cchannel theory\xe2\x80\x9d reconciled at the eleventh hour, with new data\r\ntouching the neglected \xe2\x80\x9cland\xe2\x80\x9d.\r\n\r\nThe result was certainly interesting, but it left me cold. That there\r\nexisted in the German archives some such scheme of defence for the\r\nNorth Sea coast was very likely indeed. The seven islands, with their\r\nseven shallow channels (though, by the way, two of them, the twin\r\nbranches of the Ems, are by no means so shallow), were a very fair\r\nconjecture, and fitted in admirably with the channel theory, whose\r\nintrinsic merits I had always recognised; my constant objection having\r\nbeen that it did not go nearly far enough to account for our treatment.\r\nThe ring of railway round the peninsula, with Esens at the apex, was\r\nsuggestive, too; but the same objection applied. Every country with a\r\nmaritime frontier has, I suppose, secret plans of mobilisation for its\r\ndefence, but they are not such as could be discovered by passing\r\ntravellers, not such as would warrant stealthy searches, or require for\r\ntheir elaboration so recondite a meeting-place as Memmert. Dollmann was\r\nanother weak point; Dollmann in England, spying. All countries, Germany\r\nincluded, have spies in their service, dirty though necessary tools;\r\nbut Dollmann in such intimate association with the principal plotters\r\non this side; Dollmann rich, influential, a power in local affairs\xe2\x80\x94it\r\nwas clear he was no ordinary spy.\r\n\r\nAnd here I detected a hesitation in Davies\xe2\x80\x99s rough sketch, a\r\nreluctance, as it were, to pursue a clue to its logical end. He spoke\r\nof a German scheme of coast defence, and in the next breath of Dollmann\r\nspying for English plans in the event of war with Germany, and there he\r\nleft the matter; but what sort of plans? Obviously (if he was on the\r\nright track) plans of attack on the German coast as opposed to those of\r\nstrategy on the high seas. But what sort of an attack? Obviously again,\r\nif his railway-ring meant anything, an attack by invasion on that\r\nremote and desolate littoral which he had so often himself declared to\r\nbe impregnably secure behind its web of sands and shallows. My mind\r\nwent back to my question at Bensersiel, \xe2\x80\x9cCan this coast be invaded?\xe2\x80\x9d to\r\nhis denial and our fruitless survey of the dykes and polders. Was he\r\nnow reverting to a fancy we had both rejected, while shrinking from\r\ngiving it explicit utterance? The doubt was tantalising.\r\n\r\nA brief digression here about the phases of my journey. At Rheine I\r\nchanged trains, turned due north and became a German seaman. There was\r\nlittle risk in a defective accent\xe2\x80\x94sailors are so polyglot; while an\r\nEnglish sailor straying about Esens might excite curiosity. Yesterday I\r\nhad paid no heed to the landscape; to-day I neglected nothing that\r\ncould conceivably supply a hint.\r\n\r\nFrom Rheine to Emden we descended the valley of the Ems; at first\r\nthrough a land of thriving towns and fat pastures, degenerating farther\r\nnorth to spaces of heathery bog and moorland\xe2\x80\x94a sad country, but looking\r\nat its best, such as that was, for I should mention here that the\r\nweather, which in the early morning had been as cold and misty as ever,\r\ngrew steadily milder and brighter as the day advanced; while my\r\nnewspaper stated that the glass was falling and the anticyclone giving\r\nway to pressure from the Atlantic.\r\n\r\nAt Emden, where we entered Friesland proper, the train crossed a big\r\ncanal, and for the twentieth time that day (for we had passed numbers\r\nof them in Holland, and not a few in Germany), I said to myself,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCanals, canals. Where does B\xc3\xb6hme come in?\xe2\x80\x9d It was dusk, but light\r\nenough to see an unfamiliar craft, a torpedo-boat in fact, moored to\r\nstakes at one side. In a moment I remembered that page in the _North\r\nSea Pilot_ where the Ems-Jade Canal is referred to as deep enough to\r\ncarry gun-boats, and as used for that strategic purpose between\r\nWilhelmshaven and Emden, along the base, that is, of the Frisian\r\npeninsula. I asked a peasant opposite; yes, that was the Ems-Jade\r\nCanal. Had Davies forgotten it? It would have greatly strengthened his\r\nhalting sketch.\r\n\r\nAt the bookstall at Emden I bought a pocket ordnance map [There is, of\r\ncourse, no space to reproduce this, but here and henceforward the\r\nreader is referred to Map B.] of Friesland, on a much larger scale than\r\nanything I had used before, and when I was unobserved studied the\r\ncourse of the canal, with an impatience which, alas! quickly cooled.\r\nFrom Emden northwards I used the same map to aid my eyesight, and with\r\nits help saw in the gathering gloom more heaths and bogs, once a great\r\nglimmering lake, and at intervals cultivated tracts; a watery land as\r\never; pools, streams and countless drains and ditches. Extensive woods\r\nwere marked also, but farther inland. We passed Norden at seven, just\r\ndark. I looked out for the creek, and sure enough, we crossed it just\r\nbefore entering the station. Its bed was nearly dry, and I\r\ndistinguished barges lying aground in it. This being the junction for\r\nEsens, I had to wait three-quarters of an hour, and then turned east\r\nthrough the uttermost northern wilds, stopping at occasional village\r\nstations and keeping five or six miles from the sea. It was during this\r\nstage, in a wretchedly lit compartment, and alone for the most part,\r\nthat I finally assembled all my threads and tried to weave them into a\r\ncable whose core should be Esens; \xe2\x80\x9ca town\xe2\x80\x9d, so Baedeker said, \xe2\x80\x9cof 3,500\r\ninhabitants, the centre of a rich agricultural district. Fine spire.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEsens is four miles inland from Bensersiel. I reviewed every\r\ncircumstance of that day at Bensersiel, and boiled to think how von\r\nBr\xc3\xbcning had tricked me. He had driven to Esens himself, and read me so\r\nwell that he actually offered to take me with him, and I had refused\r\nfrom excess of cleverness. Stay, though; if I had happened to accept he\r\nwould have taken very good care that I saw nothing important. The\r\nsecret, therefore, was not writ large on the walls of Esens. Was it\r\nconnected with Bensersiel too, or the country between? I searched the\r\nordnance map again, standing up to get a better light and less jolting.\r\nThere was the road northwards from Esens to Bensersiel, passing through\r\ndots and chess-board squares, the former meaning fen, the latter\r\nfields, so the reference said. Something else, too, immediately caught\r\nmy eye, and that was a stream running to Bensersiel. I knew it at once\r\nfor the muddy stream or drain we had seen at the harbour, issuing\r\nthrough the sluice or _siel_ from which Bensersiel took its name. But\r\nit arrested my attention now because it looked more prominent than I\r\nshould have expected. Charts are apt to ignore the geography of the\r\nmainland, except in so far as it offers sea-marks to mariners. On the\r\nchart this stream had been shown as a rough little corkscrew, like a\r\nsucking-pig\xe2\x80\x99s tail. On the ordnance map it was marked with a dark blue\r\nline, was labelled \xe2\x80\x9cBenser Tief\xe2\x80\x9d, and was given a more resolute course;\r\nbends became angles, and there were what appeared to be artificial\r\nstraightnesses at certain points. One of the threads in my skein, the\r\ncanal thread, tingled sympathetically, like a wire charged with\r\ncurrent. Standing astraddle on both seats, with the map close to the\r\nlamp, I greedily followed the course of the \xe2\x80\x9ctief\xe2\x80\x9d southward. It\r\ninclined away from the road to Esens and passed the town about a mile\r\nto the west, diving underneath the railway. Soon after it took angular\r\ntacks to the eastward, and joined another blue line trending\r\nsouth-east, and lettered \xe2\x80\x9cEsens\xe2\x80\x94Wittmunder _Canal_.\xe2\x80\x9d This canal,\r\nhowever, came to an abrupt end halfway to Wittmund, a neighbouring\r\ntown.\r\n\r\nFor the first time that day there came to me a sense of genuine\r\ninspiration. Those shallow depths and short distances, fractions of\r\nmetres and kilometres, which I had overheard from B\xc3\xb6hme\xe2\x80\x99s lips at\r\nMemmert, and which Davies had attributed to the outside channels\xe2\x80\x94did\r\nthey refer to a canal? I remembered seeing barges in Bensersiel\r\nharbour. I remembered conversations with the natives in the inn, scraps\r\nof the post-master\xe2\x80\x99s pompous loquacity, talks of growing trade, of\r\nbricks and grain passing from the interior to the islands: from another\r\nsource\xe2\x80\x94was it the grocer of Wangeroog?\xe2\x80\x94of expansion of business in the\r\nislands themselves as bathing resorts; from another source again\xe2\x80\x94von\r\nBr\xc3\xbcning himself, surely\xe2\x80\x94of Dollmann\xe2\x80\x99s personal activity in the\r\ndevelopment of the islands. In obscure connexion with these things, I\r\nsaw the torpedo-boat in the Ems-Jade Canal.\r\n\r\nIt was between Dornum and Esens that these ideas came, and I was still\r\nabsorbed in them when the train drew up, just upon nine o\xe2\x80\x99clock, at my\r\ndestination, and after ten minutes\xe2\x80\x99 walk, along with a handful of other\r\npassengers, I found myself in the quiet cobbled streets of Esens, with\r\nthe great church steeple, that we had so often seen from the sea,\r\nsoaring above me in the moonlight.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXVI.\r\nThe Seven Siels\r\n\r\n\r\nSelecting the very humblest _Gasthaus_ I could discover, I laid down my\r\nbundle and called for beer, bread, and _Wurst._ The landlord, as I had\r\nexpected, spoke the Frisian dialect, so that though he was rather\r\ndifficult to understand, he had no doubts about the purity of my own\r\nGerman high accent. He was a worthy fellow, and hospitably interested:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid I want a bed?\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cNo; I was going on to Bensersiel,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, \xe2\x80\x9cto\r\nsleep there, and take the morning _Postschiff_ to Langeoog Island.\xe2\x80\x9d (I\r\nhad not forgotten our friends the twin giants and their functions.) \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nwas not an islander myself?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked. \xe2\x80\x9cNo, but I had a married sister\r\nthere; had just returned from a year\xe2\x80\x99s voyaging, and was going to visit\r\nher.\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cBy the way,\xe2\x80\x9d I asked, \xe2\x80\x9chow are they getting on with the Benser\r\nTief?\xe2\x80\x9d My friend shrugged his shoulders; it was finished, he believed.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd the connexion to Wittmund?\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cUnder construction still.\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cLangeoog\r\nwould be going ahead then?\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cOh! he supposed so, but he did not believe\r\nin these new-fangled schemes.\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cBut it was good for trade, I supposed?\r\nEsens would benefit in sending goods by the \xe2\x80\x98tief\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x94what was the\r\ntraffic, by the way?\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cOh, a few more barge-loads than before of\r\nbricks, timber, coals, etc., but it would come to nothing _he_ knew:\r\n_Aktiengesellschaften_ (companies) were an invention of the devil. A\r\nfew speculators got them up and made money themselves out of land and\r\ncontracts, while the shareholders they had hoodwinked starved.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s something in that,\xe2\x80\x9d I conceded to this bigoted old\r\nconservative; \xe2\x80\x9cmy sister at Langeoog rents her lodging-house from a man\r\nnamed Dollmann; they say he owns a heap of land about. I saw his yacht\r\nonce\xe2\x80\x94pink velvet and electric light inside, they say\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s the name,\xe2\x80\x9d said mine host, \xe2\x80\x9cthat\xe2\x80\x99s one of them\xe2\x80\x94some sort of\r\nforeigner, I\xe2\x80\x99ve heard; runs a salvage concern, too, Juist way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, he won\xe2\x80\x99t get any of my savings!\xe2\x80\x9d I laughed, and soon after took\r\nmy leave, and inquired from a passer-by the road to Dornum. \xe2\x80\x9cFollow the\r\nrailway,\xe2\x80\x9d I was told.\r\n\r\nWith a warm wind in my face from the south-west, fleecy clouds and a\r\nhalf-moon overhead, I set out, not for Bensersiel but for Benser Tief,\r\nwhich I knew must cross the road to Dornum somewhere. A mile or so of\r\ncobbled causeway flanked with ditches and willows, and running cheek by\r\njowl with the railway track; then a bridge, and below me the \xe2\x80\x9cTief\xe2\x80\x9d;\r\nwhich was, in fact, a small canal. A rutty track left the road, and\r\nsloped down to it one side; a rough siding left the railway, and sloped\r\ndown to it on the other.\r\n\r\nI lit a pipe and sat on the parapet for a little. No one was stirring,\r\nso with great circumspection I began to reconnoitre the left bank to\r\nthe north. The siding entered a fenced enclosure by a locked gate\xe2\x80\x94a\r\ngate I could have easily climbed, but I judged it wiser to go round by\r\nthe bridge again and look across. The enclosure was a small coal-store,\r\nnothing more; there were gaunt heaps of coal glittering in the\r\nmoonlight; a barge half loaded lying alongside, and a deserted office\r\nbuilding. I skulked along a sandy towpath in solitude. Fens and field\r\nwere round me, as the map had said; willows and osier-beds; the dim\r\nforms of cattle; the low melody of wind roaming unfettered over a\r\nplain; once or twice the flutter and quack of a startled wild-duck.\r\n\r\nPresently I came to a farmhouse, dark and silent; opposite it, in the\r\ncanal, a couple of empty barges. I climbed into one of these, and\r\nsounded with my stick on the off-side\xe2\x80\x94barely three feet; and the\r\ntorpedo-boat melted out of my speculations. The stream, I observed\r\nalso, was only just wide enough for two barges to pass with comfort.\r\nOther farms I saw, or thought I saw, and a few more barges lying in\r\nside-cuts linked by culverts to the canal, but nothing noteworthy; and\r\nmindful that I had to explore the Wittmund side of the railway too, I\r\nturned back, already a trifle damped in spirits, but still keenly\r\nexpectant.\r\n\r\nPassing under the road and railway, I again followed the tow-path,\r\nwhich, after half a mile, plunged into woods, then entered a clearing\r\nand another fenced enclosure; a timber-yard by the look of it. This\r\ntime I stripped from the waist downward, waded over, dressed again, and\r\nclimbed the paling. (There was a cottage standing back, but its\r\noccupants evidently slept.) I was in a timber-yard, by the stacks of\r\nwood and the steam saw-mill; but something more than a timber-yard, for\r\nas I warily advanced under the shadow of the trees at the edge of the\r\nclearing I came to a long tin shed which strangely reminded me of\r\nMemmert, and below it, nearer the canal, loomed a dark skeleton\r\nframework, which proved to be a half-built vessel on stocks. Close by\r\nwas a similar object, only nearly completed\xe2\x80\x94a barge. A paved slipway\r\nled to the water here, and the canal broadened to a siding or\r\nback-water in which lay seven or eight more barges in tiers. I scaled\r\nanother paling and went on, walking, I should think, three miles by the\r\nside of the canal, till the question of bed and ulterior plans brought\r\nme to a halt. It was past midnight, and I was adding little to my\r\ninformation. I had encountered a brick-field, but soon after that there\r\nwas increasing proof that the canal was as yet little used for traffic.\r\nIt grew narrower, and there were many signs of recent labour for its\r\nimprovement. In one place a dammed-off deviation was being excavated,\r\nevidently to abridge an impossible bend. The path had become atrocious,\r\nand my boots were heavy with clay. Bearing in mind the abruptly-ending\r\nblue line on the map, I considered it useless to go farther, and\r\nretraced my steps, trying to concoct a story which would satisfy an\r\nirritable Esens inn-keeper that it was a respectable wayfarer, and not\r\na tramp or a lunatic, who knocked him up at half-past one or\r\nthereabouts.\r\n\r\nBut a much more practical resource occurred to me as I approached the\r\ntimber-yard; for lodging, free and accessible, lay there ready to hand.\r\nI boarded one of the empty barges in the backwater, and surveyed my\r\nquarters for the night. It was of a similar pattern to all the others I\r\nhad seen; a lighter, strictly, in the sense that it had no means of\r\nself-propulsion, and no separate quarters for a crew, the whole\r\ninterior of the hull being free for cargo. At both bow and stern there\r\nwere ten feet or so of deck, garnished with bitts and bollards. The\r\nrest was an open well, flanked by waterways of substantial breadth; the\r\nwhole of stout construction and, for a humble lighter, of\r\nwell-proportioned and even graceful design, with a marked forward\r\nsheer, and, as I had observed in the specimen on the stocks, easy lines\r\nat the stern. In short, it was apparent, even to an ignorant landsman\r\nlike myself, that she was designed not merely for canal work but for\r\nrough water; and well she might be, for, though the few miles of sea\r\nshe had to cross in order to reach the islands were both shallow and\r\nsheltered, I knew from experience what a vicious surf they could be\r\nwhipped into by a sudden gale. It must not be supposed that I dwelt on\r\nthis matter. On limited lines I was making progress, but the wings of\r\nimagination still drooped nervelessly at my sides. Otherwise I perhaps\r\nshould have examined this lighter more particularly, instead of\r\nregarding it mainly as a convenient hiding-place. Under the stern-deck\r\nwas stored a massive roll of tarpaulin, a corner of which made an\r\nexcellent blanket, and my bundle a good pillow. It was a descent from\r\nthe luxury of last night; but a spy, I reflected philosophically,\r\ncannot expect a feather bed two nights running, and this one was at any\r\nrate airier and roomier than the coffin-like bunk of the _Dulcibella_,\r\nand not so very much harder.\r\n\r\nWhen snugly ensconced, I studied the map by intermittent match-light.\r\nIt had been dawning on me in the last half-hour that this canal was\r\nonly one of several; that in concentrating myself on Esens and\r\nBensersiel, I had forgotten that there were other villages ending in\r\nsiel, also furnished on the chart with corkscrew streams; and,\r\nmoreover, that B\xc3\xb6hme\xe2\x80\x99s statistics of depth and distance had been\r\nmarshalled in seven categories, A to G. The very first match brought\r\nfull recollection as to the villages. The suffix _siel_ repeated itself\r\nall round the coast-line. Five miles eastward of Bensersiel was\r\nNeuharlingersiel, and farther on Carolinensiel. Four miles westward was\r\nDornumersiel; and farther on Nessmersiel and Hilgenriedersiel. That was\r\nsix on the north coast of the peninsula alone. On the west coast,\r\nfacing the Ems, there was only one, Greetsiel, a good way south of\r\nNorden. But on the east, facing the Jade, there were no less than\r\neight, at very close intervals. A moment\xe2\x80\x99s thought and I disregarded\r\nthis latter group; they had nothing to do with Esens, nor had they any\r\nimaginable _raison d\xe2\x80\x99\xc3\xa9tre_ as veins for commerce; differing markedly in\r\nthis respect from the group of six on the north coast, whose outlook\r\nwas the chain of islands, and whose inland centre, almost exactly, was\r\nEsens. I still wanted one to make seven, and as a working hypothesis\r\nadded the solitary Greetsiel. At all seven villages streams debouched,\r\nas at Bensersiel. From all seven points of issue dotted lines were\r\nmarked seaward, intersecting the great tidal sands and leading towards\r\nthe islands. And on the mainland behind the whole sevenfold system ran\r\nthe loop of railway. But there were manifold minor points of\r\ndifference. No stream boasted so deep and decisive a blue lintel as did\r\nBenser Tief; none penetrated so far into the Hinterland. They varied in\r\nlength and sinuosity. Two, those belonging to Hilgenriedersiel and\r\nGreetsiel, appeared not to reach the railway at all. On the other hand,\r\nCarolinensiel, opposite Wangeroog Island, had a branch line all to\r\nitself.\r\n\r\nMatch after match waxed and waned as I puzzled over the mystic seven.\r\nIn the end I puzzled myself to sleep, with the one fixed idea that\r\nto-morrow, on my way back to Norden, I must see more of these budding\r\ncanals, if such they were. My dreams that night were of a mighty chain\r\nof redoubts and masked batteries couching _perdus_ among the sand-dunes\r\nof desolate islets; built, coral-like, by infinitely slow and secret\r\nlabour; fed by lethal cargoes borne in lighters and in charge of\r\nstealthy mutes who, one and all, bore the likeness of Grimm.\r\n\r\nI was up and away at daylight (the weather mild and showery), meeting\r\nsome navvies on my way back to the road, who gave me good morning and a\r\nstare. On the bridge I halted and fell into torments of indecision.\r\nThere was so much to do and so little time to do it in. The whole\r\nproblem seemed to have been multiplied by seven, and the total again\r\ndoubled and redoubled\xe2\x80\x94seven blue lines on land, seven dotted lines on\r\nthe sea, seven islands in the offing. Once I was near deciding to put\r\nmy pretext into practice, and cross to Langeoog; but that meant missing\r\nthe rendezvous, and I was loth to do that.\r\n\r\nAt any rate, I wanted breakfast badly; and the best way to get it, and\r\nat the same time to open new ground, was to walk to Dornum. Then I\r\nshould find a blue line called the _Neues Tief_ leading to\r\nDornumersiel, on the coast. That explored, I could pass on to Nesse,\r\nwhere there was another blue line to Nessmersiel. All this was on the\r\nway to Norden, and I should have the railway constantly at my back, to\r\ncarry me there in the evening. The last train (my time-table told me)\r\nwas one reaching Norden at 7.15 p.m. I could catch this at Hage Station\r\nat 7.5.\r\n\r\nA brisk walk of six miles brought me, ravenously hungry, to Dornum.\r\nRoad and railway had clung together all the time, and about half-way\r\nhad been joined on the left by a third companion in the shape of a puny\r\nstream which I knew from the map to be the upper portion of Neues Tief.\r\nWriggling and doubling like an eel, choked with sedges and reeds, it\r\nhad no pretensions to being navigable. At length it looped away into\r\nthe fens out of sight, only to reappear again close to Dornum in a much\r\nmore dignified guise.\r\n\r\nThere was no siding where the railway crossed it, but at the town\r\nitself, which it skirted on the east, a towpath began, and a piled\r\nwharf had been recently constructed. Going on to this was a red-brick\r\nbuilding with the look of a warehouse, roofless as yet, and with\r\nworkmen on its scaffolds. It sharpened the edge of my appetite.\r\n\r\nIf I had been wise I should have been content with a snack bought at a\r\ncounter, but a thirst for hot coffee and clues induced me to repeat the\r\nexperiment of Esens and seek a primitive beer-house. I was less lucky\r\non this occasion. The house I chose was obscure enough, but its\r\nproprietor was no simple Frisian, but an ill-looking rascal with shifty\r\neyes and a debauched complexion, who showed a most unwelcome curiosity\r\nin his customer. As a last fatality, he wore a peaked cap like my own,\r\nand turned out to be an ex-sailor. I should have fled at the sight of\r\nhim had I had the chance, but I was attended to first by a slatternly\r\ngirl who, I am sure, called him up to view me. To explain my muddy\r\nboots and trousers I said I had walked from Esens, and from that I\r\nfound myself involved in a tangle of impromptu lies. Floundering down\r\nan old groove, I placed my sister this time on Baltrum Island, and said\r\nI was going to Dornumersiel (which is opposite Baltrum) to cross from\r\nthere. As this was drawing a bow at a venture, I dared not assume local\r\nknowledge, and spoke of the visit as my first. Dornumersiel was a lucky\r\nshot; there _was_ a ferry-galliot from there to Baltrum; but he knew,\r\nor pretended to know, Baltrum, and had not heard of my sister. I grew\r\nthe more nervous in that I saw from the first that he took me to be of\r\nbetter condition than most merchant seamen; and, to make matters worse,\r\nI was imprudent enough in pleading haste to pull out from an inner\r\npocket my gold watch with the chain and seals attached. He told me\r\nthere was no hurry, that I should miss the tide at Dornumersiel, and\r\nthen fell to pressing strong waters on me, and asking questions whose\r\ninsinuating grossness gave me the key to his biography. He must have\r\nbeen at one stage in his career a dock-side crimp, one of those foul\r\nsharks who prey on discharged seamen, and as often as not are ex-seamen\r\nthemselves, versed in the weaknesses of the tribe. He was now keeping\r\nhis hand in with me, who, unhappily, purported to belong to the very\r\nclass he was used to victimise, and, moreover, had a gold watch, and,\r\ndoubtless, a full purse. Nothing more ridiculously inopportune could\r\nhave befallen me, or more dangerous; for his class are as cosmopolitan\r\nas waiters and _concierges,_ with as facile a gift for language and as\r\nunerring a scent for nationality. Sure enough, the fellow recognised\r\nmine, and positively challenged me with it in fairly fluent English\r\nwith a Yankee twang. Encumbered with the mythical sister, of course I\r\nstuck to my lie, said I had been on an English ship so long that I had\r\npicked up the accent, and also gave him some words in broken English.\r\nAt the same time I showed I thought him an impertinent nuisance, paid\r\nmy score and walked out\xe2\x80\x94quit of him? Not a bit of it! He insisted on\r\nshowing me the way to Dornumersiel, and followed me down the street.\r\nPerceiving that he was in liquor, in spite of the early hour, I dared\r\nnot risk a quarrelsome scene with a man who already knew so much about\r\nme, and might at any moment elicit more. So I melted, and humoured him;\r\ntreated him in a ginshop in the hope of giving him the slip\xe2\x80\x94a\r\ndisastrous resource, which was made a precedent for further potations\r\nelsewhere. I would gladly draw a veil over our scandalous progress\r\nthrough peaceable Dornum, of the terrors I experienced when he\r\nintroduced me as his friend, and as his English friend, and of the\r\nabasement I felt, too, as, linked arm in arm, we trod the three miles\r\nof road coastwards. It was his malicious whim that we should talk\r\nEnglish; a fortunate whim, as it turned out, because I knew no fo\xe2\x80\x99c\xe2\x80\x99sle\r\nGerman, but had a smattering of fo\xe2\x80\x99c\xe2\x80\x99sle English, gathered from\r\nCutcliffe Hyne and Kipling. With these I extemporised a disreputable\r\nhybrid, mostly consisting of oaths and blasphemies, and so yarned of\r\nimaginary voyages. Of course he knew every port in the world, but\r\nhappily was none too critical, owing to repeated _schnappsen._\r\n\r\nNevertheless, it was a deplorable _contretemps_ from every point of\r\nview. I was wasting my time, for the road took a different direction to\r\nthe Neues Tief, so that I had not even the advantage of inspecting the\r\ncanal and only met with it when we reached the sea. Here it split into\r\ntwo mouths, both furnished with locks, and emptying into two little\r\nmud-hole harbours, replicas of Bensersiel, each owning its cluster of\r\nhouses. I made straight for the _Gasthaus_ at Dornumersiel, primed my\r\ncompanion well, and asked him to wait while I saw about a boat in the\r\nharbour; but, needless to say, I never rejoined him. I just took a\r\ncursory look at the left-hand harbour, saw a lighter locking through\r\n(for the tide was high), and then walked as fast as my legs would carry\r\nme to the outermost dyke, mounted it, and strode along the sea\r\nwestwards in the teeth of a smart shower of rain, full of deep\r\napprehensions as to the stir and gossip my disappearance might cause if\r\nmy odious crimp was sober enough to discover it. As soon as I deemed it\r\nsafe, I dropped on to the sand and ran till I could run no more. Then I\r\nsat on my bundle with my back to the dyke in partial shelter from the\r\nrain, watching the sea recede from the flats and dwindle into slender\r\nmeres, and the laden clouds fly weeping over the islands till those\r\npale shapes were lost in mist.\r\n\r\nThe barge I had seen locking through was creeping across towards\r\nLangeoog behind a tug and a wisp of smoke.\r\n\r\nNo more exploration by daylight! That was my first resolve, for I felt\r\nas if the country must be ringing with reports of an Englishman in\r\ndisguise. I must remain in hiding till dusk, then regain the railway\r\nand slink into that train to Norden. Now directly I began to resign\r\nmyself to temporary inaction, and to centre my thoughts on the\r\nrendezvous, a new doubt assailed me. Nothing had seemed more certain\r\nyesterday than that Norden was the scene of the rendezvous, but that\r\nwas before the seven _siels_ had come into prominence. The name Norden\r\nnow sounded naked and unconvincing. As I wondered why, it suddenly\r\noccurred to me that _all_ the stations along this northern line, though\r\nfarther inland than Norden, were equally \xe2\x80\x9ccoast stations\xe2\x80\x9d, in the sense\r\nthat they were in touch with harbours (of a sort) on the coast. Norden\r\nhad its tidal creek, but Esens and Dornum had their \xe2\x80\x9ctiefs\xe2\x80\x9d or canals.\r\nFool that I had been to put such a narrow and literal construction on\r\nthe phrase \xe2\x80\x9cthe tide serves!\xe2\x80\x9d Which was it more likely that my\r\nconspirators would visit\xe2\x80\x94Norden, whose intrusion into our theories was\r\npurely hypothetical, or one of these _siels_ to whose sevenfold systems\r\nall my latest observations gave such transcendent significance?\r\n\r\nThere was only one answer; and it filled me with profound\r\ndiscouragement. Seven possible rendezvous!\xe2\x80\x94eight, counting Norden.\r\nWhich to make for? Out came the time-table and map, and with them hope.\r\nThe case was not so bad after all; it demanded no immediate change of\r\nplan, though it imported grave uncertainties and risks. Norden was\r\nstill the objective, but mainly as a railway junction, only remotely as\r\na seaport. Though the possible rendezvous were eight, the possible\r\nstations were reduced to five\xe2\x80\x94Norden, Hage, Dornum, Esens, Wittmund\xe2\x80\x94all\r\non one single line. Trains from east to west along this line were\r\nnegligible, because there were none that could be called night trains,\r\nthe latest being the one I had this morning fixed on to bring me to\r\nNorden, where it arrived at 7.15. Of trains from west to east there was\r\nonly one that need be considered, the same one that I had travelled by\r\nlast night, leaving Norden at 7.43 and reaching Esens at 8.50, and\r\nWittmund at 9.13. This train, as the reader who was with me in it\r\nknows, was in correspondence with another from Emden and the south, and\r\nalso, I now found, with services from Hanover, Bremen, and Berlin. He\r\nwill also remember that I had to wait three-quarters of an hour at\r\nNorden, from 7 to 7.43.\r\n\r\nThe platform at Norden Junction, therefore, between 7.15, when I should\r\narrive at it _from_ the east, and 7.43 when B\xc3\xb6hme and his unknown\r\nfriend should leave it _for_ the east; there, and in that half-hour,\r\nwas my opportunity for recognising and shadowing two at least of the\r\nconspirators. I must take the train they took, and alight where they\r\nalighted. If I could not find them at all I should be thrown back on\r\nthe rejected view that Norden itself was the rendezvous, and should\r\nwait there till 10.46.\r\n\r\nIn the meantime it was all very well to resolve on inaction till dusk;\r\nbut after an hour\xe2\x80\x99s rest, damp clothes and feet, and the absence of\r\npursuers, tempted me to take the field again. Avoiding roads and\r\nvillages as long as it was light, I cut across country\r\nsouth-westwards\xe2\x80\x94a dismal and laborious journey, with oozy fens and\r\nknee-deep drains to course, with circuits to be made to pass clear of\r\npeasants, and many furtive crouchings behind dykes and willows. What\r\nlittle I learnt was in harmony with previous explorations, for my track\r\ncut at right angles the line of the Harke Tief, the stream issuing at\r\nNessmersiel. It, too, was in the nature of a canal, but only in embryo\r\nat the point I touched it, south of Nesse. Works on a deviation were in\r\nprogress, and in a short digression down stream I sighted another\r\nlighter-building yard. As for Hilgenriedersiel, the fourth of the\r\nseven, I had no time to see anything of it at all. At seven o\xe2\x80\x99clock I\r\nwas at Hage Station, very tired, wet, and footsore, after covering\r\nnearly twenty miles all told since I left my bed in the lighter.\r\n\r\nFrom here to Norden it was a run in the train of ten minutes, which I\r\nspent in eating some rye bread and smoked eel, and in scraping the mud\r\noff my boots and trousers. Fatigue vanished when the train drew up at\r\nthe station, and the momentous twenty-eight minutes began to run their\r\ncourse. Having donned a bulky muffler and turned up the collar of my\r\npea-jacket, I crossed over immediately to the up-platform, walked\r\nboldly to the booking-office, and at once sighted\xe2\x80\x94von Br\xc3\xbcning\xe2\x80\x94yes, von\r\nBr\xc3\xbcning in mufti; but there was no mistaking his tall athletic figure,\r\npleasant features, and neat brown beard. He was just leaving the\r\nwindow, gathering up a ticket and some coins. I joined a _queue_ of\r\nthree or four persons who were waiting their turn, flattened myself\r\nbetween them and the partition till I heard him walk out. Not having\r\nheard what station he had booked for, I took a fourth-class ticket to\r\nWittmund, which covered all chances. Then, with my chin buried in my\r\nmuffler, I sought the darkest corner of the ill-lit combination of bar\r\nand waiting-room where, by the tiresome custom in Germany, would-be\r\ntravellers are penned till their train is ready. Von Br\xc3\xbcning I\r\nperceived sitting in another corner, with his hat over his eyes and a\r\ncigar between his lips. A boy brought me a tankard of tawny Munich\r\nbeer, and, sipping it, I watched. People passed in and out, but nobody\r\nspoke to the sailor in mufti. When a quarter of an hour elapsed, a\r\nplatform door opened, and a raucous voice shouted: \xe2\x80\x9cHage, Dornum,\r\nEsens, Wittmund!\xe2\x80\x9d A knot of passengers jostled out to the platform,\r\nshowing their tickets. I was slow over my beer, and was last of the\r\nknot, with von Br\xc3\xbcning immediately ahead of me, so close that his\r\ncigar-smoke curled into my face. I looked over his shoulder at the\r\nticket he showed, missed the name, but caught a muttered double\r\nsibilant from the official who checked it; ran over the stations in my\r\nhead, and pounced on _Esens._ That was as much I wanted to know for the\r\npresent; so I made my way to a fourth-class compartment, and lost sight\r\nof my quarry, not venturing, till the last door had banged, to look out\r\nof the window. When I did so two late arrivals were hurrying up to a\r\ncarriage\xe2\x80\x94one tall, one of middle height; both in cloaks and comforters.\r\nTheir features I could not distinguish, but certainly neither of them\r\nwas B\xc3\xb6hme. They had not come through the waiting-room door, but,\r\nplainly, from the dark end of the platform, where they had been\r\nwaiting. A guard, with some surly remonstrances, shut them in, and the\r\ntrain started.\r\n\r\nEsens\xe2\x80\x94the name had not surprised me; it fulfilled a presentiment that\r\nhad been growing in strength all the afternoon. For the last time I\r\nreferred to the map, pulpy and blurred with the day\xe2\x80\x99s exposure, and\r\ntried to etch it into my brain. I marked the road to Bensersiel, and\r\nhow it converged by degrees on the Benser Tief until they met at the\r\nsea. \xe2\x80\x9cThe tide serves!\xe2\x80\x9d Longing for Davies to help me, I reckoned, by\r\nthe aid of my diary, that high tide at Bensersiel would be about\r\neleven, and for two hours, I remembered (say from ten to twelve\r\nto-night), there were from five to six feet of water in the harbour.\r\n\r\nWe should reach Esens at 8.50. Would they drive, as von Br\xc3\xbcning had\r\ndone a week ago? I tightened my belt, stamped my mud-burdened boots,\r\nand thanked God for the Munich beer. Whither were they going from\r\nBensersiel, and in what; and how was I to follow them? These were\r\nnebulous questions, but I was in fettle for anything; boat-stealing was\r\na bagatelle. Fortune, I thought, smiled; Romance beckoned; even the sea\r\nlooked kind. Ay, and I do not know but that Imagination was already\r\nbeginning to unstiffen and flutter those nerveless wings.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXVII.\r\nThe Luck of the Stowaway\r\n\r\n\r\nAt Esens Station I reversed my Norden tactics, jumped out smartly, and\r\ngot to the door of egress first of all, gave up my ticket, and hung\r\nabout the gate of the station under cover of darkness. Fortune smiled\r\nstill; there was no vehicle in waiting at all, and there were only half\r\na dozen passengers. Two of these were the cloaked gentlemen who had\r\nbeen so nearly left behind at Norden, and another was von Br\xc3\xbcning. The\r\nlatter walked well in advance of the first pair, but at the gate on to\r\nthe high road the three showed a common purpose, in that, unlike the\r\nrest, who turned towards Esens town, they turned southwards; much to my\r\nperplexity, for this was the contrary direction to Bensersiel and the\r\nsea. I, with my bundle on my shoulder, had been bringing up the rear,\r\nand, as their faithful shadow, turned to the right too, without\r\nforeseeing the consequence. When it was too late to turn back I saw\r\nthat, fifty yards ahead, the road was barred by the gates of a level\r\ncrossing, and that the four of us must inevitably accumulate at the\r\nbarrier till the train had steamed away. This, in fact, happened, and\r\nfor a minute or two we were all in a group, elaborately indifferent to\r\none another, silent, but I am sure very conscious. As for me, \xe2\x80\x9csecret\r\nlaughter tickled all my soul\xe2\x80\x9d. When the gates were opened the three\r\nseemed disposed to lag, so I tactfully took my cue, trudged briskly on\r\nahead, and stopped after a few minutes to listen. Hearing nothing I\r\nwent cautiously back and found that they had disappeared; in which\r\ndirection was not long in doubt, for I came on a grassy path leading\r\ninto the fields on the left or west of the road, and though I could see\r\nno one I heard the distant murmur of receding voices.\r\n\r\nI took my bearings collectedly, placed one foot on the path, thought\r\nbetter of it, and turned back towards Esens. I knew without reference\r\nto the map that that path would bring them to the Benser Tief at a\r\npoint somewhere near the timber-yard. In a fog I might have followed\r\nthem there; as it was, the night was none too dark, and I had my\r\nstrength to husband; and stamped on my memory were the words \xe2\x80\x9cthe tide\r\nserves\xe2\x80\x9d. I judged it a wiser use of time and sinew to anticipate them\r\nat Bensersiel by the shortest road, leaving them to reach it by way of\r\nthe devious Tief, to examine which was, I felt convinced, one of their\r\nobjects.\r\n\r\nIt was nine o\xe2\x80\x99clock of a fresh wild night, a halo round the beclouded\r\nmoon. I passed through quiet Esens, and in an hour I was close to\r\nBensersiel, and could hear the sea. In the rooted idea that I should\r\nfind Grimm on the outskirts, awaiting visitors, I left the road short\r\nof the village, and made a circuit to the harbour by way of the\r\nsea-wall. The lower windows of the inn shed a warm glow into the night,\r\nand within I could see the village circle gathered over cards, and\r\ndominated as of old by the assertive little postmaster, whose\r\nhigh-pitched, excitable voice I could clearly distinguish, as he sat\r\nwith his cap on the back of his head and a \xe2\x80\x9cfeine schnapps\xe2\x80\x9d at his\r\nelbow. The harbour itself looked exactly the same as I remembered it a\r\nweek ago. The post-boat lay in her old berth at the eastern jetty, her\r\nmainsail set and her twin giants spitting over the rail. I hailed them\r\nboldly from the shore (without showing them who I was), and was told\r\nthey were starting for Langeoog in a few minutes; the wind was\r\noff-shore, the mails aboard, and the water just high enough. \xe2\x80\x9cDid I\r\nwant a passage?\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cNo, I thought I would wait.\xe2\x80\x9d Positive that my party\r\ncould never have got here so soon, I nevertheless kept an eye on the\r\ngalliot till she let go her stern-rope and slid away. One contingency\r\nwas eliminated. Some loiterers dispersed, and all port business\r\nappeared to be ended for the night.\r\n\r\nThree-quarters of an hour of strained suspense ensued. Most of it I\r\nspent on my knees in a dark angle between the dyke and the western\r\njetty, whence I had a strategic survey of the basin; but I was driven\r\nat times to relieve inaction by sallies which increased in audacity. I\r\nscouted on the road beyond the bridge, hovered round the lock, and\r\npeered in at the inn parlour; but nowhere could I see a trace of Grimm.\r\nI examined every floating object in the harbour (they were very few),\r\ndropped on to two lighters and pried under tarpaulins, boarded a\r\ndeserted tug and two or three clumsy rowboats tied up to a\r\nmooring-post. Only one of these had the look of readiness, the rest\r\nbeing devoid of oars and rowlocks; a discouraging state of things for a\r\nprospective boat-lifter. It was the sight of these rowboats that\r\nsuggested a last and most distracting possibility, namely, that the\r\nboat in waiting, if boat there were, might be not in the harbour at\r\nall, but somewhere on the sands outside the dyke, where, at this high\r\nstate of the tide, it would have water and to spare. Back to the dyke\r\nthen; but as I peered seaward on the way, contingencies evaporated and\r\na solid fact supervened, for I saw the lights of a steamboat\r\napproaching the harbour mouth. I had barely time to gain my coign of\r\nvantage before she had swept in between the piers, and with a fitful\r\nswizzling of her screw was turning and backing down to a berth just\r\nahead of one of the lighters, and not fifty feet from my hiding-place.\r\nA deck-hand jumped ashore with a rope, while the man at the wheel gave\r\ngruff directions. The vessel was a small tug, and the man at the wheel\r\ndisclosed his identity when, having rung off his engines, he jumped\r\nashore also, looked at his watch in the beam of the sidelight, and\r\nwalked towards the village. It was Grimm, by the height and build\xe2\x80\x94Grimm\r\nclad in a long tarpaulin coat and a sou\xe2\x80\x99wester. I watched him cross the\r\nshaft of light from the inn window and disappear in the direction of\r\nthe canal.\r\n\r\nAnother sailor now appeared and helped his fellow to tie up the tug.\r\nThe two together then went aft and began to set about some job whose\r\nnature I could not determine. To emerge was perilous, so I set about a\r\njob of my own, tearing open my bundle and pulling an oilskin jacket and\r\ntrousers over my clothes, and discarding my peaked cap for a\r\nsou\xe2\x80\x99-wester. This operation was prompted instantaneously by the garb of\r\ntwo sailors, who in hauling on the forward warp came into the field of\r\nthe mast-head light.\r\n\r\nIt was something of a gymnastic masterpiece, since I was lying\xe2\x80\x94or,\r\nrather, standing aslant\xe2\x80\x94on the rough sea-wall, with crannies of brick\r\nfor foothold and the water plashing below me; but then I had not lived\r\nin the _Dulcibella_ for nothing. My chain of thought, I fancy, was\r\nthis\xe2\x80\x94the tug is to carry my party; I cannot shadow a tug in a rowboat,\r\nyet I intend to shadow my party; I must therefore go with them in the\r\ntug, and the first and soundest step is to mimic her crew. But the next\r\nstep was a hard matter, for the crew having finished their job sat side\r\nby side on the bulwarks and lit their pipes. However, a little\r\npantomime soon occurred, as amusing as it was inspiriting. They seemed\r\nto consult together, looking from the tug to the inn and from the inn\r\nto the tug. One of them walked a few paces inn-wards and beckoned to\r\nthe other, who in his turn called something down the engine-room\r\nskylight, and then joined his mate in a scuttle to the inn. Even while\r\nI watched the pantomime I was sliding off my boots, and it had not been\r\nconsummated a second before I had them in my arms and was tripping over\r\nthe mud in my stocking feet. A dozen noiseless steps and I was over the\r\nbulwarks between the wheel and the smoke-stack, casting about for a\r\nhiding-place. The conventional stowaway hides in the hold, but there\r\nwas only a stokehold here, occupied moreover; nor was there an empty\r\napple-barrel, such as Jim of _Treasure Island_ found so useful. As far\r\nas I could see\xe2\x80\x94and I dared not venture far for fear of the skylight\xe2\x80\x94the\r\nsurface of the deck offered nothing secure. But on the farther or\r\nstarboard side, rather abaft the beam, there was a small boat in\r\ndavits, swung outboard, to which common sense, and perhaps a vague\r\nprescience of its after utility, pointed irresistibly. In any case,\r\ndiscrimination was out of place, so I mounted the bulwark and gently\r\nentered my refuge. The tackles creaked a trifle, oars and seats impeded\r\nme; but well before the thirsty truants had returned I was settled on\r\nthe floor boards between two thwarts, so placed that I could, if\r\nnecessary, peep over the gunwale.\r\n\r\nThe two sailors returned at a run, and very soon after voices\r\napproached, and I recognised that of Herr Schenkel chattering volubly.\r\nHe and Grimm boarded the tug and went down a companionway aft, near\r\nwhich, as I peeped over, I saw a second skylight, no bigger than the\r\n_Dulcibella_\xe2\x80\x99s, illuminated from below. Then I heard a cork drawn, and\r\nthe kiss of glasses, and in a minute or two they re-emerged. It was\r\napparent that Herr Schenkel was inclined to stay and make merry, and\r\nthat Grimm was anxious to get rid of him, and none too courteous in\r\nshowing it. The former urged that to-morrow\xe2\x80\x99s tide would do, the latter\r\ngave orders to cast off, and at length observed with an angry oath that\r\nthe water was falling, and he must start; and, to clinch matters, with\r\na curt good-night, he went to the wheel and rang up his engines. Herr\r\nSchenkel landed and strutted off in high dudgeon, while the tug\xe2\x80\x99s screw\r\nbegan to revolve. We had only glided a few yards on when the engines\r\nstopped, a short blast of the whistle sounded, and, before I had had\r\ntime to recast the future, I heard a scurry of footsteps from the\r\ndirection of the dyke, first on the bank, next on the deck. The last of\r\nthese new arrivals panted audibly as he got aboard and dropped on the\r\nplanks with an unelastic thud.\r\n\r\nHer complement made up, the tug left the harbour, but not alone. While\r\nslowly gathering way the hull checked all at once with a sharp jerk,\r\nrecovered, and increased its speed. We had something in tow\xe2\x80\x94what? The\r\nlighter, of course, that had been lying astern of us.\r\n\r\nNow I knew what was in that lighter, because I had been to see, half an\r\nhour ago. It was no lethal cargo, but coal, common household coal; not\r\na full load of it, I remembered\xe2\x80\x94just a good-sized mound amidships,\r\ntrimmed with battens fore and aft to prevent shifting. \xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d thought\r\nI, \xe2\x80\x9cthis is intelligible enough. Grimm was ostensibly there to call for\r\na load of coal for Memmert. But does that mean we are going to\r\nMemmert?\xe2\x80\x9d At the same time I recalled a phrase overheard at the dep\xc3\xb4t,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly one\xe2\x80\x94half a load.\xe2\x80\x9d Why half a load?\r\n\r\nFor some few minutes there was a good deal of movement on deck, and of\r\norders shouted by Grimm and answered by a voice from far astern on the\r\nlighter. Presently, however, the tug warmed to her work, the hull\r\nvibrated with energy, and an ordered peace reigned on board. I also\r\nrealised that having issued from the boomed channel we had turned\r\nwestward, for the wind, which had been blowing us fair, now blew\r\nstrongly over the port beam.\r\n\r\nI peeped out of my eyrie and was satisfied in a moment that as long as\r\nI made no noise, and observed proper prudence, I was perfectly safe\r\n_until the boat was wanted_. There were no deck lamps; the two\r\nskylights diffused but a sickly radiance, and I was abaft the\r\nside-lights. I was abaft the wheel also, though thrillingly near it in\r\npoint of distance\xe2\x80\x94about twelve feet, I should say; and Grimm was\r\nsteering. The wheel, I should mention here, was raised, as you often\r\nsee them, on a sort of pulpit, approached by two or three steps and\r\nfenced by a breast-high arc of boarding. Only one of the crew was\r\nvisible, and he was acting as look-out in the extreme bows, the rays of\r\nthe masthead lights\xe2\x80\x94for a second had been hoisted in sign of\r\ntowage\xe2\x80\x94glistening on his oilskin back. The other man, I concluded, was\r\nsteering the lighter, which I could dimly locate by the pale foam at\r\nher bow.\r\n\r\nAnd the passengers? They were all together aft, three of them, leaning\r\nover the taffrail, with their backs turned to me. One was short and\r\nstout\xe2\x80\x94B\xc3\xb6hme unquestionably; the panting and the thud on the planks had\r\nprepared me for that, though where he had sprung from I did not know.\r\nTwo were tall, and one of these must be von Br\xc3\xbcning. There ought to be\r\nfour, I reckoned; but three were all I could see. And what of the\r\nthird? It must be he who \xe2\x80\x9cinsists on coming\xe2\x80\x9d, the unknown superior at\r\nwhose instance and for whose behoof this secret expedition had been\r\nplanned. And who could he be?
