Many times, needless to say, I had asked\r\nmyself that question, but never till now, when I had found the\r\nrendezvous and joined the expedition, did it become one of burning\r\nimport.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAny weather\xe2\x80\x9d was another of those stored-up phrases that were _\xc3\xa0\r\npropos._ It was a dirty, squally night, not very cold, for the wind\r\nstill hung in the S.S.W.\xe2\x80\x94an off-shore wind on this coast, causing no\r\nappreciable sea on the shoal spaces we were traversing. In the matter\r\nof our bearings, I set myself doggedly to overcome that paralysing\r\nperplexity, always induced in me by night or fog in these intricate\r\nwaters; and, by screwing round and round, succeeded so far as to\r\ndiscover and identify two flashing lights\xe2\x80\x94one alternately red and\r\nwhite, far and faint astern; the other right ahead and rather stronger,\r\ngiving white flashes only. The first and least familiar was, I made\r\nout, from the lighthouse on Wangeroog; the second, well known to me as\r\nour beacon star in the race from Memmert, was the light on the centre\r\nof Norderney Island, about ten miles away.\r\n\r\nI had no accurate idea of the time, for I could not see my watch, but I\r\nthought we must have started about a quarter past eleven. We were\r\ntravelling fast, the funnel belching out smoke and the bow-wave curling\r\nhigh; for the tug appeared to be a powerful little craft, and her load\r\nwas comparatively light.\r\n\r\nSo much for the general situation. As for my own predicament, I was in\r\nno mood to brood on the hazards of this mad adventure, a hundredfold\r\nmore hazardous than my fog-smothered eavesdropping at Memmert. The\r\ncrisis, I knew, had come, and the reckless impudence that had brought\r\nme here must serve me still and extricate me. Fortune loves rough\r\nwooing. I backed my luck and watched.\r\n\r\nThe behaviour of the passengers struck me as odd. They remained in a\r\nrow at the taffrail, gazing astern like regretful emigrants, and\r\nsometimes, gesticulating and pointing. Now no vestige of the low land\r\nwas visible, so I was driven to the conclusion that it was the lighter\r\nthey were discussing; and I date my awakening from the moment that I\r\nrealised this. But the thread broke prematurely; for the passengers\r\ntook to pacing the deck, and I had to lie low. When next I was able to\r\nraise my head they were round Grimm at the wheel, engaged, as far as I\r\ncould discover from their gestures, in an argument about our course and\r\nthe time, for Grimm looked at his watch by the light of a hand-lantern.\r\n\r\nWe were heading north, and I knew by the swell that we must be near the\r\nAccumer Ee, the gap between Langeoog and Baltrum. Were we going out to\r\nopen sea? It came over me with a rush that we _must,_ if we were to\r\ndrop this lighter at Memmert. Had I been Davies I should have been\r\nquicker to seize certain rigid conditions of this cruise, which no\r\nhuman power could modify. We had left after high tide. The water\r\ntherefore was falling everywhere; and the tributary channels in rear of\r\nthe islands were slowly growing impassable. It was quite thirty miles\r\nto Memmert, with three watersheds to pass; behind Baltrum, Norderney,\r\nand Juist. A skipper with nerve and perfect confidence might take us\r\nover one of these in the dark, but most of the run would infallibly\r\nhave to be made outside. I now better understood the protests of Herr\r\nSchenkel to Grimm. Never once had we seen a lighter in tow in the open\r\nsea, though plenty behind the barrier of islands; indeed it was the\r\nvery existence of the sheltered byways that created such traffic as\r\nthere was. It was only Grimm\xe2\x80\x99s _m\xc3\xa9tier_ and the incubus of the lighter\r\nthat had suggested Memmert as our destination at all, and I began to\r\ndoubt it now. That tricky hoop of sand had befooled us before.\r\n\r\nAt this moment, and as if to corroborate my thought, the telegraph rang\r\nand the tug slowed down. I effaced myself and heard Grimm shouting to\r\nthe man on the lighter to starboard his helm, and to the look-out to\r\ncome aft. The next order froze my very marrow; it was \xe2\x80\x9clower away\xe2\x80\x9d.\r\nSomeone was at the davits of my boat fingering the tackles; the forward\r\nfall-rope actually slipped in the block and tilted the boat a fraction.\r\nI was just wondering how far it was to swim to Langeoog, when a strong,\r\nimperious voice (unknown to me) rang out, \xe2\x80\x9cNo, no! We don\xe2\x80\x99t want the\r\nboat. The swell\xe2\x80\x99s nothing; we can jump! Can\xe2\x80\x99t we, B\xc3\xb6hme?\xe2\x80\x9d The speaker\r\nended with a jovial laugh. \xe2\x80\x9cMercy!\xe2\x80\x9d thought I, \xe2\x80\x9care _they_ going to\r\nswim to Langeoog?\xe2\x80\x9d but I also gasped for relief. The tug rolled\r\nlifelessly in the swell for a little, and footsteps retreated aft.\r\nThere were cries of \xe2\x80\x9cAchtung!\xe2\x80\x9d and some laughter, one big bump and a\r\ngood deal of grinding; and on we moved again, taking the strain of the\r\ntow-rope gingerly, and then full-speed ahead. The passengers, it\r\nseemed, preferred the lighter to the tug for cruising in; coal-dust and\r\nexposure to clean planks and a warm cuddy. When silence reigned again I\r\npeeped out. Grimm was at the wheel still, impassively twirling the\r\nspokes, with a glance over his shoulder at his precious freight. And,\r\nafter all, we _were_ going outside.\r\n\r\nClose on the port hand lay a black foam-girt shape, the east spit of\r\nBaltrum. It fused with the night, while we swung slowly round to\r\nwindward over the troubled bar. Now we were in the spacious deeps of\r\nthe North Sea; and feeling it too in increase of swell and volleys of\r\nspray.\r\n\r\nAt this point evolutions began. Grimm gave the wheel up to the\r\nlook-out, and himself went to the taffrail, whence he roared back\r\norders of \xe2\x80\x9cPort!\xe2\x80\x9d or \xe2\x80\x9cStarboard!\xe2\x80\x9d in response to signals from the\r\nlighter. We made one complete circle, steering on each point of the\r\nwind in succession, after that worked straight out to sea till the\r\nwater was a good deal rougher, and back again at a tangent, till in\r\nearshot of the surf on the island beach. There the man\xc5\x93uvres, which\r\nwere clearly in the nature of a trial trip, ended; and we hove to, to\r\ntransship our passengers. They, when they came aboard, went straight\r\nbelow, and Grimm, having steadied the tug on a settled course and\r\nentrusted the wheel to the sailor again, stripped off his dripping\r\noilskin coat, threw it down on the cabin skylight, and followed them.\r\nThe course he had set was about west, with Norderney light a couple of\r\npoints off the port bow. The course for Memmert? Possibly; but I cared\r\nnot, for my mind was far from Memmert to-night. _It was the course for\r\nEngland too._ Yes, I understood at last. I was assisting at an\r\nexperimental rehearsal of a great scene, to be enacted, perhaps, in the\r\nnear future\xe2\x80\x94a scene when multitudes of seagoing lighters, carrying full\r\nloads of soldiers, not half loads of coals, should issue\r\nsimultaneously, in seven ordered fleets, from seven shallow outlets,\r\nand, under escort of the Imperial Navy, traverse the North Sea and\r\nthrow themselves bodily upon English shores.\r\n\r\nIndulgent reader, you may be pleased to say that I have been very\r\nobtuse; and yet, with humility, I protest against that verdict.\r\nRemember that, recent as are the events I am describing, it is only\r\nsince they happened that the possibility of an invasion of England by\r\nGermany has become a topic of public discussion. Davies and I had\r\nnever\xe2\x80\x94I was going to say had never considered it; but that would not be\r\naccurate, for we had glanced at it once or twice; and if any single\r\nincident in his or our joint cruise had provided a semblance of\r\nconfirmation, he, at any rate, would have kindled to that spark. But\r\nyou will see how perversely from first to last circumstances drove us\r\ndeeper and deeper into the wrong groove, till the idea became\r\ninveterate that the secret we were seeking was one of defence and not\r\noffence. Hence a complete mental somersault was required, and, as an\r\namateur, I found it difficult; the more so that the method of invasion,\r\nas I darkly comprehended it now, was of such a strange and\r\nunprecedented character; for orthodox invasions start from big ports\r\nand involve a fleet of ocean transports, while none of our clues\r\npointed that way. To neglect obvious methods, to draw on the obscure\r\nresources of an obscure strip of coast, to improve and exploit a\r\nquantity of insignificant streams and tidal outlets, and thence,\r\nscreened by the islands, to despatch an armada of light-draught barges,\r\ncapable of flinging themselves on a correspondingly obscure and\r\ntherefore unexpected portion of the enemy\xe2\x80\x99s coast; that was a\r\nconception so daring, aye, and so quixotic in some of its aspects, that\r\neven now I was half incredulous. Yet it must be the true one. Bit by\r\nbit the fragments of the puzzle fell into order till a coherent whole\r\nwas adumbrated. [The reader will find the whole matter dealt with in\r\nthe Epilogue.]\r\n\r\nThe tug surged on into the night; a squall of rain leapt upon us and\r\nswept hissing astern. Baltrum vanished and the strands of Norderney\r\nbeamed under transient moonlight. Drunk with triumph, I cuddled in my\r\nrocking cradle and ransacked every unvisited chamber of the memory,\r\ntossing out their dusty contents, to make a joyous bonfire of some, and\r\nto see the residue take life and meaning in the light of the great\r\nrevelation.\r\n\r\nMy reverie was of things, not persons; of vast national issues rather\r\nthan of the poignant human interests so closely linked with them. But\r\non a sudden I was recalled, with a shock, to myself, Davies, and the\r\npresent.\r\n\r\nWe were changing our course, as I knew by variations in the whirl of\r\ndraughts which whistled about me. I heard Grimm afoot again, and,\r\nchoosing my moment, surveyed the scene. Broad on the port-beam were the\r\ngarish lights of Norderney town and promenade, and the tug, I\r\nperceived, was drawing in to enter the See Gat. _[See Chart B.]_\r\n\r\nRound she came, hustling through the broken water of the bar, till her\r\nnose was south and the wind was on the starboard bow. Not a mile from\r\nme were the villa and the yacht, and the three persons of the\r\ndrama\xe2\x80\x94three, that is, if Davies were safe.\r\n\r\nWere we to land at Norderney harbour? Heavens, what a magnificent\r\nclimax!\xe2\x80\x94if only I could rise to it. My work here was done. At a stroke\r\nto rejoin Davies and be free to consummate our designs!\r\n\r\nA desperate idea of cutting the davit-tackles\xe2\x80\x94I blush to think of the\r\nstupidity\xe2\x80\x94was rejected as soon as it was born, and instead, I\r\nendeavoured to imagine our approach to the pier. My boat hung on the\r\nstarboard side; that would be the side away from the quay, and the tide\r\nwould be low. I could swarm down the davits during the stir of arrival,\r\ndrop into the sea and swim the few yards across the dredged-out\r\nchannel, wade through the mud to within a short distance of the\r\n_Dulcibella_, and swim the rest. I rubbed the salt out of my eyes and\r\nwriggled my cramped legs.... Hullo! why was Grimm leaving the helm\r\nagain? Back he went to the cabin, leaving the sailor at the helm.... We\r\nought to be turning to port now; but no\xe2\x80\x94on we went, south, for the\r\nmainland.\r\n\r\nThough one plan was frustrated, the longing to get to Davies, once\r\nimplanted, waxed apace.\r\n\r\nOur destination was at last beyond dispute. _[See Chart.]_ The channel\r\nwe were in was the same that we had cut across on our blind voyage to\r\nMemmert, and the same my ferry-steamer had followed two days ago. It\r\nwas a _cul-de-sac_ leading to one place only, the landing stage at\r\nNorddeich. The only place on the whole coast, now I came to think of\r\nit, where the tug could land at this tide. There the quay would be on\r\nthe starboard side, and I saw myself tied to my eyrie while the\r\npassengers landed and the tug and lighter turned back for Memmert; at\r\nMemmert, dawn, and discovery.\r\n\r\nThere was some way out\xe2\x80\x94some way out, I repeated to myself; some way to\r\nreap the fruit of Davies\xe2\x80\x99s long tutelage in the lore of this strange\r\nregion. What would _he_ do?\r\n\r\nFor answer there came the familiar _frou-frou_ of gentle surf on drying\r\nsands. The swell was dying away, the channel narrowing; dusky and weird\r\non the starboard hand stretched leagues of new-risen sand. Two men only\r\nwere on deck; the moon was quenched under the vanguard clouds of a\r\nfresh squall.\r\n\r\nA madcap scheme danced before me. The time, I _must_ know the time!\r\nCrouching low and cloaking the flame with my jacket I struck a match;\r\n2.30 a.m.\xe2\x80\x94the tide had been ebbing for about three hours and a half.\r\nLow water about five; they would be aground till 7.30. Danger to life?\r\nNone. Flares and rescuers? Not likely, with \xe2\x80\x9chim who insists\xe2\x80\x9d on board;\r\nbesides, no one could come, there being no danger. I should have a fair\r\nwind and a fair tide for _my_ trip. Grimm\xe2\x80\x99s coat was on the skylight;\r\nwe were both clean-shaved.\r\n\r\nThe helmsman gazed ahead, intent on his difficult course, and the wind\r\nhowled to perfection. I knelt up and examined one of the davit-tackles.\r\nThere was nothing remarkable about it, a double and a single block\r\n(like our own peak halyards), the lower one hooked into a ring in the\r\nboat, the hauling part made fast to a cleat on the davit itself.\r\nSomething there must be to give lateral support or the boat would have\r\nracketed abroad in the roll outside. The support, I found, consisted of\r\ntwo lanyards spliced to the davits and rove through holes in the keel.\r\nThese I leaned over and cut with my pocket-knife; the result being a\r\nbarely perceptible swaying of the boat, for the tug was under the lee\r\nof sands and on an even keel. Then I left my hiding-place, climbing out\r\nof the stern sheets by the after-davit, and preparing every successive\r\nmotion with exquisite tenderness, till I stood on the deck. In another\r\nmoment I was at the cabin skylight, lifting Grimm\xe2\x80\x99s long oilskin coat.\r\n(A second\xe2\x80\x99s yielding to temptation here; but no, the skylight was\r\nground glass, fastened from below. So, on with the coat, up with the\r\ncollar, and forward to the wheel on tiptoe.) As soon as I was up to the\r\nengine-room skylight (that is to say, well ahead of the cabin roof) I\r\nassumed a natural step, went up to the pulpit and touched the helmsman\r\non the arm, as I had seen Grimm do. The man stepped aside, grunting\r\nsomething about a light, and I took the wheel from him. Grimm was a man\r\nof few words, so I just jogged his satellite, and pointed forward. He\r\nwent off like a lamb to his customary place in the bows, not having\r\ndreamt\xe2\x80\x94why should he?\xe2\x80\x94of examining me, but in him I had instantly\r\nrecognised one of the crew of the _Kormoran_.\r\n\r\nMy ruse developed in all its delicious simplicity. We were, I\r\nestimated, about half-way to Norddeich, in the Buse Tief, a channel of\r\na navigable breadth, at the utmost of two hundred yards at this period\r\nof the tide. Two faint lights, one above the other, twinkled far ahead.\r\nWhat they meant I neither knew nor cared, since the only use I put them\r\nto was to test the effect of the wheel, for this was the first time I\r\nhad ever tasted the sweets of command on a steamboat. A few cautious\r\nessays taught me the rudiments, and nothing could hinder the\r\ncatastrophe now.\r\n\r\nI edged over to starboard\xe2\x80\x94that was the side I had selected\xe2\x80\x94and again a\r\nlittle more, till the glistening back of the look-out gave a slight\r\nmovement; but he was a well-drilled minion, with implicit trust in the\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cold man\xe2\x80\x9d. Now, hard over! and spoke by spoke I gave her the full\r\npressure of the helm. The look-out shouted a warning, and I raised my\r\narm in calm acknowledgement. A cry came from the lighter, and I\r\nremember I was just thinking \xe2\x80\x9cWhat the Dickens\xe2\x80\x99ll happen to her?\xe2\x80\x9d when\r\nthe end came; a _euthanasia_ so mild and gradual (for the sands are\r\nfringed with mud) that the disaster was on us before I was aware of it.\r\nThere was just the tiniest premonitory shuddering as our keel clove the\r\nbuttery medium, a cascade of ripples from either beam, and the wheel\r\njammed to rigidity in my hands, as the tug nestled up to her\r\nresting-place.\r\n\r\nIn the scene of panic that followed, it is safe to say that I was the\r\nonly soul on board who acted with methodical tranquillity. The look-out\r\nflew astern like an arrow, bawling to the lighter. Grimm, with the\r\npassengers tumbling up after him, was on deck in an instant, storming\r\nand cursing; flung himself on the wheel which I had respectfully\r\nabandoned, jangled the telegraph, and wrenched at the spokes. The tug\r\nlisted over under the force of the tide; wind, darkness, and rain\r\naggravated the confusion.\r\n\r\nFor my part, I stepped back behind the smoke stack, threw off my robe\r\nof office, and made for the boat. Long and bitter experience of running\r\naground had told me that that was sure to be wanted. On the way I\r\ncannoned into one of the passengers and pressed him into my service;\r\nincidentally seeing his face, and verifying an old conjecture. It was\r\none who, in Germany, has a better right to insist than anyone else.\r\n\r\nAs we reached the davits there was a report like a pistol-shot from the\r\nport-side\xe2\x80\x94the tow-rope parting, I believe, as the lighter with her\r\nshallower draught swung on past the tug. Fresh tumult arose, in which I\r\nheard: \xe2\x80\x9cLower the boat,\xe2\x80\x9d from Grimm; but the order was already\r\nexecuted. My ally the Passenger and I had each cast off a tackle, and\r\nslacked away with a run; that done, I promptly clutched the wire guy to\r\nsteady myself, and tumbled in. (It was not far to tumble, for the tug\r\nlisted heavily to starboard; think of our course, and the set of the\r\nebb stream, and you will see why.) The forward fall unhooked sweetly;\r\nbut the after one lost play. \xe2\x80\x9cSlack away,\xe2\x80\x9d I called, peremptorily, and\r\nfelt for my knife. My helper above obeyed; the hook yielded; I filliped\r\naway the loose tackle, and the boat floated away.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXVIII.\r\nWe Achieve our Double Aim\r\n\r\n\r\nWhen, exactly, the atmosphere of misunderstanding on the stranded tug\r\nwas dissipated, I do not know, for by the time I had fitted the\r\nrowlocks and shipped sculls, tide and wind had caught me, and were\r\nsweeping me merrily back on the road to Norderney, whose lights\r\ntwinkled through the scud in the north. With my first few strokes I\r\nmade towards the lighter\xe2\x80\x94which I could see sagging helplessly to\r\nleeward\xe2\x80\x94but as soon as I thought I was out of sight of the tug, I\r\npulled round and worked out my own salvation. There was an outburst of\r\nshouting which soon died away. Full speed on a falling tide! They were\r\npinned there for five hours sure. It was impossible to miss the way,\r\nand with my stout allies heaving me forward, I made short work of the\r\ntwo-mile passage. There was a sharp tussle at the last, where the Riff\r\nGat poured its stream across my path, and then I was craning over my\r\nshoulder, God knows with what tense anxiety, for the low hull and taper\r\nmast of the _Dulcibella_. Not there! No, not where I had left her. I\r\npulled furiously up the harbour past a sleeping ferry-steamer\r\nand\xe2\x80\x94praise Heaven!\xe2\x80\x94came on her warped alongside the jetty.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho\xe2\x80\x99s that?\xe2\x80\x9d came from below, as I stepped on board.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHush! it\xe2\x80\x99s me.\xe2\x80\x9d And Davies and I were pawing one another in the dark\r\nof the cabin.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you all right, old chap?\xe2\x80\x9d said he.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; are you? A match! What\xe2\x80\x99s the time? Quick!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood Heavens, Carruthers, what the blazes have you done to yourself?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n(I suspect I cut a pretty figure after my two days\xe2\x80\x99 outing.)\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTen past three. It\xe2\x80\x99s the invasion of England! Is Dollmann at the\r\nvilla?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cInvasion?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs Dollmann at the villa?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs the _Medusa_ afloat?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, on the mud.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe devil! Are _we_ afloat?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think so still, but they made me shift.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThink! Track her out! Pole her out! Cut those warps!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFor a few strenuous minutes we toiled at the sweeps till the\r\n_Dulcibella_ was berthed ahead of the steamer, in deeper water.\r\nMeanwhile I had whispered a few facts.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow soon can you get under way?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTen minutes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen\xe2\x80\x99s daylight?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSunrise about seven, first dawn about five. Where are we bound?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHolland, or England.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre they invading it now?\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, calmly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, only rehearsing!\xe2\x80\x9d I laughed, wildly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen we can wait.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe can wait exactly an hour and a half. Come ashore and knock up\r\nDollmann; we must denounce him, and get them both aboard; it\xe2\x80\x99s now or\r\nnever. Holy Saints! man, not as you are!\xe2\x80\x9d (He was in pyjamas.) \xe2\x80\x9cSea\r\nclothes!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhile he put on Christian attire, I resumed my facts and sketched a\r\nplan. \xe2\x80\x9cAre you watched?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think so; by the _Kormoran_\xe2\x80\x99s men.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs the _Kormoran_ here?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe men?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot to-night. Grimm called for them in that tug. I was watching. And,\r\nCarruthers, the _Blitz_ is here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn the roads outside\xe2\x80\x94didn\xe2\x80\x99t you see her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWasn\xe2\x80\x99t looking. Her skipper\xe2\x80\x99s safe anyway; so\xe2\x80\x99s B\xc3\xb6hme, so\xe2\x80\x99s the\r\nTertium Quid, and so are the _Kormoran_\xe2\x80\x99s men. The coast\xe2\x80\x99s clear\xe2\x80\x94it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nnow or never.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOnce more we were traversing the long jetty and the silent streets,\r\nrain driving at our backs. We trod on air, I think; I remember no\r\nfatigue. Davies sometimes broke into a little run, muttering\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cscoundrel\xe2\x80\x9d to himself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was right\xe2\x80\x94only upside down,\xe2\x80\x9d he murmured more than once. \xe2\x80\x9cAlways\r\nreally right\xe2\x80\x94those channels are the key to the whole concern. Chatham,\r\nour only eastern base\xe2\x80\x94no North Sea base or squadron\xe2\x80\x94they\xe2\x80\x99d land at one\r\nof those God-forsaken flats off the Crouch and Blackwater.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt seems a wild scheme,\xe2\x80\x9d I observed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWild? In a way. So is _any_ invasion. But it\xe2\x80\x99s thorough; it\xe2\x80\x99s German.\r\nNo other country could do it. It\xe2\x80\x99s all dawning on me\xe2\x80\x94by Jove! It will\r\nbe at the _Wash_\xe2\x80\x94much the nearest, and as sandy as this side.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow\xe2\x80\x99s Dollmann been?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPolite, but queer and jumpy. It\xe2\x80\x99s too long a story.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cClara?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_She\xe2\x80\x99s_ all right. By Jove! Carruthers\xe2\x80\x94never mind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe found a night-bell at the villa door and rang it lustily. A window\r\naloft opened, and \xe2\x80\x9cA message from Commander von Br\xc3\xbcning\xe2\x80\x94urgent,\xe2\x80\x9d I\r\ncalled up.\r\n\r\nThe window shut, and soon after the hall was lighted and the door\r\nopened by Dollmann in a dressing-gown.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood morning, Lieutenant X\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, in English. \xe2\x80\x9cStop, we\xe2\x80\x99re\r\nfriends, you fool!\xe2\x80\x9d as the door was flung nearly to. It opened very\r\nslowly again, and we walked in.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSilence!\xe2\x80\x9d he hissed. The sweat stood on his steep forehead and a\r\nhectic flush on either cheek, but there was a smile\xe2\x80\x94what a smile!\xe2\x80\x94on\r\nhis lips. Motioning us to tread noiselessly (a vain ideal for me), he\r\nled the way to the sitting-room we knew, switched on the light, and\r\nfaced us.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell?\xe2\x80\x9d he said, in English, still smiling.\r\n\r\nI consulted my watch, and I may say that if my hand was an index to my\r\ngeneral appearance, I must have looked the most abject ruffian under\r\nheaven.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe probably understand one another,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, \xe2\x80\x9cand to explain is to\r\nlose time. We sail for Holland, or perhaps England, at five at the\r\nlatest, and we want the pleasure of your company. We promise you\r\nimmunity\xe2\x80\x94on certain conditions, which can wait. We have only two\r\nberths, so that we can only accommodate Miss Clara besides yourself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nHe smiled on through this terse harangue, but the smile froze, as\r\nthough beneath it raged some crucial debate. Suddenly he laughed (a\r\nlow, ironical laugh).\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou fools,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cyou confounded meddlesome young idiots; I\r\nthought I had done with you. Promise me immunity? Give me till five? By\r\nGod, I\xe2\x80\x99ll give you five minutes to be off to England and be damned to\r\nyou, or else to be locked up for spies! What the devil do you take me\r\nfor?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA traitor in German service,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, none too firmly. We were\r\nboth taken aback by this slashing attack.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA tr\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94? You pig-headed young marplots! I\xe2\x80\x99m in _British_ service!\r\nYou\xe2\x80\x99re wrecking the work of years\xe2\x80\x94and on the very threshold of\r\nsuccess.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFor an instant Davies and I looked at one another in stupefaction. He\r\nlied\xe2\x80\x94I could swear he lied; but how make sure?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy did you try to wreck Davies?\xe2\x80\x9d said I, mechanically.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPshaw! They made me clear him out. I knew he was safe, and safe he\r\nis.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was only one thing for it\xe2\x80\x94a last finesse, to put him to the\r\nproof.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, after a moment or two, \xe2\x80\x9cwe\xe2\x80\x99ll clear out\xe2\x80\x94silence,\r\nDavies!\xe2\x80\x94as it appears we have acted in error; but it\xe2\x80\x99s right to tell\r\nyou that we know everything.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot so loud, curse you! What do you know?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was taking notes at Memmert the other night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cImpossible!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThanks to Davies. Under difficulties, of course, but I heard quite\r\nenough. You were reporting your English tour\xe2\x80\x94Chatham, you know, and the\r\nEnglish scheme of attack, a mythical one, no doubt, as you\xe2\x80\x99re on the\r\nright side! B\xc3\xb6hme and the rest were dealing with the German scheme of\r\ndefence A to G\xe2\x80\x94I heard it all\xe2\x80\x94the seven islands and the seven channels\r\nbetween them (Davies knows every one of them by heart); and then on\r\nland, the ring of railway, Esens the centre, the army corps to mobilise\r\nand entrench\xe2\x80\x94all nugatory, wasted, ha! ha!\xe2\x80\x94as you\xe2\x80\x99re on the right s\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot so loud, you fiend of mischief!\xe2\x80\x9d He turned his back, and made an\r\nirresolute pace or two towards the door, his hands kneading the folds\r\nof his dressing-gown as they had kneaded the curtain at Memmert. Twice\r\nhe began a question and twice broke off. \xe2\x80\x9cI congratulate you,\r\ngentlemen,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, finally, and with more composure, facing us again,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cyou have done marvels in your misplaced zeal; but you have compromised\r\nme too much already. I shall have to have you arrested\xe2\x80\x94purely for\r\nform\xe2\x80\x99s sake\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you,\xe2\x80\x9d I broke in. \xe2\x80\x9cWe have wasted five minutes, and time\r\npresses. We sail at five, and\xe2\x80\x94purely for form\xe2\x80\x99s sake\xe2\x80\x94would rather have\r\nyou with us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you mean?\xe2\x80\x9d he snarled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI had the advantage of _you_ at Memmert, in spite of acoustic\r\nobstacles. Your friends made an appointment behind your back, and I, in\r\nmy misplaced zeal, have taken some trouble to attend it; so that I\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\nhad a working demonstration on another matter, the invasion of England\r\nfrom the seven _siels_.\xe2\x80\x9d (Davies nudged me.) \xe2\x80\x9cNo, I should let that\r\npistol alone; and no, I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t ring the bell. You can arrest us if\r\nyou like, but the secret\xe2\x80\x99s in safe hands.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou lie!\xe2\x80\x9d He was right there; but he could not know it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you suppose I haven\xe2\x80\x99t taken that precaution? But no names are\r\nmentioned.\xe2\x80\x9d He gave a sort of groan, sank into a chair, and seemed to\r\nage and grizzle before our very eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat did you say about immunity, and Clara?\xe2\x80\x9d he muttered. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99re\r\nfriends\xe2\x80\x94we\xe2\x80\x99re friends!\xe2\x80\x9d burst out Davies, with a gulp in his voice. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\r\nwant to help you both.\xe2\x80\x9d (Through a sudden mist that filmed my eyes I\r\nsaw him impetuously walk over and lay his hand on the other\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nshoulder.) \xe2\x80\x9cThose chaps are on our track and yours. Come with us. Wake\r\nher, tell her. It\xe2\x80\x99ll be too late soon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nX\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94 shrank from his touch. \xe2\x80\x9cTell her? I can\xe2\x80\x99t tell her. You tell her,\r\nboy.\xe2\x80\x9d He was huddling back into his chair. Davies turned to me.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere\xe2\x80\x99s her room?\xe2\x80\x9d I said, sharply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAbove this one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo up, Carruthers,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot I\xe2\x80\x94I shall frighten her into a fit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t like to.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNonsense, man! We\xe2\x80\x99ll both go then.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t make a noise,\xe2\x80\x9d said a dazed voice. We left that huddled figure\r\nand stole upstairs\xe2\x80\x94thickly carpeted stairs, luckily. The door we wanted\r\nwas half open, and the room behind it lighted. On the threshold stood a\r\nslim white figure, bare-footed; bare-throated.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it, father?\xe2\x80\x9d she called in a whisper. \xe2\x80\x9cWhom have you been\r\ntalking to?\xe2\x80\x9d I pushed Davies forward, but he hung back.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHush, don\xe2\x80\x99t be frightened,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, \xe2\x80\x9cit\xe2\x80\x99s I, Carruthers, and\r\nDavies\xe2\x80\x94and Davies. May we come in, just for one moment?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI gently widened the opening of the door, while she stepped back and\r\nput one hand to her throat.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease come to your father,\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9cWe are going to take you both to\r\nEngland in the _Dulcibella_\xe2\x80\x94now, at once.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe had heard me, but her eyes wandered to Davies.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI understand not,\xe2\x80\x9d she faltered, trembling and cowering in such\r\ntouching bewilderment that I could not bear to look at her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor God\xe2\x80\x99s sake, say something, Davies,\xe2\x80\x9d I muttered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cClara!\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, \xe2\x80\x9cwill you not trust us?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI heard a little gasp from her. There was a flutter of lace and cambric\r\nand she was in his arms, sobbing like a tired child, her little white\r\nfeet between his great clumsy sea-boots\xe2\x80\x94her rose-brown cheek on his\r\nrough jersey.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s past four, old chap,\xe2\x80\x9d I remarked, brutally. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m going down to\r\nhim again. No packing to speak of, mind. They must be out of this in\r\nhalf an hour.\xe2\x80\x9d I stumbled awkwardly on the stairs (again that tiresome\r\nfilm!) and found him stuffing some papers pell-mell into the stove.\r\nThere were only slumbering embers in it, but he did not seem to notice\r\nthat. \xe2\x80\x9cYou must be dressed in half an hour,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, furtively\r\npocketing a pistol which lay on the table.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you told her? Take her to England, you two boys. I think I\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nstay.\xe2\x80\x9d He sank into a chair again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNonsense, she won\xe2\x80\x99t go without you. You must, for her sake\xe2\x80\x94in half an\r\nhour, too.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI prefer to pass that half-hour lightly over. Davies left before me to\r\nprepare the yacht for sea, and I had to bear the brunt of what\r\nfollowed, including (as a mere episode) a scene with the step-mother,\r\nthe memory of which rankles in me yet. After all, she was a sensible\r\nwoman.\r\n\r\nAs for the other two, the girl when I saw her next, in her short\r\nboating skirt and tam-o\xe2\x80\x99-shanter, was a miracle of coolness and pluck.\r\nBut for her I should never have got him away. And ah! how good it was\r\nto be out in the wholesome rain again, hurrying to the harbour with my\r\ntwo charges, hurrying them down the greasy ladder to that frail atom of\r\nEnglish soil, their first guerdon of home and safety.\r\n\r\nOur flight from the harbour was unmolested, unnoticed. Only the first\r\nghastly evidences of dawn were mingling with the strangled moonlight,\r\nas we tacked round the pier-head and headed close-reefed down the Riff\r\nGat on the lees of the ebb-tide. We had to pass under the very quarter\r\nof the _Blitz_, so Davies said; for, of course, he alone was on deck\r\ntill we reached the open sea. Day was breaking then. It was dead low\r\nwater, and, far away to the south, between dun swathes of sand, I\r\nthought I saw\xe2\x80\x94but probably it was only a fancy\xe2\x80\x94two black stranded\r\nspecks. Rail awash, and decks streaming, we took the outer swell and\r\nclawed close-hauled under the lee of Juist, westward, hurrying\r\nwestward.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUp the Ems on the flood, and to Dutch Delfzyl,\xe2\x80\x9d I urged. No, thought\r\nDavies; it was too near Germany, and there was a tidal cut through from\r\nBuse Tief. Better to dodge in behind Rottum Island. So on we pressed,\r\npast Memmert, over the Juister Reef and the _Corinne_\xe2\x80\x99s buried\r\nmillions, across the two broad and yeasty mouths of the Ems, till\r\nRottum, a wee lonesome wafer of an islet, the first of the Dutch\r\narchipelago, was close on the weather-bow.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe must get in behind that,\xe2\x80\x9d said Davies, \xe2\x80\x9cthen we shall be safe; I\r\nthink I know the way, but get the next chart; and then take a rest, old\r\nchap. Clara and I can manage.\xe2\x80\x9d (She had been on deck most of the time,\r\nas capable a hand as you could wish for, better far than I in my\r\npresent state of exhaustion.) I crawled along the slippery sloping\r\nplanks and went below.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere are we?\xe2\x80\x9d cried Dollmann, starting up from the lee sofa, where he\r\nseemed to have been lying in a sort of trance. A book, his own book,\r\nslipped from his knees, and I saw the frontispiece lying on the floor\r\nin a pool of oil; for the stove had gone adrift, and the saloon was in\r\na wretched state of squalor and litter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOff Rottum,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, and knelt up to find the chart. There was a look\r\nin his eyes that I suppose I ought to have understood, but I can\r\nscarcely blame myself, for the accumulated strain, not only of the last\r\nthree days and nights, but of the whole arduous month of my cruise with\r\nDavies, was beginning to tell on me, now that safety and success were\r\nat hand. I handed up the chart through the companion, and then crept\r\ninto the reeling fo\xe2\x80\x99c\xe2\x80\x99sle and lay down on the spare sail-bags, with the\r\nthunder and thump of the seas around and above me.\r\n\r\nI must quote Davies for the event that happened now; for by the time I\r\nhad responded to the alarm and climbed up through the fore-hatch, the\r\nwhole tragedy was over and done with.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cX\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94 came up the companion,\xe2\x80\x9d he says, \xe2\x80\x9csoon after you went down. He\r\nheld on by the runner, and stared to windward at Rottum, as though he\r\nknew the place quite well. And then he came towards us, moving so\r\nunsteadily that I gave Clara the tiller, and went to help him. I tried\r\nto make him go down again, but he wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t, and came aft.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Give me the helm,\xe2\x80\x99 he said, half to himself. \xe2\x80\x98Sea\xe2\x80\x99s too bad\r\noutside\xe2\x80\x94there\xe2\x80\x99s a short cut here.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Thanks,\xe2\x80\x99 I said, \xe2\x80\x98I know this one.\xe2\x80\x99 (I don\xe2\x80\x99t think I meant to be\r\nsarcastic.) He said nothing, and settled himself on the counter behind\r\nus, safe enough, with his feet against the lee-rail, and then, to my\r\nastonishment, began to talk over my shoulder jolly sensibly about the\r\ncourse, pointing out a buoy which is wrong on the chart (as I knew),\r\nand telling me it was wrong, and so on. Well, we came to the bar of the\r\nSchild, and had to turn south for that twisty bit of beating between\r\nRottum and Bosch Flat. Clara was at the jib-sheet, I had the chart and\r\nthe tiller (you know how absent I get like that); there was a bobble of\r\nsea, and we both had heaps to do, and\xe2\x80\x94well\xe2\x80\x94I happened to look round,\r\nand he was gone. He hadn\xe2\x80\x99t spoken for a minute or two, but I believe\r\nthe last thing I heard him say (I was hardly attending at the time, for\r\nwe were in the thick of it) was something about a \xe2\x80\x98short cut\xe2\x80\x99 again. He\r\nmust have slipped over quietly.... He had an ulster and big boots on.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe cruised about for a time, but never found him.\r\n\r\nThat evening, after threading the maze of shoals between the Dutch\r\nmainland and islands, we anchored off the little hamlet of Ostmahorn,\r\n_[See Map A]_ gave the yacht in charge of some astonished fishermen,\r\nand thence by road and rail, hurrying still, gained Harlingen, and took\r\npassage on a steamer to London. From that point our personal history is\r\nof no concern to the outside world, and here, therefore, I bring this\r\nnarrative to an end.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nEpilogue\r\n\r\nBY THE EDITOR\r\n\r\n\r\n[For this chapter see Map A.]\r\n\r\nAn interesting document, somewhat damaged by fire, lies on my study\r\ntable.\r\n\r\nIt is a copy (in cipher) of a confidential memorandum to the German\r\nGovernment embodying a scheme for the invasion of England by Germany.\r\nIt is unsigned, but internal evidence, and the fact that it was taken\r\nby Mr \xe2\x80\x9cCarruthers\xe2\x80\x9d from the stove of the villa at Norderney, leave no\r\ndoubt as to its authorship. For many reasons it is out of the question\r\nto print the textual translation of it, as deciphered; but I propose to\r\ngive an outline of its contents.\r\n\r\nEven this must strain discretion to its uttermost limits, and had I\r\nonly to consider the instructed few who follow the trend of\r\nprofessional opinion on such subjects, I should leave the foregoing\r\nnarrative to speak for itself. But, as was stated in the preface, our\r\nprimary purpose is to reach everyone; and there may be many who, in\r\nspite of able and authoritative warnings frequently uttered since these\r\nevents occurred, are still prone to treat the German danger as an idle\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cbogey\xe2\x80\x9d, and may be disposed, in this case, to imagine that a baseless\r\nromance has been foisted on them.\r\n\r\nA few persons (English as well as German) hold that Germany is strong\r\nenough now to meet us single-handed, and throw an army on our shores.\r\nThe memorandum rejects this view, deferring isolated action for at\r\nleast a decade; and supposing, for present purposes, a coalition of\r\nthree Powers against Great Britain. And subsequent researches through\r\nthe usual channels place it beyond dispute that this condition was\r\nrelied on by the German Government in adopting the scheme. They\r\nrealised that even if, owing to our widely scattered forces, they\r\ngained that temporary command of the North Sea which would be essential\r\nfor a successful landing, they would inevitably lose it when our\r\nstanding fleets were concentrated and our reserve ships mobilised. With\r\nits sea-communications cut, the prospects of the invading army would be\r\ntoo dubious. I state it in that mild way, for it seems not to have been\r\nheld that failure was absolutely certain; and rightly, I think, in\r\nspite of the dogmas of the strategists\xe2\x80\x94for the ease transcends all\r\nexperience. No man can calculate the effect on our delicate economic\r\nfabric of a well-timed, well-planned blow at the industrial heart of\r\nthe kingdom, the great northern and midland towns, with their teeming\r\npopulations of peaceful wage-earners. In this instance, however, joint\r\naction (the occasion for which is perhaps not difficult to guess) was\r\ndistinctly contemplated, and Germany\xe2\x80\x99s _r\xc3\xb4le_ in the coalition was\r\nexclusively that of invader. Her fleet was to be kept intact, and she\r\nherself to remain ostensibly neutral until the first shock was over,\r\nand our own battle-fleets either beaten, or, the much more likely\r\nevent, so crippled by a hard-won victory as to be incapable of\r\nwithstanding compact and unscathed forces. Then, holding the balance of\r\npower, she would strike. And the blow? It was not till I read this\r\nmemorandum that I grasped the full merits of that daring scheme, under\r\nwhich every advantage, moral, material, and geographical, possessed by\r\nGermany, is utilised to the utmost, and every disadvantage of our own\r\nturned to account against us.\r\n\r\nTwo root principles pervade it: perfect organisation; perfect secrecy.\r\nUnder the first head come some general considerations. The writer (who\r\nis intimately conversant with conditions on both sides of the North\r\nSea) argued that Germany is pre-eminently fitted to undertake an\r\ninvasion of Great Britain. She has a great army (a mere fraction of\r\nwhich would suffice) in a state of high efficiency, but a useless\r\nweapon, as against us, unless transported over seas. She has a peculiar\r\ngenius for organisation, not only in elaborating minute detail, but in\r\nthe grasp of a coherent whole. She knows the art of giving a brain to a\r\nmachine, of transmitting power to the uttermost cog-wheel, and at the\r\nsame time of concentrating responsibility in a supreme centre. She has\r\na small navy, but very effective for its purpose, built, trained, and\r\nmanned on methodical principles, for defined ends, and backed by an\r\ninexhaustible reserve of men from her maritime conscription. She\r\nstudies and practises co-operation between her army and navy. Her hands\r\nare free for offence in home waters, since she has no distant network\r\nof coveted colonies and dependencies on which to dissipate her\r\ndefensive energies. Finally, she is, compared with ourselves,\r\neconomically independent, having commercial access through her land\r\nfrontiers to the whole of Europe. She has little to lose and much to\r\ngain.\r\n\r\nThe writer pauses here to contrast our own situation, and I summarise\r\nhis points. We have a small army, dispersed over the whole globe, and\r\nadministered on a gravely defective system. We have no settled theory\r\nof national defence, and no competent authority whose business it is to\r\ngive us one. The matter is still at the stage of civilian controversy.\r\nCo-operation between the army and navy is not studied and practised;\r\nmuch less do there exist any plans, worthy of the name, for the repulse\r\nof an invasion, or any readiness worth considering for the prompt\r\nequipment and direction of our home forces to meet a sudden emergency.\r\nWe have a great and, in many respects, a magnificent navy, but not\r\ngreat enough for the interests it insures, and with equally defective\r\ninstitutions; not built or manned methodically, having an utterly\r\ninadequate reserve of men, all classes of which would be absorbed at\r\nthe very outset, without a vestige of preparation for the enrolment of\r\nvolunteers; distracted by the multiplicity of its functions in guarding\r\nour colossal empire and commerce, and conspicuously lacking a brain,\r\nnot merely for the smooth control of its own unwieldy mechanism, but\r\nfor the study of rival aims and systems. We have no North Sea naval\r\nbase, no North Sea Fleet, and no North Sea policy. Lastly, we stand in\r\na highly dangerous economical position.\r\n\r\nThe writer then deals with the method of invasion, and rejects the\r\nobvious one at once, that of sending forth a fleet of transports from\r\none or more of the North Sea ports. He combats especially the idea of\r\nmaking Emden (the nearest to our shores) the port of departure. I\r\nmention this because, since his own scheme was adopted, it is\r\ninstructive to note that Emden had been used (with caution) as a red\r\nherring by the inspired German press, when the subject was mentioned at\r\nall, and industriously dragged across the trail. His objections to the\r\nNorth Sea ports apply, he remarks, in reality to all schemes of\r\ninvasion, whether the conditions be favourable or not. One is that\r\nsecrecy is rendered impossible\xe2\x80\x94and secrecy is vital. The collection of\r\nthe transports would be known in England weeks before the hour was ripe\r\nfor striking; for all large ports are cosmopolitan and swarm with\r\npotential spies. In Germany\xe2\x80\x99s case, moreover, suitable ships are none\r\ntoo plentiful, and the number required would entail a large deduction\r\nfrom her mercantile marine. The other reason concerns the actual\r\nlanding. This must take place on an open part of the east coast of\r\nEngland. No other objective is even considered. Now the difficulty of\r\ntransshipping and landing troops by boats from transports anchored in\r\ndeep water, in a safe, swift, and orderly fashion, on an open beach, is\r\nenormous. The most hastily improvised resistance might cause a\r\nhumiliating disaster. Yet the first stage is the most important of all.\r\nIt is imperative that the invaders should seize and promptly intrench a\r\npre-arranged line of country, to serve as an initial base. This once\r\ndone, they can use other resources; they can bring up transports, land\r\ncavalry and heavy guns, pour in stores, and advance. But unless this is\r\ndone, they are impotent, be their sea-communications never so secure.\r\n\r\nThe only logical alternative is then propounded: to despatch an army of\r\ninfantry with the lightest type of field-guns in big sea-going\r\nlighters, towed by powerful but shallow-draught tugs, under escort of a\r\npowerful composite squadron of warships; and to fling the flotilla, at\r\nhigh tide, if possible, straight upon the shore.\r\n\r\nSuch an expedition could be prepared in absolute secrecy, by turning to\r\naccount the natural features of the German coast. No great port was to\r\nbe concerned in any way. All that was required was sufficient depth of\r\nwater to float the lighters and tugs; and this is supplied by seven\r\ninsignificant streams, issuing from the Frisian littoral, and already\r\nfurnished with small harbours and sluice-gates, with one exception,\r\nnamely, the tidal creek at Norden; for this, it appeared, was one of\r\nthe chosen seven, and not, as \xe2\x80\x9cCarruthers\xe2\x80\x9d supposed, Hilgenriedersiel,\r\nwhich, if you remember, he had no time to visit, and which has, in\r\nfact, no stream of any value at all, and no harbour. All of these\r\nstreams would have to be improved, deepened, and generally canalised;\r\nostensibly with a commercial end, for purposes of traffic with the\r\nislands, which are growing health resorts during a limited summer\r\nseason.\r\n\r\nThe whole expedition would be organised under seven distinct\r\nsub-divisions\xe2\x80\x94not too great a number in view of its cumbrous character.\r\nSeawards, the whole of the coast is veiled by the fringe of islands and\r\nthe zone of shoals. Landwards, the loop of railway round the Frisian\r\npeninsula would form the line of communication in rear of the seven\r\nstreams. Esens was to be the local centre of administration when the\r\nscheme grew to maturity, but not till then. Every detail for the\r\nmovement of troops under the seven different heads was to be arranged\r\nfor with secrecy and exactitude many months in advance, and from\r\nheadquarters at Berlin. It was not expected that nothing would leak\r\nout, but care was to be taken that anything that did do so should be\r\nattributed to defensive measures\xe2\x80\x94a standing feature in German\r\nmobilisation being the establishment of a corps of observation along\r\nthe Frisian coast; in fact, the same machinery was to be used, and its\r\nconversion for offence concealed up to the latest possible moment. The\r\nsame precautions were to be taken in the preliminary work on the spot.\r\nThere, four men only (it was calculated) need be in full possession of\r\nthe secret. One was to represent the Imperial Navy (a post filled by\r\nour friend von Br\xc3\xbcning). Another (B\xc3\xb6hme) was to superintend the six\r\ncanals and the construction of the lighters. The functions of the third\r\nwere twofold. He was to organise what I may call the local labour\xe2\x80\x94that\r\nis, the helpers required for embarkation, the crews of the tugs, and,\r\nmost important of all, the service of pilots for the navigation of the\r\nseven flotillas through the corresponding channels to the open sea. He\r\nmust be a local man, thoroughly acquainted with the coast, of a social\r\nstanding not much above the average of villagers and fishermen, and he\r\nmust be ready when the time was ripe with lists of the right men for\r\nthe right duties, lists to which the conscription authorities could\r\nwhen required, give instant legal effect. His other function was to\r\npolice the coast for spies, and to report anything suspicious to von\r\nBr\xc3\xbcning, who would never be far away. On the whole I think that they\r\nfound the grim Grimm a jewel for their purpose.\r\n\r\nAs fourth personage, the writer designates himself, the promoter of the\r\nscheme, the indispensable link between the two nations. He undertakes\r\nto furnish reliable information as to the disposition of troops in\r\nEngland, as to the hydrography of the coast selected for the landing,\r\nas to the supplies available in its vicinity, and the strategic points\r\nto be seized. He proposes to be guide-in-chief to the expedition during\r\ntransit. And in the meantime (when not otherwise employed) he was to\r\nreside at Norderney, in close touch with the other three, and\r\ncontrolling the commercial undertakings which were to throw dust in the\r\neyes of the curious. [Memmert, by the way, is not mentioned in this\r\nmemorandum.]\r\n\r\nHe speaks of the place \xe2\x80\x9cselected for the landing\xe2\x80\x9d, and proceeds to\r\nconsider this question in detail. I cannot follow him in his review,\r\ndeeply interesting though it is, and shall say at once that he reduces\r\npossible landing-places to two, the flats on the Essex coast between\r\nFoulness and Brightlingsea, and the Wash\xe2\x80\x94with a decided preference for\r\nthe latter. Assuming that the enemy, if they got wind of an invasion at\r\nall, would expect transports to be employed, he chooses the sort of\r\nspot which they would be least likely to defend, and which,\r\nnevertheless, was suitable to the character of the flotillas, and\r\nsimilar to the region they started from. There is such a spot on the\r\nLincolnshire coast, on the north side of the Wash, _[See Map A]_ known\r\nas East Holland. It is low-lying land, dyked against the sea, and\r\nbordered like Frisia with sand-flats which dry off at low water. It is\r\neasy of access from the east, by way of Boston Deeps, a deep-water\r\nchannel formed by a detached bank, called the Long Sand, lying parallel\r\nto the shore for ten miles. This bank makes a natural breakwater\r\nagainst the swell from the east (the only quarter to be feared); and\r\nthe Deeps behind it, where there is an average depth of thirty-four\r\nfeet at low-water, would form an excellent roadstead for the covering\r\nsquadron, whose guns would command the shore within easy range. It is\r\nnoted in passing that this is just the case where German first-class\r\nbattleships would have an advantage over British ships of the same\r\ncalibre. The latter are of just too heavy a draught to navigate such\r\nwaters without peril, if, indeed, they could enter this roadstead at\r\nall, for there is a bar at the mouth of it with only thirty-one feet at\r\nhigh water, spring tides. The former, built as they were with a view to\r\nman\xc5\x93uvring in the North Sea, are just within the margin of safety. East\r\nHolland is within easy striking distance of the manufacturing\r\ndistricts, a vigorous raid on which is, the writer urges, the true\r\npolicy of an invader. He reports positively that there exist (in a\r\nproper military sense) no preparations whatever to meet such an attack.\r\nEast Holland is also the nearest point on the British shores to\r\nGermany, excepting the coast of Norfolk; much nearer, indeed, than the\r\nEssex flats alluded to, and reached by a simple deep-sea passage,\r\nwithout any dangerous region to navigate, like the mouth of the Channel\r\nand the estuary of the Thames from Harwich westwards. The distance is\r\n240 sea-miles, west by south roughly, from Borkum Island, and 280 from\r\nWangeroog. The time estimated for transit after the flotillas had been\r\nassembled outside the islands is from thirty to thirty-four hours.\r\n\r\nEmbarkation is the next topic. This could and must be effected in one\r\ntide. At the six _siels_ there was a mean period of two and a half\r\nhours in every twelve, during which the water was high enough. At\r\nNorden a rather longer time was available. But this should be amply\r\nsufficient if the machinery were in good working order and were\r\npunctually set in motion. High water occurs approximately at the same\r\ntime at all seven outlets, the difference between the two farthest\r\napart, Carolinensiel and Greetsiel, being only half an hour.\r\n\r\nLastly, the special risks attendant on such an expedition are\r\ndispassionately weighed. X\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94, though keenly anxious to recommend his\r\nscheme, writes in no blindly sanguine spirit. There are no modern\r\nprecedents for any invasion in the least degree comparable to that of\r\nEngland by Germany. Any such attempt will be a hazardous experiment.\r\nBut he argues that the advantages of his method outweigh the risks, and\r\nthat most of the risks themselves would attach equally to any other\r\nmethod. Whatever skill in prediction was used, bad weather might\r\novertake the expedition. Yes; but if transports were used transhipment\r\ninto boats for landing would in bad weather be fraught with the same\r\nand a greater peril. But transports could stand off and wait. Delay is\r\nfatal in any case; unswerving promptitude is the essence of such an\r\nenterprise. The lighters would be in danger of foundering? Beside the\r\npoint; if the end is worth gaining the risks must be faced. Soldiers\xe2\x80\x99\r\nlives are sacrificed in tens of thousands on battlefields. The flotilla\r\nwould be demoralised during transit by the assault of a few\r\ntorpedo-boats? Granted; but the same would apply to a fleet of\r\ntransports, with the added certainty that one lucky shot would send to\r\nthe bottom ten times the number of soldiers, with less hope of rescue.\r\nIn both cases reliance must be placed on the efficiency and vigilance\r\nof the escort. It is admitted, however, in a passage which might well\r\nmake my two adventurers glow with triumph, that if by any mischance the\r\nBritish discovered what was afoot in good time, and were able to send\r\nover a swarm of light-draught boats, which could elude the German\r\nwarships and get amongst the flotillas while they were still in process\r\nof leaving the siels; it is admitted that in that case the expedition\r\nwas doomed. But it is held that such an event was not to be feared.\r\nReckless pluck is abundant in the British Navy, but expert knowledge of\r\nthe tides and shoals in these waters is utterly lacking. The British\r\ncharts are of no value, and there is no evidence (he reports) that the\r\nsubject has been studied in any way by the British Admiralty. Let me\r\nremark here, that I believe Mr \xe2\x80\x9cDavies\xe2\x80\x99s\xe2\x80\x9d views, as expressed in the\r\nearlier chapters, when they were still among the great estuaries, are\r\nall absolutely sound. The \xe2\x80\x9cchannel theory\xe2\x80\x9d, though it only bore\r\nindirectly on the grand issue before them, was true, and should be laid\r\nto heart, or I should not have wasted space on it.\r\n\r\nOne word more, in conclusion. There is an axiom, much in fashion now,\r\nthat there is no fear of an invasion of the British Isles, because if\r\nwe lose command of the sea, we can be starved\xe2\x80\x94a cheaper and surer way\r\nof reducing us to submission. It is a loose, valueless axiom, but by\r\nsheer repetition it is becoming an article of faith. It implies that\r\n\xe2\x80\x9ccommand of the sea\xe2\x80\x9d is a thing to be won or lost definitely; that we\r\nmay have it to-day and lose it for ever to-morrow. On the contrary, the\r\nchances are that in anything like an even struggle the command of the\r\nsea will hang in the balance for an indefinite time. And even against\r\ngreat odds, it would probably be impossible for our enemies so to bar\r\nthe avenues of our commerce, so to blockade the ports of our extensive\r\ncoast-line, and so to overcome the interest which neutrals will have in\r\nsupplying us, as to bring us to our knees in less than two years,\r\nduring which time we can be recuperating and rebuilding from our unique\r\ninternal resources, and endeavouring to regain command.\r\n\r\nNo; the better axiom is that nothing short of a successful invasion\r\ncould finally compel us to make peace. Our hearts are stout, we hope;\r\nbut facts are facts; and a successful raid, such as that here sketched,\r\nif you will think out its consequences, must appal the stoutest heart.\r\nIt was checkmated, but others may be conceived. In any case, we know\r\nthe way in which they look at these things in Germany.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nPostscript (March 1903)\r\n\r\n\r\nIt so happens that while this book was in the press a number of\r\nmeasures have been taken by the Government to counteract some of the\r\nvery weaknesses and dangers which are alluded to above. A Committee of\r\nNational Defence has been set up, and the welcome given to it was a\r\ntruly extraordinary comment on the apathy and confusion which it is\r\ndesigned to supplant. A site on the Forth has been selected for a new\r\nNorth Sea naval base\xe2\x80\x94an excellent if tardy decision; for ten years or\r\nso must elapse before the existing anchorage becomes in any sense a\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cbase\xe2\x80\x9d. A North Sea fleet has also been created\xe2\x80\x94another good measure;\r\nbut it should be remembered that its ships are not modern, or in the\r\nleast capable of meeting the principal German squadrons under the\r\ncircumstances supposed above.\r\n\r\nLastly, a Manning Committee has (among other matters) reported vaguely\r\nin favour of a Volunteer Reserve. There is no means of knowing what\r\nthis recommendation will lead to; let us hope not to the fiasco of the\r\nlast badly conceived experiment. Is it not becoming patent that the\r\ntime has come for training all Englishmen systematically either for the\r\nsea or for the rifle?\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n*** END OF THE PROJECT GUTENBERG EBOOK THE RIDDLE OF THE SANDS ***\r\n\r\nUpdated editions will replace the previous one--the old editions will\r\nbe renamed.\r\n\r\nCreating the works from print editions not protected by U.S. copyright\r\nlaw means that no one owns a United States copyright in these works,\r\nso the Foundation (and you!) can copy and distribute it in the\r\nUnited States without permission and without paying copyright\r\nroyalties.
